                   Selected Books by the Author
1. 3D Unconditional Security—graphical overview of principle perspectives regarding unconditional security.
2. 4D Forgiveness—examines four levels of biblical forgiveness and explains how it is possible for God to
    forgive and forget our sin in one stratum while not forgiving or forgetting our sin in another stratum.
3. Breaking the Rocking Horse—introductory book regarding the distinction between having eternal life as a
    gift versus obtaining it as a reward in relation to the outer darkness and the parable of the soils.
4. Carnal Corinth—an affirmation of the categorical existence of carnal Corinthian Christians.
5. Destined for Salvation—evaluates five major models for interpreting 1Thess 5:9-10 and takes the passage
    as promising an unconditional rapture (and thus unconditional security) to all believers in Christ, even to
    those who are morally asleep. However, an incentive for being morally awake is provided by making
    salvation (in the form of millennial and eternal rulership) contingent upon being morally alert.
6. Fallen from Grace but Not from Perfection—an advanced defense of unconditional security which affirms
    that believers can fall from grace but not fall from perfection.
7. Is God a Communist?—by comparing Paul’s teaching of the workmen with Jesus’ parable of the laborers,
    this book refutes communistic views of Heaven which think that Jesus is building heavenly mansions in
    New Jerusalem for all believers regardless of the way they live.
8. John in Living Color—a color-coded translation and reference manual of the Gospel of John that focuses
    on the grammatical and aspectual indications of unconditional security.
9. Like a Tree Planted by the Water—expounds upon the OT background for the outer darkness by examining
    the precursor texts in Psalms, especially Ps 1-2.
10. Mere Christianity and Moral Christianity—a more comprehensive discussion regarding perseverance (and
    other performance-related themes) as being needed for rewards in Heaven rather than for entrance into
    Heaven. This book provides both introductory material and advanced treatments.
11. Monogamous Sex in Heaven!—at the same time in which the present author was reading books by Marrow
    and Kreeft posing that sexual intimacy continues in Heaven, he discovered that his wife had stage-3 breast
    cancer. As a therapy to help him deal with this news, he poured himself into research to see if Marrow and
    Kreeft might be correct, thereby enabling him to believe that no matter what happened, the best was yet to
    come. This is the conclusion and comfort of this book.
12. New Birth by the Sovereign Wind—refutes the Calvinistic interpretation of Jn 3:16 that limits God’s love
    to the elect. An excursus deals with the Calvinistic claim from Jn 3:3 that one must be born again before
    one can see (i.e., believe) the gospel. An additional excursus entertains a possible duality to what it means
    to see the kingdom.
13. Predestination and the Open View—a discussion of predestination that focuses on free will and the book
    of life from an open view.
14. Rewards are Eternal—is a defense of the eternal nature of biblical rewards against the proposition that
    Rev 22:5 indicates that all believers will rule in the eternal state and against the premise that Rev 21:4
    proves that misthological death will no longer exist in the eternal state.
15. Sealed and Secure—a thorough defense of a popular argument for unconditional security that also defends
    the premise of unconditional promises.
16. The Race of Grace—introductory book in which performance-related themes (such as perseverance) are
    considered essential for rewards in Heaven. Entrance into Heaven, in contrast, is regarded as free. A strong
    defense of unconditional security derived from believers being given to Jesus is a strength of this book.
17. The Outer Darkness—a magnum opus that ties together the author’s arguments for unconditional security
    and conditional rewards.
18. Woolly Wolves and Woolless Sheep—a rejection of the notion that profession of salvation is proven genuine
    by the way a person lives. This book focuses on the parable of the sheep and the goats and shows the
    inadequacy of conditional security in interpreting this parable.

             For these and other books by the author, see http://misthology.org/?page_id=35.
                      For additional material, see www.UnconditionalSecurity.org.

    Chapter 1. The Heavenly Sanctuary and Temple
        Introduction
         The assertion has been made repeatedly in my writings that the Heavenly City is part of Heaven, not all of
Heaven.1 This position was explored in detail in MMP4 and MMP5, along with the postulation of even higher
dimensions. The present treatment in MMP6 will advance that discussion without repeating it, demonstrating that
the 4D City is just a part of the 4D Heaven and that the 4D Heaven is a subdimension of a higher dimension.
         Of course, the very fact that New Jerusalem is said to come down out of Heaven from God strongly suggests
that the Heavenly City is only a part of Heaven (Rev 3:12; 21:2,10). The same affirmation could be made regarding
the temple (of God) which is in Heaven (Rev 11:19; 14:17). The Heavenly Temple is only part of Heaven. Moreover,
the Heavenly Temple might be distinct from the Heavenly City, thereby indicating multiple structures in Heaven.
Before exploring that possibility, let us deal with misthological certainties.2


        Heavenly City
       As to the Heavenly City only being part of Heaven and requiring misthological entrance, Seymour correctly
makes multiple deductions regarding Rev 22:14:

               When we come to verse 14 of Chapter 22, we learn that those who do the Lord’s
      commandments—in other words, those who obey Him—may do two things. [1] They may eat of the
      tree of life and [2] they may enter in through the gates of the New Jerusalem. These obviously
      are two great privileges that the obedient believer shall have. But remember, the tree of life is in
      the city and the city comes down from Heaven. The city is not Heaven; it is only a part of Heaven.
      Christ said, “In My Father’s house are many mansions; if it were not so, I would have told you. I go
      to prepare a place for you” (John 14:2).
               The New Jerusalem is the most glorious mansion of Heaven, but it is not the only one.
      And those who know the Lord and are obedient to Him will have the right to enter in through



1
  For various logical and experiential reasons, MMP4 and MMP5 discussed various planets in the heavenly realm.
Indeed, it was noted from NDEs that some people describe the Heavenly City as sitting on a heavenly planet. Some
even call it Planet Heaven. My preference, however, is to call it Planet Paradise. In addition to the arguments
advanced in those earlier studies, let it be noted that some interpreters, for exegetical reasons, believe that the better
country of Heb 11:16 refers to a heavenly planet (upon which the Heavenly City sits). Dake is one such writer. He
labels the planet where God dwells as Heaven: “the planet called Heaven where God dwells.” Doing so gives the
impression that Planet Heaven is the third heaven. I would say instead that God dwells on a planet in the third
heaven and call that planet, Planet Paradise, in order to avoid giving the false impression that it is the only planet
in the heavenly realm. After all, as Dake acknowledges, “the third Heaven is called the Paradise where God lives.”
Finis Jennings Dake, God’s Plan for Man: Revealing God’s Perfect Plan for All Creation, expanded index edition
(Lawrenceville, GA: Dake Publishing, 1977), 834.
2
  Soteriology is based on two biblical Greek words, soteria (salvation) and logos (teaching), and correspondingly
refers to the biblical teaching about salvation. Although the biblical concept of salvation includes many dimensions
other than deliverance from Hell, it is this aspect of salvation to which writers frequently refer when they use the
word soteriology. Thus, salvation from Hell (or, more specifically, from eternal damnation in the Lake of Fire) will
be referred to as soteriological salvation. In contrast, misthology is a coined term derived from the Greek word
misthos (reward) and logos (teaching) and refers to the doctrine of temporal and eternal rewards but is used
primarily in reference to eternal rewards. Misthological themes pertain to eternal rewards. Misthologist refers to an
advocate of unconditional soteriological security who has a sufficient degree of misthological competency so as to
recognize that the biblical teaching regarding the outer darkness pertains to misthological themes so that salvation
from the outer darkness depicts conditional misthological security.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 2
        the gates of the New Jerusalem and also to eat the 12 manner of fruit of the tree of life. We cannot
        say for certain what that entails, but it is obviously a great privilege.3

Most pertinent to the subject matter of the present book, Seymour makes two spatial affirmations in this statement:
(1) the Heavenly City is only a part of Heaven, and (2) the Heavenly City is only one of such
mansions/structures/cities in Heaven. Drawing upon a very simple illustration from MMP2, we can picture New
Jerusalem below as coming down from the third heaven (pictured as the outer higher-dimensional realm of the
multiverse), passing through the multiverse, coming to our universe, then passing through the second heaven (that
is our universe), to reach our first heavens, that is, the skies above our planet. In subsequent studies in this series,
such as MMP4 and MMP5, we have also confirmed the Heavenly City is not only just a part of Heaven but also
only one of the cities in the heavenly realm. Seymour is correct in his basic spatial affirmations.
                            Illustration 1. New Jerusalem Comes Down out of Heaven




3
    Richard A. Seymour, “Examining Difficult Passages Concerning Eternal Security,” CTSJ 14, no. 1 (2009): 64.

Additionally, Seymour makes two misthological affirmations: (1) Only obedient believers enter by the gates into
the Heavenly City, and (2) only obedient believers eat of the Tree of Life. These latter two affirmations,
however, conflict with his discussion that proceeded it, in which he erroneously supplied a soteriological identity
for overcomers in Revelation, as follows:

         Revelation 2:7
                 To him who overcomes I will give to eat from the tree of life, which is in the midst of the
         Paradise of God.

         As is too often done, some interpret a word so as to infer that a person must do more than believe in
         Christ to be assured of salvation. The word in this case is “overcomes.”
                  I have heard people say something like the following: “Only those who love the Lord Jesus
         with all their hearts and really serve Him faithfully—the overcomers—shall eat of the tree of life.”
         I agree that only overcomers will eat of the tree of life. The important question is, who are
         overcomers and how do they overcome? We answer Scripture with Scripture, interpreting the
         somewhat unclear by that which is clear. 1 John 5:5 answers the question, “Who is an overcomer?”
         The answer: “Who is he who overcomes the world, but he who believes that Jesus is the Son of God?”
         1 John 5:4 answers the second question: “How do they overcome?” The answer: “For whatever is born
         of God overcomes the world. And this is the victory that has overcome the world—our faith.” So the
         overcomers are believers. How do they overcome? By faith.
                  Overcoming, then, is not—as some would have us believe—a great struggle for more and
         more goodness; it is simply being born into God’s family. (Italics his, 62–63.)

On the contrary, as Seymour makes it clear in the rest of his article, not all believers are obedient. This admission
should have led him to a very clear conclusion.
                                         Illustration 2. Tree of Life Syllogism
      1. Only obedient believers eat of the Tree of Life. (A premise granted by Seymour.)
      2. Not all believers are obedient. (A fact acknowledged by Seymour.)
      3. Therefore, not all believers eat of the Tree of Life. (The conclusion Seymour should have made.)

As it is, Seymour is misthologically inconsistent. He fails to deduce that not all believers will eat of the Tree of
Life, even though this is the logical extension of his argument. Such is the case of those who argue that overcoming
is a soteriological theme in Revelation. Trying to make Rev 3:12 soteriological, for example, is at odds with
acknowledging that Rev 22:14 is misthological. On the other hand, the attempts by Stegall to circumvent this
problem by making Rev 22:14 a soteriological text are demonstrably defective.4 At least Seymour is superior to
Stegall in acknowledging the misthological nature of Rev 22:14. So, let us proceed with a summation of Seymour’s
position regarding these three texts.
                                           Illustration 3. Seymour’s Wedge

         The natural reading of the texts would not juxtapose the passages, inconsistently taking the passages on the
left as promising gifts and the passage on the right as promising rewards. How can Seymour assert that all believers

Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 4
will eat of the tree, but only those believers who are obedient will eat of it in the City, while at the same time
acknowledging that not all believers are obedient and that not all believers will qualify to eat of the Tree of Life in
the City? How can Seymour assert that all believers have the name of the Heavenly City written on them and are
made pillars in this Temple City and yet at the same time acknowledge only faithful believers have a right to enter
the City by the gates and eat of the Tree of Life? His attempt to drive a wedge between (1) the texts on the left (by
treating them as soteriological) and (2) the text on the right (by treating it as misthological) is thoroughly
unpersuasive.
         Misthopologists have an easy time answering such questions, of course.5 Simply be consistent and
acknowledge that only faithful believers are overcomers in the context of Revelation; consequently, only faithful
believers enter the City and eat from the Tree of Life. Unfaithful believers are excluded from the Heavenly City,
not from Heaven itself. Unfaithful believers do not eat from the Tree of Life since eating from the tree is a reward
limited to faithful believers.


        Heavenly Temple
                                                   Rev 21:22
        The term I employed above, Temple City, is based in part on the association made within Rev 3:12 between
the Temple and the City. These terms seem to be referring to the same edifice—New Jerusalem. However, an enigma
occurs when we encounter Rev 21:22, which makes this comment about New Jerusalem when it comes down out
of Heaven: “And I saw no temple in it, for the Lord God, the Almighty, and the Lamb, are its temple.” Overstreet
acknowledges the problem:

               In the Book of Revelation John referred to God’s temple in heaven fifteen times in twelve
      verses. Therefore this statement in 21:22 that he saw “no temple” in the heavenly city, the new
      Jerusalem, at first seems incongruous. That the Jews anticipated a temple in the renewed Jerusalem is
      evident….
               The statement that there will be no temple in the new Jerusalem is significant since 3:12
      directly connects the “temple of My God” with “the city of My God, the new Jerusalem, which comes
      down out of heaven from My God.” How can this apparent discrepancy be reconciled?6

Four scholastic responses to this problem are noted by Overstreet:

    (1) Ignore it.
    (2) Remove it (by posing that the temple is silently done away with in the eternal state).
    (3) Mythologize it (by posing that it is just a symbol).
    (4) Retain it (by equating it).

Overstreet chooses the latter recourse:

              John specifically detailed the dimensions of the new Jerusalem in Revelation 21:16….John
      describes the new Jerusalem as a perfect cube, the pattern for the cubic design of the Mosaic tabernacle.
      The entire city of the new Jerusalem therefore is itself the temple. “Since the Holy of Holies was
      the place where the Shekinah resided, this is especially appropriate for the celestial city.” The presence
      of God in the Shekinah glory has its final and ultimate counterpart in the new Jerusalem, “for the Lord
      God the Almighty and the Lamb are its temple” (21:22). (Pp. 461–462.)

         In discussing MMP5 with my friend, Doug Berner, he proposed the second solution, that the Heavenly
Temple exists somewhere in Heaven outside of the Heavenly City but is done away with in the eternal state (which
is a variation of Overstreet’s second solution that disassociates the temple with the City). The primary problem

  Misthopology refers to the doctrine of the Reward City (misthos + polis). Misthopological themes pertain to it.
Misthopologists are misthologists who advocate a reward view of the City.
6
  R. Larry Overstreet, “The Temple of God in the Book of Revelation,” BibSac 166 (2009): 460.

with that proposal is that the temple is strongly associated with the Heavenly City in Rev 3:12. Overstreet overlooks
the misthological limitation to overcomers but correctly notes the association of the temple with the Heavenly City
in both 3:12 and 21:22:

      John asserted that all believers have an equal place in God’s temple. In this context, with the temple’s
      immediate connection with the new Jerusalem which will descend from heaven, it seems clear that this
      is “the heavenly temple, the dwelling-place of God with his eschatological people.” It is intriguing that
      John’s first mention of the temple of God in Revelation 3:12, and his last mention of the temple in
      21:22, both directly connect the word “temple” with the new Jerusalem.
               Each believer is metaphorically a “pillar” in God’s temple. All the original readers of
      Revelation were cognizant of pillars in temples. They would naturally expect a temple to have pillars.
      Pillars were symbols of permanence and stability, as evidenced by the fact that many of the ancient
      pillars are still standing today. “To a city that had experienced devastating earthquakes which caused
      people to flee into the countryside and establish temporary dwellings there, the promise of permanence
      within the New Jerusalem would have a special meaning.” (Pp. 454-455.)

         Contrary to Overstreet, all believers do not have “an equal place in God’s temple.” Only overcomers will
have the place of prominence, denoted as being a pillar. This is a misthological promise of prominence and
permanence, not a soteriological promise of equality. Nevertheless, the accurate parts of Overstreet’s statement
regarding the association of the temple with New Jerusalem pose a problem for Berner’s proposal of disassociation.
         Consequently, as Berner and I were discussing various possibilities, I counter conjectured that (1) the
Heavenly Temple might exist in Heaven inside the 4D City but is removed in the eternal state (which is a variation
of Overstreet’s second solution that associates the temple with the City), or that (2) the Heavenly Temple might
exist in the 4D City but not exist in the 3D City during the eternal state (which is a variation of Overstreet’s fourth
solution).
         Notwithstanding, Berner’s insistence that the matter deserves a closer examination than what I posed in
MMP5 led to my follow-up investigation within the present book, MMP6. References to the temple within
Revelation, leading up to its finale in Rev 21:22, will be surveyed in this chapter in the sequence presented by
Revelation.

                                                    Rev 3:12
                                                     Misthological
          The first mention of the temple occurs in Rev 3:12. Overcomers are promised that they will be permanent
pillars in the temple of God if they hold fast. The potential of believers losing their presumptive crown in Rev 3:11
is clear. The text talks of this future reward as already presently possessed but as necessary to be retained for its
future realization. Similarly, the presumption herein is that holding fast is the form of overcoming being described
in Rev 3:12, so that one must overcome by holding fast in order to qualify for the rewards discussed in that verse.
      11
        ‘I am coming quickly; hold fast what you have, in order that no one take your crown. 12 ‘He who
      overcomes, I will make him a pillar in the temple of My God, and he will not go out from it anymore;
      and I will write upon him the name of My God, and the name of the city of My God, the new Jerusalem,
      which comes down out of heaven from My God, and My new name. (Rev 3:11-12)

        The crown is obviously a reward that can be forfeited, a misthological issue, as even Stegall admits. He
argues, nevertheless, that overcoming is a separate matter, a soteriological issue, dealing with gifts. Thus, he drives
a wedge between v. 11 and v. 12, treating v. 11 as misthological and v. 12 as soteriological (pp. 449-457). The
strained nature of his wedge is apparent in the similar rulership texts (Rev 2:26-27 and 3:21), wherein having
authority over the nations and ruling them with a rod of iron and sitting with Christ on His throne are benefits
promised to overcomers.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 6
                                     Illustration 4. Stegall’s Crown-Throne Wedge
               Mistholic per Stegall                                  Soteric per Stegall
    11
      I am coming quickly; hold fast what you He who overcomes, I will grant to him to sit down with Me on
    have, in order that no one take your crown. My throne, as I also overcame and sat down with My Father on
    (Rev 3:11)                                  His throne. (Rev 3:21)

        One justification that Stegall gives for his juxtaposition is to assert that both verses would not be talking
about rewards since this would be redundant:

         But if only a segment of believers were overcomers in Revelation 2‒3, and the seven overcomer
         statements were really offers of reward for obedient Christian living, then the two “crown” passages
         in Revelation 2:10 and 3:11 would actually be redundant since in the following verses (2:11; 3:12)
         Christ would be promising rewards again “to him who overcomes.”7 (SCFOD, 449-450.)

Is Stegall writing tongue in cheek? Amazingly, he expects his explanation to be taken seriously! Far from being
redundant, the passages are consistent and give multifaceted amplification and additional information about the
rewards being promised. Repetition with amplification is a good teaching technique. I use it. Stegall uses it. Why
think that Jesus would not use it?
         The natural reading of the texts would not juxtapose the passages, supposedly taking the passage on the left
as promising a reward and the passage on the right as promising a gift. Yet this is Stegall’s approach. On the right,
he has made rulership with Christ a soteriological benefit given to all believers unconditionally as a gift, regardless
of the way they live. Doing so is at odds with his admission that the crown is a reward that is conditioned on
believers holding fast. His argument reduces the crown to little more than a head ornament. According to his model,
sitting with Christ on His throne, and thus rulership with Christ, is given freely. So, the earned regality associated
with the crown is a travesty. He makes sitting on the throne a gift but having a crown a reward. The more natural
understanding is to associate sitting on the throne with wearing a crown. Both denote rulership—a rulership
rewarded to faithful believers. One should not try to drive a wedge between the two promises.
         If it were not for the serious tone of his writing, one would think that he was joking when he continues to
defend his position by saying:

         In keeping with the biblical distinction between salvation and rewards, it is better to interpret the two
         crown passages (Rev. 2:10; 3:11) as references to rewards and the following two overcomer
         statements (2:11; 3:12) as promises of future salvation blessings for all believers designed to give
         believers incentive to persevere in the present conflict because of guaranteed future victory.
         (SCFOD, 450.)

As I read his explanation, my thoughts are, “Seriously? You’ve got to be kidding!” However, he is not speaking in
jolly but folly. He uses repetition with amplification to restate the matter later:

         “Since you are already overcomers by your position in Me, the Overcoming One, and you are
         guaranteed ultimate spiritual victory (glorification) on that basis, won’t you fight the good fight of
         faith now in the arena of this earthly life?” Therefore the overcomer statements in Revelation 2‒3
         should be viewed as gracious, promissory incentives to live faithfully for the Lord in light of these
         guaranteed salvation blessings awaiting every believer in Christ. (SFOD, 468-469)

The basis of his argument is splendid. I have made it many times in my writings. I call it a be-what-you-are
exhortation. In TOD, I gave a list of forty-nine such passages. I then stated:

                 All of these exhortations are to be what you are. They are all addressed to genuine believers.
         Jesus and Paul both exhort believers that since they already are light they should be light in the world

7
 Dennis M. Rokser, Thomas L. Stegall, and Kurt Witzig, Should Christians Fear Outer Darkness? (Duluth, MN:
Grace Gospel Press, 2015).
Chapter 1. The Heavenly Sanctuary and Temple                                                                    Page 7
      in which they live (Mt 5:14-16; Eph 5:8). Since Jesus and Paul can exhort those who are light to be
      light, why can John not urge those who are overcomers to overcome?
              The statement is the basis of the exhortation. A statement of who you are as a believer is
      followed by an exhortation to be what you are. This would be consistent with my findings concerning
      overcoming in 1 John and Revelation. In 1 John the readers were told that they are overcomers. Within
      the book of Revelation, this statement is then followed by the exhortation for the readers to overcome.
      The statement of who you are is the basis for how you should act.
              Wong, however, uses reverse logic. He argues that the exhortation to overcome is the means
      by which to determine whether you are a believer. If you overcome, then you are a believer. If you
      do not overcome, then you are not a believer. Overcome because if you do not overcome, then you
      are not a believer. This reverse logic is wrong because it treats the exhortation as a promise and
      premise.

        In other words, my fiftieth be-what-you-are affirmation in the context of that discussion would be: you are
overcomers, so overcome (1Jn 5:4; Rev 2:7,11,17,26; 3:5,12,21). Despite our joint premise, Stegall’s logic is just
as wrong as Wong’s. All believers are overcomers positionally and ontologically, to be sure, but Jesus is not
exhorting them to become overcomers experientially so that they can receive the overcomer promises in Rev 2-3
unconditionally. Basically, Stegall’s model portrays Jesus as saying, “I want you to overcome because it really does
not matter if you overcome in terms of all the benefits promised to overcomers. You are going to inherit all these
overcomer promises anyway.” The disconnect in Stegall’s argument betrays Jesus’ intention and the believer’s
motivation. My argument, in contrast, portrays Jesus as saying, “I know you are overcomers positionally, but you
need to overcome experientially so that you can receive the overcomer rewards promised misthologically.”
        When it comes to Rev 2:25-28, Stegall does a triple whammy, trying to drive four wedges into Christ’s
promise:
      25
        ‘Nevertheless what you have, hold fast until I come. 26 ‘And he who overcomes, and he who keeps
      My deeds until the end, to him I will give authority over the nations; 27 and he shall rule them with a
      rod of iron, as the vessels of the potter are broken to pieces, as I also have received authority from My
      Father; 28 and I will give him the morning star. (Rev 2:25-28)

Stegall summarizes his interpretation: “This passage is requiring that the believer/overcomer who will receive the
salvation blessing of reigning with Christ (vv. 26-27) must also do good works in order to receive the reward of the
morning star (v. 28)” (SCFOD, 454). The following chart shows his approach.
                                      Illustration 5. Stegall’s Multiple Wedges
   Mistholic per Stegall                Soteric per Stegall                   Mistholic Per Stegall

   ‘Nevertheless what you 26 ‘And he who overcomes, and he who keeps My 28 And I will give him the

         Stegall has Jesus doing a flipflop, making a misthological exhortation on the left, switching to soteriological
promises in the middle that gives rulership freely to believers regardless of how they live, and then switching back
to a misthological promise on the right. Even more bizarre, Stegall attempts to cut out the comment about keeping
Jesus’s works until the end from the middle column and moves it to the column on the right. His argument,
essentially, is that the passage promises (stative) overcomers rulership with Christ even if they do not overcome
(experientially), yet he acknowledges that those overcomers who keep Jesus’s works until the end get an additional
degree of stellar glory (SCFOD, 452-453). To try and pull this off, he must drive a wedge (1) between v. 25 and
v. 26, (2) before the phrase highlighted in yellow in the center column, (3) again after the phrase, and (4) between
v. 27 and v. 27.
         My approach is much simpler: Treat the entire passage as mistholic, just like the other passages in
Revelation regarding overcoming. The crown, rulership, and stellar glory are rewards, as are being made a pillar in
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 8
the temple of God and having the various names written on you (namely, God’s name, New Jerusalem, and Jesus’
new name). Being made a pillar in this Heavenly Temple, in its association with New Jerusalem, indicates a
misthological permanence.

                                                         Spatial
         The true problem for the exegete in Rev 3:12 is not in taking it misthologically but in taking it spatially.
How can overcomers be promised that they will be permanent pillars in God’s temple and thus have a prominent
association with New Jerusalem in that capacity and yet be told in Rev 21:22 that New Jerusalem will have no
temple since the Father and Son are its temple? The same Greek word is used on each occasion, naos (temple,
sanctuary).8 Yet we need not insist that it is necessarily used in the same capacity in both verses. The NT terms for
temple (hieron, hagion, ta hagia, and naos) can be used interchangeably “with no real distinction between the terms
in either meaning or range,” and naos “is used in different ways in Rev” (Michel, “Ναός,” TDNT). Notwithstanding,
some commentaries (e.g., TPC, LCNT) specify that naos is being used in a more restrictive sense (to refer to God’s
dwelling) in contradiction to the broader term hieron (to refer to the whole complex). Even so, commentators
frequently assert that the only solution is to take the temple metaphorically in Rev 3:12 so that reconciling the
passages is not necessary (so BECNT, HNTC, ICC, NAC, TNTC). Such commentators are throwing in the towel
too quickly. More reasonably, Govett poses a duality: (1) a “spiritual building” and (2) a “real material temple”
(GOR, emphasis his).
         Four options are worthy of contemplation at this juncture: (1) One seemingly easy way to harmonize the
passages spatially would be to pose, as Overstreet does, that the temple in Rev 3:12 is New Jerusalem. Obviously,
John would not see a separate temple in (en) New Jerusalem in that case.9 (2) Another possibility would be to
propose that New Jerusalem is only part of the Heavenly Temple. One need not pose a temple inside New Jerusalem
in that case either. (3) Yet another possibility is that the Heavenly City is outside the Heavenly Temple. (4) Still,
another possibility is that all three of the above potentialities are true and that none of these options requires that
the temple be done away with.
         Posing a model that maintains the permanence of the temple is desirable to match the promise regarding
being made a permanent pillar in that temple. Osborne (BECNT) is on the right track:

      Yet in the New Jerusalem there will be no need for a temple, for the city itself is the Holy of Holies
      (see on 21:16) and “the Lord God Almighty and the Lamb are its temple” (21:22). There is no
      contradiction between 21:22 and 3:12, for the “pillar in the temple” here is metaphorical for the
      permanent place the believer has in the final kingdom.

Yes, being a pillar in the temple is a metaphor. Yet this acknowledgment need not mean that the temple is a
metaphor. Boxall (BNTC) rightly uses kaleidoscope to explain the nature of the reconciliation between the passages:

               There seems to be some contradiction here with the final vision of the new Jerusalem in
      Revelation 21–22, for John will see no sanctuary in that city, yet the victor is here promised that he
      will never leave it. To regard John as contradicting himself, however, is to fail to appreciate the
      kaleidoscopic nature of Revelation’s apocalyptic imagery. The city itself merges almost imperceptibly
      into a temple, as the presence of God and the Lamb are revealed inside it (21:22; it is a perfect cube,
      like Solomon’s Holy of Holies: 21:16, cf. 1 Kgs 6:20).




8
  Revelation uses naos for every occurrence of temple.
9
  Walvoord (BKC) unnecessarily makes the temple symbolic of heaven, when he could have just left it equated with
the entirety of the 3D City: “This is of course symbolic of the permanent place in heaven for believers, referred to
here as the temple of God. The entire New Jerusalem will be the ultimate temple (21:22).” Other interpreters
frequently equate the temple with the kingdom in Rev 3:12. To be sure, prominence in the kingdom is a reward
granted to the overcomers. Some interpret it as being Jesus’ body. Yet, one would not interpret Rev 21:22 as
meaning that John did not see Heaven, or the kingdom, or Jesus’ body in the City. My spatial proposals are superior
in terms of consistency.
Chapter 1. The Heavenly Sanctuary and Temple                                                                    Page 9
                               Illustration 6. Kaleidoscopic View of the Temple City




Boxall’s kaleidoscopic suggestion may be used with Venn diagrams, like an optometrist using an interchangeable
lens when checking one’s eyesight, to illustrate my perspective. If 3D New Jerusalem functionally becomes the
Holy of Holies for the 4D City and descends out of the 4D City to Earth and yet is still connected to the 4D City by
a teleportational overlap and simultaneously serves in a similar capacity to the 5D City, then all three of my above
spatial proposals will prove to be true. Additionally, being metaphorically made a 5D pillar in the literal 5D Temple
will naturally entail being a 4D pillar in the 4D City and a 3D pillar in the 3 City without requiring a separate temple
inside the 3D City. Thus, my fourth option is also viable. These options will be tentatively developed herein as we
proceed. Until this point in my writings, I have been content with a nebulous assumption of the first spatial
understanding noted above, supposing that the temple in Rev 3:12 is New Jerusalem. The present study will
investigate the feasibility of a more complex model in order to better explain all the facets of the data.

                                                     Rev 7:15
         In commenting on Rev 7:15, which is the next passage in Revelation regarding the temple, Seiss
(Revelation) observes: “John saw no temple in the New Jerusalem; but the New Jerusalem is not all of heaven.”10
True. The main point of this chapter is that New Jerusalem is just a part of Heaven. Still, where exactly in Heaven
would the Heavenly Temple be located if not in New Jerusalem? To expound upon my speculations regarding the
fourth solution posed by Overstreet, note that (1) the Heavenly Temple in Rev 7:15 is associated with the tabernacle
and the throne of God and that (2) it is a place where overcoming martyrs will serve God day and night, apparently
during the Tribulation and on into the Eternal State: “For this reason, they are before the throne of God; and they
serve Him day and night in His temple; and He who sits on the throne shall spread His tabernacle over them.” The
temporal expression, day and night, might simply be idiomatic, denoting continual service without necessarily
implying that it is going on in the Heavenly City suspended above the Earth. On the other hand, the temporal-
terrestrial link goes back to the earliest times in the OT: “While the earth remains, seedtime and harvest, and cold
and heat, and summer and winter, and day and night shall not cease” (Gen 8:22). The accusations by Satan, going
on day and night in Rev 12:10, would be quite compatible with the Heavenly City being in a cloaked state above
Earth during the Tribulation while the Bema proceedings are proceeding, as posed in MMP5. The counterpoint
might be raised that the torment of those cast into the Lake of Fire going on day and night (Rev 20:10) would be in
a remote dimension, probably completely outside the heavenly realm and multiverse. Notwithstanding, this remote
torment might be observable day and night on Earth via a portal outside the earthly Jerusalem, as posed in MMP5.
Therefore, although the Heavenly City might not experience day and night, a spatial location of the Heavenly
Temple above the Earth could be hinted at by this temporal reference. In any event, various reasons were posed in
MMP5 for locating the Heavenly City above Earth in a cloaked state during the Tribulation.
10
  J. A. Seiss, The Apocalypse: Lectures on the Book of Revelation (Grand Rapids, MI: Zondervan Publishing House,
1975).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                       Page 10

                                                    Rev 11:1-2
       The reference to the temple of God in Rev 11:1 is problematic since its outer court will be trampled
underfoot for forty-two months in Rev 11:2. Overstreet surveys some simple solutions:

               Commentators differ as to whether this should be interpreted literally in reference to God’s
      heavenly temple, or the second Jerusalem temple, or a rebuilt tribulation temple, or the professing
      apostate church. It seems clear that the text “cannot refer to a heavenly temple because the context
      portrays the Gentiles trampling down the surroundings.”48 Dispensationalists commonly “understand
      this to imply that during the great tribulation the Jewish temple will be rebuilt in Jerusalem, and Jewish
      worship will be reinstituted there, and that it is here that, in the middle of the tribulation, the Antichrist
      will take ‘his seat in the temple of God, proclaiming himself to be God’ (2 Thess. 2:4). They understand
      the reference to the holy city to mean literal, earthly Jerusalem. (Pp. 455-456.)

However, the fact that the temple of God is measured while its courts are unmeasured and thus trampled suggests a
disconnect between the temple and its courts. Therefore, in RAE I posed:

               The overcomer is given permanent misthological association with the Heavenly Temple and
      Heavenly City (Rev 3:12), though on the basis of Rev 21:22 some would question whether there is a
      literal Heavenly Temple and thus argue that perhaps Rev 7:15 refers to Ezekiel’s temple, the millennial
      temple, and that the references in Rev 11:19 and related passages are symbolic. Serving day and night
      in the temple need not refer to millennial service in Ezekiel’s earthly temple, however. Rather, they
      will not need rest as they minister continuously in the Heavenly City while the Earth experiences day
      and night. There is an antithesis between type and antitype during that tribulation when the earthly
      temple (the outer court) is desecrated by antichrist (Rev 11:1-2). The earthly temple may be considered
      the outer court of the heavenly temple. In some passages the temple (naos) seems to refer simply to
      the Holy of Holies (e.g., 1Cor 6:19), which would be the case in a number of passages in Revelation
      (3:12; 7:15; 21:22), though at times it might possibly include the [outer] Holy Place also (Rev 11:19;
      14:15,17; 15:5-8).

If the temple of God in Rev 11:1 is the Heavenly Temple located above Earth during the Tribulation, it would be
measurable and thus protected, which would be true of New Jerusalem, whereas the earthly temple is the outer court
(so Govett, GOR).

                                                    Rev 11:19
         In any case, locating the Heavenly Temple above the clouds over Earth would certainly be feasible in the
next clear reference to the Heavenly Temple: “And the temple of God which is in heaven was opened; and the ark
of His covenant appeared in His temple, and there were flashes of lightning and sounds and peals of thunder and an
earthquake and a great hailstorm” (Rev 11:19). In commenting on this verse, Patterson (NAC) notes: “The dilemma,
avoided by most commentators, is to explain why there is a temple in chap. 11 with an ark of the covenant and there
is not a temple in John’s vision of the heavenly city in chap. 21.” One suggestion Patterson makes but does not
pursue “is that two different [heavenly] edifices are in view, one that is present in heaven at the conclusion of
the tribulation period and perhaps throughout the millennium, while the temple of chap. 21 belongs entirely to the
heavenly kingdom.” This proposition is a blending of options #2 and #4 posed by Overstreet. The proposition of
two heavenly temples has merit and is worthy of further contemplation. Govett (GOR) takes this position. I will
offer a modification to the hypothesis of two heavenly temples as we proceed. For now, observe that the
tribulational heavenly temple need not necessarily be fully equated with the millennial-eternal heavenly temple.

                                                  Rev 14:15,17
        In the next passage, the Heavenly Temple is associated with sitting in the clouds, which would be an apt
description of the proposed location of New Jerusalem above Earth during the time of the Tribulation:
      15
        And another angel came out of the temple, crying out with a loud voice to Him who sat on the
      cloud, “Put in your sickle and reap, because the hour to reap has come, because the harvest of the earth
Chapter 1. The Heavenly Sanctuary and Temple                                                                 Page 11
               16
      is ripe.” And He who sat on the cloud swung His sickle over the earth; and the earth was reaped.
      17
         And another angel came out of the temple which is in heaven, and he also had a sharp sickle.

On the other hand, the fact that the angel came out of the Heavenly Temple and cried out with a loud voice to Him
who sat on the clouds could be interpreted as suggesting some distance between the angel, and thus some distance
between the Heavenly Temple, and the One sitting on the cloud, and thus Earth.

                                             Rev 15:5-16:1,17
         The next passage in Revelation regarding the Heavenly Temple is somewhat problematic for proposing that
it is New Jerusalem located above Earth during the Tribulation:
      5
        After these things I looked, and the temple of the tabernacle of testimony in heaven was opened,
      6
        and the seven angels who had the seven plagues came out of the temple, clothed in linen, clean and
      bright, and girded around their breasts with golden girdles. 7 And one of the four living creatures gave
      to the seven angels seven golden bowls full of the wrath of God, who lives forever and ever. 8 And
      the temple was filled with smoke from the glory of God and from His power; and no one was able
      to enter the temple until the seven plagues of the seven angels were finished. 1 And I heard a loud
      voice from the temple, saying to the seven angels, “Go and pour out the seven bowls of the wrath of
      God into the earth.” (Rev 15:5-16:1, see also 16:17)

Granted, I could pose that access to the inside of New Jerusalem is shut off during this time of interior smoke and
that everyone is at the Bema stadium atop the Heavenly City. But this proposal is rather strained. Would not those
who have mansions inside the Heavenly City be able to access those mansions during this time? Surely so.
Consequently, two seemingly mutually exclusive propositions for the location of the Temple of the Tabernacle of
Testimony (TTT) are suggested: (1) TTT is either a part of New Jerusalem, which immediately poses a problem
with Rev 21:22, or (2) TTT is in Heaven but outside of New Jerusalem. A third possibility is that somehow both
propositions are true and thus not truly mutually exclusive in all points. Overstreet’s observation may lead to a
solution:

              The temple’s description as the “tabernacle of testimony,” is “an appositional phrase: ‘the
      sanctuary, which is the tabernacle.’ The temple is ‘the tabernacle of testimony’ as the heavenly
      equivalent of the tabernacle that was with Israel in the wilderness.” More specifically it refers to “the
      innermost part of the temple, the Temple of the temple, the Holy of holies, the deepest centre of
      the dwelling-place and throne of God.” (Italics his, 458.)

If Overstreet is correct, the TTT is the innermost part of the temple, not all of the temple. Since the earthly
temple had various parts and is patterned after the Heavenly Temple, it stands to reason that the Heavenly Temple
has various parts as well.


          Conclusion
         The discussion thus far has led to the plausible conclusion that the Temple of the Tabernacle of Testimony
(TTT) is the innermost part of the Heavenly Temple, thus the heart of New Jerusalem. After all, as noted
above regarding Rev 21:22, Overstreet goes on to equate New Jerusalem with the Heavenly Temple. However, this
solution causes an obvious problem. There is no temple in New Jerusalem! So, unless one is going to insist that the
inhabitants of the City are not allowed into the City during the time it is filled with smoke, the original enigma
remains—unless we think outside the box. Rather than be boxed in by the four solutions that Overstreet has surmised
from the pens of previous writers, a two-temple modification of Overstreet’s Heavenly Temple summations will be
further explored in the next chapter.
Misthological Models Part 6   Page 12
       Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box
          Introduction
        Part of my hypothesis is that the Temple of the Tabernacle of Testimony (TTT) is in the 4D City but not in
the 3D City. Although the interior of the 3D City gives immediate access to the interior of the 4D City, it does not
give immediate access to all of the 4D City. To access those additional parts of the 4D City from the 3D City, one
would first have to access the shared part from the 3D City and then proceed to the exclusive parts of the 4D City.
Being the innermost part of the temple, which is accessed through the outer part of the temple, TTT is too intimately
connected to the temple to be placed anywhere in Heaven other than inside the 4D City. Plus, TTT is the location
of God’s throne. Nonetheless, TTT has a strong association with Earth, being the location from which judgments
are poured out upon Earth and being temporally tied to Earth via day-and-night service. A model is needed that
would account for all aspects of the data.


          3D City Access from Earth to 4D City
                          Illustration 7. Three-Sectioned Temple with 4D and 3D Temples




          The illustration above repurposes the OT three-sectioned typology (discussed in MMP5) to represent the
4D City as the cubical Holy of Holies and the 3D City as the intermediate link between the 4D City and Earth.
Using the 3D City as a link to the 4D City was discussed in MMP5. The new hypothesis being considered herein is
that TTT might pose a point of overlapping connection between the two Heavenly Cities. In commenting on
Rev 21:22, Wilson draws a basic deduction regarding the outer earthly tabernacle built by Moses and the heavenly
one shown to Moses: “This pattern or blueprint was of the tabernacle which was in heaven—literal tabernacle in
heaven with a blueprint given to Moses by which he was to construct the tabernacle here on the earth.”11 This
affirmation is a good place to start. Even so, it is nothing more than a launching pad from which to pose the question,
“Why was it in three sections?” Since the earthly tabernacle was in three sections, the heavenly tabernacle must be
in three sections also because the earthly tabernacle is patterned after the heavenly tabernacle. What do they signify?
From one vantage point, they might be used to represent the connection between Earth and Heaven via the 3D City.
The earthly part of the tabernacle was on Earth. The intermediate part of the tabernacle was the 3D City. The most
elevated part of the tabernacle was the 4D City. This vantage point is pictured above.
          From another vantage point, however, the heavenly tabernacle existing in three parts might signify that the
most accessible part of it is the 3D City. The intermediate part of the tabernacle is the 4D City. Yet the most
inaccessible part of it lies in yet a higher dimension, a 7D spacetime continuum reserved for the Deity. Additional
illustrations will be developed as we proceed for this option.
          For yet another vantage point, the tabernacle might even represent the Trinity. This possibility will also be
pictured as we proceed. We will explore such possibilities as we continue. For now, let us focus on the overlap of
some of the vantage points.


11
     A. Edwin Wilson, Revelation (Hayesville, NC: Schoettle Publishing Co, 2016).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 14

        Two-Part Heavenly Temple
                                Illustration 8. TTT in Overlap of 4D and 3D Cities




          For those who may prefer a Venn diagram of the conceptualization being considered herein, the above
illustration pictures (1) the part of the of the 4D City that lies outside of the interior of the 3D City in gold at the
top, (2) the 3D City in yellow at the bottom, and (3) the shared TTT in red at the center. I have broken TTT into
two parts: HT1 and HT2.
          My hypothesis is inspired in part by the conjectures posed by Govett, among others. As noted above,
Patterson acknowledges that some interpreters believe that two different heavenly edifices are in view: HT1
(Heavenly Temple 1) is present in Heaven up until the end of the Tribulation and perhaps throughout the millennium,
while HT2 (Heavenly Temple 2) is entirely heavenly, thus more readily associated with the eternal state. My
proposition is a blending of options #2 and #4 posed by Overstreet. The proposition of two heavenly temples, HT1
and HT2, has merit and is worthy of further contemplation. Govett is representative of the second option posed by
Overstreet. Govett’s comments have varying degrees of merit but are not entirely consistent at all points and are
open to strong objections regarding certain conclusions. Here is a brief survey of Govett’s key statements on these
passages from his commentary on Revelation, with some brief interjectory remarks by me marked in red:

1. (S.v. 3:12) Saints enter Heavenly Temple (HT) during the Tribulation (cf. 7:15), but the Heavenly City (HC) is
   not shown until Rev 21. [While the HC is not revealed to Earth until the end of the Tribulation this does
   not mean that it is not cloaked above Earth during the Tribulation.]
2. (S.v. 7:15) By its very nature as a tent or tabernacle, the HT is “something which may be easily taken down,”
   and its nature as a temple means that it is removed when sin is removed at the commencement of the eternal
   state. The joys associated with being a pillar in the HT are experienced in the HC. [Tabernacle suggests
   mobility, even to the point of being able to be taken into the temple, whereas temple suggests permanence,
   a place of special access to God.]
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                     Page 15
3. (S.v. 11:19) HT is in Heaven and is concealed from Earth until it is opened during the Tribulation. [The same
   can be said of the HC.]
4. (S.v. 14:17) The notation that the HT is in Heaven insinuates that there is also an ET (Earthly Temple) on Earth.
   [Good point, indicating a duality regarding the temple.]
5. (S.v. 15:5,9) No one can enter the HT while it is filled with smoke during the Tribulation. Just as the ET (Earthly
   Tabernacle) “disappeared in the temple of the city, so does this tabernacle vanish in the temple of ‘the city of
   God.’ Its contents pass away with the passing of the millennial dispensation….the tent became the temple.”
   TTT disappears “after the millennial testimony is over.” [This is an assumption. Rather than disappear, it
   might have a change in function.]
6. (S.v. 19:7-9) The saints are in the HT throughout the Tribulation rather than the HC. At the beginning of the
   millennial kingdom, the HC descends and hangs over the Earth during the millennial kingdom. The HC does
   not descend to the surface of the Earth until the beginning of the eternal state when sin is removed. “Our position
   during the millennial is transitional; or midway between the TEMPLE, and the CITY.” “Thus during the
   millennium the temple of God exists, distinct from the city of God. After the thousand years, the city is the only
   temple” (emphasis his). [Not so. The saints are in the HC throughout the Tribulation (see MMP4 and
   MMP5) rather than necessarily in the HT. For instance, they are not in the HT while it is filled with
   smoke.]
7. (S.v. 21:3-4) “The glories of the Old Jerusalem [OJ], such as they are found in millennial times, abide in the
   New [Jerusalem, NJ] forever. Its defects and imperfections have no place….On the old earth, the city and
   temple are separated. Here the city and temple are one. NJ and the HT both exist during the Millennium, but
   John does not behold NJ until the Millennium is over. The glory of OJ is transferred to NJ. “The city is but one:
   it is the dwelling of all the risen from the dead.” “There are two descents of the city. The first from its invisible
   abode to visibility…before the millennium” (Rev 19:7-9). “During the millennium it appears to be suspended
   above the….The second is its descent to the new earth., there to abide forever, after the millennium is past.” NJ
   was previously called God’s city; now, in its new phase, it is called His tabernacle. “Now, because the
   tabernacle of God’s own building is on earth, He sanctifies men in general.” “Now the city is the tabernacle.”
   [Perhaps. Or, alternatively, the City was part of the tabernacle, the part not filled with smoke during the
   Tribulation.] “Now God is the God of all men, and He brings them into a new earth together,” rather than just
   the God of the faithful remnant. The wipe away of tears from the faces of all indicates the wiping way of rewards
   so that those blessings will now be given indiscriminately and unconditionally to “failing men.” [Not so. The
   wiping away of tears is a reward, so rewards are eternal.]
8. (S.v. 21:22) The throne of God is in the HT during the Tribulation. The elders give thanks to God in the HT
   (11:16-19). “There are two Jerusalems—an earthly and heavenly. There is a temple, partly on earth, partly
   in heaven, distinct from the two cities. [Alternatively, there are at least three Jerusalems. The Heavenly
   Jerusalem is partly in the 4D Heaven and partly in the 3D Heaven.] Sin is not at an end. God still dwells in
   His heavenly temple: the Christ dwells in the temple below. But when “the outer court” of the heavenly temple
   passes away with a burning earth, the temple, too (it would appear), is set aside. Thenceforth we see only one
   city, and that city is also the one temple of God. It has already been described as “God’s tabernacle” [21:3].
   (Emphasis his.) [As a counterproposal, if the earthly Jerusalem is eternal, then the temple is likely eternal
   also.]

         Since Govett is a Gehenna Misthologist, he naturally seeks to limit rewards to the millennial period so that
he has a basis for limiting the literal burning of believers in Gehenna to one thousand years in his model. One cannot
be surprised, then, that he does away with rewards via the wiping away of tears and the supposed merger of the
Earthly Jerusalem with the Heavenly Jerusalem.
         Having thoroughly rebutted such arguments elsewhere in my writings (e.g., TOD, RAE), I will not take the
time to do so here. Instead, I will work to amend part of his argument in accordance with my proven premise that
rewards are eternal. Contrary to Govett, it is highly unlikely that the HC (Heavenly City) descends to Earth and
does away with the (Earthly City). Much more likely, NJ (New Jerusalem) will be suspended above OJ (old
Jerusalem) during the eternal state, just as it is during the Millennium. The wiping away of tears does not wipe away
the rewards of the faithful; instead, it limits the blessings associated with the Reward City to the faithful. Since the
old city (OJ) is not done away with, it is unlikely that the old temple is done way with. Although no longer functional
in terms of offering memorial sacrifices, I suspect that the old temple’s function will be transformed to serve strictly
as a memorial, that is, a museum. Therefore, I draw inverse inferences from Govett’s arguments in this capacity.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                        Page 16
Since rewards are eternal, OJ is probably not replaced by NJ. Thus, since the outer court (OJ) does not pass away,
the old temple most likely does not pass away either.
          Govett’s two-part hypothesis for the Heavenly Temple (HT) is useful for formulating an amended
hypothesis. As noted, he poses: “There is a temple, partly on earth, partly in heaven, distinct from the two cities”
(italics his). In contradistinction, I pose that the ET (Earthly Temple) was patterned after the HT, not a part
of the HT. However, the HT is indeed in two parts, comprising a double temple. HT1 is the part in the 4D City
that is unique to the 4D City, and thus not directly accessed via the interior of the 3D City. HT2 is at the Bema atop
the 3D City. Therefore, it is not actually in the 3D City. New Jerusalem has no temple within it, but its Bema atop
it can be considered part of the Heavenly Temple. When judgement is leased against Earth via the HT during the
second half of the Tribulation and smoke fills TTT (possibly HT1 and HT2), no one enters TTT. The opening of
the HT in Rev 11:19 is just past the mid-point of the Tribulation after the two witnesses go “up into heaven in the
cloud” (Rev 11:12). Per my model, the two witnesses would be going up into NJ, which is hovering above Earth.
When the Lord returns at the time of the Rapture, He “will bring with Him” all those believers who have died, and
those believers “who are alive and remain shall be caught up together with them in the clouds to meet the Lord in
the air” (1Thess 4:14-17). Logistically, how does the Lord bring those who have died with Him? He does so by
placing them in the Bema stadium atop NJ. This HC is a large spaceship. Those raptured join them atop the City at
the Bema. The Bema proceedings take place until the mid-point of the Tribulation while the City is cloaked above
Earth. Then the City or at the least the Bema portion of it is decloaked so that it can be seen from Earth, and
judgment is launched from it against Earth. The Earth responds by attacking the HC and waging war against the
Lamb, who in turn responds by wiping out the opposition with a heavenly army descending from NJ.
          My model is much more feasible than Govett’s conjecture that we are entering the HT during the Tribulation
but excluded from the HT during the smoke. In that case, where are we while we are waiting for the smoke to clear?
Some undefined location midway between the HT and the HC, as Govett seems to think? Govett poses that our
transitional position is somewhere midway between the HT and the HC during the millennium. Where exactly
would that be? My model provides literal locations that can be deduced in harmony with various pieces of
information. Unfaithful believers are sent to the Celestial Outer Darkness as a consequence of the Bema. Faithful
believers are sent to the inside of NJ in conjunction with the Bema. The Bema Throne Room (BTR) is cleared at the
midpoint of the Tribulation to be the launching platform by which to wage war against Earth during the second half
of the Tribulation. The glory of HT1 is transferred to HT2 as the throne of God is moved from HT1 to HT2.


        Two-Part Throne
         The duality of the Heavenly Temple also seems applicable to the duality of God’s throne. As to the location
of God’s throne, MMP5 suggested that the Father’s throne might be inside NJ, which would be inaccessible to
believers who do not overcome (cp. Rev 22:3). Thus, only overcomers will enter the HC and see the Father’s face.
Those believers who fail to overcome will live in the Celestial Outer Darkness. However, MMP5 also affirmed the
more comprehensive comment on Heb 12:14 made by Lang (TETTH): “‘Seeing God’s face’ is a privilege reserved
for the new Jerusalem (Rev 22:4; cf. 1 Cor 13:12; 1 John 3:2).” The Father’s throne does not necessarily have to be
inside the walls of NJ for this statement to be true. Those cast into the Celestial Outer Darkness will not be able to
see the Father’s face during the Millennium and Eternal State even if the Father’s throne is adjacent to the Son’s
throne atop the Heavenly City and thus inside the Bema Throne Room (BTR) of the Heavenly City.

                         Rev 3:21 (Overcomers Share Christ’s Throne)
                                                      Spatial Throne
         Commenting on Rev 3:21, Aune (WBC) states: “The ancient world was familiar with the image of a
bisellium, a ‘double-throne.’ In Rev 12:5 the Child is caught up to God and his throne (which may imply
enthronement), and 22:1, 3 mentions ‘the throne of God and the Lamb’ (i.e., apparently a single throne on which
both sit).” Their dual throne, at least at this point in time or in reference to this event, appears to be at the top of the
New Jerusalem (NJ) since the water of life flows “from the throne of God and of the Lamb” (Rev 22:1). Yet this
dual throne is said to be in the Heavenly City. Apparently, this dual throne is in the Bema Throne Room (BTR) atop
the HC.
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                 Page 17
                                   Illustration 9. Bisellium — Double-Throne




Jesus indicated the misthologically shared nature of this BTR with overcomers: “He who overcomes, I will grant to
him to sit down with Me on My throne, as I also overcame and sat down with My Father on His throne” (Rev 3:21).
My supposition is that the Bema Throne Room (BTR) will be shared with overcomers as the place from which they
can exercise rulership with the Lord from the Heavenly City (HC). The stadium in which believers are judged will
become the stadium from which overcomers exercise judgment.

                                     Temporal Throne Versus Eternal Throne
        Lang (TROJC) has an extended discussion entitled, “The Thrones are not the same.” He argues that the
Lord has an eternal throne located outside our universe and far above all the heavens (cp. Ps 110:1; Eph 4:10;
Heb 1:3,8,13; 2:8; 4:14; 7:26; 8:1; 9:11-12,24; 10:12-13; 12:2),12 which is distinct from the Lord’s temporal throne
that He prepares for judgment and from which He holds court with angels and judges them (Job 1:6; 2:1; Ps 9:7;
82:1-7; Is 6:1; Dan 7:9,13). The heart of his argument from TROJC is as follows:

              But the throne of Dn 7 and Rv 4 is not eternal. That which is eternal cannot be “placed”
      in position or be “set” on a given occasion, for it is already there and cannot be not there, for if it
      could be it would not be eternal. Hence of this administrative throne it is said that “Jehovah hath
      prepared His throne in the heavens (Ps 9. 7), which word “prepared” is not possible in reference
      to that which is eternal.
              Moreover, this throne of these visions is in the heavenly regions, not “far above all the
      heavens.” The Son of man comes up to it “with the clouds of heaven,” the throne is “set in heaven,”
      and the hosts of heaven surround it. It were confusion of thought to speak of a person approaching to
      and standing before a seat on which he is sitting.
              The king of England has two thrones: one at Buckingham Palace, his personal residence;
      the other in the House of Lords, for governmental purposes. It is thus with the Most High. The
      Scripture speaks of the throne of the Father, and it shows us another throne also, where God sits
      as judge on occasion, and which thrown moves from place to place as required by business of
      state….

destination reached in Eph 4:10 would be, what is in my model, 7D Heaven.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                         Page 18
               Thus the throne of these visions we are considering is temporal and movable, and is in
      the angelic regions; it is therefore not the throne which is eternal and is far above all the heavens.
               Finally, this court of angels will pass away. The scene before us will be its last session, for “not
      unto angels,” but to Christ and the saints of the high places has God determined to make the world to come
      subject (Hb 2. 5 : I Cr 6. 2, 3). This is the obvious significance of those heavenly sovereigns casting their
      crowns before the throne, for should a vassal king place his crown at the feet of his overlord it would signify
      the renouncing of his crown unto the disposition of his sovereign. There is distinct and contemporary
      evidence that this is the significance of this symbolic act. In a.d. 63 Tiridates. king of Armenia, to avoid war
      with the Romans and to secure their support, agreed to renounce his crown to the emperor Nero, on the
      understanding thãnüTshould receive it back as the emperor’s feudatory. (Italics his, 111-113.)

If Lang is correct, then the Lord has at least two heavenly thrones: one eternal residential above the angels,
the other temporal administrative in the same plane of existence as the angels. If this is the case, as seems to
be the case, then one would suspect that the compartments of the heavenly temple also have corresponding
dimensions.

         Heb 8:1-2
         I have supplemented the passages Lang cites above in TROJC by consulting his TETTH (The Epistle to the
Hebrews) for related discussion. He poses that the true tabernacle in Heb 8:2 is a temporary, movable tent (TETTH,
125). Therein, he reinforces some of his comments he made earlier in TROJC.13 By extrapolation of his argument,
I have placed Heb 8:1 in Lang’s references to the eternal throne since Christ is presently sitting there, which is the
case in Heb 1:3, concerning which Lang associates with the eternal throne (TROJC, 111). According to Heb 1:3,
when Jesus “had made purification of sins, He sat down at the right hand of the Majesty on high.” But how high
was this sitting, in the created heavens or in the eternal heavens? What about the similar statement in Heb 8:1? It
seems to be referring to the same location: “We have such a high priest, who has taken His seat on the right hand
of the throne of the Majesty in the heavens.” If we concur with Lang that this is the eternal heavens in 1:3 and
make that same identification in Heb 8:1, which seems to be the logical inference, then this poses an interesting
juxtaposition in Lang’s model between the eternal throne in Heb 8:1 and the supposedly temporary true tabernacle
in Heb 8:2. He defers the more detailed discussion of Heb 8:1 to his discussion of Heb 9:11-14.14 Unfortunately, he
does not elaborate on this juxtaposition in that later discussion. Perhaps he was at a loss to answer the conflict posed
by Heb 8:1-2 to his model. The problem, of course, is that this passage makes a close association (if not
identification) between the eternal throne in v. 1 and Lang’s supposedly temporary tabernacle in Heb 8:2.
      1
        Now the main point in what has been said is this: we have such a high priest, who has taken His seat
      at the right hand of the throne of the Majesty in the heavens, 2 a minister in the sanctuary, and in the
      true tabernacle, which the Lord pitched, not man. (Heb 8:1-2)

        Are we to assume that only one location is being indicated by the (1) plural heavens, (2) sanctuary, (3) and
true tabernacle? In commenting on Heb 4:14, Govett (GOH) notes that Christ passed through the (lower) heavens
of Heb 4:14 and now is seated in the highest heaven of Heb 8:1. As to Heb 8:1-2, he calls attention to the plural
heavens, which he takes as noting that there is more than one and that Paul was caught up to the third one.15 Govett

generically with Heaven or perhaps more specifically with the Heavenly Jerusalem located there, which would be
the 4D City in my model. They might also equate it with the True Tabernacle in Heb 8:2. However, if they are
correct in that assessment, and surely they are, then what about the additional insight posed by others in which the
True Tabernacle is the outer access to the Inner Sanctuary of Heb 8:2? Putting the pieces together would yield the
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                        Page 19
has an excellent discussion refuting those commentators who say that God exists outside of time and space and does
not have a literal body, hands, throne, or tabernacle (pp. 220-225).16 Jesus is literally seated at the Father’s right
hand. Various details require this affirmation. While being stoned to death, Stephen saw Jesus literally stand at the
Father’s literal right hand (Acts 7:55-56). Jesus assures that when He comes in the clouds, even unbelievers will
see Him sitting at the Father’s right hand (Mk 14:62), which is where He is currently sitting (Mk 16:19). Jesus
comes up to the Father’s throne in Dan 7:13. The angels are involved in activities surrounding the throne. The
throne is literal. The Father and Son are not bodiless beings floating in a static, atemporal, aspatial eternal now.
         Since the Father and Son are literal beings, having a bodily form, sitting on literal thrones, they are in spatial
locations within literal heavenly structures. Several questions naturally arise for those taking a literal approach.
Where exactly in Heaven are these structures and are multiple structures being described? This passage in Heb 8:1-
2 calls for closer scrutiny.

        Now the main point in what has been said is this: we have such a high priest, who has taken His seat

       Is the sanctuary and tabernacle one and the same? Govett says, “In one point of view, Yes! But for certain
purposes of the argument, it was well to distinguish the first chamber from the second.” Govett cites OT precedence
for making a distinction between the sanctuary as (1) the Holy Place (i.e., the Holy of Holies) and (2) as the
tabernacle (i.e., the tent of meeting):

•   Now those who were to camp before the tabernacle eastward, before the tent of meeting toward the sunrise, are
    Moses and Aaron and his sons, performing the duties of the sanctuary [sg. hagios, holy place] for the obligation
    of the sons of Israel; but the layman coming near was to be put to death. (Num 3:38)




conclusion that, at least from one vantage point, the Inner Sanctuary is a higher point of access to God, even a higher

   Nonliteral commentators include the following: “That no literal location is intended was as well understood by
Christians in the apostolic age as it is by us: they knew that God has no physical right hand or material throne where
the ascended Christ sits beside him” (Bruce, NICNT, Heb 1:13). “We are not to suppose that God has hands, or that
Jesus sits in any particular direction from God” (Barnes, BNONT, emphasis his, Mk 16:19). “We dismiss all ideas
of time and of space and of limitations when we are thinking of the heavenly world; they do not apply there, nor
does an argument that is based on such ideas. What timelessness (eternity) and spacelessness are no human mind is
able to conceive” (Lenski, LCNT, Heb 1:3). “The expressions sat down and the right hand must not be taken
literally, but rather symbolically” (Kistemaker, NTC, Heb 1:3, emphasis his). “The right hand is by a similitude
applied to God, though he is not confined to any place, and has not a right side nor left” (Calvin, CC, Heb 1:3,
emphasis his). Such statements stand in stark contrast to numerous biblical affirmations.
       For a defense of God having a literal body, see Dake (pp. 47-60). He would also pose that during the
incarnation Christ did not exist “in any sense outside of the body which he has taken to dwell in (Ps. 40:6-10; Heb
10:5-10).” Up until MMP5, I would have concurred with that statement. But adopting the MT reading of Jn 3:13 in
that treatment led me to defend the proposition that Christ was simultaneously in a heavenly body in Heaven while
in an earthly body on Earth. This revised premise is retained in MMP6.
       A friend of mine who holds to the eternal now position recently pointed out that the members of the Trinity
grow in love for each other. I mentioned, in return, that this is affirmed by Jn 10:17. The Father’s love for the Son
grew because of the Son’s obedience to the Father in laying down His life to bring many brethren to become His
co-heirs. Therefore, the Father’s love, as experienced in His dimension, grew as a result to what Jesus did in our
dimension. Consequently, the Trinity does not exist in a timeless Eternal Now. For further discussion, see MMP5.
For a simple illustration, consider that my love for my wife grew when she became the mother of our children. I no
longer loved her simply as my wife but also as the mother of our children. Our shared love for our children increased
our love for one another. In a similar manner, the shared experiences of the members of the Trinity in begetting us
and raising us gives them opportunity to increase their love for one another.
