        Commentators are divided on the issue. Lane (WBC) says that the and [kai] linking the sanctuary with the
tabernacle in Heb 8:2 “is almost certainly epexegetical” and forms a hendiadys because of the clause that follows
them: “That a hendiadys is intended by ‘the sanctuary καί the true tabernacle’ seems obvious from the sg number
of the pronoun ἥν in the relative clause that follows.”17 However, he adds: “For a contrary opinion, see Hofius,
Vorhang, 59–60, who appeals to Lev 16:16, 20, 33 as a basis for distinguishing between τὰ ἅγια (the most holy
place within the sanctuary) and σκηνὴ ἀληθινή (the heavenly sanctuary in its totality).” Govett and Hofius,
therefore, are essentially making the same argument. Govett parenthetically calls attention to the fact that there are
“two articles in Greek,” one before the sanctuary (Holy Place) and one before the true tabernacle. This construction
would normally indicate two separate structures. When two articular nouns are joined by kai, two separate nouns

17
  On the contrary, a feminine relative pronoun should not be assumed to function in a transgender capacity to refer
jointly to a neuter-feminine construction as a hendiadys. Sampson gives a more plausible explanation for the
hendiadys interpretation:

       These words may be understood as only explicative of the preceding: ‘A minister of the holy place,
       even of the true tabernacle.’ Or else we may, with more propriety, explain them as truly additional,
       giving καὶ its usual additive meaning, so that the expression will constitute a hendiadys. The adjective
       ἀληθινῆς may then be conceived as qualifying both σκηνῆς and ἁγίων, the former more immediately,
       but the latter also more remotely. The defining clause, ἣν ἔπηξεν ὁ κύριος, καὶ οὐκ ἄνθρωπος, is
       evidently constructed with reference to the structure of the lower and typical sanctuary. This was a
       tent pitched, or set up. Hence God, the creator of the highest heaven, is said to have pitched it also,
       only in allusion to the former. (Emphasis his.)

Francis S. Sampson, A Critical Commentary on the Epistle to the Hebrews, ed. Robert L. Dabney (New York:
Robert Carter & Brothers, 1856), 293. Sampson says that the construction may be simply explicative (i.e.,
epexegetical) or that it might additionally be a hendiadys; still, contrary to Lane, even in that case the phrase
introduced by the feminine relative pronoun only refers to the feminine tabernacle. However, per the argument of
Lane and those like him, who use the phrase to defend the hendiadys’ proposition on assumed grounds of joint
reference, Sampson’s more grammatically plausible suggestion weakens the proposition of a hendiadys.
         Conversely, Sampson weakens his own epexegetical interpretation of kai in Heb 8:2 by correctly discerning
in his comments on Heb 9:11 that the dia there is spatial, thus meaning through (which separates the outer heavenly
court from the inner heavenly sanctuary):

                διὰ is here employed in its original sense of ‘through. The arrangements of the typical
       sanctuary lead the writer to conceive of Christ as making a sacrifice of himself in the court, as it were,
       on earth, and then passing through the heavens as the vail separating the outer from the inner sanctuary
       of this better tabernacle, to sprinkle his own blood on the mercy-seat in heaven. The whole clause is
       therefore to be regarded as qualificative of εἰσῆλθεν εἰς τὰ ἅγια in the succeeding verse. This
       interpretation is sustained by 4:14 above, where Christ is called a great High-Priest who hath passed
       through (διεληλυθότα) the heavens. In 6:19, 20, it is said that Christ our forerunner has entered for us
       within the vail, &c.; the vail (the lower heavens) must be penetrated, to reach a position within it; 7:26.

The spatially distinctive dia of Heb 9:11 should be paired with a spatially distinctive kai in Heb 8:2. Consequently,
the kai is not exegetical, much less used to form a hendiadys.
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                        Page 21
are normally in view. In fact, in surveying the hendiadyses provided by standard reference works, only 20% of the
hendiadyses had two articles. Thus, one could reasonably infer that probability is against this construction being a
hendiadys.18 So, one option is to take the kai in its typical conjunctive manner, thus distinguishing the two structures.
Craig Koester (AYB) cites various writers holding this persuasion:

               Different parts of the heavenly sanctuary. Some maintain that the kai distinguishes “the
      sanctuary” or holy of holies from “the true tent,” which would be the outer court or the entire sanctuary.
      Such a distinction seems to be made in Heb 9:8, 11, and the biblical depiction of the Day of Atonement
      ritual uses to hagion for the holy of holies and skēnē for the entire tent (e.g., Lev 16:16).

Dods (EGT) argues that the kai distinguishes rather than equates the sanctuary and tabernacle:

      τῶν ἁγίων not = τῶν ἡγιασμένων as Œcumenius translates, but as in 9:8, 12, 25; 10:19; 13:11 = ἅγια
      ἁγίων of 9:3. In 9:2, 3, the outer part of the tabernacle is called ἅγια, the inner ἅγια ἁγίων, but ver. 8
      is conclusive proof that ἅγια without addition was used for the holiest place.

His not = can be represented with a mathematical symbol: sanctuary ≠ tabernacle. Hughes (NICNT) notes various
writers holding that position and quotes an article by Helmut Koester holding that position and who thereby insists:

               This is not a hendiadys, but expresses that Christ’s office includes both the service in the
      sanctuary of heaven itself (τὰ ἅγια) and the entering by passing through the heavenly regions (ἡ σκηνή)
      = the ascension! It also becomes clear here that the author of Hebrews is more interested in the opening
      of the way into the heavenly sanctuary than in the performance of a service within the sanctuary of
      heaven.19

Hughes (NICNT), however, counters:

                A hendiadys is indeed intended by “the sanctuary and the true tent” is confirmed by the
      singular number of the pronoun ἥν in the relative clause which follows—ἣν ἔπηξεν ὁ κύριος: our
      author could hardly have meant that the Lord set up only a heavenly holy place, especially as the focus
      of his attention is on the high-priestly entry of Jesus into the heavenly holy of holies. In fact, throughout
      these chapters our author’s perspective does not include the concept of a holy place above, as distinct
      from the holy of holies, precisely because, now that the curtain between the two has been abolished
      and the way opened up by him for all into the heavenly holy of holies which is the sanctuary of God’s
      presence, the distinction no longer exists.
                The ineptitude of this interpretation appears, too, from the fact that the qualification of 9:11,
      “not made with hands, that is, not of this creation,” applies just as little to the visible heavens as it does
      to our earth, since both belong equally to “this creation” and both are praised throughout Scripture as
      the works of the divine Creator (Gen. 1:1; Ps. 19:1, etc.). This consideration alone is enough to
      disqualify the distinction made by those who propose this type of interpretation.

So, Helmut Koester says that it “is not a hendiadys,” but Hughes insists that “a hendiadys is indeed intended.”
MacDonald equates hendiadys with Granville Sharp’s Rule, in which case the construction would be: articular noun
+ kai + anarthrous noun (with both nouns being in the same gender and number).20 That is, both nouns are united
by kai under one article. On such basis, Baugh argues that predetermined plan and foreknowledge in Acts 2:23 is a
hendiadys. Edgar disputes this claim:




18
   See Excursus on Nominal NT Hendiadys, 177.
19
   Helmut Koester, “Outside the Camp: Hebrews 13:9–14,” Harvard Theological Review, 55 (1962), pp. 309f.
20
   William G. MacDonald, Greek Enchiridion: A Concise Handbook of Grammar for Translation and Exegesis
(Peabody: Hendrickson Publishers, 1986), 117.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 22
      Baugh alleges that this is a hendiadys because it has one article. “Therefore,” he asserts, the “two nouns
      are expressly united.” He apparently means the two are not distinguished but overlap or take on
      basically the same meaning.
               This argument, however, is incorrect. The fact that the expression has a single article has
      nothing to do with a hendiadys. In addition, a hendiadys would make little sense in this passage.
      Finally, this approach is backward. (1) A hendiadys is a specific rhetorical device where two different
      words are used to express a single idea. Normally, one functions adjectivally and modifies the other,
      resulting in one concept. The lexical meanings of the terms do not become the same nor change to a
      different meaning, except for the fact that the usual meaning of one is slightly modified to function
      adjectivally. (2) Since the article is irrelevant to this rhetorical device, obviously, the article cannot
      indicate that this is a hendiadys. [See his note below] (3) In Acts 2:23, the terms do not lend themselves
      to such an interpretation. A hendiadys in this case would mean something like the planned
      foreknowledge of God or God’s foreknown (by God) plan. Neither is sensible. Does Peter state the
      illogical concept that God planned His foreknowledge, or would he bother to state the obvious fact
      that God knew His plan before He carried it out?
               Whether a construction is a hendiadys is usually a matter of opinion, an educated guess. This
      must be determined not by the syntax, but by the established meanings. The syntax of a hendiadys is
      very simple: two words joined by and. However, this is so general that it is non-definitive. Thus, there
      is no syntactical format from which first to determine that something is a hendiadys and then alter the
      meaning of the terms to fit. This is logically backwards. It is assuming the conclusion. (Emphasis
      his.)21

In support of his statement that the single article does not necessarily indicate a hendiadys, Edgar supplies the
following note:

               A. T. Robertson, Greek Grammar in the Light of Historical Research (Nashville: Broadman,
      1934), 1206, 1338, lists James 4:2 as an example. BDF, 228–29, gives Mark 6:26/Matt 14:9. N. Turner,
      Syntax, vol. 3: A Grammar of New Testament Greek (Edinburgh: Clark, 1963), 335, lists Mark 6:26;
      Luke 2:47; 21:15; Acts 1:25; 14:17; 23:6; Rom 1:5; 2 Tim. 4:1; Titus 2:13; James 5:10; 1 Pet. 4:14
      and 2 Pet. 1:16;. and John Beekman and John Callow, Translating the Word of God (Grand Rapids:
      Zondervan, 1974), 212–14, also include Matt. 4:16; Col. 1:28; and 2 Tim. 1:10. Out of seventeen
      examples, only three occur with a single article. Apparently this is the least likely construction. In
      Lucan writings there are five occurrences, but only one with a single article. That one is the article
      together with the demonstrative pronoun; thus, it is not precisely the same syntactical construction.
      The single article in Acts 2:23, therefore, argues more against this being a hendiadys than for it. Most
      of these examples have no article with either substantive.22
21
   Thomas R. Edgar, “The Meaning of Proginōskō (‘To Foreknow’),” CTSJ 9, no. 1 (2003): 53–55.

   Under their entry for kai, Moulton and Turner discuss its epexegetical use and suggest a possible hendiadys but
do not equate epexegetical with hendiadys: “The adverbial or epexegetical καί (that is, even): Mk 1:19 Lk 3:18
Jn 1:16 (that is, to receive grace upon grace) 20:30, Ac 22:25, καὶ ἀκατάκριτον and uncondemned at that), Ro 1:5

         Accordingly, we can surmise that Granville Sharp’s Rule is only one potential form of a hendiadys. A
hendiadys might have no articles, one article, or two articles. Context, rather than syntax, will have to be the final
arbitrator as to whether a hendiadys is intended, if the gender and number match. That said, it seems that Leedy has
diagrammed the sentence correctly.
                    Illustration 10. Feminine Which Modifies Famine Tabernacle (Heb 8:2)




The sanctuary (tw/n a`gi,wn) is singular neuter in Greek, while the tabernacle (th/j skhnh/j) is singular feminine, as
is the which (h]n) that follows it. The singular feminine which is naturally diagrammed as modifying the singular
feminine tabernacle. Leedy did well. The expression, which the Lord pitched, applies exclusively to the tabernacle,
not inclusively to the sanctuary, thereby indicating that the tabernacle is created, whereas the sanctuary is eternal.
             Illustration 11. Feminine Which Modifies Famine Tabernacle (Heb 8:2)—Simplified




The simplified illustration of Leedy’s diagram makes the same point. Since which is feminine, the phrase it
introduces modifies the tabernacle, which is also feminine. The which and its attendant phrase does not modify the
sanctuary, which is neuter. The sanctuary and tabernacle are two different edifices; only tabernacle is said to be
pitched by the Lord. However, if Leedy had done what Hughes wants, it would have to be diagrammed differently.

e.g. [two feminine articles:] Lk 2:47 ἐπὶ τῇ συνέσει καὶ ταῖς ἀποκρίσεσιν αὐτοῦ ‘at his intelligent answers’, 21:15,
R 1:5, 2 T 4:1, T 2:13, 2 P 1:16” (BDF, § 442, ellipses theirs). I have interjected notations to call attention to the
articles or lack thereof. For a much more exhaustive discussion, see Bullinger, who has a sixteen-page section
devoted to the topic broken up into nouns versus verbs (Figures, 657-672). The NT nominal passages he cites are:
Mt 3:11; 4:16; 24:30 (31?); Lk 1:17; 21:15; Jn 1:17; 3:5; 4:23; Acts 1:25; 3:14; 14:13; 23:6; Rom 1:5; 2:27; 11:17;
1Cor 2:4; 11:7; Eph 4:11; 5:5; 6:18 (2x); Col 2:8; 2:18; 1Thess 2:12; 1Tim 3:15; 2Tim 1:10; 4:1-2; Tit 2:13; Jam
3:9; 2Pet 1:3,16-17; Rev 5:10.

        Illustration 12. Feminine Which Modifies Neuter Sanctuary and Famine Tabernacle (Heb 8:2)




In that case, the lines following the sanctuary and the temple would have to be enjoined, then extended, and then
its phrase moved to that location as indicated above. Diagramming the which as referring to both the neuter
sanctuary and the feminine tabernacle would be a very questionable proposition, however. Therefore, Hughes’
argument is strained when he claims: “A hendiadys is indeed intended by ‘the sanctuary and the true tent’ is
confirmed by the singular number of the pronoun ἥν in the relative clause which follows—ἣν ἔπηξεν ὁ κύριος: our
author could hardly have meant that the Lord set up only a heavenly holy place, especially as the focus of his
attention is on the high-priestly entry of Jesus into the heavenly holy of holies.” Not really. Rather, the proposition
of an alleged hendiadys is rather strained since the supposed neuter-feminine hendiadys is followed by a feminine
relative pronoun rather than a neuter one. If one wanted to diagram the sentence the way Hughes advocates, one
would have to pull the pronoun out from under modifying the tabernacle and move the phrase introduced by the
pronoun to modifying the line joining both the neuter and feminine via the assumed epexegetical and (kai.), as
demonstrated by the red arrow. Hughes’ argument for performing such unnatural surgery on the diagram is
counterintuitive. The fact that the tabernacle is a heavenly holy place naturally indicates that the sanctuary, which
lies deeper within it and that is accessed through it, is also a heavenly holy place. However, it would be a heavenly
holy place in a deeper, more elevated sense, thus an inner heavenly holy of holies inside the outer heavenly holy
place. Taking the sanctuary as a heavenly holy of holies being contrasted to the mere holy place of the outer
tabernacle argues against equating the two. Context and syntax work together to refute Huges’ line of reasoning.
         In any case, Hughes overstates his reaction to Helmut Koester’s interpretation in considering Koester’s
rendering inept, in part, because Koester does not take Heb 8:2 as a hendiadys. Koester’s explanation should not be
dismissed too hastily, however. In their commentary, Harold Attridge and Helmut Koester (HERM) explain:

              Although some commentators have argued that the phrase “sanctuary and tabernacle” is to be
      construed as a hendiadys,23 it is more likely that it refers to the division of the heavenly tabernacle.24

23
   In a note at this point, Attridge and Helmut Koester interject: “Or, more properly, that καί is epexegetical. See
Westcott, p. 216: Hughes, p. 281 n. 54; Bruce, p. 161; Peterson, Perfection, 130. The argument that the relative
pronoun ἥν implies that the phrase is a hendiadys is unconvincing. If God pitched the whole tent, he pitched its
parts.”
24
   At this point, they interject: “Most commentators favor seeing a distinction here between the tabernacle as a
whole and its inner sanctum. See Michel, p. 312; Sabourin, Priesthood, 199–203; Hofius, Vorhang, 56–57; Nissilä,
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                       Page 25
      The distinction between the inner sanctuary, labeled τὰ ἅγια, and the tabernacle as a whole, labeled ἡ
      σκηνή, has precedents in the LXX.23 Hebrews generally observes the distinction, reserving (τὰ) ἅγια
      for the inner sanctuary that the earthly high priests and their heavenly counterpart enter, while using
      σκηνή for the entire tabernacle, either earthly or heavenly. The only exception to this distinction is the
      detailed description of the earthly tabernacle, the whole of which is designated τὸ ἅγιον (9:1*). In the
      same context, the inner tabernacle is named ἅγια and the outer ἅγια ἁγίων. The term σκηνή is there
      used for each section, and not for the whole, but the designations “first” and “second” prevent any
      ambiguity.
               The distinction implied here between the heavenly tabernacle as a whole and the inner portion
      is also found in some of the many Jewish texts of the period that depict the heavenly counterpart of
      the earthly sanctuary. What the author makes of this distinction requires further clarification,29 but that
      he does indeed make it is virtually certain. At this point he simply establishes the principle that follows
      from the reflections on Christ’s exaltation, that the place where Christ’s priestly act is consummated
      is the “true” tabernacle.25

While the two-temple variation posed by Helmut Koester may indeed be incomplete (if it is simply equating the
outer tabernacle with either the aerial heavens or astral heavens), the version proposed by Govett and others (of a
heavenly celestial outer tabernacle) is not so easily dismissed.
        Allen (NAC) cites various options regarding Heb 8:2:

              The “true tabernacle” is in parallel construction with “sanctuary.” Some interpret the
      “sanctuary” as a reference to the holy of holies,750 and “the true tabernacle” as a reference to the
      heavenly temple in its totality. Others take the phrase tōn hagiōn, “the sanctuary,” to refer to the
      sanctuary as a whole. Other uses of the term in Hebrews are in 9:2, 3, 12, 24, 25; 10:19; and 13:11.
      The comma after “sanctuary” signals an explanatory statement connecting two descriptions of one
      item.

      Johnson (NT breaks the options into the two primary ones being focused upon herein. The writer of
Hebrews is either describing (1) one structure or (2) one within another:

               But if ta hagia can mean sanctuary, why is it linked to “the genuine tent” by the copulative
      “and” (kai)?
               Two possibilities present themselves. One is to take “sanctuary and genuine tent” as a
      hendiadys (saying one thing through two phrases). So Koester (341) translates, “Of the sanctuary, that
      is, of the true tent.” The other possibility is to consider Hebrews to be making a distinction (as some
      other authors did) between the inner sanctuary (ta hagia) and the tabernacle as a whole (hē skēnē), as
      in Lev 16:16, 20, 33. So Attridge (216) translates, “of the sanctuary and of the true tabernacle.” But
      the decision is not a critical one, for the author’s real interest is in the distinction between the heavenly
      worship carried out by Jesus and that conducted on earth.26

         Heb 9:11-12

states: “Other writers argue on the basis of 9:11–12 that a distinction should be made between the σκηνή,

likely used anarthrous forms there also?

   Luke Timothy Johnson, Hebrews: A Commentary, ed. C. Clifton Black, M. Eugene Boring, and John T. Carroll,
1st ed., The New Testament Library (Louisville, KY: Westminster John Knox Press, 2012), 199.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 26
‘tabernacle,’ through which Jesus passed, and τὰ ἅγια, ‘the sanctuary,’ which he entered in 8:2” (WBC). Govett
(GOV) fails to specify this distinction when he comes to Heb 9:11-12, but he should have since he considers the
“greater and more perfect tabernacle” of that passage to be created but the heavenly sanctuary to be “super-
celestial.” Delitzsch, as noted below, is does better in specifying the logical differentiation.
         In any case, Lane (WBC) continues in his comments on Heb 8:2: “There is, however, no common agreement
among these writers as to the theological significance of the distinction.” Lane then provides his opinion: “Such a
distinction must be made in Heb 9:11–12, but that such a distinction is inappropriate in 8:2.” Hebrews 9 makes a
sharp distinction between the outer tabernacle, which is called the Holy Place, and the inner tabernacle, which is
called the Holy of Holies (Heb 9:2-3) and is only entered once a year (Heb 9:6-8). Thus, concerning Heb 9:11-12,
Lane summarizes: “The thrust of the argument is that the tabernacle with its division into two chambers was
constructed according to the pattern or model shown to Moses.” However, this would play into my hands in my
preferences noted above.
         In Heb 9:1-25, a sharp distinction is made between the earthly portion of the tabernacle and its heavenly
portion. The real question, though, is whether the writer of Hebrews also intents to indicate two “different parts of
the heavenly sanctuary,” a position held by various interpreters cited by Craig Koester above. However, he prefers
the second option, to take kai as epexegetical in Heb 8:2, saying, “There are good reasons to think that ‘sanctuary’
and ‘tent’ are synonyms connected by the explanatory kai (‘that is’).27 Then again, there are good reasons to think
that it might not be epexegetical. The resolution cannot be decisively determined syntactically, though for reasons
cited above, the epexegetical interpretation seems syntactically strained.
         Similarly, the situation in Heb 9:11-12 is not necessarily as cut and dry as juxtaposing an outer earthly
tabernacle as opposed to a heavenly tabernacle. Just as the kai of Heb 8:2 is debated, so also is the dia of Heb 9:11.
In Heb 9:12, Christ is said to have entered into (eiserchomai) the Holy Place (which would be Holy of Holies of v.
12) by passing “through [dia] the greater and more perfect tabernacle, not made with hands” of v. 11. The distinction
between the dia (through) of v. 11 and the eis (into) of v. 12 should not go unnoticed.
      11
        But when Christ appeared as a high priest of the good things to come, He passed through [dia] the
      greater and more perfect tabernacle [skene], not made with hands, that is to say, not of this
      creation; 12 and not through the blood of goats and calves, but through His own blood, He entered
      into (eis) the holy place [ta hagia; sanctuary, Most Holy Place] once for all, having obtained eternal
      redemption. (Heb 9:11-12; TM)

          The text explicitly states that Christ passed through the perfect tabernacle and entered into the sanctuary.
Various translations denote the difference in a variety of ways. For example, dia is translated as rendering the verb
as passed through (NET, NJB), passing through (NAB), went through (GWN, NIV) versus the verb in connection
with eis as went into (GWN) and entered (NAB, NET, NJB). Two different actions and corresponding structures
are in view. The statement that the perfect tabernacle was not made by hands (i.e., not of this creation) indicates
that it is not an earthly tabernacle created by humans on Earth, but a heavenly tabernacle created within the angelic
realm. Christ passed through this heavenly tabernacle en route to enter into the heavenly sanctuary, which, in
contrast, is not stated to have been created, thereby leaving open the possibility that the sanctuary is in an eternal
realm, which would be fitting since it is associated with eternal redemption.
          Govett (GOH) says that it “Jesus passed through all inferior heavens, till He entered the heaven of heavens
of God’s visible abode.” That deduction is intuitive enough. Hullinger notes three options taken by commentators,
but prefers the first one, which is basically what Govett has stated: “The first is to take διά in a local sense (through)
so that the idea is that just as the high priest passed through the holy place on the way to the Holy of Holies on the
Day of Atonement, so Christ passed through the heavens into the presence of God.”28 Hullinger cites various writers
in support, such as Delitzsch and Alford.




27
  Allen (NAC) also prefers the epexegetical option, though he notes the other options held by other writers.

         Delitzsch (CFTL) argues the point at length, saying concerning Heb 8:2, for example: “Τὰ ἅγια [the holies]
is also here clearly distinguished from ἡ σκηνή [the tabernacle]; while it is evident from 9:11, 12, that they stand in
very close connection with one another.”29 Delitzsch further states:

      The fact then is, that the sanctuary [of Heb 8:2a] which here presents itself to the thought of our
      author, is the place of the divine presence and throne, and the tabernacle [of Heb 8:2b] that of
      the angels and the blessed. [Therefore, God’s sanctuary is distinct from the angelic tabernacle.]
      The divine sanctuary (τὰ ἅγια) is “the place of God,” which in itself is elevated above all space
      and time, and filling all things, is not comprehended by any; it is, in brief, the uncreated heaven of
      the divine glory. But God, the absolute eternal One, has assigned place and time to all His creatures,
      as conditions of their existence. He makes use of those conditions in manifesting Himself to them;
      and the very highest creaturely existences, though having their roots of being in the eternal
      blessedness, are not independent of these creaturely forms. In accordance with this observation, it
      must be held, that there is always somewhere a glorious creaturely heaven, not forming indeed a
      definite part of the created universe, and yet having, from the very nature of those who belong to it, a
      certain definite localization; that it is always and only there wherever God vouchsafes to exhibit
      Himself to angels and to men, in glorious manifestations of His divine love. This heaven of glory is
      the ἀληθινὴ σκηνή here spoken of, “the greater and more perfect tabernacle” (of 9:11) through
      which Christ passed in order to enter that divine sanctuary of the immediate Presence, which as
      the holy of holies (sanctum sanctorum). (Italics his.)

        Rather amazingly, Delitzsch seems to be describing the highest heaven as the Eternal Heaven in a manner
that would be associated with what is popularly called the eternal now. I have color coded that part of his discussion
in yellow. In his estimation this divine sanctuary “is not essentially different from God Himself.” Using his
terminology, we might call it the Divine Heaven, the uncreated habitation of God that is essentially like God. This
is analogous to my description in MMP5 of God being an eternal 7D being who lives in His own eternal 7D
dimension. In agreement with Delitzsch, it is distinct from the lower “blessed spheres in which His glory is
manifested to the angels.” Per his discussion, we might call these the Angelic Heavens, and I have color coded
that aspect of his discussion in blue. In his estimation, Christ passed through all created heavens to reach the
eternal uncreated heavens.
        This two-fold view of Heaven being comprised by God’s heaven in distinction to the angelic heavens is
reinforced by Delitzsch’s comments on Heb 9:11-12. I will interject my corresponding 7D and 4D annotations of
my MMP5 model into his discussion since his description of the divine sanctuary seems to elevate it above the
space and time associated with the lower angelic realm and is roughly equivalent to my proposal in MMP5 that God
is a 7D being who resides in a 7D reality.

               Τὰ ἅγια [the 7D Holy Place/Sanctuary of Heb 9:12] is therefore here (as we have proved at
      8:2) the illocal place of the infinite, self-contained, self-centred Godhead, and ἡ σκηνή [the 4D
      City tabernacle of Heb 9:11] the supra-local place of divine manifestations to the angels and the
      blessed, the heaven of love in which God manifestly dwells, and in which He vouchsafes the
      beatific vision to certain of His creatures, the ναὸς τῆς σκηνῆς τοῦ μαρτυρίου of Rev. 15:5, which
      the apocalyptic seer beheld filled with incense-smoke from the δόξα and δύναμις of the divine
      presence [thus posing the heavenly tribulational as being the 4D City]. The former (τὰ ἅγια) [the
      7D Holy Place/Sanctuary of Heb 9:12] is that [7D] eternal heaven of God Himself (αὐτὸς ὁ
      οὐρανός), which is His own self-manifested eternal glory (John 17:5), and existed before all
      worlds; the latter (ἡ σκηνή) [4D City tabernacle of Heb 9:11] is the heaven of the blessed, in
      which He shines upon His creatures in the light of love. This [4D] created heaven has for its
      background the eternal [7D] dwelling-place of the Holy One, and on account of its [4D City]
      immeasurable vastness and “many mansions,” is called also οὐρανοί (heavens; comp. 8:1, 9:23,
      10:34, and 12:23, 25). (Italics his.)


29
 Franz Delitzsch, Commentary on the Epistle to the Hebrews, trans. Thomas L. Kingsbury, Third Edition., vol.

         Alford gives Delitzsch’s above comments on Heb 9:11 the praise it rightly deserves: “On the matter itself,—
the entrance of Christ into the holiest διὰ τοῦ ἰδίου αἵματος, I cannot do better than refer the student to the following
pages of Delitzsch, where he has treated it at length, and in a most interesting manner, the various hypotheses”
(AGTC). Spence-Jones (TPC) summarizes Delitzsch’s view well and prefers it over its most notable contender:

              Delitzsch, taking this view, offers a still more definite explanation; thus: “The former (τἀ
      ἅγια) is that eternal heaven of God himself (αὐτὸς ὁ οὐρανὸς) which is his own self-manifested
      eternal glory (John 17:5), and existed before all worlds; the latter (ἡ σκανῆ) is the heaven of the
      blessed, in which he shines upon his creatures in ‘the light of love’—‘the temple of the tabernacle
      of the testimony in heaven’ of Rev. 15:5, which the apocalyptic seer beheld filled with incense-
      smoke from ‘the glory of God, and from his power.’”

        Christ ascended through the lower heavenly tabernacle, which will be filled with smoke during the
Tribulation to arrive at the highest heaven, the eternal heaven, which is above all the heavens inhabited by God’s
creatures. Vaughan entertains this as a plausible interpretation: “May this be the ideal heaven of the divine self-
manifestation to saints and angels, as distinguished from the ideal heaven of the very Presence?”30 Turner tinkers
with this possibility:

              Tholuck regards the phrase as equivalent to heaven in its full sense, and the place said to be
      entered not as “heaven itself as at ver. 24, but something lying beyond heaven.” …For he is also said
      to have “passed through the heavens,” to have become “higher than the heavens,” to have “ascended
      far above all heavens,” 4:14, 7:26, Eph. 4:10. The possession of infinite dignity, glory and bliss, is
      the idea conveyed by all these expressions, not merely elevation to some definite locality. (Italics
      his.)31

        Turner seems to suggest that Christ ascended to a nonlocality beyond the heavenly localities, thus to a
nonspatial dimension. I would amend his conjecture by posing an eternal locality beyond the created localities, an
eternal heaven above the created heavens, a definite supratemporal-supraspatial locality in which God alone can
dwell. The propositions set forth by some of the older commentators seem superior to some of their newer
counterparts, though some modern interpreters seem to take the same position yet crouching it in scholastic garb.
                                      Illustration 13. Delitzsch’s Interpretation




30
 C. J. Vaughan, The Epistle to the Hebrews with Notes (London; New York: Macmillan and Co., 1890), 168.
31
  Samuel H. Turner, The Epistle to the Hebrews, in Greek and English, with an Analysis and Exegetical

        Putting the pieces together into a simplified schematic of Delitzsch’s interpretation yields the above result.
After calling attention to both compartments of the earthly tabernacle in Heb 9:1-7, the writer of Hebrews goes on
to assert in Heb 9:11-12 that Christ passed through its heavenly counterpart, a perfect tabernacle that God had
created in the angelic realm, to enter into a more holy sanctuary, leaving us to infer from his earlier comments in
Heb 4:14 and 7:26 about Christ having passed through the heavens so that He is now higher than those heavens,
and consequently that this sanctuary is in a higher heavenly realm than that associated with the angels.
        Discovering Delitzsch’s argument has provided additional exegetical support for my 7D model in MMP5,
which was a pleasant discovery during the present analysis. Notwithstanding, I reject any timeless-spaceless eternal
now, which Delitzsch seems to be describing. Nevertheless, if that is what Delitzsch intends, my proposal of God
having His own unique temporal-spatial dimension (described as 7D in MMP5) is compatible with the overall point
Delitzsch is making.

        Heb 4:14
        What does the writer of Hebrews mean when he says that Christ passed through the heavens? “Since then
we have a great high priest who has passed through the heavens, Jesus the Son of God, let us hold fast our
confession” (Heb 4:14). Delitzsch believes that Christ passed through the created heavens of Heb 4:14 to reach the
uncreated heavens and His eternal sanctuary in Heb 9:11-12, as he clarified in his comments on Heb 4:14:

      The throne of God is the final [7D] goal of the Lord’s transit through all the heavens. We must
      beware of regarding this διεληλυθότα τοὺς οὐρανοὺς [passed through the heavens in Heb 4:14]
      as parallel to the διὰ τῆς μείζονος καὶ τελειοτέρας σκηνῆς [through the greater and more perfect
      tabernacle] of [Heb] 9:11 (see note there); and indeed we are forbidden to do so by the addition in
      that passage of the epithet οὐ χειροποιήτου [“not made with hands”]. The heavens here [in Heb 4:14]
      are the created heavens, which Christ passed through in going to the [7D] “Place” of God (Ezek.
      3:12). That “Place” is God’s own eternal Doxa, the uncreated heaven (αὐτὸς ὁ οὐρανός, [heaven
      itself, Heb] 9:24) of His eternal residence and self-manifestation. We must distinguish between
      that highest heaven and the heaven of glory in which He vouchsafes to manifest Himself to the
      blessed. This latter is of necessity local…the collective whole of the “many mansions” into which
      the blessed are received. But the uncreated [7D] heaven of God Himself is His own omnipresent
      glory,—omnipresent, because absolutely without any local limitations: it may be said to be above
      all the created heavens, inasmuch as it is the super-creaturely background of all creation, and to
      be everywhere present, yet so as resting uncomprehended by the finite in its own infinitude. And
      now Jesus the exalted One, being thus above all heavens in this His [7D] Doxa with God, is
      thereby omnipresent too. This conclusion, drawn by the dogmatic theology of our church, is
      incontrovertible. Heb. 4:14, taken in connection with Eph. 4:10, is rightly adduced as proving the
      “ubiquity” of Christ. (CFTL, italics his.)

         As to Heb 4:14, various commentators point out that the extrabiblical material delves into various layers of
heaven and celestial journeys therein, particularly taken by Enoch, but the writer of Hebrews does not expound
upon such speculations. Some interpreters thereby assume that the writer of Hebrews does not share such
perspectives in his writing. Craig Koester (AYB) cites some of the examples of this broader literature but dismisses
them: “The author may have thought that heaven had a number of levels (cf. 2 Cor 12:2), but he does not actually
describe Jesus’ celestial journey (2 Enoch 1–20; Mart. Ascen. Isa. 7–9).” Bartolet has a reasonably good discussion
of the extrabiblical and biblical heavenly ascension literature. Regarding the OT backdrop, he surmises:

      The earthly temple is a gateway to heaven. For the ancient Israelite, the ark of the covenant was
      YHWH’s footstool (1 Chr. 28:2; Ps. 99:5; 132:7; Lam. 2:1;4 Ezek. 43:7), as if entering the Holy of
      Holies was entering into YHWH’s throne room itself. God’s glory fell out of heaven into the Holy
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 30
      of Holies. This is most likely what is reflected in Isa. 6:1. As Isaiah sees a vision of God, the glory of
      God flows down into the Holy of Holies like a royal robe.32

This statement is good insofar as it goes. The premise that the temple is a literal gateway/portal can be expanded in
accord with Delitzsch’s discussion to make the extrapolation that just as the earthly temple is a gateway to the
created celestial heavens, so the created celestial heavens are a gateway/portal to uncreated heavens. The 3D Earthly
Temple has a teleportal to the 3D Heavenly City, which in turn serves as a portal to the 4D City. Entering the 3D
City is “as if entering” the 4D City via means of a teleportal. These matters were discussed in MMP5. The additional
proposition posed herein, as an extrapolation of Delitzsch’s explanation, is that the 4D City could be theorized to
be a portal to the higher realms conjectured in MMP5.

         Heb 7:26
         Delitzsch’s spatial comments on Heb 7:26 are consistent with his other statements and appropriately
associate Christ’s dimensionality with His ontology, that is, His mode of existence. Dimensionality is necessarily
tied to ontology. For His glorified human nature to be a nature higher than the angels, Christ had to return, in that
exalted form of humanity, to a dimension higher than that occupied by 4D angels in His ascension. Therefore,
Delitzsch is correct on both accounts:

              “In respect to His present mode of existence, “become higher than the heavens”
      (ὑψηλότερος τῶν οὐρανῶν γενόμενος); i.e. He is now uplifted above all created heavens into the
      eternal realm of true life, or (as might be scripturally said) into the uncreated heaven itself of
      the divine nature, so that He is now become, strictly speaking, as to His mode of being, supra-
      mundane….express the super-celestial exaltation of His royal priesthood. (Italics his.)

         For our purposes, the primary spatial theories as to what the exaltation of Christ means in Heb 7:26 can be
broken down into tiered versus nontiered views of the heavens: “For it was fitting that we should have such a high
priest, holy, innocent, undefiled, separated from sinners and exalted above the heavens.” Bartolet takes the nontiered
route: “Hebrews does not give us a conception of tiered realms in the heavens themselves, but simply places the
Son as now above the heavens” (p. 282). He acknowledges that he is taking this generic bird’s eye view “in contrast
to some Second Temple speculation and possibly Paul in 2 Cor. 12:2 [regarding the third heaven]” (p. 282n107).
Hughes seeks to transcend the biblical language with philosophical terms:

      The language, as in 4:14 above, is that of transcendental reality….Paul is speaking of the same great
      reality when he affirms that “he who descended is he also who ascended far above all the heavens, that
      he might fill all things” (Eph. 4:10). The truth of the transcendental glory of our ever living High Priest
      guarantees to us the existential reality of his person and work” (NICNT, s.v. Heb 7:26).

Citing Eph 4:10 is valid. But doing so invalidates Hughes’ point if Paul’s words in that verse are accepted at face
value, as many commentators are inclined to do. The verse is best taken spatially, not existentially, as Best (ICC)
notes: “There is no reason to follow Schlier here [or Hughes for that matter] and understand ‘heaven’ existentially
rather than spatially.” Barth says that Schlier is courageous “to offer a demythologized, i.e. existential, interpretation
of the “heavenly places.” Alternatively, this demythologizing existential approach could be considered incredulous.
         Many commentators on Eph 4:10 believe that Paul is spatially thinking in terms of three or more heavens:
Pattison equates it with the third heaven or higher: “Above the material heavens, to the third heavens. Far above.—
As far above as the Deity is above creation” (emphasis his).33 Muddiman (BNTC) equates it with the third heaven
or higher: “Paul speaks of at least three heavens (2 Cor. 12:2); other Jewish writers were not so restrained.” Abbott

32
   Timothy J. Bartolet, “The Obedience of Sonship: Adamic Obedience as the Grounds for Heavenly Ascension in
the Book of Hebrews,” Ph.D. thesis (University of Pretoria, 2017), 245.
33
   R. E. Pattison, A Commentary, Explanatory, Doctrinal, and Practical, on the Epistle to the Ephesians (Boston;

merely asserting that Christ ascended to the third heavens or whether he intends to imply that He ascended even
higher, to a realm of Deity above the created third heavens, is unclear.
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                  Page 31
(ICC): “‘All the heavens’ is probably an allusion to the seven heavens of the Jews.” Arnold (ZECNT): “The fact
that the word for heaven is here in the plural is not surprising; the Hebrew word for heaven is always plural. This
may imply that there are various levels of heaven in a biblical worldview. Paul speaks, for example, of a “third
heaven” (2 Cor 12:2).” Merkle (EGGNT): “The use of πάντων (πᾶς) indicates multiple heavens.” Meyer (CEC):
“The expression ‘above all heavens’ has its basis in the conception of seven heavens, which number is not to be
diminished to three….the exaltation of Christ—clearly to be maintained as local [i.e., spatially]—as the highest of
all” (emphasis his). Up until my research in MMP5, I was inclined to think that Christ ascended to the third heaven
as the highest level of the heavens. Logistics, though, led me to pose seven levels to Heaven. Following the lead of
the more plausible arguments of other commentators would lead one to conclude that Paul had reached a similar
conclusion.
         Some commentators on Eph 4:10 clearly assert that Christ transcended beyond the three, seven, or more
heavens typically posed to a higher realm. Lincoln (WBC) is representative of this widely held perspective: “‘All,’
however, indicates that a number of heavens are in view here. Whether his readers think there are three, seven, or
more heavens (for discussion of the number of heavens in first-century cosmologies….the writer asserts that Christ
is above them all.” Bratcher and Nida (UBSH): “The phrase ‘all the heavens’ seems to reflect the Jewish idea that
there is a series of three or seven heavens, at the uppermost of which God himself lives.” “‘Whatsoever heaven
is higher than all the rest which are called heavens, into that place did he ascend.” Osborne (ONTC): “He
ascended ‘higher than all the heavens,’ referring to the Jewish picture of three—or at times of seven—heavens
(2 Cor 12:2).” After summarizing two, three, and seven leveled views of the heavens, Moule (CBSC) concludes:
“The plain meaning of the present passage, in any case, is that the Lord passed through and beyond all regions
of created blessedness into the region of the Throne.” Haynes likewise states:

              In Heb. 4:14; 7:26, we should read, “passed through the heavens” to the throne of God.
              “The Jews recognized three heavens. No one of these was ever confused with the other. The
      first was where the birds fly ‘in the midst of heaven.’ Rev. 19:17. The second is where the ‘stars of
      heaven’ are. Gen. 22:17. The third was the throne of God. Ps. 11:4. It was up to the last of these that
      Paul was transported. 2 Cor. 12:2.”34

Haynes is asserting that in Eph 4:10 Paul passed through the first and second heavens to reach the third heaven.
Christ, on the other hand, passed through the first, second, and third heavens to reach His final destination beyond
these lower heavens. Chafer seems to come to the same conclusion: “Here He is seen, in the living majesty of His
own Person, rising to the highest heaven in the exaltation which belongs to the Creator alone.”35 Pridham places
this ascension higher than being at the Father’s right hand, where Christ is adored by angels. Ascending far above
all heavens is quite different from setting at the right hand of God:

               That Christ has ascended “far above all heavens” is a truth of quite a different order from
      the testimony elsewhere given, that He was set at the right hand of God by the mighty power of the
      Father of glory. We have seen Him compared with principalities and powers, as the honoured Object
      of God’s righteous favour and delight. We see him here rising in the living majesty of His Person to
      that elevation above the highest heaven, which must be occupied by the Creator with respect to His
      own work. The Man Christ Jesus is doubtless, locally in heaven, where the angels see Him and
      adore Him. It is as having gone up thither, that He has become the Receiver of those gifts which He
      bestows on men. But that is not the point of the Apostle’s teaching in these verses. He desires rather
      to remind us of that mystery of godliness which constitutes the truth which is the foundation and glory
      of the Church. For the Church which Christ owns is the Church of the living God. Divine life, in its
      inextinguishable energy and almighty sufficiency, is the object of our contemplation here.
      Accordingly, the death and burial of Christ, facts of such vital import, and so carefully recorded in
      their place, are not now mentioned. The descent into the lower parts of the earth is viewed as an act of



34
   W. A. Haynes, The Beautiful Word Pictures of the Epistle to the Ephesians: Or, the Busy Man’s Commentary
upon the Bible (Caney, KS: Busy Man’s Bible Company, 1911).
35
   Lewis Sperry Chafer, The Ephesian Letter (New York: Loizeaux Brothers, 1935), 130.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                       Page 32
      the same Living One, who presently returns again to the accustomed throne of His dominion. Personal
      supremacy and essential Divinity are the ideas here kept before our minds. (Italics his.)36

Hodge likewise deduces that the abode of God to which Christ ascended in Eph 4:10 was above (i.e., dimensionally
higher) than the invisible yet created third heaven of which Paul spoke in 2Cor 12:2:

               But often there is a reference to a plurality of heavens, as when the expression “all heavens”
      is used. The Jews reckoned seven heavens, and Paul, 2 Cor. 12:2, speaks of the third heavens; the
      atmosphere, the region of the stars, and above all the abode of God. Above all heavens plainly means
      above the whole universe; above all that is created visible and invisible; above thrones, principalities,
      and powers. All things, all created things, are subject to the ascended Redeemer.37 (Emphasis his.)

         Transcending the angelic realms is essential to Christ’s Divinity. Dimensionality is intrinsically tied to
ontology. For His glorified human nature to be a nature higher than the angels, Christ had to return to a dimension
higher than that occupied by 4D angels in His ascension. After noting that Christ ascended higher than the three or
seven heavens, Osborne (ONTC) states that Christ filling the whole universe as a result does not refer to
“immanence, Christ filling the world with his presence, but transcendence.” Unconvincingly, Candlish tries to limit
this exaltation to soteriology:

              Here, as there, “all things” must be understood in the most extensive sense, so as to include
      the whole universe; and “fill” must mean to occupy with His presence. From this passage an
      argument has been drawn for the doctrine of the omnipresence of Christ’s glorified human
      nature. For since the Deity is always omnipresent, it can only be the humanity of our Saviour
      that becomes so by His exaltation. It may be doubted, however, whether such an abstract
      metaphysical conception is akin to the thought of Paul here. He is emphasizing the oneness of the
      descending and ascending Lord. By His descent He has become our Saviour, and by His ascending
      far above all the heavens He is everywhere present in that character, and fills all the universe, in
      the grace and love and saving power that are manifested in His incarnation and life on earth, and death
      on the cross. This is the ubiquity that is of value to us in our religious life.38

         Soteriology is necessarily linked to ontology. If Christ were not God, His death would have no atoning
value. His ubiquity cannot be limited to His functioning throughout the universe as a Savior. Delitzsch did much
better in linking Christ’s ubiquity with His exaltation as a glorified human being above realms of created spatial
limitation. Similarly, I associate God’s omnipresence with His 7D spatiality. For Jesus’ omnipresence to be
communicated to His humanity at His ascension required that His glorified humanity participates in His 7D
spatiality. Paul hints at the 4D nature of God’s love in Eph 3:18. But the only way the Lord can communicate that
love in the fulness anticipated in Eph 3:19 is if Paul’s statement a few verses later in Eph 4:10 is given its full
import. The only way the risen Christ can dwell experientially in our hearts through faith (Eph 3:14) is for Him to
communicate His presence to us by means of His omnipresence and thus be glorified as Christ Jesus (Eph 3:21).
The risen God-Man, Christ Jesus, had to raise His humanity as Jesus to the level of His deity as Christ to function
as the head of the corporate body being described in Eph 4. Bruce (NICNT, Eph 4:10) does well to note that the
spatial passing leads to ubiquity sharing:

              Similarly in the letter to the Hebrews he is said to have “passed through the heavens” in order
      to be “exalted above the heavens” (Heb. 4:14; 7:26). He ascended there, we are now told, to “fill all
      things”—to pervade the universe with his presence. This is in keeping with a possible interpretation
      of Eph. 1:23, that he is the one who “fills the universe in all its parts.” There his relation to “the church,
      which is his body,” is especially in view: so here, the “filling” of the universe inaugurated in his

      ascension is now being put into effect more particularly as he supplies the church with everything

              In the OT and other literature derived from it the filling of the universe is a divine property:
      “‘Do I not fill heaven and earth?’ says the LORD” (Jer. 23:24). Now, part of the exaltation conferred

Moule (CBSC) likewise believes that Christ passed through all the lower heavens to reach the highest heaven in
Eph 4:10 but poses a questionable dichotomy regarding the filling: “There is here no reference to a diffused and
ubiquitous corporeity, but to a pervading and energizing omnipresence … Christ is perfect God, and perfect and
glorified Man; as the former He is present everywhere, as the latter He can be present anywhere” (emphasis and
ellipses his). Moule’s assertation that God is present everywhere via His omnipresence is susceptible to the criticism
posed by a peanut butter illustration in MMP5. Are we to think that God’s omnipresence means that He is spread
out like peanut butter throughout the creation? No. As pointed out in that discussion, some authors, like Bilezikian,
give a better understanding of omnipresence: “If by omnipresence we mean that God is actually present in all places,
this is wrong….Because he is transcendent, God has the capacity to be actively present any place where he wishes
to be or where he is needed and called upon.”39 The translational gloss offered by Bratcher and Nida (UBSH) is that
“it would be possible to say ‘in order that he could be everywhere.’” As a result of His ascension, Christ, even in
His resurrected humanity, can simultaneously be everywhere He wishes to be. Basically, this better-articulated
omnipresence is what Moule attributes to Christ as a glorified Man. His glorified humanity had to participate in His
divine dimensionality. Consequently, Paul is being logically astute to assert that Christ Jesus ascended above all
created dimensions.
         Abbott (ICC) objects: “But how can the occupation of a special place in heaven have for its object presence
throughout the universe?” He mentions several theories of how omnipresence can be explained in this context. My
response from MMP5 is that Jesus can be in as many special places as He wants in 4D Heaven because of His
presence in 7D Heaven. Hendriksen and Kistemaker (NTC, Eph 4:10) cite four popular interpretations and
acknowledge that this third position is held by various commentators: “that he might fill the universe with his
omnipresence.” But Hendriksen and Kistemaker object:

              As to (3), favored by Hodge and others, it is not clear how Christ, by means of his ascension,
      could become omnipresent. As to his deity he was already omnipresent. And as to his human nature,
      unless we accept the general proposition that by means of the ascension something peculiar to the
      divine nature is communicated to the human nature—which is not the Reformed position—it is hard
      to see how that human nature could now become omnipresent. (Emphasis theirs.)

I reject this Reformed limitation of Christ’s humanity. Regarding Heb 4:14, Huges asserts: “Our author is speaking
of something far more than a spatial journey, like that of some cosmonaut: his language is that of transcendence.
Not only did Jesus ascend, but in doing so he completely transcended all the limits of time and space. His
transcendence guarantees his uniqueness, his greatness” (PNTC, emphasis his, s.v. Heb 4:14). Even if one buys into
the timeless-spaceless view of God, Huges assessment is still self-defeating. The heavenly angelic realm is not
timeless or spaceless. Angels are coming and going, ministering in real time and space in that dimension. If we
were to take Huges’ statement seriously, we would have to conclude that Hebrews is saying that Jesus ascended
above all created heavens to reach the timeless and spaceless Heaven above them. But that would mean that the
uncreated heavens He reached were not timeless and spaceless after all! A plurality of spatial heavens is necessarily
supposed. Nevertheless, I do not doubt that an eternal Transcendental Heaven is projected by the writer of Hebrews.
          Notwithstanding, this eternal Transcendental Heaven is not postulated to the exclusion of the created
Transitional Heavens through which Christ had to pass to reach it. My model would equate this Transcendental
Heaven with God’s 7D Heaven, a realm of 7D time and 7D space.40 Combining the better parts of Huges’ argument

         The tiered position is more consistent than the nontiered approach. Still, two basic options must be

exhaustive. The primary or superior vantage point, depending on whether one or two vantage points are intended,

accord with the spatial levels advocated by O’Brien and Land. O’Brien (PNTC) implies that the writer of Hebrews
makes use of this Second Temple background: “Apocalyptic sources used spatial imagery to refer to a visionary
journey through a number of heavens to reach the presence of God. Our author here makes use of a spatial metaphor
to point to the highest possible exaltation.” Lane (WBC) makes a similar affirmation:

       Similar spatial imagery is found in certain apocalyptic sources (e.g., Asc. Isa. 7–8) which refer to a

       above all heavens” (cf. Traub, TDNT 5:527–28; Bertram, TDNT 8:609). In v 26d the writer has made
       use of a spatial metaphor to denote the highest possible exaltation. The phrase resumes the motif
       of the high priest who “has passed through the heavens” to gain access to the presence of God.

Their assessment is in accord with the extrabiblical and biblical context in which Hebrews was written, affirming
spatial heavens created by the transcendent God who exists above these created heavens in a heavenly super-
celestial, supra-spatial, supra-temporal realm of His own, or, as discussed more precisely in MMP5, God lives in
an eternal 7D ultraspace.

        Biblical Heaven(s) and The Highest Heavens
        Bartolet does not discuss the OT texts that indicate multiple layers of Heaven or God’s dwelling above
those layers. However, in MMP5, I discussed the implications of such texts, for example, stating:

       Furthermore, heaven(s) and the highest heaven cannot contain God (1Kgs 8:27; 2Chron 2:6; 6:18).
       However, these statements refer to created spaces. If 7D space is uncreated and eternal, then such
       statements do not apply to it. To use the analogies of boxes, when God created our spatial and temporal
       box, I picture Him as standing outside both our time and space in His own metaphysical time and
       space.

These heaven(s) and the highest heaven(s) belong to God (Dt 10:14) and thus were created by Him, as a house for
His created hosts: “Thou alone art the Lord. Thou hast made the heavens, the heaven of heavens with all their host,
the earth and all that is on it, the seas and all that is in them. Thou dost give life to all of them and the heavenly host


reflect the Jewish idea that there is a series of three or seven heavens, at the uppermost of which God himself lives.”
Robert G. Bratcher and Eugene Albert Nida, A Handbook on Paul’s Letter to the Ephesians, UBS Handbook Series
(New York: United Bible Societies, 1993). Various sources pose seven heavens, such as 2 Enoch, Hinduism, ancient
Babylonian cult, Islam, and the Ascension of Isaiah. I derived my theory of seven heavens independently of such
sources and in contradistinction to speculations in 2 Enoch that God dwells with the angels in the seventh heaven.
My model would include God living with the angels in the third heaven. Nevertheless, some vaguely perceived or
long forgotten or distorted truth may be behind the various propositions converging on the supposition of the 7th

bows down before Thee” (Neh 9:6). The heavens and all their hosts were made by God. As Craigie (NICOT) says
regarding Dt 10:14: “God was creator and possessor of the cosmos in its entirety…. The verse stresses the absolute
sovereignty of God over all the created universe, both heaven and earth.” These created heavens and the heaven of
the heavens, which belong to Him, contain His creation and His creatures, but they cannot contain Him, the Creator.
No house, sanctuary, or tabernacle created in the heaven(s) and the highest heaven(s) can contain the Creator. Thus,
regarding 2Chron 2:6 Klein (HERM) rightly observes: “The ‘highest heavens’ belong to Yahweh (Deut 10:14) and

of “holy of holies” would, however, suggest that not all the heavens, but the highest heavens are intended [by]
cannot contain thee” (emphasis his).
         Since God cannot be contained in created spatial heavens, He must exist outside them in a higher uncreated
spatial realm. As discussed at length in MMP5, God describes Himself as “the One who is high and lifted up, who
inhabits eternity, whose name is Holy: ‘I dwell in the high and holy place’” (Is 57:15; ESV). Motyer (TOTC)
uses parallelism within Isaiah to state what Isaiah means: “Lives for ever: as ‘father of eternity’ in 9:6 means
‘eternally a father’, so here ‘inhabiter of eternity’ means ‘eternally the inhabitant’.” To be an inhabitant of eternity
in Is 57:15, God must have a place to inhabit throughout eternity. God has inhabited this place from all eternity.
God describes Himself with spatial terms when He describes Himself as an inhabitant. God has been an inhabitant
of someplace before He created anyplace. Barnes (BNONT) is superior in his assessment: “It seems to be used to
denote the elevated place of an eternal dwelling” (italics his). Ultraspace is this elevated space that is in an
eternally higher dimension than all created space.42 God has been an inhabitant of someplace before He created
anyplace. God’s ultimate habitation is spatial and eternal. He says so. He lives in His own spacetime-continuum
and has done so for all eternity.
         We should not presume that the writer of Hebrews or his readers were ignorant of these OT truths. The
deductions are straightforward. If these heavens belong to God, then God created them. No one else created them
for Him and gave them to Him! When He created these lower spatial dimensions, He would have done so from a
higher uncreated spatial dimension. He fills the lower spatial dimensions (Jer 23:24), but He is not limited to these
lower spatial dimensions. He rides upon them; He is not contained within them (Ps 68:33). The writer of Hebrews
and his readers were not ignorant of such logical inferences, and his writings may be presumed to perceive that
perspective from his writings.
         Consequently, the proposition by Warnock that the upper chambers of Solomon’s Temple (1Chron 28:11;
2Chron 3:9; cp. Eze 42:5 [cp. Amos 9:6]) represent something higher than the Holy of Holies is intriguing:

      Also above the holy of holies there was an area known as “the upper chambers” which were
      overlaid with gold, and of which we know practically nothing….
                We spoke of the holy of holies, the place of God’s glorious presence, earlier in this writing.
      Here again we have the holy of holies in this enlarged and beautified Temple. But something else is
      mentioned here, simply described as “the upper chambers.” This is about all we know about this area.
                How we would like to know more about some of these secrets that are hidden in the Word!
      There is a “secret place of the Most High” that God has made available to His own (Ps. 91:1). There
      is a life that is “hid with Christ in God” (Col. 3:3). David also said, “He shall hide me in his pavilion:
      in the secret of his tabernacle shall he hide me” (Ps. 27:5).
                We may never know too much about some of these obscure patterns of the Temple of
      Solomon.43

         The mismatch in the height of the 20-cubit height of the Most Holy Place with the 30-cubit height of the
Holy place has often led to the belief that there was a space above the Most Holy Place and perhaps below it as
well, as Selman (TOTC,2Chron 3:9) notes:

        ‘Upper chambers’ (v. 9, NRSV, RSV, REB, NEB) are not mentioned directly elsewhere, though, as the
        height of the Most Holy Place was only twenty cubits as against thirty cubits for the building, it is
        often thought that there may have been a room above and/or below. Less plausibly, the associated side
        rooms may be meant (cf. 1 Kgs 6:5–6, 8–10).

         What is the significance of this upper space? Could the biblical text be typologically depicting the higher
abode of God above the 4D City described in my MMP5 model as 7D Heaven? For 1Chron 28:11, Omanson and
Ellington (UBSH) provide a translation conducive to this understanding: “The Hebrew word rendered upper rooms
is translated ‘room on the roof’ in Jdg 3:20 by GNT, which is the sense it has in this context, so REBC says ‘roof-
chambers.’” Keil and Delitzsch’s comments on 1Chron 28:11 are even more conducive and specify that it was “the
upper chambers over the most holy place” (COTOT). Not to be outdone, Omanson and Ellington (UBSH) discuss
various interpretations of this phrase in 2Chron 3:9 and provide a very tantalizing possibility. They find the
proposition that it refers to something inside the Holy of Holies to be unlikely: “Some interpreters think that the
writer was referring to upper rooms within the Most Holy Place, but this seems unlikely since no other passage in
the Old Testament refers to such rooms there.” Yet they suggest an intriguing second possibility:

        There was an empty space of 10 cubits between the ceiling of the Most Holy Place and the ceiling
        of the Temple. Translators will find drawings of the Temple in some commentaries and biblical
        dictionaries that have steps leading up to the Most Holy Place. Other drawings, based on the second
        interpretation, show a loft, or empty space, above the Most Holy Place. If there was an empty space
        of 10 cubits between the ceiling of the Most Holy Place and the ceiling of the Temple, it may have
        been this place that was overlaid. (Italics theirs.)

         This second possibility, describing empty space over the Most Holy Place, practically shouts a higher
dimensionality than the Most Holy Place. If the cubical Holy of Holies represents the Cubical City, which is a
reasonable inference held by many and explained and illustrated at length in MMP5, then the upper chambers above
it could be referring to a higher dimension inaccessible by both angels and humans. The cherubim are represented
as being present in the Holy of Holies (Heb 9:3-5). Humans had access through the High Priest in the OT and
through Christ in the NT to this Most Holy Place. Yet neither angels nor humans access the upper chambers above
the Holy of Holies. When this proposition is augmented with plausible association with Ezekiel’s Millennial
Temple, the typological probability becomes compelling.44 Numerous lines of biblical data lead to the conclusion
that Christ not only ascended to the Holy of Holies but above it.

          Extrabiblical Heaven(s) and The Highest Heavens
          As to extrabiblical background, Bartolet continues with various sources, beginning with 1 Enoch:

                1 Enoch entails several descriptions of heaven, the throne room, and perhaps multiple layers
        within heaven itself. In 1 En. 14, Enoch ascends up into heaven in a vision. As Enoch ascends he sees
        a house (14:10) and a second house (14:15) where the latter is greater than the former…. All of this
        suggests heaven is like a great Temple with a Holy Place and a Holy of Holies where the throne is….
                1 Enoch 18 may suggest multiple layers to heaven. (Pp. 246-247.)

So, we can presume the familiarity of the writer of Hebrews and his readership with such perspectives. Similarly,
from Josephus’ Antiquities (3:123), Bartolet notes, “The Holy of Holies is for God alone,” but “the outer court and
the holy place is for the priests’ (pp. 250-251). But if human ministers in the earthly temple represent angelic
ministers in the heavenly temples, then the heavenly Holy of Holies would be reserved for God alone and not

accessed by the angels. It would be in a higher dimension than the 4D City. Bartolet basically jettisons this rich
literary background by focusing attention on the focus of Hebrews:

      Unlike some ascent structures in apocalyptic and mystic texts, Hebrews does not focus on the journey
      through the heavens and the revelation of these heavens, rather Hebrews focuses on the reality of an
