        Bartolet’s Objections
        When Bartolet reaches Heb 8:2, he acknowledges that he is running counter to the literary background of
the Second Temple literature and possibly even to Paul’s description of the third heaven in 2Cor 12:2 in order to
maintain his thesis: “Hebrews does not give us a conception of tiered realms in the heavens themselves, but simply
places the Son as now above the Heavens” (p. 282), so that Heb 8:2 “is epexegetical and does not imply two parts
of the heavenly tent” (p. 284). Moreover, Bartolet is running counter to the biblical background:

      Hebrews does refer to heaven τῆς σκηνῆς τῆς ἀληθινῆς (Heb. 8:2). ‘The tent (τὴν σκηνήν)’ (8:5; cf.
      Exod. 25:40 & 26:1) probably refers to the tent/tabernacle as a whole with its two internal rooms
      of the Holy Place and Holy of Holies. The intention of Hebrews is not to parse out division within
      heaven, but to make the analogy connect to the work of the High Priest. (P. 274)

Then, regarding the Heb 9:11, Bartolet acknowledges the force of Lane’s argument:

      If the earthly counterpart has an outer tent (the Holy Place) that the priest passed through then
      the heavenly must have two ‘tents’ that Jesus passes through. This argument is certainly
      plausible. And it has a compelling logic to it. It coheres to a tight representation between the earthly
      sanctuary and the heavenly sanctuary. (P. 289)

Yet Bartolet rejects Lane’s interpretation with several counterpoints:

              We would respond further to Lane by noting that if τῆς μείζονος καὶ τελειοτέρας σκηνῆς refers
      to outer aspects of the heavenly sanctuary, it becomes irrelevant to the argument of Hebrews which
      nowhere focuses on the symbolism of the holy place. Second, as Cockerill notes, Heb. 9:12 indicates
      what the location is with the clause εἰσῆλθεν ἐφάπαξ εἰς τὰ ἅγια. Going through the tent and entering
      εἰς τὰ ἅγια are synonymous. “Hebrews does not seem to be interested in any distinction between
      two sections in the heavenly sanctuary.” Christ enters into heaven or passes through in his
      progress to sit at the right hand of the divine throne. Hebrews is not speculating on how the
      universe is structured like all the aspects of the earthly tabernacle. Rather, the earthly most holy
      place where God’s footstool resided is surpassed by the heavenly throne room itself, and Jesus
      passed through/into it. For Hebrews, the outer section with the veil is not part of the heavenly but a
      parable for the earthly Old Covenant.

        In summary, Lane (WBC) has a good argument for a distinction in Heb 9:11-12, even translating the
passage as affirming that Christ was “passing through [dia] the greater and more perfect compartment [i.e., perfect
tabernacle] not made with hands, that is to say, not of ordinary building, he entered once for all into the real
sanctuary.” He defends the spatial sense of dia in his notes regarding v. 11, thereby affirming that Christ passed
through the heavenly compartmentalized tabernacle into the greater and more perfect sanctuary. Then, in his
comments on v. 11, he adds that the expression, not made with hands,

      indicates that the reference is not to a passage through the visible heavens but to heaven as the
      dwelling-place of God and the angels (so Hofius, Vorhang, 50–53, 65–69; Andriessen, BZ 15 [1971]
      84–85; for the OT background, see L. I. J. Stadelmann, The Hebrew Concept of the World, AnBib 39
      [Rome: Pontifical Biblical Institute, 1970] 49–52). The syntax of vv 11–12 demands that a distinction
      be made between the σκηνή, “front compartment,” through which Christ passed and τὰ ἅγια, “the
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                        Page 38
      sanctuary,” into which he entered. The way into the presence of God was through the heavenly
      counterpart to the front compartment of the earthly tabernacle (9:2, 6; so Michel, 310–11;
      Michaelis, TDNT 7:376–77; Cody, Heavenly Sanctuary, 148–50; Peterson, “Examination,” 242–44).

Clarification concerning Lane’s wording may be in order. He is not saying that Christ came “to heaven as the
dwelling-place of God and the angels.” Much to the contrary, as he goes on to clarify, the reference to passing
through is to the realm of heaven where God dwells with the angels, so that Christ passed through the heavenly
dimension where God dwells with the angels into the very presence of God; each have their counterpart to the
compartments of the earthly tabernacle.
        O’Brien (PNTC) follows Lane’s lead, manifesting the same insights and shortcomings. Regarding Heb 8:2,
O’Brien follows Lane in error by presumptuously stating:

                The text literally refers to ‘the sanctuary and (kai.) the true tent’. It is preferable to regard the
      two nouns as synonyms for the one heavenly sanctuary. On this view the connecting kai. is
      explanatory, while the two nouns are followed by the singular ‘which’ (h]n) suggesting that only one
      tent is intended. The alternative view suggests a distinction between the inner and outer shrines. But
      the main contrast here is between the heavenly and earthly sanctuaries, not between parts of the
      heavenly one. So Brace, ISO; Lane, 1:205; Weiss, 432; Ellingworth, 402; Koester, 375-376; against
      Attridge, 218.

His argument on Heb 8:2 backfires, however, because the singular which (hos) is feminine and therefore refers
syntactically to one of the antecedents, that is, the feminine tent/tabernacle, not to the neuter sanctuary. The
assumption made by O’Brien and commentators of his ilk that the relative pronoun is functioning in a transgender
capacity to refer to the neuter-feminine mismatch in a feminine capacity needs to be proven, not merely assumed.
The data argues against the gender fluidity they assume. Attridge holds the high ground in this debate, an advantage
he failed to press to his advantage in an otherwise very commendable discussion. Secondly, just because the main
contrast is between the heavenly and earthly parts of the tabernacle does not mean that a secondary contrast cannot
be intended between heavenly parts of the tabernacle. Main contrast should not be misconstrued to mean only
contrast.
         When it comes to Heb 9:11, O’Brien, Ellingworth, Attridge, and Lane are agreed as to the local sense of
dia in v. 11. Ellingworth (NIGTC), for instance, states:

               There seems little doubt, following extensive discussion, that διὰ τῆς … σκηνῆς is local
      (so Andriessen-Lenglet; Andriessen 1971, 1977.35f.; N. H. Young 1973b; Braun; Attridge; Lane;
      Giles 1973.195f. disagrees), while διὰ … τοῦ ἰδίου αἵματος, like διʼ αἵματος τράγων … (v. 11) and
      διὰ πνεύματος αἰωνίου (v. 14), must be instrumental. Efforts to make διὰ τῆς σκηνῆς primarily or
      exclusively instrumental, and thus to identify the tabernacle with the body or blood of Christ (so
      Chrysostom, Augustine, Ambrose, Calvin, Westcott, Bonsirven, N. H. Young 1973a.175–178), give a
      harsh meaning; it is preferable to think of the author as gliding from the local meaning of the first διά
      to the following instrumental phrases. There is a similar ambivalence in the use of πρός in 1:7f., in the
      use of πρώτη in 9:1f., 8, and possibly in the use of διά in → 10:20.

          Lane (WBC, Heb 9:11), as noted, states: “The context…indicates that διά relates to a space and must be
understood in a local sense (“through the compartment”) rather than in an instrumental sense.” Basically, if dia is
spatial it means Jesus went through the tabernacle; He went through that space.45 Likewise, O’Brien (PNTC) excels
in his comments on Heb 9:11, rejecting the instrumental sense of dia in v. 11, taking it in a local sense instead, and
specifying that it means Jesus passed through the heavenly tabernacle/tent as the outer compartment on His way to
the heavenly Holy of Holies:



45
  Harris (EEGNT) acknowledges that “a spatial understanding (‘through’) fits well with the image of Jesus passing
through the heavens.” Taking dia instrumentally, on the other hand, “often requires that the ‘greater and more
perfect tabernacle be understood metaphorically.”
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                       Page 39
               Many exegetes understand the preposition ‘through’ (dia with the genitive case) in an
      instrumental sense, signifying ‘by means of’, and regard ‘the tent’ metaphorically as denoting the
      whole sanctuary. It is greater and more perfect since it is superior to the whole earthly shrine (9:1),
      and ultimately it signifies the place where God is present.
               But in our judgment, it is preferable to take the prepositional phrase in a local sense. The
      ‘greater and more perfect tent’ through which Jesus passed in order to enter the heavenly Holy of
      Holies is the counterpart of the outer compartment in the earthly sanctuary. From 8:1-2 up to this
      point in our author’s homily, ‘tent’ has been used consistently in a local sense, while this explanation
      of the prepositional phrase with its suggestion of movement more closely parallels the work of the
      high priest on the Day of Atonement. This tent is the forecourt of the heavenly sanctuary and
      comprises the ‘heavens’, through which Jesus passed (4:14, which clearly has a local significance).
      Further, the imagery is anticipated in 7:26 (exalted above the heavens), which presupposes a passage
      through the heavens. In contrast with the outer tent of the earthly ritual (9:8), this tent is greater
      and more perfect, descriptions that recall the frequent comparatives used of the superior features of
      the heavenly order (1:4; 3:3; 7:22; 8:6). Christ passed through the heavenly counterpart to the front
      compartment of the earthly tabernacle, and into heaven itself, the dwelling place of God. As the more
      perfect tent it perfectly fulfilled its function of actually lead[ing] Christ, and thus ultimately believers,
      into the presence of God’.
               The further qualifying phrase, that this tent is not wade with human hands, that is to say, is
      not a part of this creation, makes it plain that Jesus did not pass through the visible heavens.
      ‘Made with hands’ was a common expression for human constructions and appeared frequently in the
      Old Testament with negative connotations for idols. Although our author was aware that the tabernacle
      was constructed by human hands, it was not idolatrous since Moses had ‘made’ it at God’s command
      (Heb. 8:5). Nevertheless it was not of divine origin, and like the Jerusalem temple, which also was
      made with hands, it would pass away (Mark 14:58; Acts 7:48). The author’s explanatory parenthesis,
      that is to say, is not a part of this creation, indicates that what is the opposite of ‘made with hands’
      points to what is transcendent and spiritual. (Italics his.)

O’Brien’s exposition Heb 9:11, therefore, poses that the earthly tent pictures the heavenly tent that Jesus passed
through to reach the Heavenly Holy of Holies. The tent that Jesus passed through in this verse is not the visible
heavens but the transcendental and spiritual heavens. Excellent! O’Brien (PNTC) also adds several good notes
to his comments above:

      80. The nearest antecedent to skhnh/ (‘tent’) here in v. 11 through which Jesus passed is in v 8, where
      ‘tent’ has reference to the front compartment of the earthly tabernacle (Lane: 2:238; Bruce, 212). The
      syntax of w 11-12 demands that a distinction be made between the skhnh/, ‘front compartments through
      which Christ passed and ta. a[gia, ‘the sanctuary’, into which he entered.

      83. Against Koester, 408-409. The preposition διά is used in other contexts in a local sense (e.g., 10:20,
      and note the prepositional prefix διά in the verb dielhluqo,ta. which means ‘passed through’ here in
      4:14 and [die,bhsan in] 11:29); Attridge, 245. Further, there are parallel passages which speak of an
      entrance ‘behind the curtain’ or into the presence of God (6:19- 20; 9:24, 25).

      86. Hebrews seems to speak of the heavens in three different ways: [1 the earthly heavens] the
      created and transitory heavens (1:10-12; 12:26), [2 the angelic heavens] the heavens through
      which Jesus passed (4:14; 9:11-12), and [3 God-only heaven] heaven as God’s dwelling place
      (9:24).

         To summarize O’Brien’s position in Pauline terms, Hebrews speaks of the heavens in three ways:
(1) the visible first and second heavens seen from Earth, (2) the invisible third heavens not seen from Earth
in which the angels have access to God, and (3) the heaven in which God alone dwells, beyond the third
heaven. Is such a position plausible? Yes! Even probable.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 40
         As noted, Bartolet acknowledges this argument by Lane and further advanced by O’Brien in Heb 9:11 “is
certainly plausible. And it has a compelling logic to it. It coheres to a tight representation between the earthly
sanctuary and the heavenly sanctuary” (p. 289). Even so, Bartolet rejects their argument because it necessarily
leads to a two-compartment view of the tabernacle in Heaven, as Bartolet summarizes: “As Lane himself notes,
passing through the heavens as an outer court could not be a reference to passing through visible heavens…. The
reference is not to a passage through visible heavens but to heaven as the dwelling place of God and the angels
[through which Christ passes]” (p. 289). In other words, the writer of Hebrews is not merely asserting that
Jesus passed through visible aerial astral heavens but is explicitly asserting that Jesus passed through the
invisible heaven where God dwelt with the angels. Thus, Jesus went through the angelic heavens on His way
to God’s uppermost heavenly sanctuary (which is counterpart of the innermost compartment of the earthly
sanctuary). In short, O’Brien succinctly takes the position argued at length by Delitzsch.
         Bartolet admits that he originally intended to support this position but instead rejected it: “Peter O’Brien
had argued strongly for this view as well...He argued that outer sanctuary was the heavens that are passed
through. This attributes more cosmological speculation to Hebrews than the author himself makes. Our
original intent had been to argue more with O’Brien’s articulation which goes beyond Lane” (p. 289n121).
One must wonder, though, if Lane and O’Brien had been consistent in applying their compelling logic in
Heb 9:11 to their exegesis of Heb 8:2, would Bartolet have been able to resist the overall consistent force of
their argument. As it is, both Lane and O’Brien undercut their own arguments in Heb 9:11 by exegetically equating
the sanctuary with the true tabernacle in Heb 8:2 and then logically distinguishing them in Heb 9:11-12. Where is
the logic in that?!?


         Rejoinder to Bartolet
         The better recourse is to accept the compartmentalization posed by Lane and O’Brien in Heb 9:11-12 and
be consistent by maintaining it in Heb 8:2. As to Bartolet’s attempt to counter Lane and O’Brien regarding Heb 9:11
by appealing to the focus of Hebrews, Bartolet needlessly supposes that this epistolary focus rejects the broader
biblical context and extrabiblical background as to the multifaceted nature of Heaven. On the contrary, the ascension
texts implemented by the epistle can be understood as presuming the familiarity of its readers with the extrabiblical
and biblical ascension material and multilayered genre of Heaven. The epistle then elevates it via temple typology.
In other words, the author of Hebrews was writing within a broader literary context that would be in harmony with
anticipating Christ’s spatial celestial journey. Both he and his readers share a common viewpoint of Heaven as
having several levels, but he elevates that understanding by linking it with complex temple typology. Admittedly,
the writer of Hebrews does not delve into the distinction between the two sections in rigid detail. Had he done so
with laser focus, it would have hindered the text from being seen through a fluidity that encourages seeing multiple
levels and vantage points. The writer’s ambiguity regarding the exact corresponding details of the celestial
ascension could be interpreted as purposefully inspiring his readers’ imaginations. Bartolet’s attempt to clip the
wings of Heb 9:11 with a rigidity that artificially binds the wings together in Heb 8:2 is much too constrained and
strained.

         Multileveled Understanding of Multileveled Heavens
         For the simple-minded (thus the milk-minded described in Heb 5:11-14), the writer of Hebrews writes at a
level that allows a surface-level understanding, simply juxtaposing the earthly and heavenly tabernacles. For the
more mature-minded, however, he encourages contemplation on the compartmentalization of the temple. For the
more heavenly-minded, he combines these two vantage points to make a third one that affirms the contrast between
the earthly tabernacle and the two-compartment heavenly temple. For the more noble-minded, he hints that the two-
compartment heavenly temple might be seen from multiple vantage points. He writes at a level conducive to
multiple layers of complexity and his readers’ various stages of maturity. He seeks to lead them into maturity by
giving them material that can be understood more deeply at various levels of maturity. He is not content to stagnate
at the elementary levels about Christ (Heb 6:1).
         Consequently, the complexity posed by Delitzsch is certainly within the realm of plausibility. If one wants
to follow Delitzsch and pose that in Eph 4:10 Paul is indicating that Christ hyperspaced to that 7D location, then
my 7D model would accommodate that argument. One might even pose that Christ hyperspaced to the highest arena
of the 4D dimension, namely, to the Father’s throne in the 4D City as the primary vantage point. Next, one could
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                    Page 41
allow that Christ transcended even that dimension on His way to the 7D Transcendent Heaven as a plausible
secondary vantage point. In any case, Delitzsch thinks that in Heb 8:1 the heavens refer to both the sanctuary
and tabernacle of v. 2 “as distinct portions of one heavenly whole.” I am inclined to agree with that basic
assessment. The heavenly temple has a Holy of Holies, either in the 7D Transcendent Heaven or in the most
elevated position in the 4D Heaven, apparently at the Father’s throne in the 4D Heavenly City. Christ took His seat
at the right hand of the Father’s throne in the heavens as a minister both in the 4D-City Sanctuary and in the True
3D-City Tabernacle.

        Trinity Tabernacle
        Or, if one prefers the 7D vantage point, Christ took His seat at the right hand of the Father’s throne in the
7D Transcendent Heaven, as a minister both in the 7D Sanctuary and in the True 4D-City Tabernacle. Or if one
wants to truly be complex, allow the possibility that Scripture allows both points of view. Govett seems to be able
to perceive the text from multiple vantage points, as noted above, when he limits his discussion by saying, “In one
point of view.” Delitzsch and Govett are probably too far ahead of their times to be widely accepted by the
commentators of our times. Even so, a little thought-provoking thinking might be appealing.

is a reflection of the essential Trinity. The inseparability of these Persons and their personalities is further indicated
in the designations of the economic Trinity as Triune mind, Triune will, and Triune emotion. In encountering God,
one encounters a threefold personality, a Triune personality, similar to the holistic trichotomous representation of
man.46

should expect the temple to convey some of the complexity of the Trinity. Could the temple also express the
distinction between the essential Trinity and the economic Trinity? Yes! The combination of Delitzsch’s comments
with my 7D model strongly suggests something along this line.

   This presentation is not the same as that by Jonathan Edwards. Although Edwards presented the Trinity as mind,

view breaks down since what he poses is a oneness that is based on singularity. There is ultimately only one person.
In his proposal, there is ultimately only one person in three eternal manifestations of mind, emotion, and will. In
the illustration herein, however, the Father is not to be equated with the Triune mind of the economic Trinity, nor
the Son with the Triune will, nor the Spirit with the Triune emotion. The Triune mind represents the Triune mind
of all three persons, not just the Father’s mind. It represents a oneness that is based on the unity of Persons. There
is an absolute eternal unity of three Persons. Or as Shedd said, “The Divine self-consciousness not a fourth
consciousness additional to the three hypostatical consciousness, but is the resultant of these three” (emphasis his).

         The essential Trinity would correspond to the inner representations of the tabernacle on Earth, in the 3D
City, and the 4D City. The earthly-heavenly dualism of the tabernacle is obvious. The dualism of the heavenly
tabernacle is contested but reasonable and held by several interpreters. A highly speculative proposition has been
posed by Delitzsch for a tabernacle that exists beyond the angelic realm of the 4D Heavenly City, leading me to
follow his lead herein by theorizing the possibility of a 7D tabernacle, as an extrapolation of my model in MMP5.
In that book, I posed that God is a 7D being who lives in an uncreated eternal 7D spacetime continuum. This
heavenly 7D spacetime continuum is God’s eternal dwelling. And maybe, just maybe, He has an eternal dwelling
within it as well and has dropped a hint to that fact in Heb 8:2, as Delitzsch has apparently discerned.

         God Dwells in Metaphysical Light
         Does God have clothing? Yes, light. He covers Himself with light as a garment (Ps 104:2). Does God
have a dwelling? Yes, light. He dwells in unapproachable light (1Tim 6:16). He is in the light (1Jn 1:7). In fact, it
could not be otherwise since God is light (1Jn 1:5). Some interpreters take these biblical statements metaphysically;
some take them metaphorically. Being a literalist, my inclination is to take them metaphysically. As the adage goes,
when studying the Bible, if the literal sense makes sense, seek no other sense, lest it become nonsense. Otherwise,
one is left posing that God is lightless light, light that does not emit light, a fire that does not burn.47

47
  God is a consuming fire (Dt 4:24; Heb 12:29; cp. Dt 9:3). Perhaps this metaphorical description of God’s character
also is a hint to God’s metaphysical composition. Fire is a form of energy, a form of light. God makes His angels
winds, spirits, and fire (Heb 1:7,14). Perhaps their character and function are in accord with their composition—
spiritual fire. Kitchen describes humans as world-born and angels as being star-born, another way of saying that
our origin is terrestrial while theirs is celestial. Lucas Kitchen Infinite Tomorrow: The Kingdom Chronicles Book
One (Audible, 2015), ch. 14. World-born versus star-born is a good description since humans are made of this world
while angels are presumably made of starlike 4D energy. As spiritual beings, angels are closer in nature to God, in
terms of their metaphysical composition, than the physical bodies of mud-made humans. Nevertheless, we are not
to think of angels as an emanation of divine substance. For one thing, God is a higher order of being. He would be
a 7D spiritual fire, whereas angels are only a 4D spiritual fire. For another thing, I am not suggesting that the 7D
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 44
         Just as a shining light bulb clothes itself in light and dwells in light, God, by His very nature, must clothe
Himself in light and dwell in light. Some translations, like the NAS, put an and between the two opening phrases
of 1Tim 6:16, and grammatically it might be understood in this manner: “[1] Who alone possesses immortality and
[2] dwells in unapproachable light.” However, the sense would seem to be: “Who alone [1] possesses immortality
and [2] dwells in unapproachable light.” Both phrases seem to be describing something that is true of God alone.
Ellicott seems to take it that way: “He in whom immortality essentially exists, and who enjoys it neither derivatively
nor by participation...‘dwelling in light unapproachable.’ In this sublime image God is represented as dwelling, as
it were, in an atmosphere of light, surrounded by glories which no created nature may ever approach.48
Mounce (WBC) cites Ellicott in approval and also quotes another author to pose that it is an “unapproached light
Dwelt from eternity.” If such commentators are correct, and they seem to be, then this light is the eternal
dwelling/habitation of God, which no created creature can approach, not even angels.
         Regarding 1Tim 6:16, Dibelius and Conzelmann (HERM) say that light is used “to designate the essence
of God or Christ and that which is the inheritance of Christians. Here, however, it refers to the place where God
lives” (emphasis theirs). These comments are good insofar as they go. Yet the question might be asked, “Is this the
place where God lives with angels?” If so, it is only relatively unapproachable, only unapproachable by mortal men,
as certain translations, such as the KJV would indicate: “dwelling in the light which no man can approach.” The
Greek text does not pose this limitation, however, as the NKV indicates: “dwelling in unapproachable light.” The
anthropomorphic limitation is reserved for the next phrase in the Greek text: “whom no man [anthropos] has seen
or can see.” The unapproachable light in which God dwells and which is associated with His essential nature is
inaccessible by any created creature, including angels, by virtue of the limitation of their created nature. Believers,
on the other hand, have an imparted nature, the very nature of God imparted to their spirit, and can develop this
nature misthologically so that they can more fully participate in God’s higher dimension.
         My conjecture is that since God’s clothing is made of light, the place that He dwells is made of light also.
This transcendent light can take various forms, such as clothing or a throne or a temple. God’s very nature generates
this light. It is not limited to His body but is emitted by His body, forming His clothing and the sphere of His
surroundings. The first law of thermodynamics is the law of the conservation of energy, which states that energy
cannot be created or destroyed; it only changes form. Its equation (E = mc2) shows that energy and matter are two
different forms of the same thing. One can be converted into the other. Light is a form of energy. We might say that
our universe is made of light; some of which has been converted to matter, a solid form of light. Our clothes and
dwellings are made of a solid form of light.
         Apparently, God’s clothing and dwelling is also made of some form of light. As noted in MMP5, many
people believe that Adam and Eve were luminous before their fall, wearing light as clothing. Angels, on occasion,
also seem to wear light as their garments. On the Mount of Transfiguration, Jesus’ “garments became as white as
light” (Mt 17:2). Overcomers will wear white garments. Perhaps these garments are made of light.
     As noted in MMP4, regarding his NDE trip to the Heavenly City, George Ritchie said, “In fact, the city and
everything in it seemed to be made of light, even as the Figure [Jesus] at my side was made of light.” From his NDE
perspective, it seemed that both the Heavenly City and the Heavenly Jesus were made of light.
         Our physical bodies, clothing, and dwellings are made of a material form of 3D light. Could the 4D bodies,
clothing, and dwellings of angels be made of some form of light 4D light? Could God’s 7D body, clothing, and
dwelling be made of 7D light? Why not? I pose that God’s 7D clothing and dwelling are made of light because God
is made of 7D light. His metaphysical essence is 7D light. He is the cause, the source, of the 7D light that surrounds
Him. His nature determines the nature of His surroundings. Could the expressions—God is Spirit (Jn 4:24), and
God is light (1Jn 1:5)—“touch upon the mystery of God’s own being,” as Brown (AYB) states regarding the latter
passage? Seemingly so. If so, God’s essence is spiritual light, that is, metaphysical light that is of an eternal higher
dimensional order than any created light.

light emitted by God is an emanation of His divine substance to create His surroundings, which would be a form of
7D pantheism. Rather, when Jesus was transformed on the Mount of Transfiguration, He gave off light without
imparting His divine substance to His surroundings. Likewise, when angels shine, they do not do so by emitting
their substance to their surroundings. In like manner, God’s shining in 7D glory does not infuse His surroundings
with His substance or nature. In all three cases, the emitter emits light without transmitting His/his substance to
create His/his emission.

caused space and time that exists because of God but is not created by God. Some say that God’s spacetime is
unbounded, thus infinite in that dimension. Perhaps so. Regardless, 7D light is uncreated yet necessarily caused
light that exists because God is light. By its nature, light shines forth from the source emitting it.

         God’s 7D Spacetime Continuum
         In any event, for present purposes, I am content to pose that the heavenly 7D spacetime continuum is God’s
eternal dwelling and leave it at that rather than insist upon a 7D tabernacle within the 7D spacetime continuum. The
only correction to Delitzsch’s model that really needs to be made is to reject any atemporal-aspatial version of
eternal-now.49 Posing that God exists in a literal, eternal spacetime continuum makes sense. Contending otherwise
can be shown to be nonsense. Delitzsch’s own analysis leads to the rejection of thinking of God’s dimension as
being static or timeless. Jesus passed through the angelic heavens into the divine heavens, per Delitzsch’s own
reconstruction of events. This proves that God’s dimension is not static or timeless. Jesus entered that dimension at
a point in time not only in that dimension, but which correlated with time in our dimension. Therefore, even in the
highest heavens, God must exist in a spacetime continuum that correlates with our spacetime continue. See MMP5
for further illustrations and discussion.

         Name Change
         As discussed in MMP5 (and illustrated in its videos51), New Jerusalem (NJ) is a 3D spaceship that moves
from place to place both inside and outside our universe, both in Heaven and in the Highest Heaven (that is, the 4D
City). We might picture the 4D City as the docking station for the 3D City. The BTR (Bema Throne Room atop NJ)
is the “thrown [that] moves from place to place as required by business of state” and for other purposes, such as
those described in MMP5. Moreover, God has prepared this Heavenly City (Heb 11:16) for those believers who
love Him (Dt 7:9; 1Cor 2:9; Jam 1:12; 2:5), in that Jesus describes it as the place which He goes to prepare (Jn 14:2-
3) for those believers who love Him (Jn 14:21-23). Therefore, my working hypothesis is that the BTR is the throne
that God prepared/established for judgment in Ps 9:7 (cp. discussion by Lang noted above) and from which God
judges angels, which is in accord with my argument in MMP5 that Lucifer was cast from NJ. This Heavenly City
was prepared. It is not eternal in that it does not extend infinitely into eternity past. Nevertheless, NJ does extend
infinitely into eternity future. Consequently, contrary to Lang, this throne will not pass away in the sense of being
done away with. On the contrary, as he himself argues regarding heaven and Earth not fleeing way in Rev 20:11
and related texts (pp. 345-349), heaven and Earth will be changed (Heb 1:10), which “implies continued existence”
(p. 346). I propose that the Heavenly Throne atop the Heavenly City undergoes a similar change, even a name
change, resulting in it being called New Jerusalem. The hypothesis I posed in MMP5 regarding the name change
was:

              In conjunction with the Enochian background, one might pose that the term New Jerusalem in
      TDan 5:12 might not be a Christian redaction after all but a natural juxtaposition of the pre-existing
      heavenly misthological Enochian concept with the affirmation that “Jerusalem shall no longer undergo
      desolation” in TDan 5:13. The Heavenly Jerusalem was anticipated to become the New Jerusalem
      when it becomes the new home of the righteous believers and is naturally called New Jerusalem in
      contradiction to the old earthly Jerusalem, which was susceptible to desolation. If so, one might even
      pose that Jesus (Rev 3:12) and John (Rev 21:2) got the term New Jerusalem from Jewish traditions
      going all the way back to the early patriarchs, as evidenced by TDan 5:13. In this case, both Jesus and
      John were using a term that would have been understood by their listeners as having a rich history.

When the angelic court in BTR is turned over to the Bride of Christ, the Heavenly City will be known as New
Jerusalem. After that point in time, as noted above, the Heavenly “Jerusalem shall no longer undergo desolation.”
The throne from which Lucifer was and will be expulsed becomes the new throne of the Bride. The BTR has ample
room for the Lord to judge all believers; however, not all believers will successfully pass that BTR judgment or
pass judgment from it. Only believers who are found to have been victorious (i.e., experiential overcomers in the
manner described in Revelation) will be able to exercise judgment from the throne from which they are judged.
They will stand before Jesus’ throne. Pass the judgment. Then pass judgement from His throne. Those who pass the
judgment of the BTR will be those who pass judgment from the BTR. Their scene of judgment will become the
location from which they pass judgment. Praise God for the Judgment Seat of Christ! For those believers who
prepare for it, it is prepared for them!

                                           Misthological Throne
        Although there would be enough room in the BTR for all believers, those who argue that all believers will
rule from this shared throne room draw numerous erroneous inferences. Stegall is one such writer who seeks to
argue that all believers are overcomers and thus rulers. He seeks to circumvent a misthological interpretation of
Rev 3:21 by countering:

               The inference is made that in order to be an overcomer the believer must overcome in the same
      way that Christ overcame, namely, practically by His work, so that Christ is essentially saying, “To
      him who overcomes in the same way that I overcame, I will grant to sit with Me on My throne.”
      However, hōs, here, is simply stating the fact of Christ’s overcoming as well as ours, not the manner
      of overcoming. After all, we do not overcome in the same way that Christ did in order to become
      overcomers, namely, by dying for the sins of the world and rising gloriously from the dead!
      Furthermore, observe how the conjunction hōs is used in a similar overcomer passage where Christ
      refers to Himself and His reign over the world as He describes the future blessings promised to
      overcomers: “He shall rule them with a rod of iron; they shall be dashed to pieces like the potter’s
      vessels—as [hōs] I also have received from My Father; and I will give him the morning star”
      (Rev. 2:27-28). Will believers receive authority from the Father in the coming kingdom in exactly the
      same way or to the same extent as Christ? No; His position, authority, and reign will be unparalleled
      and supreme, ours will be delegated and shared. Thus, the point of comparison in 2:27, similarly with
      3:21, is the simple fact that we will receive the privilege of reigning just as Christ also received from
      the Father. (SCFOD, emphasis his, 431n28.)

In contrast to the Scripture in general and Christ in particular, Stegall’s response seeks to drive a wedge between
the manner in which Christ overcame and rules and the way believers overcome and rule. Christ overcame in order
to rule over the nations, and Christians likewise must overcome in order to rule over the nations. The fact is both
the outcome and manner are related. For misthological rulership, overcoming is not optional for either Christ or
Christians. Through His cross, Christ won the ability to be able to rule as a Man over humanity, and we must
likewise earn this right through the experiential appropriation of His cross and the daily bearing of our cross. Jesus
put it this way:
      23
        And He was saying to them all, “If anyone wishes to come after Me, let him deny himself, and take
      up his cross daily, and follow Me. 24 “For whoever wishes to save his life shall lose it, but whoever
      loses his life for My sake, he is the one who will save it. 25 “For what is a man profited if he gains the
      whole world, and loses or forfeits himself? 26 “For whoever is ashamed of Me and My words, of him
      will the Son of Man be ashamed when He comes in His glory, and the glory of the Father and of the
      holy angels. (Lk 9:23-26)

The hymnist put it this way:

                                               Must Jesus bear the cross alone,
                                                  and all the world go free?
                                               No, there’s a cross for everyone,
                                                 and there’s a cross for me.

        Jesus was not calling upon us to bear our cross in order to save our lives from soteriological loss but from
misthological loss. Stegall acknowledges that this Lukan passage is dealing with the potential loss of rewards, citing
Lk 9:25 as a precursor to his explanation: “Believers who have built their ministries as a fleeting monument to
themselves instead of a lasting memorial to Jesus Christ will experience a great loss in that day, as all self-glorifying
works will be incinerated—annihilated. Only that which magnifies Christ will be permitted to continue into
eternity” (SCFOD, 207). Not only is there a correlation between Christ’s cross and our cross, but there is a
misthological causation as well. The Lord is going to be ashamed (epaischunomai) of some believers because they
have not born their cross (Lk 9:26).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 48

                                           Misthological Shame
          As to the Lord being ashamed of some believers in Lk 9:26, ashamed is a compound form of shame:
epaischunomai (epi + aischunomai) is a compound of aischuno. This latter form is used in 1Jn 2:28: “And now,
little children, abide in Him, so that when He appears, we may have confidence and not shrink away from Him in
shame [aischuno] at His coming” (NAS). However, this is a weak translation, as explained in TOD:

               The fact that we shrink away from God in shame does not mean that the shame is self-imposed.
      Quite the contrary, the verb for be ashamed (aischuno) in 1Jn 2:28 is passive and thus would mean to
      be put to shame or disgraced (see FB). In his Word Pictures, Robertson concurs that it means to be put
      to shame since it is passive. Paul likewise uses the passive form in 2Cor 10:8 and Phil 1:20, where the
      NAS correctly translates it as, “Be put to shame.” Unfortunately, the NAS failed to translate the passive
      properly in 1Jn 2:28. This shame is not self-inflicted. This shame is God-inflicted. Such believers will
      be put to shame by God, before God, and shrink away in shame from God. Various translations bring
      out different nuances of what is being said. The DBY tries to bring out several subtleties: “Be put to
      shame from before him at his coming.” The preposition apo is best translated as from. I would
      translate it thus as: “Be shamefully put away from Him.” He will shamefully have us cast away from
      His presence into the outer darkness when He returns if we fail to abide in Him. The outer darkness is
      a place for the outcasts who are cast out from before Him at the execution of the Bema judgment
      associated with the rapture.

Jesus uses the passive verb for ashamed (epaischunomai) with a direct transitive object in both Mk 8:38 and Lk 9:26
to warn that He will be ashamed of those believers who are ashamed of Him. As for the nominal form, Jesus uses
it concerning believers experiencing shame at His judgment: “I advise you to buy from Me gold refined by fire, that
you may become rich, and white garments, that you may clothe yourself, and that the shame [aischune] of your
nakedness may not be revealed; and eye salve to anoint your eyes, that you may see” (Rev 3:18). Although Stegall
may be blind to the misthological implications of this text, these white garments must be bought by believers. They
are costly; therefore, they are misthological. Jesus likewise warns, “Behold, I am coming like a thief. Blessed is the
one who stays awake and keeps his garments, lest he walk about naked and men see his shame [aschemosune]”
(Rev 16:15). As in the case of the potential crown in Rev 3:11 and the potential right to the Tree of Life and
Heavenly City in 22:19, we are dealing with the future realization of a potential reward. These white garments are
described presumptively as if we already have them or don’t have them and as something that we must keep by
staying awake.

                           Presumptively Having/Lacking White Garments in Rev 3:4
        Some garment texts are proleptic (describing these garments as a future reward); some of these texts are
presumptive (describing these future garments as already possessed or already lacking). Some texts are a
combination of the presumptive and proleptic perspectives. The combined perspective is evident in Rev 3:4: “But
you have a few people in Sardis who have not soiled their garments [himation]; and they will walk with Me in
white; for they are worthy.”
                           Illustration 18. Stegall’s White-Garment Wedge in Rev 3:4
               Mistholic per Stegall                                 Soteric per Stegall
 4b                                              4a
   And they will walk with Me in white; for they    But you have a few people in Sardis who have not soiled
 are worthy. (Rev 3:4b)                          their garments. (Rev 3:4a)

         Stegall fails to address the soteric-mistholic dichotomy that his argument poses within Rev 3:4. According
to his thesis, having white garments is a soteriological gift promised to all believers, but walking with Christ in
white garments is a reward limited to faithful believers:

      Revelation 2‒3 contains a pattern of Christ promising a reward to faithful believers in a particular
      church (2:10, 23, 25; 3:11) followed later by a promise of salvation blessings to the overcomers within
      that church (2:11, 26-28; 3:12). Revelation 3:4-5 follows this same pattern of a reward promised to
      faithful believers (v. 4) followed by a promise of salvation blessings to all believers (v. 5). In these
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                      Page 49
      verses, Christ states that the faithful believers in Sardis “shall walk with Me in white, for they are
      worthy” (v. 4) and that the overcomer “shall be clothed in white” (v. 5). Wearing white will be a
      blessing possessed by all the saved (v. 5); whereas walking with Christ in white garments (v. 4) will
      be an added blessing or reward conditioned on one’s walk of fellowship with the Lord now. (P. 457.)

His argument collapses when his dichotomy is applied to v. 4. Contrary to his summational glosses regarding v. 4,
his thesis erroneously drives a wedge between Rev 3:4a and 3:4b. The connection between the two halves is
apparent. White in 3:4b refers to the garments of 3:4a. The two halves of the verse combine to form the singular
conceptual term white garments. Per his argument, all believers qualify for having these white garments. Yet the
counter implication of Rev 3:4b is that only some of the believers within Sardis had unsoiled garments. They alone
qualify as having the white garments described in v. 4.
         The first half of the verse describes these future white garments as being currently possessed by the few
believers within Sardis who had not soiled their garments. These few faithful believers are presumptively described
as already having the white robes they will need to walk with Christ. Describing them as if they already possess
their future garments is a presumptive vantage point (presumed present possession = presumptive perspective).
         The second half of the verse describes their anticipated future experience attributed to their having such
garments. Their actual future possession of these white garments will qualify them to walk with Christ. Their future
experience of walking with Christ in these white garments is a proleptic vantage point (promised future possession
= proleptic perspective). The two halves of the verse combine to present a beautiful presumptive-proleptic
misthological picture of the white garments.
         Since only a few of the believers in Sardis are described as presently having unsoiled garments, the inverse
is logically necessitated. Most of the believers in Sardis are indicted as if they already possess soiled garments.
They do not have white garments. Describing their future garments as already soiled is a presumptive vantage point.
Their present possession of such soiled garments is a presumptive perspective. Rewards can be either positive or
negative. Likewise, proleptic and presumptive rewards can be either positive or negative. The positive presumptive
reward of having white garments that qualify faithful believers for walking with Christ is logically juxtaposed with
the implied inverse for unfaithful believers. The negative presumptive reward of having soiled garments that
disqualify unfaithful believers for walking with Christ is the logical contrapositive mandated by the verse. Not all
believers will be found to have white garments at the Bema.
         Per Stegall’s own argument, v. 4 pertains to a reward promised to faithful believers. He focuses on v. 4b
but ignores the implications of v. 4a because v. 4a is highly detrimental to his thesis. Since v. 4b pertains to rewards,
having unsoiled garments is a positive reward limited to faithful believers. Having soiled garments, in contrast, is a
negative reward limited to unfaithful believers.
                                      Illustration 19. Contrapositive of Rev 3:4
        If (A) unsoiled garment, then (B) faithful believer. (Conditional proposition: If A, then B.)
        If (not B) unfaithful believer, then (not A) soiled garments. (Contrapositive: If not A, then not B.)

Having unsoiled garments (i.e., white garments) is a positive reward limited to faithful believers.
Having soiled garments (i.e., not having white garments) is a negative reward limited to unfaithful believers.

                                 Presumptively Lacking White Garments in Rev 3:18
         In like manner, Jesus warns the church at Laodicea: “Because you say, ‘I am rich, and have become wealthy,
and have need of nothing,’ and you do not know that you are wretched and miserable and poor and blind and
naked, I advise you to buy from Me gold refined by fire, that you may become rich, and white garments
[himation], that you may clothe yourself, and that the shame of your nakedness may not be revealed; and eye
salve to anoint your eyes, that you may see” (Rev 3:17-18). These believers are described as being naked, as needing
to buy white garments so that they will not be found misthologically naked at the Bema! This passage destroys
Stegall’s argument. So how does he deal with it? With a misleading gloss! He says, “Himation is used to describe
the clothing of the Church (Rev 3:4-5, 18)” (p. 186). This oversimplification is a misdirection. Most of the church
at Sardis was missing this clothing, and the entire church of Ladacia was missing this clothing! Contrary to Stegall,
only the faithful remnant of the Church qualifies for white garments. The believers within the church of Laodicea
are described as presumptively/presently lacking these future rewards and being presumptively/presently naked.
The negative presumptive state of lacking white garments and being naked is the misthological perspective
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 50
conveyed. It is enjoined with the proleptic anticipation of having their shame revealed for all to see at the Bema.
Not all believers presumptively have white garments. Some believers are presumptively naked. They will not be
found to have white garments at the future Bema.
        The summation provided by Witzig in SCFOD (pp. 364-365) to bolster their mutual Rokser-Stegall-Witzig
perspective regarding the church of Laodicea is a hit-and-miss but mostly a miss:

               They were so displeasing to Jesus that He said, “I will vomit you out of My mouth”
      (Rev. 3:16). They were distasteful to Him. You would think that God would unleash lots of wrath and
      punishment upon these wicked saints. “Let ’em have it, Jesus. Hit ’em hard.” But what do we see? In
      verse 19 Jesus says, “As many as I love, I rebuke and chasten. Therefore be zealous and repent.” He
      does not offer them wrath, but chastening. And He exhorts them to change their mind about their high
      spiritual opinion of themselves. And then in verse 20, He offers them His fellowship. If they will hear
      His voice, He will join them at the dining table, a biblical picture of fellowship. Why, Jesus? They are
      so out of touch with reality, so into themselves, so undeserving! And His answer? “As many as I love
      I chasten.” No wrath, no anger, no punishment—but a strong rebuke, an exhortation to change their
      mind, and an appeal to sit with Him and learn how things really are. Although we do not know what
      form this chastening actually took, we do know that the chastening was for their profit, it was designed
      to lead them in the direction of personal holiness, and it was designed to bring forth peaceable fruit. It
      was done out of love, not anger.

No punishment? What about being spit out of Christ’s mouth, being found naked, not having white garments at the
Bema, and then having to deal with the shameful consequences after the Bema? Like Stegall, Witzig ignores the
fact that these unfaithful believers are described as lacking white garments.

                                     Pronoun or Adverb (Houtos) in Rev 3:5
                          Illustration 20. Stegall’s White-Garment Wedge in Rev 3:4-5
                 Mistholic per Stegall                                  Soteric per Stegall
 4
  ‘But you have a few people in Sardis who have not 5 He who thusly overcomes will clothe himself in white
 soiled their garments; and they will walk with Me garments, and I will not erase his name from the book of
 in white; for they are worthy. (Rev 3:4)           life, and I will confess his name before My Father, and
                                                    before His angels. (Rev 3:5; TM)

         Stegall acknowledges that walking with the Lord in white is misthological but attempts to make being
clothed in white garments soteriological by applying them to the whole Church. He rightly acknowledges that his
entire argument collapses if the adverb houtos (thus, thusly) in Rev 3:5 refers back to overcomes. Even apart from
this consideration, his argument has just been shown to be in self-apparent ruins in Rev 3:4,18. Still, let’s play
along. Perhaps he thinks that by appealing to some technical material regarding v. 5, he can keep the reader from
noticing the appalling weakness of his argument in v. 4 and v. 18.
         In the order of the Greek text in v. 5, this adverb houtos occurs after the verb overcome and before the verb
clothed: overcome thus clothed. Normally, the adverb is translated something along the lines as, “He who overcomes
will thus be clothed in white garments.” In this case, it is taken as referring to what proceeds: be clothed in white
garments. However, it could refer to what precedes: he who overcomes. In that case, it could be translated as: “He
who thus overcomes.” Stegall objects by claiming:

       In the twenty-two other uses of houtōs in John’s writings (John 3:8, 14, 16; 4:6; 5:21, 26; 7:46; 11:48;
      12:50; 13:25; 14:31; 15:4; 18:22; 21:1; 1 John 2:6; 4:11; Rev. 2:15; 3:16; 9:17; 11:5; 16:18; 18:21),
      he never uses the term to modify a participle as would be necessary here if the term referred to the
      articular participle, ho nikōn, as if the verse were saying, “he who thus overcomes.” All English
      translations are consistent with this observation. (SCFOD, emphasis his, 459)
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                    Page 51
Stegall is mistaken on multiple accounts. (1) Contrary to his assertion, not all English translations render it as a
pronoun rather than an adverb.52 DBY renders it as a pronoun: “He that overcomes, he shall be clothed in white
garments.” This would be conducive to my argument that the overcomer is the one clothed in the white garments
described in Rev 3:4.53 Likewise RWB: “He that overcometh, the same shall be clothed in white raiment.” See also
KJV, WEB, and YLT. (2) BBE, GNV, NKJ, and TNT do not even translate houtos. (3) GWN renders the adverb as
modifying overcomes rather than clothed: “Everyone who wins the victory this way will wear white clothes.”54
Thus, the overcomer of v. 5 is the one who wins the victory in the manner described in v. 4. (4) CSB places the
adverb at the beginning, which is a great location for allowing it to modify both overcomes and clothed, which
would be my dualistic personal preference: “In the same way, the victor will be dressed in white clothes.”
(5) Contradictory to Stegall’s assertion that John never uses houtos “to modify a participle,” it modifies the
preceding participle in Jn 13:25.




52
   The CT uses the adverb houtōs (long vowel: ou[twj). The MT and CT use the pronoun houtos (short vowel: ou[toj).
In my transliterations, I do not normally distinguish between long and short vowels, and as a rule, I will not herein.
However, on this occasion, I must call attention to the distinction because Stegall has seemly failed to notice the
difference, and the pronominal text counters his position. Even though I normally prefer the MT, I will engage
Stegall on his own turf by adopting his adverbial rendering of the CT for the sake of argument.
53
   Other examples of a stative participle followed by the pronoun can be noted. “The one who endures to the end,
he is the one who will be saved” (Mt 10:22; TM). Likewise, “the one who abides in Me and I in him, he is the one
who bears much fruit” (Jn 15:5; TM).
54
   Govett (GOR) takes houtos as referring to the overcomer. Metzer acknowledges that it is “possible that οὕτως
refers primarily to the preceding ὁ νικῶν (the one who conquers).” Roger L. Omanson and Bruce Manning Metzger,
A Textual Guide to the Greek New Testament: An Adaptation of Bruce M. Metzger’s Textual Commentary for the
Needs of Translators (Stuttgart: Deutsche Bibelgesellschaft, 2006), 530. Lenski takes it as referring “to v. 4 where
the promise is stated in the plural” (LCNT). Kistemaker (NTC) concurs: “What is the import of the word thus? It
seems natural to take the adverb in its context of the preceding verse (v. 4b) and say that ‘thus’ should be taken
together with white garments as a consequence of walking with Jesus” (emphasis his). Thayer (GELNT) places its
occurrence in Rev 3:5 in the category of referring to what precedes with the following definitions for that category:
“By virtue of its native demonstrative force it refers to what precedes; in the manner spoken of; in the way described;
in the way it was done; in this manner; in such a manner; thus, so.” Benedict takes it in this manner, citing Thayer
and then adding:

      Revelation 3:16 and 11:5 have similar uses of this word as referring backwards to what precedes. The
      concluding promise takes on the color of what had just gone before and identifies those who were just
      spoken of with the overcomers. The overcomers then are those who have not defiled their garments
      and they shall walk with Christ in white because they are worthy (Rev. 3:4). The words are also used
      in 16:6 and in that passage mean deserving.

Richard R. Benedict, “The Use of Nivkaw in the Letters to the Seven Churches of Revelation,” Th.M. thesis (Dallas
Theological Seminary, 1966), 29.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 52
                             Illustration 21. Thus Modifies Leaning Back (Jn 13:25)




As seen above from Leedy’s SBLGNTSD, the adverb modifies the participle that proceeded it in Jn 13:25: “He,
leaning back thus on Jesus’ breast, said to Him, ‘Lord, who is it?’” The adverb is only proceeded by a potential
participle as its reference on five occasions in John’s writing (Jn 4:6; 13:25; 18:22; Rev 3:5, 18:21), and in two of
these occasions (Jn 18:22; Rev 18:21) it is used to introduce a quote and would thus not be applicable to the
construction in Rev 3:5. So, if one wants to look at this data in the manner Stegall construes the discussion, one
time this adverb modifies the proceeding participle, and one time it does not. The probability is equally divided.
However, the sample size from which to draw a probability inference is too small to be truly significant. At best,
looking at the other Johannine occurrences seems to indicate that it could go either way in Rev 3:5. Therefore,
Stegall has misconstrued the data by making it seem as if John’s alleged lack of using it to modify a participle on
other occasions in his writing is a significant omission. To be fair, though, Leedy does diagram Rev 3:5 in the
manner argued by Stegall.
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                 Page 53
                                Illustration 22. Thus Modifies Clothed (Rev 3:5)




Per Leedy, the adverb modifies the verb that follows it in Rev 3:5: “He who overcomes shall thus be clothed in
white garments; and I will not erase his name from the book of life, and I will confess his name before My Father,
and before His angels.” Diagramming it in this manner is reasonable. Per the above assessment, we might grant it
a 50-50 probability of being diagrammed in that manner. For the sake of argument, let us suppose that Stegall and
Leedy are correct as to which verb is modified by this adverb. Does this prove Stegall’s point? Not by a long shot!
What Stegall does not tell his readers is that in most of these Johannie passages, houtos refers to what proceeds in
an explanatory relation rather than in disjunctive isolation to what precedes. Read the passages for yourself and
make your own determination. I have placed the translation of houtos in red below and included verses from the
context where appropriate to make it easy for you to make your own assessment.

    1. “The wind blows where it wishes and you hear the sound of it, but do not know where it comes from and
       where it is going; so is everyone who is born of the Spirit.” (Jn 3:8)
    2. “And as Moses lifted up the serpent in the wilderness, even so must the Son of Man be lifted up. 15 that
       whoever believes may in Him have eternal life. 16 “For God so loved the world, that He gave His only
       begotten Son, that whoever believes in Him should not perish, but have eternal life.” (Jn 3:14-16).
    3. “And Jacob’s well was there. Jesus therefore, being wearied from His journey, was sitting thus by the well.
       It was about the sixth hour” (Jn 4:6).

The above renditions are illustrative of the normal ways in which houtos is typically translated: in this manner, in
the same manner, manner, thus, so, in this way, in the same way, way. They tend to be different ways of saying the
same thing. For example, let’s redo this list and translate the occurrences in reference to manner:

    1. “The wind blows where it wishes and you hear the sound of it, but do not know where it comes from and
       where it is going; in the same manner is everyone who is born of the Spirit” (Jn 3:8).
