        the same manner you are lukewarm, and neither hot nor cold, I will spit you out of My mouth” (Rev 3:15-
        16).
    19. “16 And the number of the armies of the horsemen was two hundred million; I heard the number of them.
        17
           And this is how I saw in the vision the horses and those who sat on them: the riders had breastplates the
        color of fire and of hyacinth and of brimstone; and the heads of the horses are like the heads of lions; and
        out of their mouths proceed fire and smoke and brimstone” (Rev 9:16-17).
    20. “And if anyone desires to harm them, fire proceeds out of their mouth and devours their enemies; and if
        anyone would desire to harm them, in the same manner he must be killed” (Rev 11:5).
    21. “And there were flashes of lightning and sounds and peals of thunder; and there was a great earthquake,
        such as there had not been since man came to be upon the earth, so great an earthquake was it, and in this
        manner mighty” (Rev 16:18).
    22. “And a strong angel took up a stone like a great millstone and threw it into the sea, saying, ‘In the same
        manner will Babylon, the great city, be thrown down with violence, and will not be found any longer’”
        (Rev 18:21).

        Rendering houtos as in the/this same manner is a perfect fit, showing the clear connection between what
proceeds and what precedes. A few texts might take a moment of reflection, but the point is readily apparent. In
Jn 3:16, by lifting His Son up on a cross, the Father demonstrates His love for the world in the same manner just
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 56
illustrated in the statement proceeding it, regarding Moses lifting a serpent up on a pole. In Jn 4:6, Jesus is sitting
by the well in the manner just described, that is, He was sitting wearily. In Jn 13:25, John is leaning back on Jesus’
chest in the manner just described in Jn 13:23. The only text that is not a good fit is Rev 9:17. Therefore, of the
twenty-three times John uses houtos, only once would it be inappropriate to translate it as in the/this same manner.
Manifestly, based on his well-established usage, rendering it in this manner is entirely appropriate for Rev 3:5. The
only question that now remains is where to place the adverb. Some interpreters would place it as modifying the verb
which proceeds it: “He who overcomes in the same manner will be clothed in white garments.” The immediate
question then becomes: Overcomes in what manner? The obvious answer would be found in the verse that proceeds
it: By not soiling their garments and being worthy to walk with Christ in white (Rev 3:4). But Stegall admits that
v. 4 is a misthological passage dealing with a reward. Their walking with Christ in white in v. 4 is conditioned on
their experiential purity, their overcoming experientially. Therefore, Stegall rightly acknowledges that taking houtos
in this manner would destroy his whole argument. Consequently, he stringently argues that the adverb modifies the
verb that follows (i.e., clothed) rather than the one that proceeds (i.e., overcomes).
         So, let us give Stegall the benefit of the doubt and adopt that binary understanding and make it clear that
this is what John supposedly meant with a translation favoring that perspective: “He who overcomes will in the
same manner be clothed in white garments.” According to this perspective, John is supposedly talking
exclusively about how they are clothed, not about how they overcome. And Stegall must make this dichotomy
because otherwise his whole argument falls apart. He cannot have the soteriological overcoming of v. 5 being
explained by the misthological overcoming of v. 4. Even so, his argument still fails. Even in this favorable
translation, the immediate question is: “Is clothed in what manner?” In v. 4, those who are walking with Christ in
white are not doing so because they have received this privilege as a gift. Rather, as Stegall acknowledges, they
have earned this privilege as a reward. Therefore, houtos would be most naturally understood as carrying this same
perspective from v. 4 into v. 5. They are clothed in the same manner, that is, they are clothed in white as a reward.
Restated in theological terms, John should be understood as saying:
      4
       But you have a few people in Sardis who have not (experientially) soiled their garments; and they
      will walk (misthologically) with Me in white; for they are (misthologically) worthy. 5 ‘He who
      overcomes will in the same manner be clothed (misthologically) in white garments; and I will not
      erase his name from the book of life, and I will confess his name before My Father, and before His
      angels” (Rev 3:4-5; TM).

Of course, one does not necessarily have to translate the adverb as in the same manner to see that it is being used
in this manner. MRD renders it: “He that is victorious, shall be so clothed in white robes.” Okay, let’s ask the
question: “So clothed in what manner?” Obviously, the manner just described in v. 4, making the victorious
overcomer another way of describing those who have kept their garments clean and are worthy of walking in white.
Aune (WBC) concurs with Stegall in taking the adverb as modifying clothed and yet rightly perceives the
misthological implication: “The οὕτως, ‘thus, so,’ refers to the reward of white garments mentioned in v 4b.”
         Accordingly, even granting Stegall the adverbal association he so desperately needs still shows his
argument to be unnatural and contextually strained to the breaking point. For myself, though, I see no reason to
pose a dichotomy. John has placed the adverb in a location that can be taken as either referring to the verb that
proceeds it or the verb that follows it or dualistically to both verbs. I prefer the latter: “In the same manner, he
who overcomes will be clothed in white garments.” He who overcomes in the manner described in the previous
verse (i.e., experientially) will be clothed in the manner described in the previous verse (i.e., misthologically). Yet,
let us continue to engage Stegall from his limited perspective. He maintains:

      In Revelation 3:5, the word “thus” (houtōs) is clearly used as an adverb to modify the word that comes
      after it, peribaleitai (“be clothed”), rather than ho nikōn (“he who overcomes”). This passage is simply
      teaching that all who are overcomers (believers) will wear white (v. 5), but those who are believers
      (overcomers) and who walk worthily shall not only wear white but also receive the reward of walking
      with Christ in white (v. 4). This pictures a greater degree of intimacy with the Lord. The Greek text
      does not support the idea that overcoming in verse 5 is defined or explained by walking worthily in
      verse 4. (SCFOD, 460, italics his.)
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                                        Page 57
Stegall’s statement is false on multiple accounts. First, I have just demonstrated that even if houtos is used to modify
the verb that comes after it, as Stegall insists that it must, it still strongly supports the idea that being in the manner
clothed in verse 5 is defined or explained by walking worthily in verse 4. Second, just because the adverb modifies
the verb that follows it may not necessarily prohibit it from dualistically modifying the verb that proceeds it. Third,
Stegall interjects the conjunction and, as if John said:
       4
        But you have a few people in Sardis who have not (experientially) soiled their garments; and they
       will walk (misthologically) with Me in white; for they are (misthologically) worthy. 5 ‘And He who
       overcomes will be clothed (soteriologically) in white garments; and I will not erase his name from the
       book of life, and I will confess his name before My Father, and before His angels” (Rev 3:4-5).

John did not say what Stegall proports him as saying. Had John meant and, he could easily have said so!55 The fact
that John does not insert an and argues against Stegall’s position. In fact, the lack of an and here (and even more so
the presence of the pronoun or adverb houtos) argues against Stegall’s understanding of and in Rev 2:26, which he
used to introduce his discussion of Rev 3:4-5:

       In Revelation 2:26, the description of the recipient is twofold—“he who overcomes and keeps My
       works to the end.” Similarly in Revelation 3:4-5, all overcomers will “wear” white garments (v. 5),
       but only those who “walk” in a “worthy” manner will receive the privilege of being able to “walk
       with” Christ in white (v. 4), which is a reward of greater privilege and intimacy with Christ. (SCFOD,
       italics his, 457.)

To the contrary, the overcomers of v. 5 are the ones who wear the white garments as described in v. 4. There is no
and to break them into two different groups. Instead, John uses an adverb or pronoun to equate them. Consequently,
the and (kai) of Rev 2:26 is best taken epexegetically: “He who overcomes, that is [kai], he who keeps my works
until the end, to him I will give authority over the nations” (TM). Since the white garments of Rev 3:5 are
misthological, in harmony with the fact that those lacking them will experience misthological shame, the remaining
parts of the verse should also be understood misthologically. Not having your name erased if you are faithful is a
misthological litotes, meaning that it will be confessed/praised. Jesus’ promise about having your name confessed
before the Father and His angels if you are faithful recalls His promise: “And I say to you, everyone who confesses
Me before men, the Son of Man shall confess him also before the angels of God” (Lk 12:8). Having one’s name
confessed in this manner is plainly a reward, not a gift.



55
   Beckwith uses this same type of observation to refute those making claims similar to Stegall: “Some render οὕτως
likewise, the victor likewise, as well as the unsullied, shall be clothed in white; but in that case ὁμοίως, or ὡσαύτως
would be used; and this would imply a certain distinction between the ὁ νικῶν and the unsullied, whereas the latter
must be included in the former” (emphasis his). Isbon T. Beckwith, The Apocalypse of John: Studies in Introduction
with a Critical and Exegetical Commentary (New York: Macmillan Company, 1919), 476. Osborne (BECNT)
concurs: “It could mean ‘likewise’ and introduce a class of conquerors distinct from those in v. 4, but that is unlikely
here. The faithful in Sardis (3:4) are certainly also ‘overcomers.’ As Beckwith (1919: 476) points out, for this
meaning, the writer would more likely have used ὁμοίως or ὡσαύτως….The faithful minority are the conquerors
who will receive the rewards promised.” John does not say likewise; he does not say and; he says in like manner.
Beale (NIGTC) makes much the same point:

                Although some commentators think that v 5 addresses a different group of people than v 4,
       there is no convincing basis for such a distinction.
                Οὕτως (“thus”) is probably inferential, stressing that the overcomer will conquer in the same
       manner as just described in v 4 (by continuing not to “stain their garments”). However, οὕτως could
       refer primarily to the immediately preceding ὁ νικῶν (“the one overcoming”), in which case it would
       emphasize only the idea of overcoming itself: “the one overcoming—by overcoming”—will inherit
       the following promises.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 58
                                                Middle Voice in Rev 3:5
         Benedict calls attention to the fact that the verb for clothe (periballo) in Rev 3:5 “occurs in the middle voice
so that this is something which the believer does” (p. 30). As he points out, it is also in the middle voice in Rev 19:8,
where the Bride clothes herself with fine, white, clean linen (which is defined in the verse as being the righteous
works of the saints), so misthological righteousness is in view in both verses. I have discussed the significance of
this self-clothing at length in various places in my writings, calling attention, for example, to the related use in
Rev 3:18, where those who clothe themselves in white are the overcomers who buy the clothing for themselves.
The clothing is provided, but it is not provided free of charge. It must be purchased by an overcoming lifestyle.
Even so, it would be appropriate to call particular attention, as Benedict does, to its probable use as a direct reflexive
middle in Rev 3:5, meaning the subject performs the action directly on him or herself. Consistency would dictate
that since Rev 3:18 and 19:8 are best translated as a direct reflexive, the same would be true of Rev 3:5, which is
the only other occurrence of the middle for periballo in Revelation. Other biblical examples could be cited, such
as:

     •   “And she took her veil and covered herself” (Gen 24:65; LXE).
     •   “So she removed her widow’s garments and covered herself with a veil” (Gen 38:14).
     •   “He clothed himself in sackcloth” (1Kgs 21:27; TM).
     •   “He clothed himself in sackcloth” (2Kgs 19:1; TM).
     •   “Esther clothed herself in her royal robes (Esther 5:1; TM).
     •   “Clothe themselves with their works” (Is 59:6; TM).
     •   “Yet I say to you that even Solomon in all his glory did not clothe himself like one of these” (Mt 6:29).
     •   “Do not be anxious then, saying…With what shall we clothe ourselves?” (Mt 6:31)
     •   “Having clothed himself with a linen sheet” (Mk 14:51; TM).
     •   “Having clothed himself in a white robe” (Mk 16:5; TM).56
     •   “Clothe yourself in your garment” (Acts 12:8; TM).

Extrabiblical examples could be cited as well, such as: “Clothe yourself with the cloak of righteousness from God;
place the headband of the glory of the Eternal One upon your head (Bar 5:2; TM).
         The takeaway is that by overcoming we can clothe ourselves with good works that will misthologically
manifest as royal white garments so that we do not appear shamefully half naked at the Bema, clothed in nothing
but misthological sackcloth. Putting the pieces together, a good translation for Rev 3:4-5a would be: “But you have
a few people in Sardis who have not soiled their garments; and they will walk with Me in white; for they are worthy.
In this manner, he who overcomes will clothe himself in white garments” (TM). It is not rocket science to figure
out that those who have soiled garments do not have white garments. Stegall’s argument fails for numerous reasons.
Failing to equate the unsoiled garments of v. 4 with the white garments of v. 5 is one of them. The white garments
of v. 5 are the unsoiled garments of v. 4. Keep your garments white! This is the only way you will find yourself
clothed in these white garments at the Bema.


56
  The objection might be raised that the angel in Mk 16:5 was arrayed in white robes by God rather than by himself.
For sake of argument, let us suppose that angels were originally given white robes by God and arrayed in these
white robes without having to put them on. Even in such a case, keeping their robes unsoiled by sin would have
been their responsibility. Presumably, those angels who fell lost their white robes. In any case, being able to be
present at the tomb to announce that Lord had risen, while wearing white robes (cp. Mt 28:3; Lk 24:4; Jn 20:12),
would be an honor reserved for those angels who kept their garments unspoiled by sin. The parallel is obvious
enough. Believers have been given white robes presumptively. The faithful cannot be commended for not soiling
their garments in Rev 3:4 unless they already have white garments. Presently, we do not actually have these white
garments experientially. However, we must have these white garments in some sense for the commendation to make
sense. We have them in the same way that we presently have crowns that we may forfeit (Rev 3:11) or parts in the
Tree of Life or the Holy City that can be taken away (Rev 22:19). Namely, we have them presumptively. The only
way to clothe yourself in white garments in the future, via eschatological realization, is to keep yourself pure in the
present. We are presently producing the righteous acts experientially that will compose our realized garment
misthologically (Rev 19:8).
Chapter 2. Thinking Outside the Temple Box                                                               Page 59

        Conclusion
         New Jerusalem (NJ) is just part of Heaven as indicated by the fact that it comes down out of Heaven. At
that juncture, NJ becomes the Heavenly Temple (HT). Up until that point, the HT was comprised of two parts.
Heavenly Temple 1 (HT1) is the part of the HT inside the 4D City that is transportationally adjacent to the Bema
atop the 3D City. The Bema serves as Heavenly Temple 2 (HT2).
         The Bema is a teleportational point of contact to the HT1 of the 4D City. The Bema stadium forms the
mobile part of the HT that can move in 3D space via its position atop NJ. This BTR (Bema Throne Room) is also
the transportational juncture point of the Heavenly City (HC) used to bring postmortem believers to Earth for the
Rapture and to be joined there by living believers from Earth at the time of the Rapture.
         Overcomers are granted permanent regal status in association with NJ and HT2. HT1 will cease to be a
functional temple during the eternal state. Instead, HT1 will serve as a memorial, a museum in the 4D City. The
answer to the riddle as to how HT can be both removed and yet remain is answerable by acknowledging its dualism
and bifunctional spatiality. As a place where sin is atoned, HT2 is functionally removed. At that juncture, as a
place where God’s presence is manifested, the Father’s throne is moved inside of NJ, making NJ the new
location of HT.

The illustration above depicts the presence of the 3D Heavenly City above Earth as the intermediate link to the 4D

TTT may be inside the 4D City but outside of the 3D City. Therefore, in the illustration, HT1 is pictured somewhere
inside of the upper part of the 4D City, while HT2 is pictured atop the 3D City, and thus technically outside of the
actual 3D City proper.
                         Illustration 24. TTT Inside 4D Temple but Outside 3D Temple




To answer the riddle posed by the exegetical data, the hypothesis posed herein is that TTT might be inside of the
4D City but outside of the 3D City. The inside of the 3D City has immediate (teleportational) access to the inside
of the 4D City, but the 3D City does not have immediate (teleportational) access to all the 4D City. Rather, the 3D
City is a subset of the 4D City (3D ⊂ 4D). To be sure, this theory is highly conjectural, but it seems to be a more
robust model than the simplistic solutions thus far posed.
       Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers
        Introduction
          Discussion of the typological association between the Holy of Holies in Solomon’s temple and Ezekiel’s
millennial temple, as they apply to New Jerusalem, was provided in MMP5. The inner cubicle dimensions of the
Most Holy Place are typically associated with the Cubical City (i.e., the 4D Heavenly City). The outer, inward-
sloping, three-tiered walls should be associated with the Pyramidal City (i.e., the 3D Heavenly City). The
proposition made therein is that the Heavenly City is cubical on the inside but pyramidal on the outside. The inner
and outer walls are transitional because they reflect the teleportational nature of the walls of the City. The walls
have the potential to transfer one from the outer 3D Pyramidal City to the internal 4D Cubicle City when used in a
teleportational capacity. Cropped illustrations regarding Eze 41:7 from Block (NICOT) and Beattie were also
provided in MMP5. The biblical data regarding Solomon’s temple and Ezekiel’s Millennial temple combine to help
fill in the details. A detail not discussed in MMP5, however, was the upper chambers since they are not directly
applicable to the Heavenly City. They are indirectly applicable to the discussion of New Jerusalem, nevertheless,
because these upper rooms suggest a dimension higher than New Jerusalem, or a higher New Jerusalem than the
one pictured in relation to Earth in Revelation. Biblical data indicates that Jesus may have passed through the 3D
City and 4D City on His way to this upper dimension, and that this upper dimension is His eternal residence.
                       Illustration 25. Fundamental Mismatch of Tabernacle and Temple




         But why is there a mismatch between the dimensions of the Tabernacle and those of the Temple? Why is
the Holy Place of the Temple taller than its Holy of Holies? Why make the Holy Place ten cubits higher than the
Holy Place in the Temple? What is signified typologically by this change? Before suggesting a hypothesis for the
typological significance, we need to first investigate the three most common propositions as to the architectural
relationship the mismatch creates between the location of the Holy of Holies in relation to the Holy Place. The
architectural relationship will provide a clue as to the typological significance.


        Popular Examples of the Upper Room Chamber
         An abbreviated quotation from Omanson and Ellington (UBSH) was provided earlier regarding the
interpretive options for 2Chron 3:9. The full quote is as follows:

              And he overlaid the upper chambers with gold: The meaning of the Hebrew expression
      rendered upper chambers is not clear. The following interpretations have been suggested:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 62
               (1) Many versions take the Hebrew as referring to “upper rooms” in the Temple on the sides
      of the Temple (see 1 Kgs 6:5–6, 8–10; 1 Chr 28:11). Following this interpretation, CEV has “The walls
      of the small storage rooms were also covered with gold.”
               (2) Some interpreters think that the writer was referring to upper rooms within the Most Holy
      Place, but this seems unlikely since no other passage in the Old Testament refers to such rooms there.
               (3) But another interpretation is also possible. According to 1 Kgs 6:20, the Most Holy Place
      was 20 cubits high. And according to the MT of 1 Kgs 6:2, the Temple was 30 cubits high. This seems
      to suggest that either (a) the Most Holy Place was raised 10 cubits from the ground and that steps led
      up into it, or (b) there was an empty space of 10 cubits between the ceiling of the Most Holy Place and
      the ceiling of the Temple. Translators will find drawings of the Temple in some commentaries and
      biblical dictionaries that have steps leading up to the Most Holy Place. Other drawings, based on the
      second interpretation, show a loft, or empty space, above the Most Holy Place. If there was an empty
      space of 10 cubits between the ceiling of the Most Holy Place and the ceiling of the Temple, it may
      have been this place that was overlaid … with gold. This seems to be the basis for the SEM rendering,
      which reads “He also had the ceilings covered with gold” (similarly TOB, LPD). (Bold and italics theirs;
      highlight mine.)

Omanson and Ellington suggest that translators should consult drawings of the Temple. Good idea. Do an internet
search for cross sections of Solomon’s Temple, and you will quickly find examples of the most popular options. A
few illustrations I found depict the roof above the Most Holy Place as lowered so that there is no room above it,
which would correspond to the first option noted by UBSH. However, the vast majority of the illustrations show
the height of the Temple as being uniform over both the Holy Place and the Most Holy Place, which leaves us with
the other two basic interpretations posed by UBSH as being most prevalent. I did not observe any examples of their
second interpretation, which makes sense since this position is untenable, as they note, because the Most Holy Place
is cubical within and thus would not have rooms within it.57 As to the possibility they pose in 3a, it would be easy
to illustrate by portraying steps that raise 10 cubits from the Holy Place up to the Most Holy Place. Yet I did not
observe any examples of anyone adopting this position. Most of the illustrations show an upper room chamber:
some show steps leading from the Holy Place up to the Most Holy Place, while others show no steps. The latter
option would correspond to the 3d option posed by UBSH, which I have highlighted in yellow within their quote.
For simplicity’s sake, I will adopt that option (no steps and an Upper Room Chamber), yet the other option (steps
and an Upper Room Chamber) would be preferable for those who prefer complexity.58 In that case, the
corresponding area below the Most Holy Place associated with steps leading up to the Most Holy Place would
represent 3D space, while the area above the Most Holy Place would represent 7D space.

      In the Iron Age temples of Arad and Ain Dara and the Late Bronze Age temples at Tel Lachish and
      Tel Beth She’an, the floor of the Holy of Holies was higher than that of the Outer Sanctum…. In all
      of these temples there was a staircase from the Outer Sanctum to the Holy of Holies. Thus, in our
      proposal for the reconstruction of Solomon’s Temple…we add a staircase.

He makes a good argument based on extrabiblical inference. In MMP5, I used similar types of arguments from
extrabiblical pyramids to conjecture the dimension for the top of the pyramidal 3D City.

                                Illustration 30. Temple Cross Section without Steps




         Having now an accurate model for the temple with steps, I created a comparable cut-away view for the
temple without steps in Illustration 30, which represents the model I adopted in MMP5. In this model, the inside of
the chambers are five cubits high from floor to ceiling. The ceiling is not included in the five cubits. Also, the
ceiling/floor is made thick in each case so that the top of the side chambers reaches the top of the Holy of Holies.
Since the steps going up to the Holy of Holies and the associated space under the Holy of Holies created by the
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                      Page 67
steps has been removed, this model is very feasible, creating the simplistic overlap for the three-tiered outer
chambers tiers and the internal cubical chamber, just as I posed in MMP5.61
                                     Illustration 31. 4D-HT1 Versus 3D-HT2




         Analysis of the biblical data regarding the heavenly temple/tabernacle herein has led to the postulation of a
duality regarding its nature. Heavenly Tabernacle 1 (HT1) was theorized to be associated with the 4D Cubical City,
while Heavenly Tabernacle 2 (HT2) was theorized to be associated with the 3D Pyramidal City. This hypothesis
finds support from other interpreters who likewise perceive a duality regarding the heavenly temple/tabernacle.
Consequently, in principle, they affirm the distinction between HT1 and HT2. They do not, however, offer
specifications as to their distinctive locations. The unique portion of my hypothesis is that HT1 is associated
with the 4D City in contradistinction to the association of HT2 with the 3D City. This pinpoints the spatial
locations. Two competing models have been offered regarding its typological association, depending on whether
the temple has steps leading to the Most Holy Place.
                 Illustration 32. Temple Cross Section with Steps and with 4D-3D City Overlap




61
    Images without stairs include Biblical Archaeology Society Online Archive. Also, see
https://www.rotation.org/topic/temple-resources and https://www.mozaweb.com/en/Extra-3D_scenes-
Solomon_s_Temple_Jerusalem_10th_century_BC-172839.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 68
          The spatial distinction between HT1 and HT2 is readily apparent if the temple has steps. In this case, the
three levels of the side chambers would match the three major levels of the 3D City posited by others. As a result,
the top of the 3D City would be considerably lower than the top of the 4D City. The gap at the top between the final
tier of the chambers and the Most Holy Place would serve to distinguish the top of the 3D City from the top of the
4D City and be quite conducive for illustrating the distinction between HT1 and HT2.
                Illustration 33. Temple Cross Section without Steps and with 4D-3D City Overlap




         If the temple has no steps, then the distinction between HT1 and HT2 is not as pronounced. Even so, the
inner slope of the outer chamber walls still suggests an external pyramidal access to the internal cubical chamber.
Accordingly, the pyramidal shape would have a smaller top than the cubical chamber and thus leave room for a
distinction between HT1 and HT2. The hypothesis of an Upper Room Chamber is equally compatible with either
of these models. Steps or no steps makes no difference to the plausibility of an upper chamber as long as the steps
are not protracted into 10 cubic high stairs, leaving no room for an upper chamber. But as one can easily discern
from an online search, that possibility is not a concern, only a theoretical consideration.62 Actually, the presumption
is the opposite, as demonstrated by the ISBE: “As the height of the Temple internally was 30 cubits, it is obvious
that above the most holy place there was a vacant space 20 cubits long and 10 high. This apparently was utilized as
a chamber or chambers for storage or other purposes.”63

                                 Illustration 35. Temple Cross Section with Steps




         This gapped 2D cross-section is just another way to illustrate the same proposition made in Illustration 32
(Illustration 32 = Illustration 35). Regardless, in terms of the overall conclusion, steps versus no steps makes no
difference. For convince sake, the model with steps will be adopted as the working premise in the present
book. Thus, it is presented last in the above series as being the model chosen for our preliminary working premise.
The three side chambers go around three walls of the temple and correspond to the three levels of 3D New Jerusalem.
         The Tabernacle in the Wilderness was laid out on a horizontal plane. There were no steps involved from
one area to another, but the priests proceeded from the outer court, into the holy places and from there into the holy
of holies, on one horizontal plane. Warnock begins his section, “Ascending Realms In The Temple,” by
acknowledging: “The Tabernacle in the Wilderness was laid out on a horizontal plane. There were no steps involved
from one area to another, but the priests proceeded from the outer court, into the holy places and from there into the
holy of holies, on one horizontal plane.”64 Nevertheless, because of the mountainous nature of the Temple, the
confirmation of an upper court in Jer 36:10, and the Songs of Ascending in Ps 120-134, he postulates ascending
realms for the temple, thus providing some biblical grounds for preferring the model with steps, which, when taken
in conjunction with Garfinkel’s extrabiblical support for proposing steps, gives good grounds for preferring the
model with steps.

64
 George H. Warnock, “From Tent to Temple.” Available at https://www.bibleword.com/GWH/totemple.htm#chapter3.
Accessed 11/12/2022.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 70
         Warnock proceeds to pose that when Jesus talked about going to prepare a place for us in Jn 14:2,23, He
intended a connection to the three stories of the Heavenly Temple located in the third heaven. Associating the place
that Jesus is preparing for us with New Jerusalem is nothing new. Along with others, I have made that association.
Moreover, associating it with the three-tiered Heavenly City is nothing new. I have already made that association
in MMP5. What is new, at least in that I had not considered the possibility, is that Warnock next goes on to propose
that the upper chambers is the secret place of the Most High in Ps 91:1 and that God makes this place available to
us as His Bride via the secret places of the stairs (SSol 2:14), as confirmed by David (Ps 27:5).

    •     “He who dwells in the secret place [sether] of the Most High shall abide under the shadow of the
          Almighty” (Ps 91:1; NKJ).
    •     “For in the time of trouble He shall hide me in His pavilion; in the secret place [sether] of His tabernacle
          He shall hide me; He shall set me high upon a rock” (Ps 27:5; NKJ).
    •     O my dove, that art in the clefts of the rock, in the secret places [sether] of the stairs, let me see thy
          countenance, let me hear thy voice; for sweet is thy voice, and thy countenance is comely” (SSol 2:14;
          KJV).

        Warnock writes more so at a devotional level, so I was not inclined to give his statements any exegetical
weight. At most, I initially thought we might say that intimacy with God is like sharing a secret place with God and
is expressed in poetic imagery; however, I assumed that we could not literally share a heavenly secret place with
Him because He is on a throne in a very public arena accessed by thousands of angels. My perception of God’s
throne would be more like that seen in Micaiah’s vision when he said: “I saw the Lord sitting on His throne, and all
the host of heaven standing by Him on His right and on His left” (1Kgs 22:19). God’s throne is a hustling and
bustling place. It is not a secret place. So, is the Bible just speaking metaphorically about God’s secret place? Is it
just a metaphorical picture of intimacy with God? Is it merely poetic imagery offering psychological security?
Commenting on Ps 91:1, Terrien (ECC) allows: “The ‘secret place’ may well be the consciousness of psychological
security, not a geographical construction of public worship (Pss 32:7; 119:114; cf. Pss 27:8; 31:21; 61:5).” Granted,
David does associate it with the imagery of wings: “Let me dwell in Thy tent forever; let me take refuge in the
shelter [sether] of Thy wings” (Ps 61:4). Still, literality should not be conceded so easily. Better answers are
available.


         Ps 61:4
    For one thing, when David said, “I will dwell in the house of the Lord forever” (Ps 23:6), one should not assume
that he was doing nothing more than writing a psalm conveying mere psychological security. He could very well
be talking about living in New Jerusalem, as I have argued in LTPW. Consequently, when David says that he will
dwell in the Lord’s tent/tabernacle [ohel] forever (Ps 61:4), one should not dismiss the possibility of a literal future
fulfillment as the basis for his present psychological encouragement. “I will abide in Your tabernacle forever; I will
trust in the secret shelter of Your wings” (TM). Phillips (JPCS) gives a very suggestive interpretation of Ps 61:4:


                 “I will abide in Thy tabernacle for ever: I will trust in the covert [the secret place] of Thy
        wings. Selah.” The word translated “abide” literally means “to be a house guest for the night.” David
        wanted to be a house guest in God’s tent, the one he himself had pitched to house the sacred ark in
        Jerusalem.
                 As an ordinary Israelite, even though a king, David had no access into God’s tent. Only
        the priests could go into the Tabernacle and only the high priest, once a year, on the day of
        atonement could go into God’s immediate presence. Even then he could not stay there. After
        hastily performing the necessary rituals, he had to retire. He could not go back into God’s presence
        again for a whole year.
                 David, of course, was a spiritual man, one of the few people in Old Testament times who
        saw beyond the rites and rituals to the realities they symbolized. He wanted to be God’s guest
        for the night. But then he lifted his aspiration out of the time dimension into the eternal, where,
        as he well knew, God really dwelt: “I will be a house guest for the night in Your tent for ever!”
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                            Page 71
                 “I will trust in the hiding place of Thy wings,” he added. David was thinking of the sacred
       ark, which was covered by the mercy seat, God’s throne. David knew that the figures of the cherubim
       were fashioned out of the same piece of solid gold that comprised the mercy seat. He knew, too, that
       the wings of the cherubim were outspread to cover the mercy seat and the ark. David thought of the
       security a person would enjoy who could creep as a house guest into God’s pavilion, stay there forever,
       and know the overshadowing protection not merely of the wings of an anointed cherub, but of the
       eternal, uncreated, self-existing God Himself. “Selah!” said David, “There, what do you think of that!”
       It is the language of a man who has entered into a dimension of Old Testament theology rarely grasped
       by those who lived before the gospel age. [Insert regarding the secret place is his.]

         Phillips asserts that David knew that God inhabits an eternal dimension. Of course, David knew that! As
already discussed, Isaiah asserts as much (Is 57:15). If Isaiah could perceive that God was an eternal inhabitant of
eternity, and thus must have a place to inhabit throughout eternity, then why could David not share the same
perspective, believing that God has inhabited this place from all eternity. This place is secret in that it is inaccessible,
not secret in that it is incomprehensible. David knew that God, as an eternal being, had to dwell in a transcendent
eternal dimension beyond all created spacetime continuums. David was not only a deep thinker; he was also a
prophet; plus, he knew about New Jerusalem. He knew that one day he would live in New Jerusalem (see LTPW,
MMP5). Further, he knew that the Wilderness Tabernacle was patterned after heavenly things, including New
Jerusalem. David was not just engaging in wishful thinking. He was not just writing poetry; he was writing
prophecy. Eventually, the psychological comfort conveyed by the metaphorical imagery of being in the secret place
under God’s wings would give way to the spatial comfort of dwelling forever in God’s heavenly tabernacle. The
shadowy secret place of God’s wings would find literal spatial fulfillment when it became the literal secret place
anticipated by the Tabernacle. Even in this passage, a literal premise for the secret place is discernable.
         Exegetical investigation is in order to see if any more secrets regarding this secret place might be
discernable and perhaps even answerable. The postulation of an upper chamber above the Holy of Holies is well
received by many in scholastic circles, so Warnock’s proposition should not be dismissed as mere devotional
sentimentality without first looking into the possibility of an underlying connection. Should the upper chambers of
the Temple be associated with the secret place? David aspired to live in the Reward City for eternity. Yet might he
have aspired for something more? He knew that God created the Reward City to be an eternal reward for the faithful
(Heb 11:16). Thus, he knew that this Created City, which houses myriads of angels, was not eternal in terms of
eternity past. God not only dwells in the Heavenly City, but He must also dwell in a higher dimension that is truly
eternal, that transcends the dimension inhabited by the angels in the Heavenly City. Just as David knew that one
day he would rule Israel from an earthly throne and also rule much more vast domains from a heavenly throne in
New Jerusalem, he could deduce that God rules from a truly eternal throne and also from a lower dimensional
angelic throne. Might he aspire to access a higher dimension in reaching God that was higher than what angels
might aspire?
         After all, it was David who wrote Ps 2:4-5! “What is man, that Thou dost take thought of him? And the son
of man, that Thou dost care for him? Yet Thou hast made him a little lower than God, and dost crown him with
glory and majesty!” The writer of Hebrews uses this passage to extrapolate that Jesus, as the resurrected God-Man,
and His human brethren who follow Him will be made higher than the angels (Heb 2:6ff). Since the writer of
Hebrews could draw this inference from what David wrote, one can suppose that David could reach the same
conclusion. The real question is, “Will Jesus’ followers be merely higher than angels in terms of ruling power or
also in terms of divine nature?” In my writings, I have argued that the Bride has a higher nature than angels, as even
anticipated in the OT (see LTPW). In MMP5, I deduced that this means that the Bride must also have a higher
dimensional nature (cp. Ps 45:11, etc.). I suspect that David was able to come to the same conclusion.


        Ps 27:4-6
       David wanted to build a house (bayith) for God to dwell (yashab) in on Earth, but God told him that
Solomon would build this house instead (2Sam 7:5-13; 1Chron 17:4-12). Moreover, God promised that Solomon
would dwell (yashab) in His house forever: “I will settle [yashab] him in My house and in My kingdom forever”
(1Chron 17:14). However, David warned Solomon that fulfillment of this promise was conditioned on Solomon’s
obedience (1Chron 28:6-9), which is consistent with David’s affirmation that dwelling with God on His holy hill is
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 72
a reward limited to the faithful who do works of righteousness (Ps 15:1-2). Likewise, in Ps 23:6, David’s
anticipation of dwelling in God’s house forever is attributed to his walking in the paths of righteousness (Ps 23:3).
The same conceptual reality is in view: misthologically dwelling with God. This motif occurs again in Ps 27:4,
where David expounds further upon this desire:
      4
        One thing I have asked from the Lord, which I will seek: That I may dwell [yashab] in the house
      [bayith] of the Lord all the days of my life, to behold the beauty of the Lord, and to seek Him in His
      temple [hekal]. 5 For in the day of trouble He will conceal me in His pavilion [sok]; in the secret
      shelter [sether; place] of His tabernacle [ohel] He will hide [sathar] me; He will lift me up on a rock.
      6
        And now my head will be lifted up above my enemies around me; and I will offer sacrifices with
      shouts of joy in His tabernacle [ohel]; I will sing, yes, I will sing praises to the Lord. (Ps 27:4-6; TM)

         One should not limit David’s hope to the temporal dimension. For instance, David does not limit the one
thing he seeks to be able to dwell in God’s house all the earthly days of his life. In Ps 23:6, he invests this otherwise
seemingly temporal expression, days of my life, with an eternal aspiration: “Surely goodness and lovingkindness
will follow me all the days of my life, and I will dwell in the house of the Lord forever.” This deduction is confirmed
by David’s prayer in Ps 61:4: “Let me dwell in Thy tent [tabernacle] forever.” Along with other OT writers, David
believed that he would share in the resurrection.65 Like the other heroes of faith mentioned in Hebrews 11, David
65
  As discussed in MMP5, OT saints believed in the physical resurrection. To the list discussed therein, additional

reference he cites in support is E. Jocob, “Immortality,” IDB. In comparison to other such articles in other Bible
Dictionaries, it is a good article. Nevertheless, rather than reasoning forward based on the OT passages and
background material, which is the standard practice, I will reason backward, having already provided some of my
forward reasoning in MMP5. Since David and other OT saints believed that the faithful would live in the Heavenly
City (Heb 11:16), they, of course, believed in the better resurrection (Heb 11:35). Consequently, having reached
that conclusion from the more restricted scope of evidence in MMP5, I am inclined in MMP6 to accept Dahood’s

Your celestial mountain (Ps 61:3). After his body fails and falls in death, God will strengthen him with a new body
that enables him to be with God (Ps 73:24-26). David will walk with the Lord in the land of the living after his
death (Ps 116:9). David will be with the Lord after his resurrection (Ps 139:18). Lead me into Paradise when my
time comes (Ps 139:24). Considering Dahood’s comments on Ps 43:3, I have added Ps 84:4 for consideration. The
LXX says that they praise God evermore, thus, eternally. One might also consider Ps 26:8 and 46:4.
         Of course, the common perception is that Zionist psalms like Ps 43:3 simply refer to earthly Jerusalem.
However, for my misthological perception of the Holy Hill/Mountain (Ps 24:3-5; 15:1-2), see LTPW and MMP5.
Therein, I discussed the misthological implication of these texts and others, such as the courts of the temple in
Ps 65:4. In that discussion, I gave an extended quote from Phillips (JPCS, Ps 65:4), who believes that David was
looking forward to the millennial temple. I expand that argument to conclude that David foresees more than just the
millennial temple. He sees Mount Zion as being a place where those who are faithful among redeemed humanity
will be able to dwell in the house of the Lord, the heavenly Mount Zion. In such cases, the courts equate to both the
inner and outer courts of the temple as being misthopologically approachable and livable, equatable with New
Jerusalem. The historical background for Ps 84 might have been “originally a pilgrimage psalm for the
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                        Page 73
was looking forward to the reward of being in the Heavenly City and experiencing a better resurrection (Heb
11:16,35). Certainly, his hope included dwelling in the house of the Lord during the eternal days of his life. As
expounded upon in LTPW in connection with this verse, the lyrics of the chorus from Surely Goodness and Mercy
are applicable:

                                   Surely goodness and mercy shall follow me
                                        All the days, all the days of my life.
                                   Surely goodness and mercy shall follow me
                                        All the days, all the days of my life.
                                 And I shall dwell in the House of the Lord forever;
                                      And I’ll feast at the table spread for me.
                                   Surely goodness and mercy shall follow me
                                        All the days, all the days of my life.

Looking with David beyond the temporal to the eternal dimension of this realization, one may conclude that for
those believers who qualify, dwelling in the House of the Lord will be theirs eternally: all the days of their life will
be forever. As Dahood (AYB) surmises: “The heavenly habitation of Yahweh is meant here, as in Ps 23:6….The
allusion is to God’s dwelling in heaven; cf. Ps 61:5 [i.e., Ps 61:4]”. Likewise, in commenting on Ps 23:6, Dahood
says, “After a peaceful life under the guidance and protection of Yahweh, the psalmist looks forward to eternal
happiness in God’s celestial abode. Cf. especially Ps 27:4.” Dahood further notes that like hekal (palace), “which
in a number of passages refers to God’s celestial habitation,” bayith here “signifies the heavenly dwelling of
Yahweh.” For instance, in Ps 11:4, David says, “The Lord is in His holy temple [hekal]; the Lord’s throne is in
heaven.” Dahood does well to link the passage with being treasured and sheltered in Yahweh’s celestial temple.66
Still, more seems to be implied by David’s desire for God to conceal and hide him in this heavenly abode.
         McGee’s response (TTB) is a little more satisfying: “Where was the secret place of the tabernacle? It was
inside the Holy of Holies. No one could go there but the high priest.” Smith and Lee concur: “The secret place of
the Tabernacle was where God Himself dwelt, in the Holy of Holies. Into the secret of His own presence hath He
brought us. ‘Your life is hid with Christ in God’ (Col. 3:3)” (emphasis theirs).67 If the Holy of Holies represents
New Jerusalem, which is entirely plausible, then David is looking forward to being in the Reward City. Very good.
Still, what if Christ is seated even higher? One might wonder if New Jerusalem is the only sense in which David
anticipated being hidden in God’s abode. Jacobson and Tanner (NICOT) suggest more than one meaning: “The
Lord’s tent [tabernacle] (ʾōhel) is a frequent metaphor for the temple, but also a metaphor for God’s protecting


sanctuary....but the present psalm is clearly a Zion-psalm” (Tate, WBC), anticipating God’s heavenly home as its
ultimate vantage point (Phillips, JCPS). Just as Jesus traversed through the heavens to reach the ultimate heaven,
we must progress through the historical and contemporary applicability to reach the ultimate applicability. Having
a secret place in our heart for the Lord temporally is a precursor to having a secret place prepared for us in His
bridal chambers eternally.
66
    Regarding the treasured/hidden ones of Ps 83:3, multiple dualities are discernable: treasured-hidden,
soteriological-misthological, Israel-Bride, and rapture-Tribulation. See my article, “Hidden Ones.” Available at:
http://misthology.org/pdf/articles/Hidden_Ones.pdf. Accordingly, no dichotomy needs to be posed between being
treasured and/or hidden in Ps 27:5. Likewise, the passage can have an intended temporal, historical, and
misthological range of application. The historical backdrop provides the imagery for the temporal communion and
protection that we experience now is a foretaste of what awaits us in the future. This foretaste should serve as an
appetizer to whet our appetites and stimulate our thinking as we set our minds on the things above. Yes, our life is
hidden with Christ. Yet the realization of the glory associated with that union is contingent on seeking for and
thinking about the things above where Christ is (Col 3:1-4). This glory is misthological (RBVGB). If Christ has
passed through the lower heavens to a higher heavenly realm, then we are seated with Him there provisionally and
presumptively. Contemplating the full implications of what it means to be seated with Christ in heavenly places is
a valuable means for coming into the full realization of those implications. The Queen, representing the Bride of
Christ, will be led into the King’s palace/home/temple (hekal) along with her companions (Ps 45:15).
67
   James Smith and Robert Lee, Handfuls on Purpose for Christian Workers and Bible Students, series I–XIII, five-
