Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 74
presence. Both senses are meant here.” Just as there is at least a duality regarding the meaning of tent, a multiplicity
to the way David desired to be hidden is perceptible. Barnes (BNONT) takes a position that can be expounded upon:

               In the secret of his tabernacle. In the most retired and private part of his dwelling. He would
      not merely admit him to his premises; not only to the vestibule of his house; not only to the open court,
      or to the parts of his house frequented by the rest of his family; but he would admit him to the private
      apartments—the place to which he himself withdrew to be alone, and where no stranger, and not even
      one of the family, would venture to intrude. (Emphasis his.)

         The Holy of Holies is not the most secret place in God’s temple, to which the tabernacle was but a shadowy
forerunner. Something more reclusive and private is detectable in the temple. David had a hand in providing the
plans for the temple. My supposition is that the Holy Spirit used David’s desire to be hidden in God’s tabernacle to
create a place to signify it in the plans for the Temple so that God would more fully disclose its significance via
progressive revelation. As a result, this secret place is not so secret that we cannot discern its ultimate design. The
secret place in Ps 27:5 is the precursor to the secret place of the Temple, which, in turn, is the typological indicator
of the realm above the heavenly Holy of Holies.
                              Illustration 36. Temple and Tabernacle Cross Sections




         The above illustration juxtaposes a cross-section of the temple (shown in the middle) with a cross-section
of the tabernacle (shown at the bottom). The eternal thrones of the Father and Son are depicted at the top. As noted
in the previous chapter, Hebrews describes the Holy Place of the tabernacle as the outer tabernacle (Heb 9:5). The
Holy of Holies of the tabernacle is thus the inner tabernacle. When Christ ascended, He passed through the “greater
and more perfect tabernacle not made with hands, that is to say not of this creation” (Heb 9:11). This is the heavenly
counterpart of the earthly tabernacle made by human hands. The heavenly counterpart was created by God, not by
humans. Christ passed through this heavenly counterpart, the perfect tabernacle, to reach the heavenly ta hagia (the
holy place) in Heb 9:12, rendered as the Most Holy Place by NKJ. If the perfect tabernacle refers to the full heavenly
counterpart, then it refers to both parts of the Heavenly Tabernacle, both the Holy Place and the Holy of Holies. On
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                        Page 75
the other hand, if the heavenly tabernacle through which Christ passed is only the Holy Place, then one can argue
that Christ passed through it merely to reach the Holy of Holies associated with the angelic realm. The more natural
implication is that Christ passed through the entire Heavenly Tabernacle to reach the 7D eternal holy place where
God alone dwells (thus the Eternal Most Holy Place—EMHP) rather than through just part of the Heavenly
Tabernacle only to reach the 4D holy place created by God (thus a Created Most Holy Place—CMHP). If this is
the case, then the MHP for humans has a corresponding MHP (CMHP) for angels, which in turn has a corresponding
MHP (EMHP) for God alone. Commentators are divided on the issue, but those like Delitzsch, who interpret it to
mean that Christ passed through both parts of the Heavenly Tabernacle to reach the eternal realm of the Heavenly
Sanctuary (i.e., EMHP), have the better argument. The ta hagia of Heb 8:2 is rendered as sanctuary by the NKJ,
but consistency would dictate that it is referring to the same edifice, the eternal one in this case, as Delitzsch
maintains. In accordance with such arguments and the Hebrew grammar, it is best to distinguish this Eternal
Sanctuary from the true tabernacle which the Lord pitched not man in Heb 8:2 so that the writer of Hebrews is
understood as affirming that Christ is a minister in both locations: in both the eternal realm and the created heavenly
realm.
         The Heavenly Tabernacle is where God dwells with angels. The Earthly Tabernacle is where God met with
His human followers in OT times. In the eternal state, the tabernacle of God will be among men, and He will dwell
among them (Rev 21:3). The outer portion of His tabernacle will be suspended in space above Earth and even
accessible by Terran kings of the Earth who will bring earthly riches to Bride (Ps 45:12; Rev 21:24-26). We might
liken this portion of God’s house, that is, 3D New Jerusalem, to the living room where the Lord and His Bride will
entertain their mortal guests. The living room is part of God’s house, part of His temple, but it is not the entirety of
His house. The Lord and His Bride will also have an office in their house, so to speak, from which they can conduct
commerce throughout the multiverse. We thus might liken the 4D Heavenly City in the inner portion of His
Heavenly Tabernacle as serving such a function. However, the three-chambered temple has an additional Upper
Room/Chamber that the two-chambered tabernacle does not have. The position I will be developing as we proceed
is that this new additional chamber typologically prefigures the place that Jesus left to prepare for us in His
ascension. It was no coincidence that Jesus made the promise of this Prepared Place as a new addition to His Father’s
house in the upper room where He was celebrating the Lord’s Supper with His foot-washed disciples. It represents
the upstairs master bedroom, a place of intimacy reserved for the Lord and the Bride. A portion of mortal humanity
will have access to the 3D City, and it will be a door for immortals to the 4D City. But only the Bride will have
access to the 5D City. We will not jump to such conclusions. Nevertheless, such conclusions will be reached by
proceeding with the analysis of the OT background and NT context.


        Ps 91:1
        What does it mean for a believer to dwell in the secret place [sether] of the Most High in Ps 91:1? As for
that chapter, Psalms 91 is a messianic song having primary application to Jesus (cp. Ps 91:11-12 with Mt 4:6) yet
having an inclusive secondary application to His followers. Since the Psalm is uninscribed, the author is unknown.
Even so, even if David was not the author, he was familiar with it, as Franz explains:

              Psalm 91 is uninscribed, but some commentators attribute it to Moses and suggest it is a
      continuation of Psalm 90. The superscription of that psalm says: “A Prayer of Moses the man of God.”
      Psalm 91 starts out:

             He who dwells in the secret place of the Most High shall abide under the shadow of the
             Almighty. I will say of the LORD, “He is my refuge and my fortress [Masada], My God,
             in Him I will trust” (91:1–2).

            This would have been a psalm David knew by heart. He understood theologically that the
      LORD was his fortress/stronghold and his trust was in God.68



68
  Gordon Franz, “Picture Postcards from the Psalmists,” ed. Bryant G. Wood, Bible and Spade 22, no. 1 (2009):
18.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 76
        God hid Moses in the cleft of the rock to protect Moses when God was showing as much of His glory to
Moses as possible (Ex 33:20-23). Moses knew what it was like to have special access to God. Moses also knew
what it was like to talk with God from within the Tabernacle with no one else having access to it at that time
(Ex 33:8-11). Whether it be inside the cleft of the rock covered by God’s hand or inside the Tabernacle with a pillar
of cloud standing at its entrance, Moses had spatial locations he could associate with secret time with God. David
could associate Masada as a spatial reality to God being his fortress. David’s association of the Tabernacle and the
Temple with being a secret meeting place with God is natural, but it may also have been theological.
    Although Hossfeld and Zenger (HERM) translate sether as protection in Ps 91:1 rather than secret place, they
have no problem associating it with the temple:

      In the ancient Near Eastern concept of the sanctuary, the temple itself has a special protective function.
      As a sacral space (temenos) set apart from the profane world, the sanctuary has its own special
      protective rights (including asylum in the Temple and the obligation to keep peace there). And as the
      place of community festivals with sacrificial meals, music, and dance, it is linked with notions of
      intensity and integrity of life.
               That there is allusion here to the Temple at Jerusalem is suggested by the divine epithet “Most
      High,” which is an integral part of Zion theology (cf. esp. 46:5*; 47:3*). (Emphasis theirs.)

        As to the terminology, Estes (NAC) prefers the term secret place with the added connotation of protection:
“The noun sēter, which is rendered as ‘protection’ by CSB, is better translated ‘secret place,’ that is, a place of
privacy and protection; and it is similar to the thought in Pss 32:7; 119:114 that the Lord is the hiding place for
his people.” Secret shelter would be an apt translation. Spence-Jones (TPC) mentions other possibilities but
likewise concludes that in this context, the secret place refers to the most holy place of the tabernacle and temple:

               I. WHAT IS THE SECRET PLACE OF THE MOST HIGH? The idea of this “secret place” is
      frequently met with. 1. Sometimes it tells of some secret hiding-place, such as David often resorted to
      when a fugitive; and the sure protection of God is likened to such safe shelter. 2. At other times, the
      central tent of the commander of an army seems to be meant, as in Ps. 27:5, “He shall hide me in his
      pavilion,” etc. The enemy would have to break through rank after rank of the encamped army ere he
      could reach the well-guarded central tent of the leader. So inaccessible to the foe, so strongly placed
      was it, that it is taken as an emblem of our security in God. 3. But it is to the most holy place of the
      tabernacle and temple that we think allusion is here made. That sacred chamber was
      emphatically the secret place of the Most High. It was entered but once a year, and then only by
      one person, the high priest, bearing the blood of atonement. For all the rest of the year no footfall
      was heard in that secret place, no eye looked upon the glory of God that shone forth there. That
      loneliness told of the sad alienation that had sprung up between God and man through man’s
      sin. But that secret place was the earthly dwelling-place of God. There, between the cherubim,
      his glory shone forth, and there he was said to dwell.
               II. BUT WHAT IS IT TO DWELL THERE? Literally, no man ever dwelt there. We are driven,
      therefore, to seek the spiritual meaning of this word. And we note that: 1. Israel entered there in the
      person of the high priest, when he bore in his hand the atoning blood, which he was about to sprinkle
      upon the mercy-seat. All Israel found entrance there in their high priest, their representative. And
      whilst they continued in the faith of God, obeying and trusting him, they spiritually dwelt in that secret
      place, and, as a fact, were under the shadow—the high priest was so literally—of the Most High. No
      evil befell them, no plague came nigh their dwelling. It was well with them indeed. 2. And we enter
      and dwell there when, in the name of the Lord Jesus Christ, we come to God, pleading his all-
      sufficient sacrifice and atonement, of which the blood borne by the high priest told. And we dwell
      there as we continue in that precious faith. Then we, too, are under the shadow of the Almighty.
      The Law’s condemnation, sin’s power, earthly care, death, and the grave, can do us no harm; we are
      under the sure and blessed shelter of our God. (Italics his.)

        This proposition is very good insofar as it goes and is probably intended by Scripture for a bird’s eye
vantage point. Yet closer scrutiny suggests something more than just a mystical temporal communion. TPC does
not make a sharp distinction between the Tabernacle and Temple regarding the secret place, which is fine for this
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                      Page 77
vantage point. Still, as already noted, TPC hints that 1Kgs 8:27 indicates that God dwells in heaven above the
highest created heavens since created heavens cannot contain God. The angelic realm can contain the angels, for
God created that realm as a heavenly realm where He could dwell among angels. Notwithstanding, God also dwells
in a higher eternal realm that is inaccessible by angels. By their nature, angels are limited to their 4D reality.
Although they can visit the lower 3D reality, they cannot go higher than their 4D reality. The corresponding angelic
realm where God is reached by the Holy of Holies might be considered secret in terms of earthly 3D access, just as
TPC suggests; nevertheless, it is not a secret place in terms of heavenly 4D access. God’s heavenly 4D throne is the
hub of angelic activity, with myriads of angels coming and going. Now, if there was a place above what is indicated
by the Temple (in which only God dwells and to which angels have no access), this would better suit the moniker
secret place. A loft above the Holy of Holies in the Temple would fit that depiction. But could OT writers conceive
of God having His own personal dwelling or sanctuary above the angelic realm? The better question would be,
“Why not?”


        Jer 17:12
         Jeremiah supplies an interesting OT perspective on God’s throne that might refer to both its eternality and
duality: “A glorious throne on high from the beginning is the place of our sanctuary” (Jer 17:12). Holladay (HERM)
comments: “Yahweh’s habitation extends through time and space: it is not historically contingent…. The diction
which others may use for the temple precincts Jrm [Jeremiah) declares is appropriate only for Yahweh’s heavenly
sanctuary.” McKane (ICC) likewise states that Jeremiah’s description “does not refer to a historical beginning, but
to a transcendental origin which temporalities cannot contain.” John gives this OT realization NT clarity when he
says, “In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God, and the Word was God” (Jn 1:1). This with
denotes spatial association. Likewise, in the beginning does not mean that the Word came into being but that from
the beginning (1Jn 1:1) the Word of Life was already with God.
         The OT background for this NT affirmation, that the Christ existed from the beginning, need not indicate a
temporal beginning, as Yarbrough (BECNT, Jn 1:1) explains:

               The first point is that in Jesus Christ what is eternal and transcendent has become palpably
      immanent. In other words, John affirms the incarnation. Transcendence or eternality is implied by
      the words “what was from the beginning” (1:1). Ἀπʼ ἀρχῆς (ap’ archēs) occurs frequently in the
      LXX to refer to what extends back to the dawn or even predawn of time (Wis. 6:22; 9:8; Sir.
      16:26; Isa. 43:13). Habakkuk 1:12 LXX asks, “O Lord, are you not from everlasting [ἀπʼ ἀρχῆς]?”
      Regarding idols, Wis. 14:13 says, “Neither have they existed from the beginning [ἀπʼ ἀρχῆς] nor will
      they exist forever.” In contrast,. Old Testament messianic prophecy says that the origins of the
      Promised One will be “from the God has existed ἀπʼ ἀρχῆς; his existence transcends creation’s
      temporal boundaries beginning [ἀπʼ ἀρχῆς], from days of eternity” (Mic. 5:2 [5:1 LXX]). Without
      minimizing the historicality of Jesus’s existence, John evokes the horizon of eternity past as he opens
      his epistle.

         Likewise, Jeremiah’s use of the phrase from the beginning in Jer 17:12 need not denote a temporal point of
origin for God’s glorious throne or our sanctuary, as Lundbom (AYB) illustrates: “Reference here cannot be to the
Jerusalem Temple or any prior sanctuary. Yahweh’s heavenly Temple, like Wisdom, was there from the beginning;
cf. Prov 8:22–23.” This biblical text suggests eternality: “The Lord possessed me at the beginning of His way,
before His works of old. From everlasting I was established, from the beginning, from the earliest times of the
earth.” The same deduction can be argued for Jer 17:12.
         Keil and Delitzsch (K&D) object since the plural pronoun our would seem to be out of place if the reference
is to an eternal sanctuary:

               Ver. 12 is taken by many ancient comm. as a simple statement: a throne of glory, loftiness
      from the beginning, is the place of our sanctuary. This is grammatically defensible; but the view
      preferred by almost all moderns, that it is an apostrophe, is more in keeping with the tension of feeling
      in the discourse. The “place of our sanctuary” is the temple as the spot where God sits throned amidst
      His people, not the heaven as God’s throne: Isa. 66:1. This the pronoun our does not befit, since heaven
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 78
      is never spoken of as the sanctuary of Israel. Hence we must refer both the preceding phrases to the
      earthly throne of God in the temple on Zion. (Emphasis theirs.)

        Conversely, Lange (ACHS) does not find this plural pronoun our to be a problem, even for our sanctuary
in connection with God’s eternal transcendent throne:

              The Lord’s throne appears in the Old Test[ament] in three degrees. First, Jerusalem is thus
      named (3:17), second, the ark of the covenant (Exod. 25:22; Ps. 80:2; 99:1), third, the proper, so to
      speak, and transcendent throne (Isa. 6:1; Ezek. 1:26; Dan. 7:9; Ps. 9:5; 11:3; 47:9; 110:1). These three
      degrees are however so connected, that he who forsakes one does the same to the other. The prophet
      has primarily in view here, as at any rate in 14:21, the visible throne of the Lord.—Height of
      beginning….It is very often used to designate the transcendent abode of Jehovah….For that
      transcendent abode is from the beginning, eternally existing (comp. Ps. 93:2), and Zion also as chosen
      from eternity is in idea the eternal dwelling-place of God.…Place of our sanctuary….Even the
      sanctuary of Israel (‫ )מקדש‬is a double one, an earthly and a heavenly. The former is made according
      to the type of the latter (Exod. 25:8, 9, 40; 26:30). Thus though the expression refers primarily to the
      earthly sanctuary the heavenly is not excluded. (Bold his; highlight mine.)

If Jeremiah is referring to God’s eternally existing transcendent abode, then it is in a higher dimension than that
which He created to share with the angels. Christ would have had to pass through the Heavenly Tabernacle to reach
this Transcendent Abode, which I postulate is 7D Transcendent Heaven. The writer of Hebrews appears to be
insinuating that this is what occurred at Jesus’ ascension. As to Jeremiah’s our, it may not only be a double entendre,
but a triple entendre, referring to the 3D earthly sanctuary, 4D heavenly sanctuary, and 7D transcendent sanctuary.
Even so, would it not be better termed His sanctuary unless it is a sanctuary to which we at least have some type of
limited access? The first-person plural possessive, our, suggests that we have a shared access with God to this
Transcendent Heaven, which is in harmony with the desire expressed in Psalms to abide in God’s secret abode. For
logistical and biblical reasons, in MMP5 I posed that God is 7D, the Bride is 6D, and they share a 5D a nuptial
chamber, perhaps a 5D Heavenly City—only accessible by Her and Her Lord. MMP6 provides several lines of
exegetical evidence to further support this supposition. If something along these lines is correct, then Jeremiah’s
first-person plural possessive our could be taken as suggestive of a 5D sanctuary shared by the Lord with His Bride.


        Jn 1:1; 3:13,31
         As briefly indicated above, my assessment is that John’s opening affirmation in Jn 1:1 gives NT clarity to
the OT realization that Yahweh’s heavenly transcendent throne is above all created heavens. This assessment is
reinforced by several factors. Frist, as already noted, the opening verse implies a spatial relation between the Father
and Son: “In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God, and the Word was God” (Jn 1:1). This with
denotes spatial association, and in the beginning does not mean that the Word came into being but that from the
beginning (1Jn 1:1) the Word of Life was already with God. John affirms that Jesus came from Heaven (Jn 3:13,31;
6:38).69 He was with the Father in Heaven in close intimate proximity (Jn 1:18). Jesus came forth from the Father
and returned to the Father (Jn 16:18). Naturally, the Father is in the heavenly realm from which Jesus came and to
which He returned. The Holy Spirit also descended from Heaven (Jn 1:32). The heavenly Father, heavenly Son, and

When I presented a brief synopsis of this material for our Sunday School class, Doug Berner suggested that Jesus
was talking about a higher dimension of Heaven in Jn 3:13 than that which Enoch, Elijah, and Moses would have
had access to in their ascensions (5/14/2023). Precisely. Berner’s suggestion finds confirmation in Jn 3:31 and is
far superior to the typical explanation provided by the commentators, such as Barnes (BNONT), who asserts that
Jesus was merely saying that He is the only one who had ascended and returned, so as to be qualified to speak of
the things there” (emphasis his). Barnes misses the point. Barnes’ comments on 3:31 are better. Jesus is superior in
nature to all, even the angels. Since nature is tied to dimensionality, John is referring to a higher dimension than the
angelic heavens. Jesus is the only one to ascend in this manner because only the eternal Deity can access the eternal
realm. Only Jesus, as 7D Deity, has left the eternal realm to visibly communicate eternal truths about that realm.
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                       Page 79
heavenly Spirit co-existed in an eternal heavenly realm that has their same dimensionality. For example, we are 3D
beings who live in a 3D realm. Angels are 4D beings who live in a 4D realm. The Trinity is, by my calculations, a
7D Elohim. Thus, God is a 7D being who lives in a 7D realm. Such deductions flow naturally from the Johannine
context of Jn 1:1. The next verse reinforces this deduction: “He was in the beginning with God” (Jn 1:2). Jesus was
with the Father and with the Spirit in this eternal realm before time (as angels and we know it in our created realms)
began. Since Jesus existed within and with the Trinity before creation, He must dwell in a dimension higher than
the dimensions which He created.
         The beginning of time, as angels and we know it in our respective realms, begins in the next verse: “All
things [pas-nominative neuter] were made through him, and without him was not any thing made that was made”
(Jn 1:3). All things, referring to all created things, would include both the 4D and 3D realms and creatures: angels
and their realm, along with us and our realm. Paul expresses it this way: “For by Him all things [pas-nominative
neuter] were created, both in the heavens and on earth, visible and invisible, whether thrones or dominions or rulers
or authorities—all things have been created by Him and for Him” (Col 1:16). Jesus created these 3D and 4D
spacetime continuums. Since Jesus created all things, He must dwell in an eternal dimension higher than the
dimensions which He created. To use a biblical analogy, the builder of a house is greater than the house he built.
“For He has been counted worthy of more glory than Moses, by just so much as the builder of the house has more
honor than the house. For every house is built by someone, but the builder of all things is God” (Heb 3:3-4). Jesus
is greater than the 3D and 4D dimensions which He created. Just as a builder of a house can enter the house which
he builds but is not limited to that house in that he can go outside of that house, Jesus created our 3D and 4D houses.
He can come and go in and out of these houses as He desires. His ability to go outside the 4D house indicates that
He is not limited to the 4D spacetime continuum. As a matter of logical consistency, the OT, Jesus, John, and the
writer of Hebrews acknowledge this ability in various ways. One way the writer of Hebrews indicates this is by
affirming that Jesus passed through the 3D and 4D tabernacles on His way to His higher dimension, which I
calculate to be a 7D realm.
         John proceeds to provide confirmatory evidence in his gospel, stating, regarding Jesus, that “He who comes
from above is above all, he who is of the earth is from the earth and speaks of the earth. He who comes from heaven
is above all things [pas-genitive masculine/neuter]” (Jn 3:31; TM). English translations leave out the things because
in the genitive pas could be either neuter or masculine. In a footnote, Morris (NICNT, 1995) suggests that John was
noncommittal: “It might be masculine, with the meaning “above all men” (i.e., superior to all teachers), or it might
be neuter, “above all things.” Perhaps John would not care to be tied exclusively to either meaning.” I suspect that
it is the translators who do not want to be tied down to either meaning, though a few translate it as if it were
masculine (GWN, NJB, NLT). Morris shows a proclivity to this limited masculine perspective in his comment in
the body of his text: “Being ‘from above,’ he is superior to all on earth. ‘The one who is from the earth’ is a general
term that has its application to the whole human race.”
         Morris leans the wrong way in this preference. John is not saying that Jesus merely speaks with greater
authority than anyone on Earth. When Jesus left Heaven, He took a lower form and appearance to be sure (Phil 2:6-
8), but He did not surrender His divine nature or His ability to speak with divine authority. When it came to an
encounter with Satan, Jesus rebuked him (Mt 4:10), which is something that even Michael the archangel would not
do (Jude 1:9). John in no way wishes to convey the notion that Jesus speaks with an authority lower than the angels!
To the contrary, even during His arrest, when Jesus referred to Himself with the divine title “I AM” (ego eimi) they
drew back and fell to the ground (Jn 18:6). John underscores this divine utterance by recording it three times in this
context:
      5
        They answered him, “Jesus the Nazorean.” He said to them, “I AM.” Judas his betrayer was also
      with them. 6 When he said to them, “I AM,” they turned away and fell to the ground. 7 So he again
      asked them, “Whom are you looking for?” They said, “Jesus the Nazorean.” 8 Jesus answered, “I told
      you that I AM. So if you are looking for me, let these men go.” (Jn 18:5-8; NAB)

Michaels (NICNT) expresses an affirmation made by many commentators regarding this passage: “Clearly, the
Gospel writer intends us to visualize an extraordinary scene in which more than six hundred men are literally
‘bowled over’ by two simple words (esō eimi). Just to make sure we perceive the connection, he repeats the two
words.” Overpowering deific self-revelation, not mere self-identification, is conveyed by their falling to the ground.
Borchert calls it the “terrifying mystery of the ultimate reality.” As Osborne (VBV) notes that soldiers are not easily
knocked off their feet:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 80

      Jesus is revealing himself as the “I AM,” and people cannot remain standing before God’s actual
      revelation of himself. Certainly the pagan soldiers do not understand what has happened. They are
      trained never to fall to the ground; to fall is to die in the midst of battle. As they picked themselves off
      the ground, they undoubtedly asked something like, “Was that an earthquake? What could have
      knocked us off our feet?” We the reader know exactly what happened. They were suddenly in the
      presence of deity.

         The parsing guides I consulted do much better and consider pas to be neuter in Jn 3:31.70 This verse is
logically consistent with Jn 1:3 and Jn 3:13. Garland enjoins present-day counterparts to explain the significance of
their actions:

      Jesus responds, “I AM” (ego eimi). This literally floors them—they all fall to the ground (18:6). Jesus
      has no need of twelve legions of angels (Matt. 26:53); his word alone is sufficient to confound his
      enemies.
               The Revised Standard Version reads that it is a “band” that comes to arrest Jesus. This
      translation is somewhat misleading since the posse is specifically said to be a speira or cohort of troops,
      which normally refers to a Roman auxiliary infantry unit with a complement of 600–1000 men. John
      says it is commanded by a tribune (Greek, chiliarch), equivalent to a colonel (18:12). In addition to
      these soldiers, John mentions officers (hyperetas) from the chief priests and from the Pharisees (who
      make their only appearance here in the Passion narrative). …What is ironic about this is that Jesus is
      clearly prepared to lay down his life freely, but when fearful leaders send out a first-century S.W.A.T.
      team to seize him they fall helplessly to the ground as soon as he opens his mouth. They are hopelessly
      outnumbered by one.71 (Emphasis his.)

         Jesus, as an army of one, confronts them and bowls them over like bowling pins by the power of His word.
John repeatedly links Jesus’s ontological I-AM declarations with His spatial WHERE-I-AM affirmations.72 Ontology
is inseparably linked with dimensionality. Jesus is superior in both nature and location. He descended from this
superior location without surrendering His superior nature because higher dimensional beings can suppress the
fuller expression of their higher nature when descending into a lower realm, as angels also do when they appear in
a human form. Yet, in doing so, Jesus did something angels cannot do. He simultaneously remained in the higher
realm when He descended to the lower realm.
         I have already provided discussion of Jn 3:13 in MMP5: “No one has ascended to heaven but He who came
down from heaven, that is, the Son of Man who is in heaven” (NKJ). Indeed, in that discussion I commended Morris
(NICNT, 1995) for the connection he drew between Jn 3:13 and 1:18, which says that Jesus is in the bosom of the
Father:

      The copula “is” expresses a continuing union. The only begotten is continually in the bosom of the
      Father. When the Word became flesh his cosmic activities did not remain in abeyance until the
      end of his earthly life. There are mysteries here that we cannot plumb, but we must surely hold that
      the incarnation meant the adding of something to what the Word was doing, rather than the
      cessation of most of his activities.

Lenski also has an excellent discussion of Jn 3:13:


70
   Baker’s Analytical Greek New Testament. Software: BibleWorks, Logos, and Blue Letter Bible.
71
   David E. Garland, “John 18–19: Life through Jesus’ Death,” Review and Expositor 85, no. 3 (1988): 486.
72
   Consider that His generic where-I-am-going (pou hupago) statement in Jn 8:14 is intensified into a WHERE-I-
AM-Going (hopou ego hupago) declaration in Jn 8:21-22 and then climaxes in an I-AM (ego eimi) affirmation of
deity in Jn 8:58, for which they try to stone Him. This WHERE-I-AM-Going (hopou ego hupago) is also reiterated
in Jn 14:4, where it is immediately proceeded by a WHERE-I-AM (hopou ego eimi) in Jn 14:3. In that same context,
it is further preceded by a WHERE-I-AM-Going (hopou hupago ego) in Jn 13:33 and is apparently conditioned on
having a misthological place (meros) with Jesus (Jn 13:8). Hopou is linked with meros. See Jn 13:8, 127.
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                       Page 81
      He cannot change his divine nature, cannot lay it aside, cannot cancel even temporarily his divine
      Sonship, his unity of essence with the Father and the Spirit. This is unthinkable although men have
      tried to think it.…a durative, timeless present, like ὤν in 1:18, names the person according to his
      enduring condition or being, “he that is, ever and ever is in heaven.” And this designation ὁ ὤν dare
      not be altered into something else such as mere communion with heaven and thus with God. It denotes
      being. We may have communion with God, and yet who would dare to express that by saying that we
      ὄντες, “are,” in heaven.

         Christ did not give up His omnipresence during His incarnation. Various writers were documented in
MMP5 as making that affirmation and making such connection between Jn 3:13 and 1:18. Jesus remained in the
Father’s bosom even while He was on Earth. Indeed, Jn 5:19-20 continues that perception. During Jesus ministry
on Earth, He was still able to see the Father, as Lenski relates: “The Son constantly ‘sees’ the Father and what the
Father “is doing.” By using the present tense and thereby confirming His ability to see the Father even now, during
His time on Earth, Jesus confirms His existing ability to see the Father. Brown (AYB) also points out in this
connection: “There is reference to a (pre-existent?) vision of the Father in 6:46, 8:38. Jesus is the only one who has
ever seen the Father.” On those occasions, Jesus confirmed His pre-existing experience of seeing the Father. On
this occasion, however, He confirms His existing ongoing experience of the seeing the Father.
         The point to be stressed in the present discussion is that Jesus did so via His 7D nature from a 7D location.
Christ was simultaneously in the 7D Heaven via His 7D deity while on Earth during His 3D incarnation. Better
interpreters of Jn 3:31 perceive this type of implication. Kruse (TNTC, 2017) states a fraction of the verse’s
implication: “The one who comes from heaven is above all. This implies not only Jesus’ superiority to John, but
also his pre-incarnate existence in heaven” (emphasis his). Harris (HERM) does much better: “πάντων may well be
masc. (‘all others/all teachers/the whole human realm’) but more probably it is neut., ‘everything on earth,’
encompassing both the human and the non-human realms.” This affirmation of Jesus’ pre-incarnate existence
in Heaven within a context that confirms His omnipresence in terms of His simultaneous existence in Heaven and
on Earth, in a broader context that affirms that He created all things, can only mean that He is above all things so
that He is above all human and non-human entities and realms.73
         Klink (ZECNT) does well to link Jn 3:31 with 3:13 and the prologue: “His origin ‘from above’ connects
directly to what was revealed about Jesus in the prologue and should be used to support the force and meaning here.
His origin defines who he is in every way. Like a genealogical statement, this insight gives context to his identity.”
As an aside regarding the prologue, I might add a deduction from the present study. Many translations render Jn 1:18
as saying, “No man has ever seen God.” John does not specify in this verse that he is limiting his statement to human
beings, that is, a man (anthropos). Conversely, in Jn 1:6, he does make this specification: “There came a man
[anthropos], sent from God, whose name was John.” Klink links 3:31 with 3:13 and that verse with 1:18. Good.
But how does limiting seeing God to Jesus assert Jesus unique identity? Klink rightly notes regarding Jn 1:18a, “No
one has ever seen God… the theophanies of the OT (e.g., Exod 24:9–11), in no way imply that God’s essential
being had been seen….The meaning of the opening clause is quite clear: no person can see God. The exception is
Jesus Christ, described here as ‘the unique Son, God’ (μονογενὴς θεὸς).” What about angels?
         Angels have seen God. They even see His face continually (Mt 18:10), but this does not mean that they
share the unique relationship with the Father that Jesus does. So how does Jn 1:18a play into affirming Jesus as
God’s unique (monogenes) Son? My solution is simple: 4D angels have not seen God in 7D. They can only see God
as He reveals Himself to them in 4D. Only the 7D Son who is in the 7D bosom of the Father can see the essential
73
   Lenski’s rejoinder is: “Since the entire contrast from v. 27 onward deals with persons, ‘above all’ must mean not
‘above all things’ but ‘above all men’ (LCNT, Jn 3:31). My surrejoinder is that Lenski correctly acknowledges that
Jn 3:13 is to be understood as spatially affirming that Jesus was simultaneously in Heaven while on Earth. Lenski
rightfully insists that this verse assures dual location, not merely extended communion because Jesus could not have
canceled “his unity of essence with the Father and Spirit,” so that by virtue of His shared essence, Jesus had to be
in that higher dimension while on Earth (LCNT, Jn 3:13). Maintaining this greater point assures the lesser point.
Limiting oneself to the lesser point conflicts with the greater point. Moreover, in commenting on the all things (pas-
accusative neuter) in Jn 3:36, Lenski properly cites Eph 1:10,22, thereby including all things in Heaven and on
Earth. The earthly-heavenly scope for all things in 3:36 requires an earthly-heavenly scope for all things in 3:31.
The explicit neuter in v. 36 confirms the implicit neuter in v. 31. That the Father has given all things into the hands
of the Son in v. 36 makes it clear that the Son is above all things in v. 31.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 82
7D face of the Father. Many translations render Jn 1:18a more comprehensively as, “No one has every seen God.”
This translation is better and is the one used by Klink. In terms of His essential 7D being, only Jesus, not angels,
has ever seen God. Only the fully transcendent Son can see the fully transcendent Father. Thus, Jn 1:18a is just one
more way that John implies a transcendent reality to which even angels do not have access.
          Hendriksen and Kistemaker (NTC, Jn 3:31) do well to link Jn 3:31 with Paul’s affirmation: “In view of his
heavenly origin, is higher than all (cf. Eph. 1:20–23).” Fine. But how does Jesus’ heavenly origin make Him
higher than other heavenly beings? Their comments on that passage fail to answer that question: “The
enumeration of the mighty ones ‘far above’ whom Christ was assigned his place of pre-eminence is almost the same
as that in Col. 1:16. From that passage, in the light of Col. 2:10, as well as from the present Ephesian passage when
compared with 3:10, it is clear that the reference is, or is primarily, to angels” (NTC, Eph 1:21). Explaining
Eph 1:20-23 in terms of Col 1:16 will not suffice to answer the question because the latter passage asserts that Christ
is superior to angels because He created them. But Eph 1:20-23 does not assert His superiority by means of creation
but by means of location: being seated with the Father in the heavenly places for above all angelic powers. What
does this location have to do with proving that Jesus is higher than all other heavenly beings? Through various
cross-references, Hendriksen and Kistemaker defer their primary answer to Eph 4:10, where they say that “higher
than all the heavens….must not be taken in a merely literal sense but in a sense of majesty.” Good. Still, the converse
is just as true and much more in tune with the point Paul is making. Christ literally ascended higher than all these
heavens populated by lesser rulers. He ascended to a higher dimension and therefore has a higher rank, nature, and
authority. His origin from this higher dimension shows that He is higher than all beings in lower realms. As John
states, “He who comes from above is above all” (Jn 3:31a). Barnes correctly surmises: “Is above all. In nature, rank,
and authority. Is superior to all prophets (Heb 1:1, 2); to all angels (He. 1:4–14)” (emphasis his). He who comes
from a dimension above those in other dimensions is higher in rank, nature, and authority than those in those lower
dimensions. In Jn 1:15,30, John states that Jesus has a higher rank because of His temporal preeminence. In Jn 3:31,
he states that Jesus has a higher rank because of His spatial preeminence.
          In anticipation of His ascension, Jesus will later pray: “And now, glorify Thou Me together with Thyself,
Father, with the glory which I had with Thee before the world was” (Jn 17:5). This before is a temporal indicator,
taking us back to the time before the time of Jn 1:1, to eternal time before created time. The with is a spatial
reference, taking us back to an eternal realm before the created realm. A fully transcendent glory, time, and place
are thus indicated. Amazingly, Jesus desires to share this glory and location with His followers:
        22
          And the glory which Thou hast given Me I have given to them; that they may be one, just as We are
        one; 23 I in them, and Thou in Me, that they may be perfected in unity, that the world may know that
        Thou didst send Me, and didst love them, even as Thou didst love Me. 24 “Father, I desire that they
        also, whom Thou hast given Me, be with Me where I am, in order that they may behold My glory,
        which Thou hast given Me; for Thou didst love Me before the foundation of the world. (Jn 17:22-24)

Indeed, just prior to this prayer in the Garden of Gethsemane, while they were in the Upper Room, Jesus had
promised that He would prepare a place in His Father’s house for His faithful followers (Jn 14:2-3). A correlation
between this prepared place and the Upper Room Chamber of Solomon’s temple would be a reasonable association.
This anticipation is strengthened by the observation that these latter two texts are the finale in a progressively clear
series of such texts.


             Jn 7:34,36; 12:26; 14:3; 17:24
        Jesus’ spatial Where-I-AM (hopou eimi ego) statements in the Gospel of John compose another group of
Johannine texts frequently taken as confirming Jesus’ simultaneous presence in Heaven and on Earth. Of course,
Jesus’s ontological I-AM (ego eimi) statement in Jn 8:58 is widely recognized as being a claim to deity and was
taken as such on the historical occasion in which He made it: “Jesus said to them, Most assuredly, I say to you,
before Abraham was, I AM” (NKJ). The Jews attempted to stone Him for making this deific claim. Jesus uses the
emphatic combination of ego and eimi on a variety of occasions. However, all five of His Where-I-AM (hopou eimi
ego) spatial texts seem to confirm His deity as well:

    •        You shall seek Me, and shall not find Me; and where I am [hopou eimi ego], you cannot come. (Jn 7:34)
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                        Page 83
     •    “What is this statement that He said, ‘You will seek Me, and will not find Me; and where I am [hopou
          eimi ego], you cannot come’?” (Jn 7:36)
     •    If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am [hopou eimi ego], there [in that place, ekei] My
          servant also will be [estai]; if anyone serves Me, the Father will honor him. (Jn 12:26; TM)
     •    “In My Father’s house [oikia] are many dwelling places [mone]; if it were not so, I would have told you;
          for I go to prepare a place [topos] for you. And if I go and prepare a place [topos] for you, I will come
          again, and receive you to Myself; that where I am [hopou eimi ego], there you may be also” (Jn 14:2-3).
     •    Father, I desire that they also, whom Thou hast given Me, be with Me where I am [hopou eimi ego], in
          order that they may behold My glory, which Thou hast given Me; for Thou didst love Me before the
          foundation of the world. (Jn 17:24)

                                                  Affirmations
         At the very simplistic level, one can equate the last two references in the above list, as is done by Osborne
and Comfort (CBC): “In 14:2–3 he said he was going to heaven to prepare a place for them and would return for
them so that they would always be with him. The prayer of 17:24 is toward the fulfillment of that promise.” If the
place being prepared in Jn 14:2-3 is misthological, which it is, so is the place being requested in Jn 17:24. Therefore,
the most straightforward interpretation is to regard Jn 17:24 as describing a misthological location. A more precise
identification seems possible, however.
         As one proceeds through the Gospel of John, it becomes increasingly clear that the place where Jesus is
talking about is not merely Heaven but the Father’s house and that Jesus abides there already, even while on Earth.
The spatial association of the phrase is confirmed in Jn 12:26 and then again in Jn 14:2-3 with additional imagery.
Many commentators perceive the connection between this series of texts and Jn 3:13. In fact, Augustine elaborates
on the connection in his discussion of Jn 7:34:

         Therefore said He this: “Ye shall seek me, and shall not find me: and where I am, ye cannot come;”
         [Jn 7:34] because they were to seek Him after the resurrection, being pricked in their heart with
         remorse. Nor did He say “where I will be,” but “where I am.” For Christ was always in that place
         whither He was about to return; for He came in such manner that He did not depart from that
         place. Hence He says in another place, “No man has ascended into heaven, but He who came down
         from heaven, the Son of man who is in heaven.” [Jn 3:13] He said not, who was in heaven. He spoke
         on the earth, and declared that He was at the same time in heaven. He came in such wise that He
         departed not thence; and He so returned as not to abandon us. What do ye marvel at? This is God’s
         doing. For man, as regards his body, is in a place, and departs from a place; and when he comes to
         another place, he will not be in that place whence he came: but God fills all things, and is all
         everywhere; He is not held in places according to space. Nevertheless the Lord Christ was, as regards
         His visible flesh, on the earth: as regards His invisible majesty, He was in heaven and on earth;
         and therefore He says, “Where I am, thither ye cannot come.” Nor did He say, “Ye shall not be able.”
         but “ye are not able to come;” for at that time they were such as were not able. And that ye may know
         that this was not said to cause despair, He said something of the same kind also to His disciples:
         “Whither I go ye cannot come.” Yet while praying in their behalf, He said, “Father, I will that where
         I am they also may be with me.” [Jn 17:24] And, finally, this He expounded to Peter, and says to him,
         “Whither I go thou canst not follow me now, but thou shalt follow me hereafter.”74

Govett (GOJ, Jn 7:36) also notes the tense and association with Jn 3:13: “We should have expected, “Where I shall
be.’ But Jesus speaks of the truths which concern Himself in His Deity, as well as His Humanity. So to Nicodemus
He said, ‘The Son of Man who is in heaven’” (emphasis his). Plummer (CGT; Jn 7:34) agrees and focuses on the
tense: “The present tense implies His continual presence with the Father; ‘where I am,’ not ‘where I shall be’”
(emphasis his). Mounce (REBC, Jn 7:34) likewise states: “‘It is theologically accurate to say with Augustine that

74

York: Christian Literature Company, 1888), 191–192.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                         Page 84
‘Christ was ever in that place to which he would return.’ The verb (eimi, ‘I am’) is a gnomic present expressing a
timeless fact). Brown (AYB, Jn 7:34) concurs and expounds:

                where I am. One would expect “where I go,” as in 8:21; and some have suggested that eimi
       here is from the verb ienai, “to go,” rather than from the more usual einai, “to be.” However, more
       likely this reflects the divine use of egō eimi—see App. IV. It is Augustine who captures the
       atemporality of Jesus’ statement, “Christ was ever in that place to which he would return” (In Jo. XXXI
       9; PL 35:1640). (Emphasis his.)

Bernard (ICC, Jn 7:34) agrees: “‘And where I am,’ sc. in my essential being, in the spiritual world, ‘you cannot
come.’ There is no contradiction between μεθʼ ὑμῶν εἰμί of v. 33 and this statement; for the former only asserted
His visible, bodily presence, whereas the latter (εἰμὶ ἐγώ) spoke of His spiritual home.” He says, “εἰμί is the
essential present” (ICC, Jn 12:26, italics his). Vincent (WSNT, Jn 7:34) similarly connects it with Jesus’ essential
nature: “Where I am. In absolute, eternal being and fellowship with the Father. I am (ἐγὼ εἰμι) is the formula of
the divine existence (8:58). The phrase carries a hint of the essential nature of Jesus” (emphasis his). Robertson
(WPNT, Jn 7:34) agrees with Vincent that it refers to “the essential eternal spiritual home of Christ ‘in absolute,
eternal being and fellowship with the Father’ (Vincent).”

                                        Surrejoinders to Rejoinders
         Despite such confirmations by various interpreters and contrary to the texts, Harris (EGGNT, Jn 7:34) tries
to deny the time and asserts that it refers to the future: “I am (εἰμί referring to fut. time, as in 12:26; 14:3).” Regarding
Jn 12:26, he claims: “Εἰμί is futuristic in sense (note the following fut. ἔσται and cf. 7:34; 14:3).” To the contrary,
eἰμί (eimi) is present in tense and sense (note it is distinguished from the following fut. ἔσται). Jesus contrasts rather
than equates the present and future forms of eimi in this verse!
         With seemingly better scholastic plausibility, Michaels (NICNT) translates it as I am. Nevertheless, in a
footnote he allows that it might be an Attic future tense:

       “I am” (εἰμί), both here and in verse 36, can also be read as εἶμι, an Attic future of ἔρχεσθαι (hence
       as “I shall go”; see v. 35, “Where will this man go?”). As Danker points out (BDAG, 286), this is a
       possible reading in 12:26, 14:3, and 17:24 as well (see also BDF, §99[1]). I have left it “I am” in order
       to acknowledge that a different verb is used here than the ὑπάγω of v. 33, of 8:21, 22, and of 13:33.

The problem with this conjecture is that it is refuted by his own acknowledgment that Jesus uses a different verb to
denote the future location: “So he said to them again, “I am going [ego hupago], and you will seek me and will die
in your sin. Where I am going [hopou ego hupago], you cannot come. So the Jews said, ‘Will he kill himself, since
he says, ‘Where I am going [hopou ego hupago], you cannot come’?” (Jn 8:21-22, TM). “Little children, I am with
you a little while longer. You shall seek Me; and as I said to the Jews, I now say to you also, ‘Where I am going
[hopou ego hupago], you cannot come’” (Jn 13:33).75 Where-I-AM (hopou eimi ego) is not the same thing as Where-
I-AM-GOING (hopou ego hupago). Where-I-AM denotes present location. Where-I-AM-GOING denotes future
location. Two different verbs are used. To equate the two is to conflate the texts. Thus, with solid textual
justification, Dods (ExpGT) surmises: “Where I am” [not εἶμι, ‘I will go’]” (insert his).76

75
   The MT changes the word order. The standard word order is repeated in Jn 14:4 (MT). CT has ego as possibly
missing in Jn 14:4.
76
   Jesus also distinguishes past and present coming: “I know where I came [aorist erchomai] from, and where I am
going; but you do not know where I come [present erchomai] from, or where I am going” (Jn 8:14). Augustine
(NPNF) also explains the distinction in a consistent manner:

                 “I know whence I came and whither I go.” He who speaks to you in person has what He
       has not left, and yet He came; for by coming He departed not thence, nor has He forsaken us by
       returning thither. Why marvel ye? It is God: this cannot be done by man; it cannot be done even by
       the sun. When it goes to the west it leaves the east, and until it returns to the east, when about to rise,
       it is not in the east; but our Lord Jesus Christ both comes and is there, both returns and is here.
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                         Page 85
         Likewise, contrary to the texts, Klink (ZECNT, 7:34) asserts: “When Jesus states that he goes where they
‘are unable to come’ (οὐ δύνασθε ἐλθεῖν), he is not speaking of a definitive place, but to the distinction between
persons.” Klink then proceeds to shoot his argument in the foot when commenting on Jn 14:2, where he correctly
argues for the traditional spatial interpretation. Nevertheless, he tries to simplify the verse by concluding: “Without
denying the level of complexity presented to us by this verse, it is important that we not miss its overt
simplicity….The focus of this text is not merely the place but the person” (ZECNT, Jn 14:2a). So, as for spatial
implications, he wants to treat it as “simply referring to the heavenly abode of God.” I counter that the reverse could
just as easily be maintained: Without denying the overt simplicity, it is important that we do not dismiss the
underlying complexity. The focus of this text is not merely the person but the place. Klink cites an excellent article
by Brayan and summarizes Brayan’s position as “drawing from an elaborate collection of similar language in the
OT and other Jewish literature that portrayed the temple as the eschatological dwelling place of the followers of
God.” Klink objects that Bryan “interprets the metaphor too tightly (too physically),” that is, too spatially in contrast
to those who interpret the passage too spiritually, so that both “inappropriately fuse the historical and cosmological
realities so important to the Gospel.” In counterpoint, one may adopt both the traditional spatial interpretation for
the bird’s eye vantage point intended by Jesus for a simplistic overview but the more detailed spatial fusion “of
historical and cosmological realities,” to which Klink objects, as being an equally valid deeper vantage point.77
         In any case, the pro-spatial arguments are decidedly superior. Jesus asserts that He is also in Heaven at the
same time He was incarnate on Earth. Where in Heaven? While Jesus was in the Upper Room, the specific location
is said to be in the Father’s house. Moreover, in the High Priestly Prayer, it seems to be associated with an eternal
locality that has existed from eternity past, long before the foundation of the world. These additional clues suggest
a transcendent location for His hopou eimi ego (Where I AM) that accords with His transcendent nature as ego eimi.

      Hear the evangelist himself speaking in another place, and, if thou canst, understand it; if not, believe
      it: “God,” saith he, “no man hath ever seen, but the only-begotten Son, who is in the bosom of the
      Father, He hath declared Him.” He said not was in the bosom of the Father, as if by coming He had
      quitted the Father’s bosom. Here He was speaking, and yet He declared that He was there; and when
      about to depart hence, what said He? “Lo, I am with you always, even unto the end of the world.”

          Morris (NICNT) gives a weaker and less consistent interpretation, juxtaposing “past fact” with “present
mission.” Yet, regarding the present tense for the bread that comes down out of heaven in Jn 6:33, he acknowledges
that “the present tense denotes continuing action.” The same tense and expression occur in Jn 6:50, where Morris
rightly states that the aorist tense for eat indicates “once-for-all action of receiving Christ….Anyone who takes this
decisive action will live forever.” Likewise, Morris properly understands that the aorist for the living bread that
came down from heaven refers to the incarnation. The vital life-giving connection between the heavenly Christ and
the earthly Jesus remained intact in His ongoing action of imparting life to those who believed in Him. Through the
imagery of manna that continually came from Heaven to Earth, Jesus pictured His continuous download of life from
His heavenly self through His earthly self to those who believe in Him. The heavenly Eternal Logos via the earthly
Incarnate Logos remained the Life-Giving Logos, the Word of Life. Bernard (ICC, Jn 6:33) is succinct: “The Bread
of God is that which is ever descending [not He who descends] from heaven” (parenthetical insert his). Jesus is
asserting that, like manna, He is perpetually coming down out of Heaven to give life.
77
   Klink (ZECNT) poses three options for Jn 14:1a: (1) spatially explicit, referring to the heavenly temple, so Bryan;
(2) spiritual; (3) spatially generic, referring to God’s heavenly abode, so Klink. Yet, when commenting on Jn 14:1b,
Klink adds an additional spiritual sense to his generic spatial preference and cites McCaffrey in support. However,
in reality, McCaffrey argues for a spatially explicit understanding as referring to the heavenly temple and
secondarily to a spiritual interpretation at the deeper level. McCaffrey’s argument is much better suited for affirming
Bryan’s position than for affirming Klink’s preference. Bryan rejects McCaffrey’s additional spiritualization, saying
that it reads “more into the text than is warranted” (p. 196). I share Bryan’s assessment. McCaffrey argues well for
double understanding, but I would pose a deeper spatial-communal understanding as a secondary meaning. For my
interaction with their discussions, see Bridal Temple (p. 93), Bryan (p. 109), and Error! Reference source not
found. (p. Error! Bookmark not defined.). The “deeper spiritual meaning at a second level” posed by McCaffrey
is to suggest that the “physical temple is spiritualised [sic.] by the sacrificial transformation of the passion-
resurrection of Jesus.” The literalist can rightly respond to the supposed deeper spiritualization, “Bah hum bug.”
