(Roma: Pontifical Biblical Institute, 1988), 248.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 86
His spatial I AM is linked with His eternal I AM in such a way as to lead to the supposition that bridal believers
will dwell with the Eternal Logos in His eternal home in places He has commenced to prepare (hetoimazo) from
the point of His ascension. Previously, I have simply equated this post-ascension preparation in Jn 14:2 with the
Prepared City of Heb 11:16 since God is noted to have prepared (hetoimazo) this City as a reward for the faithful.
However, the creation of a higher addition to the Father’s house might be entailed. Since the Heavenly City for
which Abraham and other OT faithful believers sought was already prepared/created, some additional
preparation/creation might be suggested by this promise in the Upper Room of a future prepared place. Even the
OT provides hints regarding the nature and location of this future, prepared place.


        Singular Versus Plural Upper Chamber(s)
         The possibility that the upper chambers of Solomon’s Temple (1Chron 28:11; 2Chron 3:9) represent
something higher than the Holy of Holies has already been discussed herein. Where did Solomon get the plans?
From his father, Daivd. Acting under the inspiration of the Spirit (1Chron 28:19), David was the earthly mastermind:
“Then David gave to his son Solomon the plan of the porch of the temple, its buildings, its storehouses, its upper
rooms, its inner rooms, and the room for the mercy seat; and the plan of all that he had in mind” (1Chron 28:11-
12a). Most of the online depictions of this upper chamber picture it as being windowless, even though Solomon
overlaid it with gold (2Chron 3:9). Why overlay a room with gold that was never intended to see the light of day?
Even if it had windows but no ground access, what would be the purpose of overlaying it with gold if it was to have
no visitors?
         My speculation is that Solomon intended it to represent God’s secret Transcendent Heaven to which David,
his father, aspired to share with God to the fullest extent possible, at a level unassessable by even the angels. The
Tabernacle did not have steps to the Holy of Holies nor an upper loft above it. Nevertheless, under the inspiration
of the Holy Spirit and in accord with progressive clarity of typology in progressive revelation, David instructed
Solomon to add both details to the Temple (steps and an upper chamber) so that the Temple might more fully
represent the heavenly reality. The Holy of Holies is exalted above the Earth, as indicated by placing the Holy of
Holies above the area created by the steps leading up to it in the Temple. Yet a space even more exalted than the
Holy of Holies is also indicated by the secret chamber placed above it in the Temple.
         The plural versus singular nature of this upper space in 1Chron 18:11 is puzzling. Commentators normally
simply equate it with the plural of 2Chron 3:9. For example, Curtis and Madsen (ICC): “the upper chambers, cf. 2
Ch. 3:9.” Lange (ACHS): “its upper rooms (above the most holy place, 2 Chron. 3:9)” (emphasis his). So also ICC
and K&D. Boda (CBC) elaborates: “The ‘upstairs rooms’ were roof chambers in Solomon’s Temple (2 Chr 3:9;
2 Kgs 23:12), also found above large ancient houses (Judg 3:20; Jer 22:13) and at the gates (2 Sam 18:33) and
corners (Neh 3:31, 32) of walls.” In this case, though, this observation makes little sense since this roof area was
enclosed and inaccessible. Henry (MHCWB) seems to be more on target: “Even the upper chambers, or rather the
upper floor or roof—top, bottom, and sides, were all overlaid with gold” (emphasis his). Rendering it as the upper
floor or roof and understanding it to be enclosed would make the comment by Omanson and Ellington (UBSH) on
2Chron 3:9 more comprehensible as referring to “an empty space.” Dillard (WBC) refers to it as a singular room:

               The context describes the Most Holy Place (3:8–14), but no “upper chambers” are mentioned
      elsewhere specifically in connection with this room; cf. 1 Chr 28:11; 1 Kgs 6:2, 20 specify that the
      ceiling height of the building was thirty cubits, while that of the Most Holy Place was twenty cubits.
      The inner chamber of several excavated temples was built on an elevated platform; if the Most Holy
      Place were built on a similar platform five cubits high, a space of five cubits would remain above the
      Most Holy Place and below the ceiling of the remainder of the building. Perhaps this area above the
      Most Holy Place was gilded with the gold leaf.

Yet the Hebrew for aliyyah/aliyah is plural in this verse, as is the Greek. Even so, it is depicted as an enclosed
empty space above the Most Holy Place by online illustrations and rightly so, probably five cubits high, as Dillard
has noted, due to the cubic high steps leading up to the Most Holy Pace. Klein (HERM) concurs with Dillard but
translates it as plural rooms:
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                         Page 87
         If the inner sanctuary was built on a raised platform five cubits high, as the inner chambers of several
         excavated temples were similarly raised, there would still be a five cubit space above it, and this space
         (the upper chambers) Dillard proposes was gilded with the gold leaf. Perhaps this unclear and
         functionless reference to upper chambers (‫ )והעליות‬is only another attempt to include in the temple
         things that David had contributed: “David gave to his son Solomon the plan of the vestibule and its
         rooms, its treasuries, its upper rooms (‫)ועליתיו‬, and its inner chambers, and of the room of the mercy
         seat” (1 Chr 28:11). (Emphasis his.)

        Boda (CBC) likewise concurs with Dillard and translates it as plural rooms. After stating, “The reference
to “upper rooms” is not developed any further and is unknown from other sources,” he then adds this note:

         Japhet (1993:555) claims this reference to upper rooms is related to the entire Temple structure and
         not to the Most Holy Place alone, but the flow of this section suggests that the description is still of
         the Most Holy Place. Better is the suggestion of Dillard (1987:29) that the height of the Most Holy
         Place was given as 20 cubits in 1 Kgs 6:2, 20, even though the building height was 30 cubits. If the
         Most Holy Place was on a raised platform of 5 cubits, this would leave 5 cubits above the sanctuary
         for upper rooms.

But why would multiple inaccessible rooms have been built above the Holy of Holies? Scholars taking this point
of view seem to ignore this question. On the other hand, why would the plural form for rooms be used if a singular
empty space is entailed? Scholars taking that point of view seem to ignore that question. I will venture my own
conjecture: The formal plurality is used within a mysterious contextual singularity to allow a duality. Two vantage
points are intended but only come to light when progressive revelation is allowed to enlighten one’s contemplation.

                                                     Singularity
         As to singularity, the hypothesis has been posed herein that the Upper Room Chamber represents God’s
eternal realm. God built the Heavenly City (Heb 11:10). He pitched the heavenly sanctuary and tabernacle
(Heb 8:2). These realms are not eternal in terms of eternity past. They have a beginning point. These created
heavenly realms are represented by the Holy Place and Most Holy Place. As created realms, they cannot
contain the Creator. He existed in an uncreated realm before He created the created realms. His uncreated
realm is pictured by the empty space above the Most Holy Place (1Chron 28:11; 2Chron 3:9). The 3D Tabernacle
was replaced with the 3D Temple, which supplied this added detail regarding this inaccessible realm. The 3D
Temple is itself patterned after the 4D Heavenly Realm. Nevertheless, both the 3D realm and the 4D realm are
created realms. God’s uncreated eternal realm is further aloft and pictured by a singular empty loft.
         God is probably a 7D being who lives in a 7D dimension. Thus, this 7D loft is His true secret place. It
cannot be accessed by 3D or 4D creatures. The transcendence pictured by the transition from the 3D Holy Place to
the 3D Most Holy Place is but a shadow of the transcendence from the 4D Heavenly Outer Tabernacle to the 4D
Heavenly Inner Tabernacle. The ultimate transcendence, however, is from the 4D Heavenly Inner Tabernacle to
God’s 7D Ultimate Tabernacle. God dwells aloft in this 7D secret place. His creatures cannot reach Him here. They
cannot rise spatially to His level. Nevertheless, their prayers can transcend this spatial limitation. Believers can even
experience mystical communion with the Lord in this 7D secret place (Ps 27:5; 91:1).
         Jesus confirms that the Father dwells in a secret place when He refers to our “Father who is in the secret
place” (Mt 6:6,18; KJV). Osborne (ZECNT) confirms its heavenly spatial nature: “Yet here it says the Father ‘who
exists in the secret place’ (τῷ ἐν τῷ κρυφαίῳ), probably meaning that his existence is in the secret realm, namely,
heaven. The emphasis is on the development of ‘in secret’ (ἐν τῷ κρυπτῷ): first, the Father dwells ‘in the secret
places’ and then he sees what is done ‘in secret.’” Yet, in a footnote to v. 6, Osborne cites an article by Ellingworth,
which can be found online.78 Ellingworth says that the expression can mean that God is (a) in a separate heavenly
secret place from which the prayers are being offered or (b) in the same earthly secret place from which the prayers
are being offered. Ellingworth prefers the latter simply because it takes the secret place as being the same, which is
unpersuasive circular reasoning. Much more convincingly, Osborne prefers the first option. The immediate context


78
     See https://archive.translation.bible/tbt/1989/04/TBT198904.html?num=446&x=-365&y=-78&num1=.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 88
twice confirms the Father’s location as being in Heaven (Mt 6:1,9), and the broader context repeatedly refers to our
“Father who is in heaven” (Mt 5:45; 7:11,21), as does the rest of the book (Mt 10:32-33; 12:50; 16:17; 18:10). Jesus
tells us to address this secret prayer to “our Father who is Heaven,” not to “our Father who is in our prayer closet
with us.” The Father’s transcendence is spatially confirmed by His heavenly location. His immanence is
experientially expressed by His seeing, hearing, caring, and answering.
          Ellingworth mistakenly thinks that the same secret place was being affirmed but correctly believes that the
translation should be the same: secret place. My expanded gloss is: “Pray to your Father who is in His heavenly
secret place, and your Father who sees you in your earthly secret place will reward you” (Mt 6:6; TM). As to our
earthly secret place, France (TNTC) notes: “This was an inner room, secluded, probably windowless, and possibly
with the only lockable door in the house; it is thus proverbial for a secret place.” Not only is it proverbial, but it is
also typological. Our windowless, lockable secret place has its intermediate antitype in the 3D secret place above
the Holy of Holies and its ultimate antitype in the Father’s 7D ultraspace, His secret place. His singular secret place
finds its most personal typological counterpart in our singular prayer closet. Jesus makes this communion personal
and singular: “But you [singular], when you [singular] pray, enter into your singular inner room, and when you
[singular] have shut your singular door, pray to your Father who is in His heavenly secret place, and your Father
who sees you in your singular earthly secret place will reward you.” The singular open space above the Holy of
Holies signifies the Lord’s transcendent realm. Angels cannot reach that realm, but you can reach it through your
prayer closet. Rather, than having to settle for kneeling before the Father’s crowded 4D throne, surrounded with
masses of angels, you talk with your Daddy privately one-on-one. You have personal access to His secret place
from your secret place. The contextual singularity of the Lord’s secret upper chamber finds its ultimate personal
and typological counterpart in your prayer closet. So pray!

                                                     Plurality
        As sweet as this personal communion is presently, it may very well be surpassed by the communal
communion that the Lord typified via His Last Supper in the Upper Room. Some commentators believe that the
Lord’s Supper was held in the aliyah. Freeman and Chadwick acknowledge this possibility but note a weakness
with this view:

               In the New Testament the aliyah is referred to under the name “upper room.” It was in such a
      place that the disciples gathered immediately after the ascension of the Savior (Acts 1:13). In a room
      of this kind the corpse of Tabitha or Dorcas was placed. Here the widows whom she had helped wept
      over her, and here Peter restored her to life (Acts 9:37, 39). In a similar place in the city of Troas, Paul
      once preached until midnight, and put a young man to sleep (Acts 20:7–9).
               Some commentators believe that the “upper room” where Jesus ate the Passover with his
      disciples was a room of this description (Mark 14:15 and Luke 22:12). But the Greek word used for
      this room is not the same word used elsewhere for aliyah.79

         Regardless, Jesus held this supper in an upper room. Even if it was an enclosed upper room rather than
merely an aliyah on the roof, then it still accords with the aliyah above the Holy of Holies being an enclosed upper
room. The Lord’s Supper was held in a room that typologically would have represented that of the Temple’s loft
and ultimately a realm beyond the Holy of Holies. The Upper Room of the Lord’s Supper typologically and
purposefully represents the Upper Room Chamber(s) of the Temple.
         During this Upper Room discourse, the Lord cryptically talked about plural dwelling places in the Father’s
house in connection with His preparing a singular place for His followers: “In My Father’s house are many dwelling
places; if it were not so, I would have told you; for I go to prepare a place for you. And if I go and prepare a place
for you, I will come again, and receive you to Myself; that where I am, there you may be also” (Jn 14:2-3). In
MMP5, I argued that the Prepared Place is the Prepared City, that is, New Jerusalem, the 3D Heavenly City. Yet I
also allowed a temporal-eschatological dualism and considered an additional universal-misthopological dualism.
However, I rejected the later possibility for three reasons:



79
  James M. Freeman and Harold J. Chadwick, Manners & Customs of the Bible (North Brunswick, NJ: Bridge-
Logos Publishers, 1998), 260.
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                        Page 89
              First, the context is limiting the Father’s dwelling to faithful believers in Jn 14:23. Presumably,
      the same would be true in Jn 14:2. Second, the OT Davidic background associates participation in
      God’s house with reward and Mt Zion, not the universe (see LTPW). Third, being Christ’s house is a
      reward: “Christ was faithful as a Son over His house whose house we are, if we hold fast our confidence
      and the boast of our hope firm until the end” (Heb 3:6).

         Such considerations rule out making the universe part of the reference. Basically, the passage is
misthological; it does not apply universally to the entire universe. The Father’s house is not to be equated with His
entire kingdom. His house is His dwelling in the kingdom where His presence is visibly manifested. However, such
observations would not hinder a dualistic application to misthological rooms in the Father’s secret place. Having
access to the Father’s secret place is a reward. Temporally, it is conditioned on praying secretly (Mt 6:6).
Eschatologically, it is conditioned on obeying Jesus lovingly (Jn 14:23). My speculation is that Jesus chose to have
His Last Supper in an upper room to link it with the Father’s secret place and then blurred the lines between
singularity and plurality, which is likewise a feature of the upper chambers above the Holy of Holies as described
in the OT. Plural chambers above the Holy of Holies, as suggested by the plural form of aliyyah in 1Chron 28:11
and 2Chron 3:9, would suggest multiple dwellings. To the extent that is possible, the 7D God shares this space with
the 6D Bride. The plurality of the commentators enjoins with the singularity of the illustrators for this/these upper
room(s).
         This theory accords well with my conjecture in MMP5 that the Lord has built 5D chambers for His Bride,
one dimension above the 4D Heavenly City. The Lord’s house has a secret room that can only be accessed by the
Bride. Angels can access the 3D City and the 4D City, but they cannot access the bridal chambers. These 5D
chambers are reserved for the Bride in a dimension above the 4D City. Jesus passed through the heavenly tabernacle
to prepare bridal chambers for His 6D Bride in the 5D Heavenly City. The preparations He is making in the 3D City
are only a shadow of the preparations He is making in the 5D City. Consequently, the present study has found
further reason to confirm the proposition posed in MMP5:

      Since the beauty of the 6D Bride is to exceed the previous perfection of the 4D Lucifer, I pose that
      Jesus is presently preparing a 5D Heavenly City that will far exceed the beauty of the 4D Heavenly
      City that was inhabited by Lucifer. Surely, the Lord will have something better than a hand-me-down
      City to offer His Bride. Yes, She will have a residence in the 4D City and the 3D New Jerusalem from
      where She can interact with Her 4D and 3D subjects. However, Her getaway castle will be far greater
      than anything we can imagine—a 5D Heavenly City—only accessible by Her and Her Lord.

                                              Bridal Chambers
         My supposition that the location of the 5D City above the 4D City refers to bridal chambers is based on
logical inference, typological association, and historical precedence. Concerning the latter, we need to consider the
probable historical background for Absalom’s action in 2Sam 16:22: “So they put up the tent for Absalom on the
top of the house, and Absalom went in to his father’s women before the eyes of all Israel” (BBE). Commentators
frequently note that Absalom did this on the same roof from which David lusted after Bathsheba. For instance, Auld
(OTL) states:

               The advice is acted upon beyond the letter. Ahithophel has specified that all Israel should hear
      about what Absalom will do. But it is so arranged that “all Israel” will see. Seeing is particularly
      important. The roof of the king’s house, where the tent is erected for this proxy revenge on David, is
      precisely the locus from which he saw Bathsheba in the first place. Like Israel’s insulting the
      Philistines by defeating one of their posts in Benjamin (1 Sam 13:4), like Hanun’s insulting David by
      abusing David’s ambassadors (2 Sam 10:4–6), so now Absalom throws down a gauntlet to his father
      by deliberately mistreating his womenfolk.80

       That Absalom did this tent-on-the-roof routine simply, or even partially, as a revenge on David for
Bathsheba is highly unlikely. Absalon had other reasons for the tent on the roof. God is the one who orchestrated

80
  A. Graeme Auld, I & II Samuel: A Commentary, ed. William P. Brown, Carol A. Newsom, and Brent A. Strawn,
1st ed., The Old Testament Library (Louisville, KY: Westminster John Knox Press, 2012), 520.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 90
the historical context to bring this tent on the same roof as retribution for David’s murderous lust. Absalom intended
something else by placing the tent on the roof.
         Spence-Jones (TPC) is correct that the text actually refers to the tent, not just a tent, yet he misses the full
significance: “Hebrew, the tent; that constantly used by David and his family for the enjoyment of the cool evening
breeze, and which the citizens of Jerusalem had frequently seen erected on the flat roof of David’s house. It was
when walking on this roof that David had given way to guilty passion, and now it is the scene of his dishonour”
(emphasis his). Lange (ACHS) makes the same shortsighted observation:

              Ver. 22. They spread the tent; the Article [so the original, but it may properly be omitted in
      an English translation, because the definiteness is not obvious—TR.] indicates that it was the tent
      designed for the roof, used by the king and his family for protection against sun, wind and rain.
      Thenius: “the expression: the tent is an evidence that the author is relating events of his time.” On the
      roof, the same where David’s look at Bathsheba led him into the path of sin, whose evil results for
      him are completed in this deed of Absalom. (Insert and emphasis original.)

          Absalom did not choose this tent-on-the-roof location simply because that is where they enjoyed cool
breezes. The definite article does more than denote it as the tent designed for the roof. More is entailed by locating
it in its historical context as an expression of his time. The parenthetical remark supplied by ACHS above explains
why English translations fail to translate the article, but, counter to ACHS, they improperly omit it, rendering it as
a tent. The only two translations I observed that properly translate it as the tent are the BBE and YLT.
Notwithstanding, the definiteness is obvious enough that several commentators have perceived its historical and
biblical allusion as referring to the bridal chamber:

      The tent was, most likely, the bridal tent (cf. Ps 19:5[4]; Joel 2:16), and the whole proceedings were,
      more or less, equivalent to a royal wedding (so Stolz, 262) but with wider implications. Fokkelman
      (King David, 210) assumes that this tent was erected on the same roof from which David had seen and
      desired Bathsheba. This association may have existed, at least, in the mind of the narrator. (Hubbard,
      WBC)

      They pitched the tent, the bridal tent of the Semites which has survived, in the canopy of the Jewish
      wedding ceremony, to our own day. (Smith, ICC, italics his.)

              The use of the word tent in this context suggests a bridal tent set up for newly married people
      (compare Psa 19:4)….
              In the sight of all Israel: this probably does not mean that all the people of Israel watched the
      actual act of sexual intercourse, but rather that they were able to see Absalom go into the tent on the
      palace roof with David’s concubines, and that they knew what would happen inside. (Omanson and
      John Ellington, UBSH, emphasis theirs.)

      The tent that Absalom pitched in the sight of all Israel was probably a bridal tent. Absalom made the
      people of Israel fully aware that he was engaging in sexual relations with his father’s concubines.
      Putting the tent on the roof of the palace was an insolent act that was guaranteed to stir the populace
      one way or another. (Radmacher, NIBC, bold original.)

      The “tent” (ʾōhel) may have been intended to symbolize the ḥuppâ (“pavilion”) occupied by the
      bridegroom and bride on their wedding night (as in Ps 19:4–5; cf. Joel 2:16 [“chamber”]; for similar
      customs among modern nomadic Arabs, see the observations of W. Robertson Smith, Kinship and
      Marriage in Early Arabia [Cambridge: Cambridge Univ. Press, 1885] 167–69). (Youngblood, REBC,
      emphasis and insert original.)

The most pertinent section of Smith’s treatment to which Youngblood refers is as follows:
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                           Page 91
      The common old Arabic phrase for the consummation of marriage is banâ ‘alaihâ, “he built [a tent]
      over his wife.” This is synonymous with “he went in unto her” (dakhala, and Heb. (hyl,Þae ab) and is
      explained by the native authorities by saying that the husband erected and furnished a new tent for his
      wife (Misbâh, Baid. on Sûr. ii. 20, etc.). This explanation must have been drawn from life, for though
      the wife of a nomad has not usually a separate tent to live in, a special hut or tent is still erected for her
      on the first night of marriage, (ZDMG., vi. 215 ; xxii. 153). In Northern Arabia this is now the man’s
      tent, and the woman is brought to him (Burckhardt, Bedouins i. 107. Comp. Agh. ix. 150, 11, odkhilat
      ilaihi; Psalm xlv. 15 [E.V. 14]). But it was related to me in the Hijâz as a peculiarity of Yemen that
      there the dokhla or “going in” takes place in the bride’s house, and that the bridegroom if home-born
      must stay some nights in the bride’s house, or if a foreigner must settle with them. This Yemenite
      custom, which obviously descends from an old prevalence of beena marriage or Nair polyandry, must
      once have been universal among all Semites, otherwise we should not find that alike in Arabic, Syriac
      and Hebrew the husband is said to “go in” to the bride, when as a matter of fact she is brought in to
      him. And with the Hebrews the tent plays the same part in marriage ceremonial as with the Arabs.
      Thus, in 2 Sam. xvi. 22, “they pitched for Absalom on the roof” not a tent, as our version has it, but
      “the tent” proper to the consummation of marriage, identical with the hPx huppa or bridal pavilion of
      Ps. xix. 6 (E. V. 5), Joel ii. 16. So fr[, ères, the covered bridal bed (Cant. i. 16), is primarily a booth,
      Arabic ‘arsh. In all these cases the bridal bed with its canopy is simply the survival of the wife’s tent;
      and originally the tent belonged to the wife and her children, just as it did among the Saracens, for
      Isaac brings Rebekah into his mother Sarah’s tent (Gen. xxiv. 67), and in like manner in Judges iv.
      17.81 (Insert original.)

Not only is the canopy above the bridal bed a carryover but so is the canopy used for the Jewish wedding, as
Hastings (DOTB) notes: “The canopy under which Jewish couples are married at the present day still retains the
name, as it is a survival of the ancient chuppah or bridal tent” (emphasis his). As I have discussed several places in
my writings, Klein and Spears dedicate their third chapter, “The Betrothal,” for explaining, from the Hebrew
background, that the Lord’s Supper represents a betrothal.82 Moreover, the Lord’s Upper Room action of washing
their feet (Jn 13:1-14), promising not to drink wine until the consummation (Mt 26:29; Mk 14:25), and promising
to prepare a place for them (Jn 14:2), which all took place in the Upper Room, all correspond to details related to
the betrothal. What better location for the Lord’s Supper, in which He would be establishing His bridal betrothal,
than an upper room to represent a bridal chamber?
         Constable’s quote of Bailey regarding Jn 14:2 adds the bridal dimension: “This truth may reflect the
marriage custom of the bridegroom, who would go to the bride’s house and bring her to his father’s house,
where an apartment would have been built for the new couple” (TCENB). Gromacki (p. 112) elaborates:

              Jesus came the first time and paid the purchase price for his Bride (the church) by his death
      on the cross (Eph 5:25). After his death and resurrection Jesus ascended into heaven. Jesus told his
      disciples in John 14:2, “I go to prepare a place for you.” Just as the groom would go to his father’s
      house to prepare a place for his bride prior to the wedding feast, so Jesus has gone to the Father’s house
      in heaven to prepare a place for his bride, the church. Jesus promised his disciples in the upper room
      that he would return for them. “And if I go and prepare a place for you, I will come again and receive
      you to myself; that where I am, there you may be also” (John 14:3).
              During the time when the groom and bride were apart, the bride was to prepare herself for her
      wedding day. Paul emphasizes the importance of the bride living a holy life in 2 Corinthians 11:2:
      “For I am jealous for you with godly jealousy. For I have betrothed you to one husband, that I may
      present you as a chaste virgin to Christ”
              The groom would secretly come and surprise his bride and there would be a wedding. At the
      rapture of the church, Jesus will return for his bride, the church (1 Thess 4:13–18; 1 Cor 15:51–58).

      He will take his bride to heaven to be with him. During the time when the groom and bride were apart,
      the bride was to prepare herself for her wedding day. Paul emphasizes the importance of the bride
      living a holy life in 2 Corinthians 11:2: “For I am jealous for you with godly jealousy. For I have
      betrothed you to one husband, that I may present you as a chaste virgin to Christ”

      rapture of the church, Jesus will return for his bride, the church (1 Thess 4:13–18; 1 Cor 15:51–58).

Why prepare? Because being a prepared Bride is contingent on doing so. Gromacki acknowledges: “John says that
the fine linen is the righteous acts of the saints. So in a sense Christians are now preparing the wedding clothes that
they will wear at the marriage of the Lamb” (p. 114). The appropriate conclusion, which Gromacki falls short of
admitting, is that only prepared believers will compose the Bride. Although all believers will be raptured, only
faithful believers will be found at the Bema to have the fine white linen necessary to compose the Bride. Bridal
misthologists are keenly aware of this fact.
         The comments by Beasley-Murray (WBC) on Jn 14:2-3 also acknowledge the bridal association:

      The Father’s “house” with its many dwellings is most plausibly a pictorial representation of the
      transcendent dwelling of God, such as is depicted under the figure of “the city of the living God, the
      heavenly Jerusalem” in Heb 12:22, a symbol which is greatly elaborated in the apocalyptic vision of
      the City of God in Rev 21:9–22:5….
      ….“The “going away” of Jesus to the Father’s house to prepare a place for his own and his “coming
      again” to take them to it is as truly pictorial imagery as the descent of the Bride-City to a mountain for
      the benefit of earth’s inhabitants in Rev 19–21; it does grave injustice to the Evangelist and Seer alike
      to impute to them wooden literalness in their symbolic descriptions.

Unfortunately, his discussion is disappointing in regarding the Father’s house as merely pictorial imagery rather
than a literal City. The imagery points to a literal reality. Wiersbe (TBEC, Rev 21:1-5) acknowledges the literal
nature of the City, though he admits difficulty in trying to imagine it: “Even despite Scripture’s description, it is
difficult to imagine what the eternal city will be like. John characterizes it as a holy city (see Rev. 21:27), a prepared
city (see John 14:1–6), and a beautiful city, as beautiful as a bride on her wedding day. He amplifies these
characteristics in Revelation 21–22” (emphasis his). Even so, Wiersbe’s comments on Jn 14:2-3 are woefully
inaccurate: “It is unfortunate that some unbiblical songs have perpetuated the error that faithful Christians will have
lovely mansions in glory, while worldly saints will have to be content with little cottages or even shacks. Jesus
Christ is now preparing places for all true believers, and each place will be beautiful.” A bridal misthologist he is
not. Only faithful believers will have these heavenly mansions. Unfaithful believers will not be allowed into the
Heavenly City. Still, one can appreciate the fact that Wiersbe connects the City with a literal reality. The same can
be said of Woychuk, who states regarding Jn 14:2-3:

      Surely heaven is an actual and literal place. Christ is there now, suiting it and making it all ready for
      our abode. We do not know what heavenly architecture is like, but we do know that the Christ who
      has sown the fields with beautiful flowers and shaped the majestic mountains and valleys and molded
      the dry land out of the midst of a furious sea, and who fills the air with rain and dew to refresh the
      earth, is the same One whose hand is now designing the eternal home of the redeemed. Heaven will
      be inconceivably more beautiful than this world is. It will be the fair display of God’s astonishing
      grandeur and consummate beauty, so that we can hardly fathom here below the majesty and glory of
      the place which He has chosen for His own peculiar residence.
               Christ spoke of heaven as the place of ‘many mansions.’ The apostle Paul referred to the ‘third
      heaven’ (2 Cor. 12:2). On another occasion Jesus Christ alluded to heaven as ‘paradise’ (Luke 23:43).
      John related heaven to the ‘city’ whose name is the ‘new Jerusalem’ (Rev. 21:2, 23; 22:1–2). It seems
      evident to some that the holy city must be identified with heaven. At all events the Scripture describes
      how the tree of life is in the midst of the street of this center: “In the midst of the street of it and on
      either side of the river was there the tree of life” (Rev. 22:2). Compare Revelation 2:7 and its statement
      that “the tree of life is in the midst of the paradise of God.” If the tree of life is in the midst of God’s
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                        Page 93
        paradise and the same tree is found in the midst of the holy city, then we have a reason for linking new
        Jerusalem with heaven. All the various designations of heaven—the Father’s house, third heaven,
        Paradise, holy city—stand for the same place. Certainly such a location is a literal, physical and actual
        place. This is the spot where we shall live in the future.83

The primary problem with Woychuk’s assessment is that he chucks everything together, making the house, city,
and land all refer to the same thing. A more accurate delineation would note that the Heavenly City is in Paradise,
which, in turn, is in the third heaven. These items should not be equated. The fact that they are all heavenly does
not make them equatable with Heaven. They are parts of Heaven.

                                                 Bridal Temple
         The various details we have of heaven should not be thrown into a blender to make a heavenly smoothie,
so that the exegetical richness of the text is lost. Bryan’s comments on Jn 14:2-3 are vastly superior:

        Commentators have typically passed over this passage as a generalized description of heaven as the
        dwelling place of God. Most do not even mention the possibility that “the Father’s house” of 14:2 be
        taken as a reference to a Temple of some sort, though a similar expression occurs in 2:16 in relation to
        the Jerusalem Temple and references to the Temple as God’s house are frequent in Jewish literature.
        A few older commentators were prepared to find a reference to a heavenly Temple, though with scant
        evidence. Similarly passed over is the possible reference to the Temple with the term “place” at the
        end of v. 2 and again in vv. 3–4. As with the phrase “my Father’s house,” the term “place” has already
        been used by John to refer to the Temple in the expression of the Jewish leaders’ fear that if Jesus were
        allowed to continue the Romans would take away “our place” (11:48; cf. 4:20). This term too is
        frequently found in Jewish literature in reference to the Temple, including a number of texts cited
        above that express or serve the idea that the Temple would be the eschatological dwelling of the
        righteous. So then in 14:2 we appear to have not one but two expressions that are not only used
        elsewhere in John to refer to the Temple but are widely paralleled in references to the Temple found
        in the OT and intertestamental literature.
                 As the background of 14:2, R. Brown suggests Deut 1:33, where God rebukes the faithless
        fear of the Israelites, who do not trust in the LORD, “who goes before you on the way to seek out a
        place for you to camp.” Brown suggests that this typology indicates that Jesus goes before the disciples
        to prepare a place for them in the promised land. However, the parallel between seeking a place to
        camp and preparing a heavenly dwelling is not close. Moreover, τόπος never appears as a designation
        of the land, though it frequently appears as a reference to the Temple. A more likely influence on the
        idea expressed in John 14:2 is the LXX of Exod 15:17, a text that shaped Second Temple thinking
        about the eschatological Temple in sources as diverse as 2 Maccabees and 4QFlorilegium. Exod 15:17
        LXX translates the Hebrew ‫“( מכון לשׁבתך‬place of your dwelling”) with ἕτοιμον κατοικητήριόν
        σου (“your prepared dwelling place”) and then immediately picks up the verbal form of the adjective
        in the following apposition, ἁγίασμα, κύριε, ὃ ἡτοίμασαν αἱ χεῖρές σου (“the sanctuary, O LORD, which
        your hands prepared”). The notion first found in Exod 15:17 of a sanctuary that the Lord prepared by
        his own hands to be his dwelling place had already given rise to the belief among some that the
        eschatological Temple would be a Temple not made with hands [cp. Heb 9:11]. This presupposes a
        very close association or perhaps even identification of the eschatological Temple with the heavenly
        Temple, which was necessarily a Temple not made with hands. In view of the close verbal parallels
        with a text known to have informed discussions of the heavenly and/or eschatological Temple and the
        numerous conceptual points of contact with Jewish ideas about the Temple, it seems likely that John
        was speaking of a heavenly Temple that, though built by God, Messiah would prepare to be the
        eschatological dwelling of his people. As with the texts surveyed above, John 14 envisions the Temple
        as the eschatological dwelling place of the righteous.
                 If John 14:2 refers to the Temple, a number of important features of the text emerge. First, it
        is striking on any interpretation of the “Father’s house” that, though the house already exists and

83
     N. A. Woychuk, “Is Heaven A Place?,” BibSac 109 (1952): 74–75.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 94
      already contains many rooms, Jesus nevertheless goes “to prepare a place.” If the text refers to the
      Temple, the many rooms are the rooms of the heavenly Temple that Jesus prepares to serve as dwelling
      places for his followers.84

Bryan does an excellent job in noting the temple association and providing background analysis for linking Jn 14:2-
3 with a heavenly Temple prepared as an eschatological dwelling place for the righteous. His discussion dove tails
into the above exegesis regarding Jesus having entered “the greater and more perfect tabernacle, not made with
hands, that is to say, not of this creation” (Heb 9:11). The place Jesus is preparing is a Transcendent Temple with
chambers created by His hands.85
        In commenting on the bride being brought into the king’s chambers in SSol 1:14, Jamieson, Fausset, and
Brown (JFB) combine the temple allusion with the bridal motif:

               chambers—Her prayer is answered even beyond her desires. Not only is she permitted to run
      after Him, but is brought into the inmost pavilion [i.e., the secret place], where Eastern kings admitted
      none but the most intimate friends (Es 4:11; 5:2; Ps 27:5). The erection of the temple of Solomon was
      the first bringing of the bride into permanent, instead of migratory, chambers of the King. Christ’s
      body on earth was the next (Jn 2:21), whereby believers are brought within the veil (Eph 2:6;
      Heb 10:19, 20). Entrance into the closet for prayer is the first step. The earnest of the future bringing
      into heaven (Jn 14:3). His chambers are the bride’s also (Is 26:20). There are various chambers, plural
      (Jn 14:2). (Emphasis theirs.)

The observation that I am adding to this discussion is that the Bride’s secret place is associated with the secret
temple chambers above the Holy of Holies and thus cannot be limited to the 3D City or 4D City but must also
include a 5D Transcendental Temple/City. In doing so, I am taking Paul literally when he says: “But just as it is
written, ‘Things which eye has not seen and ear has not heard, and which have not entered the heart of man, all that
God has prepared for those who love Him. For to us God revealed them through the Spirit; for the Spirit searches
all things, even the depths of God” (1Cor 2:9-10). Such preparations cannot be limited to the 3D City or 4D City.
People have seen both the 3D City and 4D City. OT saints, among others, saw the 3D City. Many people have seen
the 4D City in near death experiences and have lived to tell the tale. Many people have heard about New Jerusalem.
The deeper truths to which Paul refers cannot be so obvious as these. The 5D City, on the other hand, has not been
seen by anyone, unless by Enoch, though that would be highly debatable, and I am skeptical. People have not heard
about a heavenly Temple City that transcends the Heavenly Jerusalem explicitly mentioned in Scripture. The only
way I can perceive it is through the logical implications of various scriptural inferences.
         This postulation of a 5D Temple/City reminds me of the anomalies that lead to the discovery of Neptune.
Discrepancies between mathematical projections and planetary observations resulted in some significant
discrepancies between the projections and observations regarding the orbit of Uranus. Various theories were posed
to explain the anomaly: solar gravitational influence, observation error, or the existence of an as-of-yet undiscovered
planet. In time, the latter answer proved to be correct with the discovery of Neptune. In like fashion, I pose the
existence of an as-of-yet unseen 5D Transcendent Temple City. My exegetical projections lead to this as being the
most satisfying postulation.

   Steven M. Bryan, “The Eschatological Temple in John 14,” BFBR, Vol. 15 (2005): 193–195. The rest of his article

   In passing, consider two riddles. When Jesus said that He would raise the temple of His body in Jn 2:19, was He
anticipating doing so from the Transcendent Temple? He said that He would raise Himself from the dead in
accordance with the Father’s authority (Jn 10:17-18). Lange discusses various interpretations for authority. A more
complete analysis would tie in His duality. While on Earth, He was in Heaven. Also, when in the Earth, He was in
Heaven. That is, when He died, He was in the subterranean Hades within the Earth, while also in Heaven. When
He said that He would raise Himself from the dead, was He talking about doing so through the agency of His divine-
human spirit (which descended into Hades) or by means of His divine spirit (which remained in Heaven)? Secondly,
when He ascended into Heaven in His resurrected form, did He lay aside having a separate divine form so that from
that time forward, He would only have the divine-human nature?
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                      Page 95

        Heb 9:3
       Apparently, Jesus, Paul, and the writer of Hebrews have left us clues to this effect. As to the latter,
commentators commonly assume that the Most Holy Place of Heb 9:3 is the secret place alluded to in Scripture.
For example, Calvin (CC) states:

               Then, after this [Holy Place] is mentioned, the most secret place [the Most Holy Place], which
      they called the holy of holies, still more remote from the view of the people, and it was even hid from
      the priests who ministered in the first sanctuary; for as by a veil the sanctuary was closed up to the
      people, so another veil kept the priests from the holy of holies.

         In commenting on Lev 16:1-19, MacLaren (EOHS) likewise asserts: “The Epistle to the Hebrews tells us
where we are to look for the realities of which these ceremonies were the foreshadowing. The veil was rent at the
crucifixion. Christ has gone into ‘the secret place of the Most High.’” Certainly, commentators should not be faulted
for making this frequent association. From this limited, earthly vantage point at which they write, the earthly Holy
of Holies was a secret place. Even so, was this Most Holy Place really a secret place in the mind of the writer of
Hebrews? If so, why does he not call it a secret place? Why do commentators find it necessary to put these words
into his mouth? More likely, the writer of Hebrews does not regard it to be a secret place in relation to its heavenly
counterpart, which is inhabited by “myriads of angels” and “the saints of righteous having been made perfect” (Heb
12:22-23; TM). God has His secrets, to be sure. Nevertheless, in the words of Isaiah, the writer of Hebrews, along
with other biblical writers, has left us clues regarding the treasures of darkness regarding the hidden wealth of
God’s secret places (Is 45:3). Historically, this hidden treasure would have been understood as secret treasure
concealed in subterranean vaults or rooms—not to the treasures themselves as being dark or evil. This particular
truth is hidden by Scripture in the Upper Room Chamber above the Holy of Holies. The God who dwells in
unapproachable light (1Tim 6:16) nonetheless makes earthly darkness His secret place (Ps 18:11). The darkness
of the windowless Upper Room Chamber above the Holy of Holies signifies its secrecy; its location signifies its
brilliancy.


        Eph 4:10
         As to the Pauline association with a dimension higher than the third heaven, various considerations have
been posed herein for concluding that Christ hyperspaced (cp. huperano) to the Father’s secret place in Eph 4:10.
Rather than being content with making the Heavenly City Jesus’ ultimate destination, as posed in MMP5, the present
treatment here in MMP6 further advances reasons for regarding the Father’s 7D throne as the terminus point. Jesus
passed through the Heavenly City on His way to the Father’s eternal realm. Of course, the one presupposes the
other. The Lord can be in multiple places and multiple dimensions at one time. When He sat at the Father’s right
hand in the 7D dimension, He did so in the 4D City and 3D City as well. Similar conclusions have been reached by
various other interpreters as noted herein. One unique proposal that I have made, not only herein but in MMP5,
is that the 3D City overlaps the 4D City to some extent. They are enjoined, but not equated, by the heavenly
temple typology. A unique speculation added herein is that just as the 3D City overlaps part of the 4D City,
the 4D City overlaps part of the 5D City. They from subsets (i.e., 3D ⊂ 4D ⊂ 5D). After all, the Upper Room
Chambers are part of the heavenly temple typology also.

To aid the imagination for conceptualization, I am using an extrapolation of my 3D and 4D illustrations to create a
hypothetical representation of the 5D City:

             •   a 3D Pyramid for the 3D City (a three-sided face),
             •   a 3D Cube for the 4D City (a four-sided face),
             •   a 3D Pentagon for the 5D City (a five-sided face).

Some interpreters believe that the Heavenly City is pyramidal (thus having a three-sided face); some believe that it
is cubical (thus having a four-sided face); some believe that it is both (since it is multidimensional). As explained
earlier in this series, I fall into the latter category, with the caveat that I prefer a truncated pyramid. Nevertheless,
the basic extrapolation would anticipate a five-sided face for the 5D City. These simplistic 3D representations for
the cube and pentagon are used because truly picturing a 4D cubical city, much less a 5D pentagonal city, may not
be feasible.86 On the other hand, the fact that some have given eyewitness testimony to the actual cubical shape of
the 4D City as seen during their NDE would suggest that to 4D beings, which would be the case of those in spiritual
bodies during their NDE, the City is truly cubical. In that case, it is possible that the 5D City might actually have
the shape pictured above when viewed from a 5D vantage point.
         In any case, a proposition added herein is that the heavenly places to which Paul refers may not be limited
to the 3D and 4D Heavenly City but include the 5D Heavenly City. The translations posed in MMP5 would still be
applicable, but now the explanation would be expanded to include the 5D City as well. A couple of examples will
suffice:

         •    “Which He brought about in Christ, when He raised Him from the dead, and seated Him at His right
             hand in the Heavenly City, hyperspaced above all rule and authority and power and dominion, and
             every name that is named, not only in this age, but also in the one to come.” (Eph 1:20-21; TM).
         •   “He who descended is Himself also He who hyperspaced above all the heavens, so that He might fill
             all things” (Eph 4:10; TM).

         The amazing thing is that Christ presumptively shares this lofty location with us (Eph 1:3; 2:6). The
realization of that proposition requires the conjecture of a 5D City. To some extent, the Bride must share a place
with Christ in this hyperspace (Eph 1:3; 2:6), which is located above all the heavens and thus above the lower
created heavens that are associated with angelic powers. Therefore, the supposition of a 5D New Jerusalem that is
totally new is proposed. Just as the earthly Jerusalem has a counterpart that is called New Jerusalem, which can
operate in our 3D space and hence can be called the 3D New Jerusalem, Christ hyperspacing above its 4D
counterpart and sharing this hyperspace with His Bride necessitates the proposition of a 5D City. She will sit in this
location and have bridal chambers prepared for Her in this location. Both of these biblical affirmations are spatial
in their nature. The Lord’s pattern of preparing a 3D City for the Bride to interface with a 4D City strongly suggests
a 5D City for the Bride. She will not hyperspace to empty space. The 3D Bridal City will be above Earth and provide
access to the 5D Bridal City above the 4D dimension.


        Bridal Threshold to 5D City
         One might even speculate that when the 3D Heavenly City is made new at the end of the millennial kingdom
and thereafter called New Jerusalem in association with the New Heaven and New Earth (Rev 21:1-2), this
renovation of the 3D City is the point in time in which it is given overlapping interior teleportal access to the 5D
City. Satan had access to the former 3D City, but he will not have access to the renovated 3D City nor to the newly
created/accessed 5D City. Perhaps the Lord will even delay the Bride being able to open this 5D wedding present
until the commencement of the eternal state when the renovation of the 3D City, as the doorway to the 5D City, is
complete. The 3D City would be the bridal threshold, so to speak, over which Jesus carries His Bride to the 5D
bridal chamber.
         When I shared a brief overview of this chapter for our Sunday School class, Doug Berner inquired about
the relationship of this proposal with the timing of the hidden ones in Is 26:19-21. This was one of the passages he
had discussed in his book, When God Intervenes, as an OT rapture passage. I had written an article giving a positive
assessment of his position in my article, Hidden Ones, though I did not deal with this passage. At the time I wrote
the article, I was more interested in posing a dualistic interpretation that would allow an application to both Israel
and the church-age Bride for the passages I was discussing at the time.87 One of the principal OT rapture texts from
Berner’s book which I discussed was Zeph 2:3: “Seek the Lord, all you humble of the earth who have carried out
His ordinances; seek righteousness, seek humility. Perhaps you will be hidden in the day of the Lord’s anger.” As
I noted in the article, the conditional nature of being hidden might be taken by advocates of a full rapture as being
a weakness in Berner’s argument. I countered that, although Berner and I hold to the full rapture position, his
argument was completely compatible with that position when enjoined with sound misthology. Only humble
believers who seek righteousness (i.e., faithful believers) will be hidden from God’s wrath. If unfaithful believers
are raptured at that time, as the full-rapture position postulates, they will be delivered from God’s eschatological
wrath (1Thess 5:9) but submitted to God’s Bema wrath (Rom 2:5-8; 12:17-19; Eph 5:6-7; 1Thess 4:6; Heb 3:11;
4:3; 10:26-39). I discuss such texts in “Misthological Wrath” in TOD. In that discussion, I interacted with an article
by Tanner, which can now be found online.88 Tanner correctly argues that Heb 10:26-31 warns believers that if they
commit apostasy, they face the danger of God’s punishment in the Day of the Lord (DOL), as described from the
context of Is 26:20:


       God was calling them (like Abraham) to a heavenly country, more specifically, to the New Jerusalem
      of Messiah’s kingdom in the “world to come” ([Heb] 2:5; 11:16; 12:22). There they would have a
      “lasting city” (13:14)….
               If they make the perilous step of abandoning their confession, there would be a two-fold loss
      for them. First, they would jeopardize the potential reward that God promised those who remained

      faithful ([Heb] 6:11-12)….Second, abandoning their confession would place them in position to

               …In the final verses of the chapter (vv 37-39), the author draws from the Septuagint
      rendering of Isa 26:20 and Hab 2:3-4 to remind them that their endurance should be focused on the
      Lord’s return—“for yet in a very little while, He who is coming will come and will not delay.” In so
      doing, the author has expressed a Messianic understanding of Hab 2:3. This is significant for the author
      of Hebrews, for the next verse of Habakkuk focuses on the “righteous one” who is expected to live by
      faith. In quoting from Habakkuk 2:4, however, the author of Hebrews has deliberately reversed the
      lines. He first cites Hab 2:4b and then 2:4a, apparently to accentuate the words “my righteous one.”…
               Thus, there are two alternatives available for God’s “righteous one”: (1) he may “live by faith,”
      i.e., walk with God by faith, an option that will be encouraged in chapter 11; or (2) he may “shrink
      back,” in which case, the Lord will not take delight in him, i.e., He will not be pleased with him. The
      crucial observation here is to note that the pronoun in the words “if he shrinks back” refers back to
      “the righteous one.” Yet, shrinking back must be avoided, for such a choice could result in
      “destruction,” undoubtedly a severe judgment akin to what the author had spoken of in 10:27,31…..
               ….Just as the wilderness generation greatly sinned when they rebelled against the LORD at
      Kadesh-Barnea (and were severely judged), so our author is concerned that his readers might sin on a
      par with their Old Testament counterparts….
               …Six of the final seven words from Heb 10:27 are found in the LXX translation of Isa
      26:11….
               The similarity of wording with the Septuagint translation of Isa 26:11 suggests that this
      is the source of his allusion. The context is appropriate with its contrast between the faithful and
      those who act wickedly. Of greater significance, however, is the eschatological setting in which this
      chapter occurs. Chapters 24-27 of Isaiah are a depiction of the coming judgment of God in the “Day
      of the Lord” which is followed by kingdom blessing….
      …. The opening of [Isaiah] chapter 26 clarifies that the initiation of the kingdom is a millennial event:
      “In that day this song will be sung in the land of Judah.” This also suggests that the call to rejoice in
      [Is] 25:9 primarily has in mind believing Jews in the aftermath of the Great Tribulation, which would
      make the “song” of [Is] 26:1ff. particularly relevant to Jewish readers and thus quite appropriate for
      the author of Hebrews.
               This “kingdom song” of the righteous is even more relevant to the Book of Hebrews when we
      observe that it begins with rejoicing that the righteous and faithful are given the privilege to enter the
      “strong city”—undoubtedly Jerusalem in this context ([Is] 24:23; 27:13). The song of Isa 26 begins by
      stating: “We have a strong city….Open the gates, that the righteous nation may enter, the one that
      remains faithful” ([Is] 26:1-2). The author of Hebrews held out the eschatological heavenly
      Jerusalem as the ultimate hope of New Covenant believers: “For here we do not have a lasting city,
      but we are seeking the city which is to come” ([Heb] 13:14; cf. 11:16; 12:22)….
      ….Then in Isa 26:11 (following the LXX text), the prophet declares, “O Lord, Your arm is raised
      high (i.e., posed to strike in judgment), but they do not see it. But when they perceive this, they shall
      be ashamed. The zeal (of God) shall seize this ignorant people, and then fire shall devour the
      adversaries.”
               In both the Hebrew text as well as the Septuagint translation, the prophet seems to have in
      mind the wicked among Israel who fall under God’s judgment at the time of the “day of the Lord.”…
      ….At the very least, however, we could conclude: if God does not withhold his awesome judgment
      against His own covenant people as depicted in Isaiah 24–27, there is no reason to think He would
      spare those who forsook the New Covenant. That is, if rebellious Jews of the Tribulation will
      certainly receive God’s judgment, so will those who rebel in the days when the author of Hebrews
      writes. The allusion to Isa 26:11 would be particularly meaningful to the readers who were of
      Jewish descent….
      ….Since God was not one who refrained from bringing severe judgment upon His people under the
      Old Covenant, there is no reason to think that He would do otherwise for those who had participated
      in the New Covenant….

      …. In this light, “the day” probably refers to the eschatological “day of the Lord” that Scripture
      often mentions. In the Old Testament, the “day of the Lord” was a time of judgment that God
      was bringing on the whole world, including Israel….
      …. Thus, the mention of “the day” in Heb 10:25 suggests that there may be a punishment in store for
      apostates in connection with “the day of the Lord.” Furthermore, the following context to Heb 10:26-
      31 points us to such a conclusion. In Heb 10:35-36, the author speaks about the time when rewards
      will be dispensed and about those who endure faithfully receive “the promise.” This is connected with
      the Second Coming, because in Heb 10:37 he states, “For yet in a very little while, He who is coming
      will come, and will not delay.” Although the Second Coming will be good news for most believers,
      there will be some for whom this will not be so. John, for instance, wrote, “And now, little children,
      abide in Him, so that when He appears, we may have confidence and not shrink away from Him in
      shame at His coming” (1 Jn 2:28; cf. 4:17). Thus, in Heb 10:38 (quoting the LXX of Hab 2:4), the
      author reminds us that God’s “righteous one” is to live by faith—an enduring faith that he is about to
      illustrate in chapter 11—and not “shrink back,” i.e., to cowardly turn away from the Lord. He ends the
      chapter by saying, “We, however, are not of those who cowardly turn back resulting in destruction,
      but we are of those who exhibit an enduring faith resulting in the preservation of soul” [my own
      translation]. There is no need to take “destruction” (ajpwleia) here as a reference to hell….
      …. The reference to the fact that the Lord will not be delayed in His “coming” (Heb 10:37) together
      with the idea that some who have done the will of God will be rewarded and receive “the promise”
      (10:35-36) may suggest that the “worse punishment” in store for the apostates is a negative experience
      at the judgment seat of Christ. The coming “day of the Lord” would not only mean the pouring out of
      the King’s wrath in the Tribulation, but also the time when believers have to appear before the
      judgment seat of Christ (2 Cor 5:9-10; Rom 14:10-12)….
      …. In reminding his readers of such judgment, the author drew upon Isaiah 26:11, a verse from a
      context describing God’s eschatological judgment upon the world, including His own covenant
      people. In that context, “fire” was used as a metaphor of God’s judgment upon the earth’s inhabitants
      prior to the establishment of the Messianic kingdom, not as a symbol of hell….
      …. The eschatological overtones of the chapter (including the reference to reward in verse 35) might
      suggest that the punishment will take place primarily at the judgment seat of Christ. The author may
      have in mind being denied the right to God’s “rest” (ch 4) as well as the right to share in the Son’s
      inheritance and exercise of dominion in the kingdom (“the world to come”). This, however, does not
      rule out that God may also inflict temporal punishment on the guilty one. (Italics his.)

        To summarize Tanner’s above discussion, the Writer of Hebrews (WH) is implementing Is 26:20 and its
context in his epistolary warnings to believers about the judgment from God that they can expect if they turn back
from following the Lord. Isaiah says that God’s fire on the DOL will destroy His enemies (Is 26:1), which WH
applies to regenerate believers if they become apostates. The Jews are God’s OT covenant people. Regenerate
church-age believers are God’s NT covenant people. Both God’s OT and NT covenant people will experience His
judgment at the commencement of the DOL. Per the joint warning of both biblical writers, we are warned that both
Jews and church-age believers will experience the fire of God’s judgment when the DOL commences. One
implication of the warning in Hebrews is that believers may jeopardize their access to the Reward City, thereby
missing out on the reward promised even under the OT covenant to God’s faithful believers. However, in addition
to jeopardizing their participation in this reward, these NT believers place themselves in danger of being subject to
God’s fiery wrath at the DOL. God’s NT covenant believers can either be faithful and hide in secret chambers
during the time of the DOL wrath (Is 26:20), apparently in the Heavenly City promised under the OT covenant, or
they can shrink back and be consumed by the fire of the DOL, which will consume God’s OT covenant people
temporally during the DOL (Is 26:11) and His NT covenant people misthologically at the Bema at the
commencement of the DOL (Heb 10:27).89
        As one can see, Tanner’s discussion applies Is 26:20 to church-age believers via the warning of Heb 10:26-
31. This OT text is one of the three OT texts that Berner cites as pertaining to the rapture (Is 26:19-21; 57:1; Zeph

2:3). Berner calls special attention to the fact that the first passage commences with a resurrection and then proceeds
with the injunction for God’s people to hide themselves during this period of eschatological wrath:

                Zephaniah and Isaiah refer to a future time when a group of believers (the meek and righteous)
       will be hidden supernaturally from the manifestations of God’s anger or fury [or wrath] which will be

       described as entering a condition or state of peace. These people who are taken away and hidden

          To be sure, Berner’s argument runs counter to prevalent dispensational thought. For instance, Pentecost
takes Is 26:19 as dealing with the resurrection of Jewish believers, but then he contends that although “the Old
Testament clearly taught the fact of the resurrection…no revelation was made concerning the time element
involved.”91 Actually, the time element is provided by the text and context for Is 26:19, as Berner has noted, and is
perhaps confirmed by WH. Isaiah, WH, Tanner, and Berner apply this wrath to believers at the commencement of
the DOL. So how can Pentecost reject the timing?
          Dispensationalists typically associate the Is 26:19-21 with the public return of the Lord and thus a
posttribulational resurrection of Jewish believers.92 Nevertheless, the timeframe is disputed by dispensationalists.
Some dispensationalists, following Darby, place the resurrection of these Jewish believers at the beginning of the
Tribulation. Stanton insists that Darby and his associates are wrong and counters that this resurrection of Jewish
believers occurs at the end of the Tribulation.93 Stanton does so in order to refute the claim of posttribulationists,
who argue that Darbyists place the resurrection of the Church as occurring at the same time as that of Israel, which
is after the Tribulation according to the OT, so that posttribulationists erroneously place the rapture at the end of
the Tribulation. Yet this posttribulational timing is counter to the sequence presented in Is 26:19-21, and Stanton’s
timing is not needed to refute posttribulationists. As to identifying those raised in Is 26:19 as Jewish believers,
Smith (NAC) makes this common assumption: “Since the dead who will live are identified as ‘your dead’ and ‘a
corpse’ in 26:19, the dead must refer to Israelites. If 26:20 is an exhortation to ‘my people’ then ‘your’ dead must
refer to Hebrews.” Not necessarily. This presumption is not shared by all interpreters and is indeed presumptuous.
Oswalt (NICOT) gives a much more thorough and plausible explanation:

               The task of determining the speaker throughout the verse is not easy, particularly in view of
       the change in persons in the first line (your dead … my corpses). Presumably these words could have
       been said by God, the people, or the prophet. The people are the easiest to dismiss in the light of v. 20,
       where they are directly addressed. It appears that v. 19, like v. 20, is intended as a consolation to the
       people and an encouragement to their faith. The other two choices are more difficult to decide between.
       If God is the speaker, then he answers the questions and speaks the imperative to the “dwellers in the

         dust.” This is attractive and logically correct, since, as Young says, only God can command the dead
         to rise. At the same time, there is no indication of a change of speaker from the previous verses
         where God was being addressed. Thus, it seems that the prophet remains the speaker. In that
         case “your dead” could refer to God or to the people. If it is to God, it is reminiscent of Ps. 116:15,
         “Precious in the sight of the Lord is the death of his saints” (cf. also Ps. 97:10; Rev. 14:13). The
         dead are not abandoned, they still belong to God. This interpretation would be consistent with the
         MT “my corpses” (collective) in which the reference would be to the prophet speaking as a member of
         the nation. If the people are addressed in “your dead,” however, then “my corpses” makes no sense
         and needs to be emended to “their.”94

If the prophet is the speaker, as Oswalt believes to be the case, then your dead refers simply to God’s people.
Nonetheless, Oswalt claims that if God is speaking, “my corpses” necessarily refers “to the prophet speaking as a
member of the nation,” which would mean that the resurrection of the Jewish people is intended. However, Oswalt’s
claim is shortsighted. In his translational note, Watts (WBC) gives two options: “Wildberger (985) thinks MT
contains a gloss—a note by a reader who wants his own body included in the resurrection. But if the speaker is
God himself, the 1st-person suf. lays claim to all the corpses of the righteous as his [God’s] own.” This latter
understanding is superior in that if the prophet intended to include himself, our corpses would be more natural than
my corpses. As Gray (ICC) observes: “My corpses, i.e. the dead bodies of the writer’s fellow-countrymen…would
be unnatural.” Gray believes “Yahweh is still addressed: Yahweh’s dead are those who died loyal to Him, or even
were slain for His sake (cp. Ps 47:10 (9)).” God is referring to the dead which belong to Him as being the ones who
He raises at the time. This need not necessarily be OT Jewish believers.
          Granted, the reading of the KJV would be compatible with making it a reference to the prophet and the
Jewish people since it renders the expression as singular, to the effect my corpse (i.e., my dead body).
Notwithstanding, this type of translation runs counter to the text and the preponderance of translations, which render
it as a first, second, or third pronoun (my, your, their) with a plural number of bodies/corpses.

     •    Plural: my dead bodies (ASV, DBY, ERV, JPS), their dead bodies (BBE), my corpses (CJB), their bodies
          (CSB, ESV, NIB, NIV, NLT, RSV), my slain (DRA), their corpses (GWN, NAB, NAS, NAU, NRS), they
          that are in the tombs (LXE), your corpses (NET, NJB), corpses (TNK).
     •    Singular: my body (GNV), my dead body (KJV, NKJ, RWB, WEB, YLT).

Smith (NAC) cites an article explaining that the discrepancy is due to whether it is viewed as “a gentilic ‘as corpses’
rather than a noun with a first person suffix ‘my corpse.’” He and the article prefer the gentilic plural, as does the
majority of the translations. Blenkinsopp (AYB) says that the singular my corpse is “impossible” with the plural
form of rise which follows it; therefore, he renders it as their corpses [plural] will rise [plural]. TWBC likewise
believes that God is being addressed and that plural bodies are in view:

                 “Judah, the speaker here, refers to the dead saints (v. 19) both as Thy dead—speaking to
         God—and as my dead bodies (ASV). This is a most explicit OT prediction of the bodily resurrection
         of believers. 20,21. God’s comforting invitation to his people. They are to take refuge in him during
         the grim time of the Tribulation, when he will be punishing the unconverted. (Bold and italics his.)

UBSH similarly states: “Thy dead probably refers to God’s people who have died” (emphasis original). Skinner
(CBSC) acknowledges that God is being addressed, which would make the people being raised God’s people, but
Skinner then reads an assumption into this acknowledgement: “The words are addressed to God, with the exception
of an apostrophe to the buried Israelites in the middle of the verse….Thy dead shall live, my dead bodies (collect.
in Hebr.) shall arise. The dead saints are at once Jehovah’s dead and Israel’s” (emphasis his). Skinner assumes that
Israel’s dead are jointly intended. If one is taking a dispensational framework, this would be in harmony with
Darby’s dispensationalism. However, Skinner’s inference does not necessarily follow. According to v. 20, God will
rapture those people who are His at the time. Given the timing, this would be church-age believers (per standard

94
  John N. Oswalt, The Book of Isaiah, Chapters 1–39, The New International Commentary on the Old Testament
(Grand Rapids, MI: Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing Co., 1986), 486.

dispensationalism). Consequently, as to v. 19, one can simply state that the prophet is saying that God will
correspondingly resurrect those who are His at the time. That is, those raised will be the counterparts of those
raptured. Thus, one could either argue, based on v. 20, that only church-age believers are in view (in accord with
standard dispensationalism as to who is raptured). Otherwise, one could contend that OT and NT believers are both
in view (so Darby dispensationalism).
         19
            Your dead [oh, Lord—the prophet is speaking] will live; their corpses [or My dead bodies—with
         capital “M” if God is speaking at this point] will rise [at the time of the rapture at the beginning of the
         DOL]. You who lie in the dust, awake and shout for joy, for your dew is as the dew of the dawn, and
         the earth will give birth to the departed spirits. 20 Come, My people [God speaking, thus capital “M”],
         enter into your inner private chambers [cheder, in the 3D City], and close your doors behind you;
         hide for a little while [10 years until the end of the Tribulation], until indignation runs its course. 21
         For behold, the Lord is about to come out from His place to punish the inhabitants of the Earth for
         their iniquity [during the DOL] and the Earth will reveal her bloodshed and will no longer cover her
         slain. (Is 26:19-21; TM)

         The inner private chamber (cheder) is the bridegroom’s counterpart to the bride’s bridal chamber
(chuppah) in Joel 2:16: “Let the bridegroom come out of his inner private chamber and the bride out of her bridal
chamber.” In the context of Is 26:19-21, it is the hidden inner private chambers. Taken jointly with Ps 27:4-6,
Is 26:19-21 as describing the bridegroom’s private chamber where the Bride is hidden in the 3D City for the first
3½ years of the DOL during the Bema proceedings until she is transferred to her bridal chambers in the 5D City as
described by Ps 27:4-6, thus at the beginning of the Tribulation. In Jn 14:2-3, Jesus refers to both: the 3D City as
part of the preexisting Father’s house and the 5D City as the new addition He is leaving to prepare for the Bride.
          Berner believes that only church-age believers are in view because of the timing and mental/emotional
state of those being bidden to hide.95 They are not hiding in fear; rather, they are shouting for joy. The dead who
are raised and shout for joy during this time, while the fiery wrath of the Tribulation envelopes Earth, are numbered
with those bidden to hide. Their bodies will leave Earth while God’s wrath is poured out upon Earth. They will not
be running to hide in fear in some place on Earth during the middle of the Tribulation. Instead, they are shouting
for joy. Nor are they jumping for joy at the end of the Tribulation. Instead, they are hidden during that time of wrath.
We are left to deduce that their bodies are joining their departed spirits in a departure from Earth at the beginning
of the DOL.
         The remaining point of contention to be considered would be between those holding a full rapture versus
partial rapture. Those misthologists holding to a full rapture would limit this DOL judgment to the Bema for church-
age believers who are living at that time. Conversely, those misthologists holding to a partial rapture can pose a
joint temporal-misthological DOL fire for NT believers who are alive at that time. If unfaithful believers are not
raptured at the beginning of the DOL, many of them will be killed during the commencement of the DOL and face
misthological destruction at the Bema, while those who are not killed will have to face the temporal fires of the
DOL and potential destruction at the Bema. I point out this alternate possibility for the sake of my partial-rapture
friends who hope that the Lord will extend mercy to unfaithful believers by giving them a chance to prepare for the
Bema by enduring the DOL and thereby qualify for the Reward City. Being left behind to live through the
Tribulation and have a second chance to qualify for the Reward City would be an example of judgment tempered
with mercy.
         In any event, the above analysis allows me to concur with Berner that Is 26:20 pertains to the rapture. Per
my preexisting models, the Bride will be judged at the Bema immediately after the rapture, possibly until the start
of the Tribulation.96 This Bema judgment will take place at the Bema stadium atop the 3D City, which will be
cloaked above Earth and thus can be well described as hidden chambers. The new postulation being posed herein
is that sometime after the conclusion of the Bema, the Bride will be given access to a higher 5D bridal chamber,
which can be likened to being carried across the threshold. For those already raptured and judged, this could take
place at the beginning of the Tribulation immediately following the Bema. For those who will be judged at the end
of the Tribulation, this could be at the beginning of the Millennial state. Per my estimation, the 6D Bride would
then be complete.
