         My hypothesis is that believers who are born during the millennial kingdom or eternal state cannot qualify
for a 6D transformation or the 5D bridal suite, though they can become 4D rulers. Nevertheless, I would entertain
the theoretical possibility that the Lord might delay access to the 5D realm by 6D rulers until the end of the
millennial kingdom and have them rest in the 3D City until that point as a foretaste of that 3D-to-6D spatial
transition. More likely, the wedding will be consummated in the 5D City at the beginning of the Tribulation by
those believers who are qualified to enter it. Those believers who are not judged until the end of the Tribulation
would not make their transition to the 5D City until that point.


        Conclusion
         At the beginning of this chapter questions were posed as to why there is a mismatch between the dimensions
of the Tabernacle and those of the Temple. Why is the Holy Place of Temple taller than its Holy of Holies? The
answer posed herein is that it was to make a new room atop the Holy of Holies, a hidden upper chamber. The
architectural relationship provides a clue as to the typological significance: A new addition was to be added to the
Heavenly Temple that would be dimensionally superior to the heavenly Holy of Holies and thus in a higher
dimension than the 4D Heavenly City. Additionally, the side chambers that wrap around the sides of the Temple
could signify the slopes and levels of the 3D City, which is reputed to be divided into three primary sections. The
relative position of the 3D City would thus be lower than that of the 4D City. Steps would lead from the relative
position of the 3D City up into the 4D City.
                          Illustration 39. Tabernacle and Temple Mismatch Explained




         Various proposals regarding the location of the upper chamber(s) of Solomon’s Temple were evaluated,
with a location above the Holy of Holies being superior. The only real question regarding preferred interpretations
concerns its height. Is it 10 cubits high or only 5 cubits high? If the latter, then the Holy of Holies would have been
on a 5 cubic high platform and had steps leading up to it. Both options are popular in online depictions of the temple.
Either option works for the thesis being advanced within, and both options were demonstrated as being compatible
with the hypothesis that the outer three-tiered chamber represents the three-tiered 3D City. Although MMP5
presumed the version without steps, the greater precision being pursued in MMP6 regarding the Upper Room
Chamber finds the stepped version more probable and has adopted that as the working model herein. These steps,
Chapter 3. Secret Upper Temple Chambers                                                                     Page 105
along with the upper chamber(s), are details that were added in the transition from the tabernacle to the temple.
David passed on the plans for the temple to Solomon.
         The plausibility of linking the upper room chamber(s) with the secret place of the Most High was then
investigated. While one would be hard pressed to insist that this was the exclusive association intended by the
biblical text, one would also be hard pressed to prove that was not the inclusive association intended by the text.
The desire for a secret place in God’s protective abode is expressed in various forms in Scripture and gave rise to a
desire to have a secret place in God’s tabernacle (e.g., Ps 27:5), and thus eventually in God’s temple. Apparently,
the temple plans passed on to Solomon gave a spatial expression to the secret place in a location superior to the
Holy of Holies, namely, in the upper chamber(s).
         This proposition accords well with the OT perception that God has an eternal transcendent location that
exists above the heavenly realities pictured by the Holy Place and the Most Holy Place. The first plural possessive
of our in Jer 17:12 was examined in light of this plausibility. Moving to the NT, further collaboration was provided
for affirming Jesus’ eternal preexistence in a spatial location superior to that of the angels and for concluding that
He did not surrender His transcendence when He took on human form to accomplish incarnate immanence. A
hypothesis was proposed to address the puzzling nature of the empty space as being either (1) singular (according
to the popular illustrations) or (2) plural (according to the actual biblical text and commentators). Regardless, the
Father’s transcendent secret place is shared with faithful believers via their bridal intimacy with the Son. The
Johannine plurality of the bridal chambers as part of the Father’s house that Jesus promised while in the Upper
Room accords well with the plurality of the upper chambers built in the secret place in the temple above the Holy
of Holies. The singular 5D Cities has plural dwellings—accommodations for all overcomers. Other biblical data
from the writer of Hebrews and Paul confirms that Christ hyperspaced through the lower heavenly dimensions to
pass to a dimension superior to the angels and that He shares this space with us, thereby giving exegetical
collaboration to the logical extrapolation for a 5D City proposed in MMP5.

Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                Page 106
and thus larger than the 3D City, it is pictured as smaller in typological relation because its secret hidden nature
necessarily means that very little is known about it. Also, although it is probably more spacious than the other two
Cities, the other two Cities will be used to entertain guests. The 5D City, in contrast, will be reserved exclusively
for the Bride. It will be truly spacious indeed for Her needs.

            Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple
        Introduction
         While the previous chapter affirmed the misthological nature of the Heavenly Temple, the focus was on its
transcendent nature, so as to pose a 5D Transcendent Temple City. Conversely, the present chapter will focus on
the misthological nature of this City within the parameters of the transcendence provided by the previous chapter.
A full-fledged defense of the misthological nature of the City is not necessary since my writings have supplied
numerous arguments to that effect. Even so, in a misthological series, defending the misthological nature of the text
is to be expected. Given the focus of the present book, this misthological affirmation will be limited to temple texts.


        Spatial Affirmations
                                                       Bryan
         To begin with, however, recapping some of the spatial implications of the primary texts regarding this
misthological 5D Transcendent Temple City is in order. A section of Bryan’s article was quoted in the previous
chapter. His whole article is excellent. His first section is entitled: “The Temple as the Eschatological Dwelling
Place of the Righteous in Jewish Literature.” The repentant remnant is to be regathered (Dt 30:4) and given
eschatological access to the Lord’s house (hoikos) on Mount Zion (Is 2:2), which would be the eschatological
temple: God’s dwelling place, His sanctuary (Ex 15:17). In conjunction with the reference to the place (topos) God
will appoint for Israel in 2Sam 7:10, this is called God’s holy place (hagios topos) in 2Macc 2:17-18. Other texts,
such as 1En 39:4 and 71:16, confirm that the righteous will have their dwelling places in this heavenly Temple. In
his next section, “Messiah as Builder of the Eschatological Temple,” he briefly surmises a position defended in his
book, Jesus and Israel’s Traditions of Judgement and Restoration. The Messiah was expected to be the one who
would build this Temple. See, for example, Zech 6:12-13. In his third section, “Messiah and Temple in John 14,”
part of which was quoted previously at length, Bryan poses that the place prepared by Jesus and the Father’s house
would both be terms associated with this Temple in both the OT and in intertestamental material. The LXX
rendering of Ex 15:17—referring to the Lord’s Prepared Dwelling Place (hetoimos katoiketerion) as the sanctuary
/ holy place (hagiasma) which the Lord prepared (katartizo)—gave rise to the intertestamental belief that the
eschatological Temple (Prepared Place) would not be made by human hands. This background makes it likely that
Jesus was alluding to this eschatological expectation of the Prepared Place (hetoimazo topos) in Jn 14:2-3.
         Bryan concedes that Jesus identifying the temple with His body in Jn 2:19-21 has probably been the greatest
deterrent to commentators considering other possibilities, such as what he poses. He notes that McCaffrey sought
to bridge the gap by posing both associations, though Bryan found doing so unwarranted. I share Bryan’s assessment
of McCaffrey’s duality in this regard. McCaffrey posed the spatial understanding of the eschatological Temple as
being the level one meaning, and then a spiritual/mystical union and communion with the risen Christ was posed as
the deeper secondary meaning. Like Bryan, I found this argument unconvincing. Notwithstanding, McCaffrey’s
arguments for Johannine duality are otherwise very good and probably contain a level of truth that carries over to
Jn 14:2-3. I would pose that the text is intended to convey at least two spatial levels. The level one spatial
interpretation in my assessment is that this Johannine gospel text refers to the Johannine apocalyptic vision of New
Jerusalem. The level two spatial interpretation is that it refers to the Father’s Transcendent Temple at a higher level.
Accordingly, much of McCaffrey’s discussion can be read profitably in support of this refitted explanation.

                                                   McCaffrey
        McCaffrey’s first chapter is devoted primarily toward an exegesis of Jn 14:2-3 to demonstrate the
reasonableness of a spatial understanding of the text as being its first level of meaning. In Jn 14:2, Jesus tells His
disciples that He is going to go and prepare a place for them at My Father’s house (oikia). Previously, in Jn 2:16,
Jesus referred to the earthly temple in Jerusalem as, My Father’s house (oikos). Surely, John does not intend this to
be coincidental. McCaffrey’s level-one assessment is on target:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 110
      Moreover, the important and striking verbal link seems to indicate rather that the evangelist designedly
      repeats the phrase in 14:2–3 in order to recall the first reference to it in 2:16 (comp. 2:17); and so
      deliberately describes the goal of Jesus’ journey in order to evoke the idea of the Jerusalem temple
      with the phrase “in my Father’s house”. This is all the more likely against the OT and Jewish
      background where the Jerusalem temple is “the house of God” par excellence. But Thomas confesses
      ignorance about where this Father’s house is located in space (14:5). No such ignorance would be
      possible with reference to the Jerusalem temple alone.97

From Jn 13:1,33; 16:28, 17:1, 11-13, McCaffrey makes a reasonable deduction: “The Father’s house might also be
understood with reference to the heavenly temple….The Father’s house, then, where Jesus journeys in 14:2–3, may
be rightly understood in accord with Johannine perspectives as a reference to “heaven” (οὐρανός), the transcendent
sphere of God, where the Father of Jesus dwells” (p. 31).
         As for the many dwelling places (monai is the plural of mone) in Jn 14:2 already existing in the Father’s
house, Jesus uses a “present tense (εἰσιν). Here and now they [these monai] exist permanently. The preposition ‘in’
(ἐν) should be taken in a simple local [spatial] sense. It indicates the place where the many possibilities for dwelling
exist permanently for the disciples, namely, in the Jerusalem temple, or in the heavenly temple where God, the
Father of Jesus, dwells” (p. 35).
         As for the place (topos) that Jesus goes to prepare in Jn 14:2, McCaffrey notes that this term could be
referring to the temple, as is the case in Jn 4:20 and 11:48, and as it is so taken by some interpreters. However, these
two occurrences are articular, referring to the place, that is, the temple. Jesus uses an anarthrous form in Jn 14:2.
He goes to prepare a place. “As such it is open to interpretation with reference to any specific τόπος whatever, like
a ‘sanctuary’ or ‘temple’” (pp. 37–38). Unfortunately, McCaffrey does not address the possibility that Jesus is going
away like a bridegroom to add a new addition onto the Father’s preexisting house in preparation to take His Bride
to His Father’s house. Other interpreters can be consulted for this proposition, however. My hypothesis being
entertained herein is that they and McCaffrey are both correct. The Father’s house, the Heavenly City as
described in Scripture, already has many dwelling places. Jesus will prepare mansions for His followers
there. However, in addition, Jesus will also create a new place as a spatial addition, a secret place to be added
to the Father’s Heavenly Temple. This new creation/location will be the 5D Transcendent Temple City. It
will be added as an additional spatial location above the preexisting 4D Heavenly City (and accessed via the
3D Heavenly City). The tenses of the verbs suggest this to be the case: A new place is being added to a preexisting,
multi-dwelling house. Also, the Jewish bridal tradition lends further support.
         Further confirmation of this addition is confirmed by the present tense of Jesus' statement: where I am
(Jn 14:3). As McCaffrey explains, “Jesus is already where he promises to take the disciples later (παραλήμψομαι
ἵνα … καὶ ὑμεῖς). The verb εἰμί must be given its full value as a present (εἰσιν, 14:2a)” (p. 44). McCaffrey makes a
partially astute observation at this point:

      He belongs at all times to the heavenly and transcendent sphere of God. How, then, can Jesus already
      belong to the heavenly sphere of God and at the same time belong to the sphere of men? There does
      not seem to be any adequate solution to this apparent conflict of perspectives in the text—at least at
      the first level of meaning.
                However, the use of the phrase ὅπου εἰμὶ ἐγώ in 14:2–3 is also an indirect reference to the
      divine name (comp. 7:34, 36; 17:24). The use of εἰμί is open to a much deeper level of meaning. It
      may indicate the present of the divine mode of being which transcends the human mode of being-in-
      the-world (cf. 1:1). This present εἰμί of the phrase ὅπου εἰμὶ ἑγώ is a-temporal. (P. 45.)

        McCaffrey is correct in his opening statement highlighted above. He is incorrect in assuming that there is
no spatial solution. To the contrary, as various exegetes would confirm, John repeatedly affirms that Christ Jesus
was simultaneously in Heaven with the Father while incarnate on Earth with men. Moreover, as argued in MMP5,
this heavenly location is not a-temporal; it is supertemporal in relation to our time but ultratemporal in relation to
God’s time, God’s ultraspace, His 7D spacetime continuum.


97
  James McCaffrey, The House with Many Rooms: The Temple Theme of Jn. 14:2–3, vol. 114, Analecta Biblica
(Roma: Pontifical Biblical Institute, 1988), 30.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                              Page 111
         McCaffrey’s second chapter is devoted to lexical and thematic studies. As to house being rendered by oikia
versus oikos, the LXX does not necessarily distinguish them in all cases. Oikos is its preferred term and “used in a
metaphorical sense to designate the house of God: his temple,” so that the phrase house (oikos) of God “becomes a
fixed term for the sanctuary or temple. The phrase also designates the eschatological temple” (p. 49). A few TM
examples from the LXX should suffice to illustrate this point:

     1.    “Treasures of the house (oikos) of the Lord…the temple (naos; Heb: hekal) of the Lord” (2Kgs 24:13).
     2.    “I give to the house (oikos) of my God…the holy temple/house (oikos)” (1Chron 29:3).
     3.    “The first temple/house (oikos)…the foundation of this temple/house (oikos)” (Ezra 3:12).
     4.    “Concerning the house temple (oikos hieron) of God” (Ezra 6:3).
     5.    “Of the temple/house (oikos) of God…from the temple/house (oikos; Heb: hekal)” (Ezra 6:5).
     6.    “I will enter Your house (oikos)…I will worship at Your holy (naos; Heb: hekal)” (Ps 5:7).
     7.    “That I may dwell in the house (oikos) of the Lord…to meditate in His (naos; Heb: hekal)” (Ps 27:4).
     8.    “Your house (oikos), your holy (naos; Heb: hekal)” (Ps 65:4).
     9.    “House (oikos) of the Lord…(naos; Heb: hekal) of the Lord…(naos; Heb: hekal) of the Lord” (Eze 8:16).

        More texts could be added as well where house of God/Lord in Hebrew is equated with temple
(2Chron 29:16; 36:7; Ezra 5:14-15; 8:17; Neh 3:31; 6:10; Dan 5:3; Zeph 1:9; 8:9). The inner court is part of the
temple/house (oikos). Sometimes the temple is simply called the house of the Lord or the house (1Kgs 6:1ff).98
McCaffrey lumps references to Heaven being God’s habitation with reference to the Temple being His habitation
to conclude: “In the OT heaven is represented as God’s temple” (P. 54). This statement equates Heaven with His
Temple. A more carefully nuanced position would assert that the Lord does not merely live in Heaven (Dt 26:15;
Ps 2:4; Eccl 5:2; Is 63:15), He lives in a Temple in Heaven (2Sam 22:7; Ps 11:4; 18:6; Is 6:1; Hab 2:20; Jonah
2:7; Micah 1:2-3). Although Heaven cannot contain God (1Kgs 8:27; 2Chron 2:6; 6:18), He does localize His
presence in this Heavenly Temple. David aspired not merely to reach Heaven, but to dwell in the presence of the
Lord in His house, His Heavenly Temple (Ps 23:6; 27:4). Nevertheless, McCaffrey asserts:

          However, it is only in the Book of Enoch that the heavenly temple becomes designated explicitly as
          the goal of eschatological bliss. Here we have the fullest description of the heavenly temple in Jewish
          literature. It is a magnificent crystal building, and contains an inner house (i.e. holy of holies) in which
          God is enthroned in great majesty:

                  “In every respect it so excelled in splendour and magnificence and extent that I cannot
                  describe to you its splendour and its extent. And its floor was of fire, and above it were
                  lightings and the path of the stars, and its ceiling also was flaming fire. And I looked and
                  saw therein a lofty throne: its appearance was a crystal … And the Great Glory sat
                  thereon, and His raiment shone more brightly than the sun and was whiter than any snow”
                  (1 En 14,16–18. 20).

                  The cult in this heavenly temple is also described. The author tells how Levi travels through
          the heavens to the “highest of them all” where he sees the temple and its priesthood and cult:

          “For in the Highest of all dwelleth the Great Glory, in the holy of holies, far above all holiness. And
          in (the heaven next to) it are the angels of the presence of the Lord, who minister (λειτουργοῦντες) and
          make propitiation (ἐξιλασκόμενοι) to the Lord for all the sins of ignorance of the righteous. And they
          offer to the Lord a sweet-smelling savour, a reasonable and bloodless offering (λογικὴν καὶ ἀναίμακτον
          προσφοράν)” (1 En 3,4–6; comp. 5,1–2)….
                    This symbolism of Enoch can best be explained as a final culmination of the OT
          tradition.…Ideas traditionally associated with the OT explanation of eschatological happiness and the
          eschatological temple are now predicted of the heavenly temple. (Pp. 60–62.)
98
  My more generic English search in the NAS counted the expression house of God or house of the Lord occurring
318 times in 285 verses. In other verses, it is referred to simply as My house or just house. The association of the
house of the Lord/God with the Temple is therefore firmly established by hundreds of OT verses.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 112

McCaffrey erroneously cites 1En 3:4-6 for the expression containing in heaven next to. This text is actually from
the Testament of Levi, which purportedly describes seven levels of heaven, which I am not using to defend my thesis
that the Lord’s Transcendent Temple could be in a higher realm, adjacent to the angelic realm. I would also articulate
some of McCaffrey’s assertions differently. For instance, he notes the upper rooms of the temple plans that David
gave to Solomon, but he seems content to regard them as rooms in the temple (p. 67). Biblical and extrabiblical
material concur in depicting God as living in a heavenly house, that is, a holy temple, but I would add the supposition
of a secret realm superior to that of the angels. In any event, McCaffrey comments regarding the Heavenly Temple
itself are on target:

              The Heavenly Temple. In the OT the heavenly temple is inhabited by immortal beings. It is
      the dwelling-place of Yahweh’s innumerable court (1 Kgs 22:19f; Jb 1:6–12; 2:1–7; Is 6:1f; Ze 3:1–
      7; Dn 7:9f). There the just will abide with God (Wis 3:9). They will share the lot of the saints and be
      numbered among the angels (Wis 5:5). (Emphasis his, 73.)

         Concerning the last passage he cites, Wis 5:5, Winston (AYB) similarly states: “We have here a conception
similar to that found in Daniel, I Enoch, and the Qumran scrolls. The just, it is believed, can be elevated to the
heavenly sphere to join the angelic host (Dan 12:2–3; I Enoch 104:2, 6; Similitudes of Enoch 39:5; 1QH 3:19–23;
II Bar 51:5–13).” Concerning the passage in 1En 104:2-6, Nickelsburg (HERM) states: “The three exhortations
around which this section is organized (vv 2, 4, 6) assure the righteous of their coming heavenly bliss, moving
climactically from simile to outright statement: ‘you will shine like the luminaries of heaven; you will have great
joy like the angels of heaven; you will be companions of the hosts of heaven’” (emphasis his.) Such extrabiblical
texts support my biblical analysis in concluding that righteous believers are the ones who will live in the Heavenly
Temple City with God and be surrounded by and ministered to by holy angels. Even so, the present book seeks to
move beyond that previously established misthological conclusion to consider a spatial sphere superior to the
angels. McCaffrey pulls back from considering such advanced propositions, being content to maintain a rather
traditional vantage point:

              Finally, in the Jewish tradition the heavenly temple is peopled with immortal beings. However,
      Jn 14:2–3 must be seen to abstract entirely from this aspect of the tradition. We cannot determine from
      our text whether or not the μοναί in the Father’s house are inhabited or not by celestial beings, or
      angels, prior to the preparation, or after it either. We only know that prior to the preparation, Jesus
      abides in the Father’s house permanently, and after the preparation it will be peopled by the disciples.
      The text says no more. We must respect this economy of the text. (P. 75.)

Actually, the text does say more. McCaffrey just did not follow through on the full implications of his own verbal
analysis regarding Jesus’ statement: Where I am. As it is, he proceeds to defend a double meaning of oikia:

               At this second level of meaning there is no doubt that the Father’s house refers to the heavenly
      temple, not the Jerusalem temple; for Jesus travels there through his passion-death (πορεύομαι). If this
      is so, then it is hardly surprising that the term οἰκία is used instead of the more common LXX term for
      the Jerusalem temple οἶκος. (P. 177.)

              Finally, it should be noted also that had John used the term οἶκος in Jn 14:2a the reference to
      the Father’s house would much more readily have been understood of the Jerusalem temple only. All
      the more so with the definite article. The only other use of the phrase “the house of my Father” with
      the term οἶκος (and the definite article) in the fourth gospel to designate a specific place is with
      reference to the Jerusalem temple (Jn 2:16–17). (P. 179.)

         To be sure, at least a double meaning is intended by Jesus’ use of oikia rather than oikos for house in Jn 14:2.
McCaffrey goes on to argue at length for a deeper spiritual meaning in terms of union/communion. Yet he misses
the full import of his own argument. In reality, the deeper union/communion pertains to bridal misthological
communion through union in a higher spatial dimension of the Heavenly Temple.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                             Page 113
         Earlier, McCaffrey had rightly noted: “It is this union of Jn 14:23 in the believer which initiates in time the
whole unifying process of Jn 14:2–3 in anticipation of the final union in the heavenly temple of the Father’s house
at the end of time” (p. 176). Note, however, that this union is not freely given but is a reward contingent on the
believer’s loving obedience: “Jesus answered and said to him, “If anyone loves Me, he will keep My word; and
My Father will love him, and We will come to him, and make Our abode [home; mone] with him” (Jn 14:23).
Therefore, the immediate context, per McCaffrey’s own analysis, makes it clear that misthological issues are at
stake. This temporal mone (abode/home) is the necessary precursor to the eschatological oikia (house). Being at
home with God in this context is conditioned on a believer’s obedience to God. Those believers who want to be
welcomed to God’s Temple Home in the future must be at home with God in the present. Present fellowship with
God in the home of one’s heart is required for future fellowship with God in the home of His Temple.
         McCaffrey’s discussion is excellent in this regard when he discusses the similar link between Jn 14:2-3 and
Jn 12:26: “The goal of discipleship is described in almost identical terms in Jn 14:2–3 and Jn 12:26b” (p. 196). “The
servant of Jesus must follow him and he will attain in the future to the goal where Jesus is at present. There will be
some kind of identification between the disciple and Jesus in the future” (p. 199). McCaffrey fails to make the bridal
connection. However, other interpreters have perceived the bridal imagery of Jn 14:2-3, and they may be consulted
for this insight. Nevertheless, the importance of McCaffrey’s insight, though limited, is not to be overlooked. He
immediately proceeds to add:

                    The present εἰμί is not the equivalent of a future “shall be”. The full force of the present εἰμί
          is to be retained in contrast to the future ἔσται. The goal is already in some way attained by Jesus, even
          at present (εἰμί), but access to Jesus there, or identification with him, is only possible for his follower
          in the future (ἔσται). No reason is given for this. (P. 199.)

In contrast to some interpreters, he correctly insists upon the present implications of the present tense in these
Where-I-AM statements:

      •    “If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am, there shall My servant also be; if anyone
           serves Me, the Father will honor him.” (Jn 12:26).
      •    “And if I go and prepare a place for you, I will come again, and receive you to Myself; that where I am,
           there you may be also” (Jn 14:3).

         Granted, Jesus does not provide an explicit explanation for the present tense or why present obedience is
required for the believer to reach this location where Jesus already presently abides. Still, the reasons are not
incomprehensible. They are discernable by breadcrumb trails that John has left for us from other such statements in
his gospel, as covered in the previous chapter.99 These two texts compose the heart of similar such passages that
confirm that Jesus was simultaneously in Transcendent Heaven while on Earth. Other interpreters have also made
this deduction, and thus their contribution should be added to the discussion.
         Like most interpreters, unfortunately, McCaffrey fails to differentiate soteriological and misthological
implications. For instance, he says, “The possibility of access to Jesus is opened up to all men by the ‘exaltation’ of
Jesus in Jn 12:32. But the promise of actual access to Jesus is given only to the follower of Jesus in Jn 12:26b”
(p. 199). Such is the fallacy of Lordship salvation, conditioning soteriological benefits on (1) believing in Jesus and
(2) on following Jesus in ongoing discipleship. Still, FG misthologists are more astute at making this delineation,
so adding their contribution to the discussion is critical. Entrance into Heaven is a free gift offered to all those who
believe in Jesus for eternal life. Access to the more restricted places and experiences in Heaven are rewards offered
to all believers who follow Jesus in ongoing discipleship. In principle, this distinction is easy to make. In exegesis,
making this differentiation consistently is admittedly not always easy. For instance, the FG interpreter encounters
a significant challenge for delineation in Jn 17:24, which McCaffrey proceeds to discuss:

          He prays that his disciples may rejoin him one day (unspecified) after his departure from this world in
          order to be with him in the eternal dwelling-place, or temple of his Father, in heaven. For the moment
          this heavenly sphere is unattainable by the disciples (comp. Jn 13:33).

99
     See Jn 1:1; 3:13,31 (p. 82) and Jn 7:34,36; 12:26; 14:3; 17:24 (p. 82).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 114
               This heavenly sphere of divine existence is where the Father of Jesus dwells in contrast to the
      human sphere of existence in-the-world which Jesus is leaving, and where the disciples must continue
      to dwell (Jn 17:11b; comp. Jn 17:15a)….The opening gesture of the prayer also designates heaven as
      the dwelling-place of the Father. So Jesus prays for all future believers to be there with him in the
      eternal temple. This is where Jesus himself abides permanently.
               But the implications of the phrase ὅπου εἰμὶ ἐγώ of Jn 17:24b may also be determined still
      further. The ἐγώ ωἰμι of the phrase indicates the present of the divine mode of existence-in-God which
      transcends the human mode of existence-in-the-world, the eternal being-in-God as distinct from the
      temporal being-in-the-world. This heavenly a-temporal mode of existence is specified further in the
      immediate context as an in-being of Jesus in the Father….
               ….Moreover, our understanding of κἀκεῖνοι in Jn 17:24c with reference to all future believers
      supports our extension of the meaning of the ὑμεῖς of Jn 14:3c beyond the disciples (directly and
      explicitly designated) to all future believers represented by them….
               ….The promise of Jn 14:3c is addressed directly (and explicitly) to the disciples only, but the
      prayer of Jn 14:24bc is for all believers (including the disciples). (Pp. 208-211)

So, does Jesus’ prayer guarantee that all believers will be in this Where-I-AM place even if they do not follow Him
in discipleship? Answering this question is a considerable challenge facing FG misthologists. In preparation for
such an answer, the misthological implications of the primary passages surveyed above in McCaffrey’s analysis
will be provided in the next section.


        Misthological Affirmations
                                                     Jn 12:26
         This misthological nature of Jn 12:26 is readily apparent and frequently acknowledged. Dillow cites it in
conjunction with Jn 12:25 as dealing with rewards: “He who loves his life loses it; and he who hates his life in this
world shall keep it to life eternal. If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am, there shall My servant
also be; if anyone serves Me, the Father will honor him” (Jn 12:25-26). Dillow’s focus is on the misthological nature
of eternal life in v. 25:

      When eternal life is presented as an acquisition in the present (19 times, 44%), faith alone is the only
      condition mentioned. The fact that one third of the time (35%) the acquisition of eternal life [is]
      conditioned on works surely signifies that the two different aspects of eternal life are in view: its initial
      acquisition by faith alone, and an enhanced experience of eternal life subsequent to its initial
      acquisition conditioned on faith plus obedience. The former relates to gaining life and the latter living
      it, leading to an abundant inheritance in the future.100

In a section he entitles, “Kinds of Rewards,” Stegall mentions the praise/honor that a believer may receive in v. 26
as being one of these rewards. I will quote his entire section below to call attention to another one of Stegall’s
inconsistencies.

                                              KINDS OF REWARDS
              If a believer’s works are determined to be truly “good” by the Lord at the Bema, what kind of
      reward can he expect? Rewards in Scripture can be viewed broadly in two categories—the crown
      rewards and the non-crown rewards. A great variety of rewards will be awaiting faithful believers at
      the Judgment Seat of Christ. These will come in the form of praise from God (Matt. 25:21, 23; 1 Cor.
      4:5; 1 Peter 1:7), honor (John 12:26; 1 Peter 1:7), and glory (Matt. 13:43; Rom. 8:18; 2 Cor. 4:17;
      1 Peter 1:7; 5:4). God also promises believers an abundant entrance into Christ’s kingdom (2 Peter
      1:5-11) if they have added to their faith a godly character and good works. In addition, believers who
      were poor on earth but rich in good works will have great treasure in store for them in Heaven (Matt.

100
  Joseph Dillow, Final Destiny: The Future Reign of The Servant Kings, 20th edition (Monument, CO: Paniym
Group, 2014), p. 219.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 115
      6:19-21; Heb. 10:34-35) and a great inheritance with Christ (Matt. 5:5; Heb. 6:12; 11:8-9; 12:17). If
      this were not enough, faithful believers can also expect to receive from Christ diverse positions of
      privileged service and rulership in the Kingdom (Matt. 19:28; 25:21, 23; Luke 19:17, 19; 1 Cor. 6:2-
      3; 2 Tim. 2:12; Rev. 2:26-27; 20:4, 6; 22:3, 5). Unger describes these assigned positions of service,
      saying, “It seems apparent that these assignments will not only embrace executive and administrative
      offices, ruling and reigning, but judicial duties as well.” In conjunction with Christ’s reclaiming from
      Satan dominion over the world (Rev. 5), the Lamb’s wife or bride, the Church, is seen in Revelation
      19:7-9 dressed magnificently and ready to return with Him (Rev. 19:11-21) to reign in His kingdom
      as co-ruler and queen. (SCFOD, italics his, 219-220.)

If Stegall were consistent, which he is not, he would acknowledge that being a member of the Bride, thus being
dressed and white and ruling with Christ as such, is a reward limited to faithful believers who are rich in good works
and have added a godly character to their faith. From his own analysis, these are the conditions noted in red for such
rewards highlighted in yellow. However, he erroneously believes that the Bride is composed of all believers. His
statement here refutes that erroneous assumption that he and his coauthors repeatedly make in SCFOD. For the
moment, however, it suffices to note that Stegall properly cites Jn 12:26 as a Where-I-AM verse that is dealing with
rewards but that he fails to acknowledge that being with Jesus in this location is a reward. In this verse, Jesus says:

             If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am, there shall My servant also be;
                                 if anyone serves Me, the Father will honor him.

         Two rewards are promised in the verse: (1) being with Jesus in this Where-I-AM location, and (2) being
honored by the Father. Both rewards are contingent on serving Jesus. FG writers frequently acknowledge the honor
as a reward but fail to note that the where-there (hopou, ekei) location is also a reward in this verse, even though
Jesus uses two words to underscore the spatial nature of this reward.101 Not all commentators on Jn 12:26 are so
obtuse, though, regarding its misthological spatial nature. In fact, Michaels (NICNT) commendably says that both
parts of the verse are fused together to form a singular reward:

               For now, the accent is rather on the reward of true discipleship and the logical outcome of
      “following” him, that is, as he says, “where I am, there my servant will be.” This is understood as an
      “honor,” for as Jesus rephrases it, “If anyone would serve me, the Father will honor him.” Jesus has
      already told the Pharisees that “where I am you cannot come” (7:34), or “Where I go you cannot come”
      (8:21), and even his own disciples (including Martha and Mary) that “me you do not always have”
      (12:8; compare 13:33). Yet now he signals that this will not be the case forever as far as his true
      “servants” are concerned. Wherever he is going, if they “follow” him faithfully, even, if need be, to
      martyrdom, their place with him is assured.

         The reward of being with Jesus in this Where-I-AM place is the specific honor that Jesus is promising in
this passage and other such passages, and it will be granted by the Father. Wahlde (ECC) confirms the misthological
nature of this location: “Verse 26b (‘Where I am, there also my servant will be) could refer to the ‘place’ where
suffering and death take place, but it is more likely that the reference is broader and refers both to the place of
suffering and to the place of reward which the believer will share with Jesus.” I will term “the place of reward”
as the Reward Place. Borchert (NAC) calls attention to both the honor and location and then dovetails the latter into
the former:

      Following Jesus for the disciple may involve the ultimate cost of discipleship, namely the death of the
      disciple. Yet the disciple is promised the ultimate honor or reward of being “where I am” (hopou
      eimi egō). This statement is packed with Johannine meaning. Not only does one note the presence of
101
   Examples of this inconsistency among FG interpreters to cite honor as a reward but failing to include location
as a reward include the following: Evans, TEBC; Wilkin, GNTC; Seymour, “Difficult,” 36. Of course, FG
interpreters are not the only ones who note that honor in this verse is a reward while failing to note that location is
also a reward. Other examples would include Newman and Nida (UBSH) and Frederick Dale Bruner (GJ:C), The
Gospel of John: A Commentary (Grand Rapids, MI; Cambridge, U.K.: Eerdmans, 2012).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 116
      the familiar words egō eimi (though in reverse order) but the answer to the questions of “whence” and
      “whither” or “where” (cf. 8:14) that haunted Jesus’ critics are given a new focus. While the critics did
      not know “where” he was going (8:14), it is precisely “there” (12:26) that his servants “will be” also.
      This expectation of being with Jesus, which is further discussed in chap. 14, is the promised hope of
      the faithful “follower” of Jesus.

         Jesus is going to the Reward Place. His faithful followers will have the honor of following Him to that
location as part of their reward. Spence-Jones (TPC) breaks Jn 12:26 down into a discussion of service and reward.
Regarding the latter, he associates misthological glory with this location: “They who know and appreciate Christ
deem it the highest and purest happiness to be ‘with’ him, to share his conflict, to hear his encouraging voice, to
participate in the glory of his victory.” Whitacre (IVPNTC) likewise calls attention to the twofold misthological
nature of the verse: “The reward of such obedience, even through death, is twofold: to be with Jesus and to be
honored by the Father. Jesus has been living in the presence of God and is returning to the presence of God, so this
is a promise of being with Christ in the presence of God.” Henry (MHCWB) affirms the duality of the reward/wage:

              Here are the wages which Christ promises to his servants; and they are very rich and noble.
              First, They shall be happy with him: Where I am, there shall also my servant be. To be with
      him, when he was here in poverty and disgrace, would seem but poor preferment, and therefore,
      doubtless, he means being with him in paradise, sitting with him at his table above, on his throne
      there….
              Secondly, They shall be honoured by his Father….The rewarder is God himself, who takes the
      services done to the Lord Jesus as done to himself. The reward is honour, true lasting honour, the
      highest honour; it is the honour that comes from God.

Barnes (BNONT) is on the correct path:

               Where I am. See ch. 14:3; 17:24. That is, he shall be in heaven, where the Son of God then
      was in his divine nature, and where he would be as the glorified Messiah. See Notes on Jn. 3:13. The
      natural and obvious meaning of the expression “I am” implies that he was then in heaven. The design
      of this verse is to comfort them in the midst of persecution and trial. They were to follow him to any
      calamity; but, as he was to be glorified as the result of his sufferings, so they also were to look for
      their reward in the kingdom of heaven, Re. 3:21: “To him that overcometh will I grant to sit with
      me in my throne.” (Italics his.)

        If Barnes means Jesus is promising that faithful believers will be in Heaven, he is mistaken.
Soteriologically, we can deduce that all believers, faithful and unfaithful alike, will be in Heaven. If, however,
Barnes means that only faithful believers will sit with Christ in Heaven on His throne, then he is on the right track.
After all, Barnes acknowledges that this will be “their reward…in heaven.” Misthologically, we can deduce,
contrary to Stegall, that only faithful believers will sit with Christ on His heavenly throne. Similar comments can
be made regarding Lenski (LCNT):

               The fact that Jesus is speaking of a place to which he will presently go and where he will
      remain permanently, is indicated in the broad character of the promise, which, while it is again worded
      in the singular (“my own slave”) and thus is directly personal, is at the same time universal, including
      every servant of his. He refers to heaven, or, as he stated repeatedly to the Jews (7:33; 8:14), the
      heavenly presence of his Father. On the future sense of εἰμί see R. 869–870. “It is not merely prophecy,
      but certainty of expectation that is involved.” Both ἐγώ and the possessive adjective are strongly
      emphatic’ “where I myself go,” I, the Lord and divine Master, “there also my own slave shall be.” The
      two belong together. Yet this is not an association like that of a common master and his servant; for
      we know that in heaven all such service will be ended. This is the association in which Jesus receives
      us to himself (14:3), where we share in his glory and honor and sit with him in his throne (Rev. 3:21).
      Where did earth ever show a master who treated his slaves thus? Think of our poor service here, even
      though it be διακονία, and this reward that is promised us’ The future ἔσται [of Jn 12:26], “shall
      be [in this Where-I-AM-Place],” is divine promise.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 117

         The misthological nature of this Where-I-AM-Place is readily apparent and repeatedly confirmed by various
interpreters. Opinions differ on exactly where this Where-I-AM-Place is. Plummer (CGT) gives a broad definition:
“Where I am, i. e. ‘in My kingdom.” Is Jesus thus making entrance into the kingdom a reward? Some misthologist
might use this line of argument to assert that all believers will be in the soteriological spatial realm of the kingdom,
but only faithful believers will be in the misthological spatial realm of the kingdom. Such a perspective would be
compatible with some arguments I posed in MMP5. Differences of opinion regarding the exact location of this
Where-I-AM-Place are completely understandable. Any objection to its misthological nature would be completely
inexcusable.

                                                    Jn 14:2-3
                                    Primary Interpretive Options for Jn 14:2-3
         A generic spatial understanding of this Where-I-AM location in Jn 12:26 is open to objection if its
counterpart in Jn 14:2-3 gives greater specificity. In turn, a soteriological understanding of Jn 14:2-3 is open to
objection since the misthological nature of its counterpart in Jn 12:26 is beyond question. Nevertheless, many
interpreters object to a misthologically spatial understanding of Jn 14:2-3. Indeed, some tone it down by using the
passage to affirm misthological diversity amidst soteriological security. For many years I shared that popular
understanding. Yet contemporary scholarship is inclined to object. Bruner (GJ:C) summarizes and provides
proponents of both positions:

               Our earliest church parents saw rewards in Jesus’ promise. For example, Irenaeus, Against
      Heresies 5:36:2, in ACCS NT 4/b: 121: “All things belong to God, who supplies all with a suitable
      dwelling place, … according as each person is or shall be worthy.” Tertullian, Scorpiace 6, also in
      ACCS NT 4/b: 121: “How shall there be many mansions in our Father’s house, if not to match the
      diversity of what each deserves [citing 1 Cor. 15:41]?” Augustine, John, 67:2, in NPNF1 7: 321: “the
      many mansions point to the different grades of merit in that one eternal life.” Also in the early part of
      the last century, Bernard, 2:531–33, who cited Origen and Clement of Alexandria in support.
               But most post-ancient commentators have objected to the interpretation of rewards and have
      seen Jesus’ meaning to be mainly the expansive promise of many homes at his Parousia—Christ’s final
      coming to take his disciples to be with him; thus, e.g., Calvin, 2:75; Meyer, 391–92, Schlatter, 292–
      93; Lagrange, 372–73; Bultmann, 603 n. 1; Becker, 2:460; Brown, 2:620 and 626. (Emphasis his.)

         In other words, ancient commentators believed that all believers would be with Jesus in this Where-I-AM
location, but faithful believers would have bigger and better mansions in that location as their reward. From this
perspective, being in the Father’s house is a gift; having a nice mansion in His house is a reward. More recent
commentators object that the text itself does not delineate between the nature of the mansions. Some presume,
accordingly, that having a mansion is not merited. From this more modern premise, the passage is taken as Appling
soteriologically to all believers. As a result, the basic options for the passage can be broken down into three
categories:

                                      1) Exclusively soteriological (no rewards).
                                      2) Soteriological-misthological (variable rewards).
                                      3) Exclusively misthological
                                         a) with veritable rewards
                                         b) without veritable rewards.

                                     Soteriological Interpretation for Jn 14:2-3
         As explained earlier in this series, since Dillow affirms a misthological view of the outer darkness, he had
to reject a spatial view of New Jerusalem because, per his soteriological view of the Bride, this would place the
outer darkness in New Jerusalem! Accordingly, FG interpreters taking a soteriological view of the Bride tend to
deny the spatial nature of the outer darkness and/or the spatial nature of New Jerusalem. As for Dillow, he cites my
position which I took in TOD in one of his footnotes and provides this counter within that note:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 118
                  We are told here that unfaithful Christians will bow down and confess. when a Christian
         confesses, he is forgiven. “If we confess our sins, He is faithful and righteous to forgive us our sins
         and to cleanse us from all unrighteousness” (1 John 1:9). Therefore, claims that unfaithful believers
         will not be restored must be dismissed [TOD, 519-564].
                  In view of the glorious promises of heavenly life promised to justified saints, we can be assured
         that this remorse cannot continue throughout the millennium or eternal state. All will be “with Him,”
         and not in the “outer darkness” (John 14:3). In fact we I will “always be” with Him
         (1 Thessalonians 4:17.) The glories experienced by all saints are so incredible that they have never
         “entered the heart of man” (1 Corinthians 2:9), and they are available to all “who love Him,” that is
         all who are justified. The nature of heaven itself precludes unending remorse.102 (Emphasis his, 761.)

Dillow proceeds to argue that “the Lord will rejoice in the tardy repentance of those for whom He died” (p. 762). I
have provided my rejoinder in MMP1 to his appeals to 1Jn 1:9 and to tardy repentance as supposedly wiping away
all remorse. See MMP1 discussion for refutation of his allegation on those matters. The present discussion is focused
on his appeal to Jn 14:3. The fact that all believers will be with Jesus in His kingdom does not mean that all believers
will be with Jesus in New Jerusalem. Thus, Dillow’s with-Me appeal to 1Thess 4:17 poses no obstacle to a
misthological understanding of Jn 14:3. His appeal to 1Cor 2:9 is self-refuting since not all believers love the Lord.
The promise of 1Cor 2:9 is limited to believers who love the Lord, and Jn 14:23 indicates that the abiding experience
being discussed in Jn 14:3 is likewise limited to believers who lovingly obey the Lord.
         Rokser also seeks to use similar arguments as those taken by Dillow against those taking a misthological
view of the outer darkness and tries to discredit it as a “practical absurdity” (SCFOD, 55). The practical and doctrinal
objections Rokser poses are not truly logistical or exegetical obstacles, however. The fact that all believers will be
with the Lord does not give us the right to insist that all believers will be with the Lord equally. In fact, Rokser
along with his co-authors of SCFOD demonstrate this inescapable fact with their own arguments. They assert all
believers will be given white garments, but only faithful believers will walk with Christ in white. So, per their own
arguments, not all believers are equally with Christ. In his explanation within SCFOD, Stegall asks us to imagine
their co-authored position with the following illustration:

         Wearing white will be a blessing possessed by all the saved (v. [Rev 3:]5); whereas walking with
         Christ in white garments (v. 4) will be an added blessing or reward conditioned on one’s walk of
         fellowship with the Lord now. This principle is found throughout Scripture (Gen. 3:8; 5:22, 24; 6:9;
         Lev. 26:11-12; Deut. 23:14; 2 Chron. 17:3-4; 2 Cor. 6:16; Eph. 4:1; Rev. 2:1). Even though all
         believers will be in a state of purity and fellowship with God after the Rapture by virtue of being in
         sinless, glorified bodies, walking with Christ in white in the coming Kingdom will be a reward for a
         walk of purity and intimacy with the Lord now. Imagine being at a royal ball hosted by a wealthy
         foreign monarch where hundreds of well-dressed guests are assembled inside the royal palace and you
         are among them. It is an honor just to be present on such a grand occasion. But imagine the honor you
         would have if, during the ball, the king invited you to leave the palace for a while and go for a walk
         with him on the palace grounds. What a privilege that would be! This is the privilege that awaits the
         overcomers in white (Rev. 3:5) who are also found “worthy” to walk with Christ (Rev. 3:4). (SCFOD,
         457-458.)

        Per his own illustration, unfaithful believers will be with Christ remotely, but only faithful believers will
be with Christ intimately. So, in his illustration, is the palace a jail for unfaithful believers, despite their supposedly
having glorified bodies? Are unfaithful believers not allowed to walk outside the palace in their white garments
with Christ? Are their white garments prison uniforms? Or are they allowed out of the palace just as long as they
do not walk with Christ since, per Stegall’s argument, the right of walking with Christ is a right reserved for faithful
believers? Walking with Jesus in white, per SCFOD, is a reward limited to faithful believers, whether those faithful
believers are in the royal palace or not.




102
      Dillow, 761.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 119
        Royal Palace/Place
        The SCFOD template provided by Rokser (pp. 55-57) might run counter to his expectations. A reasonable
revision of his imaginary dialogue is in order:

      Unfaithful believer: Lord, can you explain why I’m being cast outside of the royal banquet and into
      the outer darkness outside of it when the SCFOD authors promised me this would not happen?

      Jesus Christ: Because I promised you it would happen in my warnings about the outer darkness.

      Unfaithful believer: But Lord, those authors told me that the place you were preparing in Your Father’s
      house in Jn 14:2-3 did not exclude unfaithful believers so that I would be with you in the Heavenly
      Palace/Place that you were preparing even if I did not prepare.

      Jesus Christ: You should have read My warning for yourself regarding the wedding feast and outer
      darkness, in Mt 22:1-14 for example, and discerned that those SCFOD authors inverted My illustration
      and ignored the context of My promise in Jn 14:2-3.

      Unfaithful believer: True, Lord. But I trusted those SCFOD authors, and they told me that I did not
      have to prepare in order to have a place in the Prepared Place/Palace.

      Jesus Christ: You are responsible for your own actions and for not preparing for the prepared place.

      Unfaithful believer: Yes, Lord. I can understand my responsibility for not preparing since You warned
      me in Your word to prepare. Still, I do not understand why I am out here in the outer darkness, and
      those SCFOD authors are in the royal banquet. In Jam 3:1, You said that teachers would incur a stricter
      judgment. So, if their false teaching was a major contributing factor to my being excluded from the
      Prepared Place/Palace, why are they being allowed into it while I am being excluded from it? Why are
      they not out here with me in the outer darkness?

      Jesus Christ: Good question.

         Mt 22:1-14
         As for the inversion by the SCFOD authors, simply read Mt 22:1-14 for yourself and see for yourself that
the illustration they provide of the royal palace is an inversion, and thus a perversion, of Jesus’ illustration. In the
Lord’s illustration, greater intimacy is experienced with Him inside the palace rather than outside of the palace.
Outside of the palace is the outer darkness. According to Jesus’ illustration, the outer darkness is not a place of
execution or even exclusion from the kingdom. Rather, it represents spatial exclusion from the place where the
banquet is occurring in the palace within the kingdom. Dillow correctly interprets the outer darkness as being
misthological but fails to acknowledge the full implications of its spatial nature. He correctly argues at length that
Jesus’ command to depart from Me has application to unfaithful believers. Consequently, even his own argument
necessarily requires spatial implications, even though he tries to tone down those implications by taking the
Heavenly City, heavenly banquet, and outer darkness as being merely metaphorical.
         In TOD, I provided a quote from Wuest’s expanded translation of Mt 8:12 regarding the spatial nature of
the outer darkness: “But the sons of the kingdom shall be thrown into the darkness, that darkness which is outside
of the King’s banqueting house. There, in that place, there shall be audible weeping and lamentation and gnashing
of the teeth” (Wuest, WNT, 18). I also pointed out that the ekei (which as noted in BDAG is translated as there, in
that place) signifies the spatial significance: “There [spatially], there [experientially] will be weeping and gnashing
of teeth” (Mt 8:12; 22:13; 25:30; Lk 13:28).
         Peterson wrongly equates this spatial location with Hell: “In each of its seven occurrences ‘weeping and
gnashing of teeth’ is preceded by the adverb ekei, which means ‘there, in that place,’ indicating the suffering of the
wicked in hell itself.” Still, he is to be commended for defending its spatial nature. In a footnote, he notes an
attempted counter by an annihilationist who claims that ekei means on that occasion:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                 Page 120
              If however we look at the third passage (Matt 24.51), we shall see that no place is mentioned.
      ‘There’ means ‘on that occasion.’ It is at the throne of judgment, as the real nature of the wicked is
      revealed to them in all hideousness, in despair and misery because of what they have lost and missed,
      as they hear the sentence, perhaps through the temporary suffering, which, as we shall see, precedes
      their destruction, that the weeping and gnashing of teeth are heard” (Life 100). On the contrary, the
      expression “there there will be weeping and gnashing of teeth” is exactly the same in the four texts
      cited above.

         In other words, as Peterson points out, even in the context of Mt 24:51, Jesus says, “There, in that place,
[ekei] there shall be weeping and gnashing of teeth.” Annihilationism, and for that matter many FG misthologists,
are wrong in denying the spatial nature of the outer darkness. The outer darkness involves a literal exclusion from
the literal Reward City.

        1Jn 2:28
        What about 1Jn 2:28? All three authors of SCFOD acknowledge that the verse applies to unfaithful believers
experiencing shame as a result of the Bema: “And now, little children, abide in Him, so that when He appears, we
may have confidence and not shrink away from Him in shame at His coming” (SCFOD: Rokser, 50; Stegall, 213,
217, 224; Witzig, 402). Yet, despite their multiple admissions regarding this text, none of these writers explain what
it means for such a believer to shrink away from Christ in shame, even though they acknowledge that such believers
may have “no reward” and “no crown” (p. 402). How can unfaithful believers shrink away from Christ and yet be
with Christ in a sense imagined by the authors of SCFOD? The biblical imagery is much more consistent. Unfaithful
believers will be excluded in shame from the royal palace and must be content with the palace grounds because
they do not have white garments. Moreover, as I explained in TOD, 1Jn 2:28 has spatial implications:

               The fact that we shrink away from God in shame does not mean that the shame is self-imposed.
      Quite the contrary, the verb for be ashamed (aischuno) in 1Jn 2:28 is passive and thus would mean to
      be put to shame or disgraced (see FB). In his Word Pictures, Robertson concurs that it means to be put
      to shame since it is passive. Paul likewise uses the passive form in 2Cor 10:8 and Phil 1:20, where the
      NAS correctly translates it as, “Be put to shame.” Unfortunately, the NAS failed to translate the
      passive properly in 1Jn 2:28. This shame is not self-inflicted. This shame is God-inflicted. Such
      believers will be put to shame by God, before God, and shrink away in shame from God. Various
      translations bring out different nuances of what is being said. The DBY tries to bring out several
      subtleties: “Be put to shame from before him at his coming.” The preposition apo is best translated
      as from. I would translate it thus as: “Be shamefully put away from Him.” He will shamefully have us
      cast away from His presence into the outer darkness when He returns if we fail to abide in Him. The
      outer darkness is a place for the outcasts who are cast out from before Him at the execution of the
      Bema judgment associated with the rapture.

Kruse (PNTC) likewise elaborates:

              What it means to be confident (lit. ‘have confidence’, parrēsia) in this context is immediately
      explained as being unashamed (lit. ‘not put to shame’, mē aischynthōmen). The word ‘confidence’
      (parrēsia) is found four times in 1 John and refers both to confidence at Christ’s coming/in the
      judgement (2:28; 4:17) and confidence in prayer (3:21; 5:14) (see ‘A Note on the Bases of Assurance’,
      pp. 198–200, for a discussion of the uses of parrēsia in 1 John). To be confident here, then, is
      equivalent to not being put to shame. What all this means is best understood in the light of 4:17, where
      the author speaks about having confidence on the day of judgment. The author’s point in 2:28 is that
      if people remain in Christ (following the teaching they heard from the beginning and which the
      anointing, the Holy Spirit, continues to teach them), then, when Jesus Christ appears and judges his
      people, they may be confident and unashamed before him.

        Strecker and Attridge (HERM) acknowledge that in 1Jn 2:18, it “means that they will not be put to shame
on that day.” They provide a footnote discussing the passive versus reflexive middle voice. They go on to say,
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                          Page 121
“Although the OT usage adopted in the NT identifies ‘being put to shame’ with ‘being disgraced’ or ‘being
excluded,’ such an extreme consequence is not expressed here, even though it belongs to the background of this
text and is hinted in the admonition to ‘have no fear’” (cf. 4:17*).” To the contrary, the extreme consequence of
being disgraced and excluded is hinted at in the context of the passage they cite for those believers who do not
lovingly abide in God’s love and thereby develop a mature (“perfect”) love for God and for the brethren (cp. 1Jn
4:12-18).
        As to the passive-versus-middle debate, one need not insist on an either-or-approach, as Akin (NAC)
explains in a footnote:

              The verb may be translated as either middle or passive. If translated in the middle voice, it
      means “to be ashamed.” If translated in the passive voice, it carries the idea of being put to shame.
      Brown distinguishes between the two: “The passive reflects a legal situation where one is disgraced,
      while the middle has more the psychological aspect of the individual’s feeling shame” (Epistles of
      John, 381). Those who favor the passive voice understand the verb to mean that God will openly put
      these unfaithful to shame for their refusal to abide in him (see Marshall, Epistles of John, 166–67;
      and Schnackenburg, The Johannine Epistles, 165). Those who favor the middle voice emphasize the
      personal shame involved in standing before God, having been unfaithful to him (see Westcott, Epistles
      of John, 82; and Brooke, Johannine Epistles, 66). Smalley seeks to find a balance in the two positions
      when he suggests both interpretations may be held together: “[John] could be saying that the person
      who has been faithful to the truth of Jesus need not, at the end, be ashamed in the presence of Christ,
      and (furthermore) will not be disgraced by him” (1, 2, 3 John, 131).

Unfaithful believers will be both ashamed and disgraced. The passive and middle are not mutually exclusive. Those
shamefully put away will be ashamed. Smalley (WBC) provides a lengthy explanation to the same effect:

               The phrase μῆ αἰσχυνθῶμεν ἀπʼ αὐτοῦ (“we may not be disgraced before him”) may be
      construed in two different ways. If ἀπό is understood in its usual sense of “from,” the meaning is that
      those who keep the faith will have no need to run away in shame from the presence of the glorified
      Jesus (so Westcott, 82; Brooke, 66, both taking αἰσχυνθῶμεν, “we may not be disgraced,” as a middle
      form; cf. Houlden, 86). See Prov 13:5 (LXX); 1 Pet 4:16. However, ἀπό in this context may also be
      interpreted as the equivalent of ὑπό (“by,” as in John 5:19; Jer 12:13, LXX, uses the actual expression
      αἰσχύνομαι ἀπό, “to bear the shame of,” in this way); and in this case the significance is that the
      believer who abides in Jesus will not be put to shame, or “disgraced” by him, at the final judgment (so
      Haas, Handbook, 74; Marshall, 166; Schnackenburg, 165). This meaning certainly has support from
      other biblical texts (cf. Isa 45:24, LXX; Phil 1:20). In the Gospels, especially, we may note the direct
      parallel at Mark 8:38 = Luke 9:26, using the compound ἐπαισχύνομαι, “be ashamed,” and the idea of
      rejection by the Son of man at the judgment (without using αἰσχύνομαι) present in Matt 10:33 = Luke
      12:9. (Houlden, 87, draws in the “shame” of Peter before Jesus after denying him, Luke 22:61–62; see
      also Rev 6:16–17.)
               It is not easy to decide between these twin interpretations; indeed, it is possible that once again
      they may be held together. The weight of the supporting evidence is in favor of the second (“judgment
      by Christ, rather than … psychological feelings in his presence”; Marshall, 166 n.9). But the other
      meaning may also form part of John’s thought. In other words, he could be saying that the person who
      has been faithful to the truth of Jesus need not, at the end, be ashamed in the presence of Christ, and
      (furthermore) will not be “disgraced” by him. The opposite truth, in any case, is implied. Those
      who deny Jesus (see vv 22–23) now can expect to be denied by him eventually.

Still, for translational purposes, UBSH gives preference to the passive over the middle voice, favoring the juridical
sense of be put to shame:

             According to the latter interpretation the clause lit. means “we may not be ashamed (away)
      from him.” This leads to a rendering like that of RSV, or to “we may … need not hide in shame from
      him” (TEV), ‘we will not be embarrassed to face him,’ ‘we will not feel shame in his presence.’ This
      meaning fits the preceding part of the clause, in as much as it also refers to the feelings we have.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 122
               It is also possible, however, to keep to the passive meaning. This leads to such renderings of
      the verb as, ‘to be put to shame,’ ‘to suffer disgrace.’ Then from means, “from the side of,” and
      indicates where the disgrace comes from, or it introduces the one who makes ashamed. With some
      further adjustments this may result in, ‘we may not suffer disrepute before him,’ ‘he will not make us
      ashamed.’ This rendering agrees with the idea of judgment associated with Christ’s second coming
      and the basically juridical terms of v. 29. Therefore it seems to be the slightly better one.

They prefer the passive understanding. Rather, than merely being ashamed, unfaithful believers will be made
ashamed by Christ. Marshall (NICNT), as noted above by others, also prefers the judicial approach.

      Such people will not share the fate of those who will be ashamed before Christ at his coming. This
      may be taken to mean that they will shrink back in shame as they realize their unworthiness to come
      into his holy presence. But it is more likely that it means that they will be put to shame by Christ;
      they will be disgraced openly. For them the coming of Jesus will mean judgment and rejection.

To be sure, he along with others of the Arminian camp might think of it as a soteriological rejection. However, a
misthologist can easily contend that it is a misthological rejection. For example, Derickson (EEC) correctly
perceives that misthological issues rather than soteriological issues are at state:

              The term John uses to describe the believer’s possible experience in Jesus’ presence is
      αἰσχύνω. Its aorist form, though, can be viewed as either passive or middle voice. As a passive it
      communicates the sense of feeling shame or being put to shame, being disgraced. As a middle
      voice verb in combination with ἀπʼ αὐτοῦ, it can best be translated by “shrink away in shame” (Brooke,
      66; Marshall, 166; Westcott, 83). In Phil 1:20 Paul contrasts αἰσχύνω with παρρησία even as John does
      here, using them as antonyms (Johnson, 66). This same sense of shame can be found in the OT in
      regard to the disobedient of the nation of Judah facing God’s judgment (Brown, 381).
              Of note is John’s inclusion of himself with the first person plural (Lieu, 116). He is not warning
      about losing one’s salvation or being declared unregenerate by Christ at His appearing. Rather, he is
      describing the emotional response a believer will have when brought before Christ at His appearing.
      Every believer, including John, will have one of two responses to Jesus when they stand in His
      presence for the first time: the believer either will be comfortable in His presence or will be looking
      for somewhere to hide in humiliation.

         The Lord Jesus will give them a place to hide in their humiliation. That place is the outer darkness. It is a
literal place. As explained in RBVGB, in my assessment unfaithful believers will have redeemed bodies but not
glorified bodies. Their bodies will not glow with glory. Sending them to the outer darkness is not only an act of
judgment but also an act of mercy. They would be mercilessly embarrassed to have to be in New Jerusalem in such
a state of humiliation. Just as someone wearing a bathing suit would feel out of place at church during the Sunday
morning worship service, so unfaithful believers would feel out of place being in the Temple City where ongoing
worship is common. The Lord will provide them with a place where their humiliated state will not be such a source
of embarrassment, a place better suited for their predicament. He can have fellowship with them there, not in New
Jerusalem. They will still be in Heaven after all. When the Lord wants a day at the beach, so to speak, He can visit
them at their beach shanties. For those who are presently content with the idea of just having a shack in the corner
of glory land, they may indeed find out that is all they get. They will not have as intimate a dwelling with the Lord
as those who live with Him in the rooms prepared by Him in the royal palace for the faithful. An exclusively
soteriological view of Jn 14:2-3 is impossible to maintain with anything approaching biblical consistency. This
passage cannot be used to rule out misthological spatial distinctions.

                            Soteriological-Misthological Interpretation for Jn 14:2-3
         The quote I supplied in the introduction of this present book, MMP6, from Seymour is an example of a
soteriological-misthological understanding of the Father’s house:

             The city is not Heaven; it is only a part of Heaven. Christ said, “In My Father’s house are
      many mansions; if it were not so, I would have told you. I go to prepare a place for you” (John 14:2).
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 123
              The New Jerusalem is the most glorious mansion of Heaven, but it is not the only one. And
      those who know the Lord and are obedient to Him will have the right to enter in through the gates of
      the New Jerusalem and also to eat the 12 manner of fruit of the tree of life. We cannot say for certain
      what that entails, but it is obviously a great privilege. (P. 64.)

Seymour correctly acknowledges that entering by the gates into New Jerusalem is a reward limited to the faithful;
however, he leaves the question unanswered as to whether unfaithful believers might enter by other means. Some
FG interpreters pose that the unfaithful will be able to enter through less glorious ways. I counter that unfaithful
believers will not be able to enter the Reward City. Such arguments aside for the moment, however, Seymour’s
understanding of the Father’s house would evidently even allow for heavenly beach scanties to be included. His
interpretation is reminiscent of that of Barnes (BNONT):

               In my Father’s house. Most interpreters understand this of heaven, as the peculiar dwelling-
      place or palace of God; but it may include the universe, as the abode of the omnipresent God.
               Are many mansions. The word rendered mansions means either the act of dwelling in any
      place (ver. 23, “we will make our abode with him”), or it means the place where one dwells. It is taken
      from the verb to remain, and signifies the place where one dwells or remains. It is applied by the Greek
      writers to the tents or temporary habitations which soldiers pitch in their marches. It denotes a dwelling
      of less permanency than the word house. It is commonly understood as affirming that in heaven there
      is ample room to receive all who will come; that therefore the disciples might be sure that they would
      not be excluded. Some have understood it as affirming that there will be different grades in the joys
      of heaven; that some of the mansions of the saints will be nearer to God than others, agreeably to 1
      Co. 15:40, 41. But perhaps this passage may have a meaning which has not occurred to interpreters.
      Jesus was consoling his disciples, who were affected with grief at the idea of his separation. To comfort
      them he addresses them in this language: “The universe is the dwelling-place of my Father. All is his
      house. Whether on earth or in heaven, we are still in his habitation. In that vast abode of God there are
      many mansions. The earth is one of them, heaven is another. Whether here or there, we are still in the
      house, in one of the mansions of our Father, in one of the apartments of his vast abode. This we ought
      continually to feel, and to rejoice that we are permitted to occupy any part of his dwelling-place. Nor
      does it differ much whether we are in this mansion or another. (Emphasis his.)

Wilkin (GNTC) likewise states:

               “My Father’s house” refers to heaven or to the whole universe. Many commentators suggest
      that heaven and some vague sort of heavenly dwellings are in view. This is highly unlikely because
      the eternal abode of believers is the new earth, not heaven (Rev 21:1–27). The dwellings (mansions)
      refer to literal homes or apartments which someday will come down with the New Jerusalem out of
      the new heavens onto the new earth. Revelation 21:2, which refers to the descending New Jerusalem
      as a bride prepared for her husband, probably parallels John 14:2, which speaks of Jesus preparing a
      place for His disciples. The New Jerusalem with its golden buildings is prepared by Jesus both for His
      disciples’ joy, and for His own. (Emphasis his.)

This interpretation is very attractive if combined with an exclusively misthological interpretation of New Jerusalem.
In such a view, all believers will be somewhere in the Father’s house, but only faithful believers will be in New
Jerusalem. Yet, in such a case, contrary to Barnes, I would contend that it does make a difference whether we are
in this mansion versus some heavenly beach shanty. Along this line, I argued in TOD that overcomers (i.e. bridal
saints) will possess dwelling places in the Bridal City (Jn 14:2-3). Subcomers will not.

        Many Mansions Prepared for You
        As I discussed at length in TOD, a pastor brought up Jn 14:2-3 to counter the idea of kingdom ranking. He
was taking an exclusively soteriological view of the passage, such as that noted above, supposing that all believers
have a place prepared for them in heaven, so no substantial distinction exists in Heaven between the faithful and
unfaithful. I conceded at that time that the passage might be taken as applying to all believers, at least by broad
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 124
implication. Jesus is referring to coming in the rapture, and since the rapture is for all believers, it could be argued
that every believer has a place prepared for him or her (in some sense of the word) in the kingdom. Still, even this
broader application in no way proves that all believers have the same identical place prepared for them. Even if the
Father’s house refers to His future kingdom, it is a place in His future kingdom that is being prepared, not the
kingdom itself. Every believer will be in this future kingdom. Thus, in that sense they will all be in the same place—
in the same kingdom. But within that kingdom there will be distinctions, as this very passage itself indicates. In this
