kingdom there are many dwelling places, which suggests that there is an individualization of the preparation:
individual places, not identical places. Unfaithful believers will find that the place in the kingdom prepared for them
is the outer darkness.
         I referred the pastor at the luncheon to the passage in Mt 20:20-28, which deals with first and last place at
the table. The place prepared for you in His kingdom is dependent upon your service. Your service, that is, your
good works, determine whether the place being prepared for you is first, last, or least place in His kingdom. The
place prepared for you is dependent upon your preparation for that place. The building project is still going on and
is dependent upon the building materials that you are sending ahead of you. That dwelling place, for some believers,
may be a trailer park rather than a mansion. Or if Alcorn is correct, some might not even get a trailer:

         We might imagine that some of us are sending ahead sufficient materials for pup tents, some for studio
         apartments, some for trailer homes, some for ranch houses, and others for great mansions….
              If this seems too fantastic, remember that we are simply trying to put together and understand
         Christ’s own words. Obviously he meant something—if not this, then what?
              The reason these concepts seem so foreign is that we tend to be so preoccupied with our life here
         that we never stop to think about life in heaven. And we overlook the fact that heaven is consistently
         described in the Bible not in ethereal, vague, or abstract means, but in very tangible and surprisingly
         earthly ways.103

The pastor at the luncheon said that Jesus finished the preparation of the place for us in His death. Nonsense. When
Jesus finishes preparing that place for us, He is coming back for us. It is still being prepared. As I pointed out to
this pastor, the reason Jesus has not returned is because the place is still being prepared. It is a reward concept. If
you want a mansion, you better work for it. There will be a mansion for every overcomer. The more overcomers
there are the more preparation that must be done. Even from this limited misthological perspective that I held in
TOD at the time, it still would be concluded that the reason there are so many places is because Jesus is preparing
them one by one.

          Reward City
          Additionally, I also noted in TOD that the way Seymour ties together Jn 14:2 with Rev 22:14 is highly
anticipatory of the position developed therein. In his quote supplied above, Seymour is on the brink of suggesting
that this City is itself a reward. He certainly implies that living in this City is a reward limited to faithful believers
since he pictures it as the mansion prepared for obedient believers. Unfaithful believers will be given other mansions
(i.e., other cities) in which to live. For reasons, explained in TOD, I concluded that New Jerusalem is a City of Rest
that unfaithful believers are prohibited from even entering at all.

        Bridal City
        I went on to argue in TOD that, in terms of technical precision, the focus of Jesus’ promise in Jn 14:2 is
not on the many mansions (regardless as to whether these dwellings are cities, trailer parks, or pup tents) but on
the special mansion for His Bride—those believers who prepare themselves for His coming. There are a number
of reasons to conclude that Jesus is only preparing one place out of many. It may be argued that this does not limit
the rapture to faithful believers; all believers will have places (monai) in the kingdom. Even so, it appears that the
special place that Jesus is still preparing will be available only to those believers who are preparing for Him.
Although many dwelling places (monai) are already in existence, the particular place that Jesus is still working on
and preparing is a special place (topos). The Father’s house already has many dwelling places (in the sense of ample
room for all believers) already ready. Jesus did accomplish this for us in His death. All believers go to be with the

103
      Randy C. Alcorn, Money Possessions and Eternity (Wheaton: Tyndale House Publications, 1989), 175.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 125
Lord (in some connection with the Father’s house) when they die or are raptured. Nevertheless, a new addition is
being added on to these rooms for the Bride—the bridal suite. Jesus is still preparing the bridal suite.
         In the context immediately preceding this passage, Jesus limited the you to those believers who are not only
soteriologically bathed but subsequently washed in practical sanctification. Those believers are the ones who will
have a misthological part (meros) with Him (Jn 13:8-10). Jesus promises these bathed believers, who have been
subsequently washed, that He is preparing a bridal suite for them and that they will be the ones who will be with
Him in His Father’s house (Jn 14:1-3). Only those believers who submit to the prenuptial washing of practical
sanctification will be worthy of being presented to the Lord as His Bride.
         In Jn 14:3, Jesus says that He will come back and take (paralambano) us to this place He is preparing. If
this refers to the nuptial taking of a bride, then the promise of a place that is actively being prepared is limited to
overcomers at the time of the rapture. Although the rapture is not limited to overcomers, being taken to this prepared
place is limited to them. Sweigart points out a possible connection with the initial Matthean usage of this verb in
which Joseph was told to take Mary as his wife (Mt 1:20). This nuptial taking of a bride by her husband is quite
probable in Jn 14:3, and Sweigart finds it as likely in Jesus’ reference to not knowing the day or hour in the present
Matthean Olivet context:

      Our Lord was speaking metaphorically using Jewish nuptial imagery. As we have learned previously,
      when a Jewish young man betrothed a bride they did not immediately live together. In fact, the
      bridegroom returned to his father’s house to build a room onto the house where he was to live
      with his bride after the marriage was consummated. Although this is foreign to our culture, the
      purpose of this arrangement was so that the young bride could learn from her mother-in-law how to
      properly care for her new husband. So the groom was responsible for building a new room onto
      the house. However, to prevent the young man from doing a sloppy job because of his desire to
      be with his bride, the son was only allowed to go bring his bride after the father had inspected
      AND APPROVED the new dwelling that the son had prepared. In this sense then only the father
      knew when the bridegroom would go for the bride.104 (Caps his.)

Sweigart’s comments are insightful in many respects. The Prepared Place is an addition onto the Father’s
existing house. When the Lord finishes preparing the rooms in His Father’s mansion for His Bride (that is,
finishes adding the Bridal Chamber), His Father will tell Him to go, take His Bride, and bring Her home.
Jesus is preparing a room for His Bride right now, awaiting His Father’s approval to take His Bride, who in
turn is composed of those believers who are preparing themselves for His return.
         Just as Joseph was told to take (paralambano) Mary as his wife (Mt 1:20), with the result that he took
(paralambano) her as his wife (Mt 1:24), so Jesus will come to take (paralambano) His Bride to Himself (cp.
Dt 25:5; 1Sam 25:39). Thus, in TOD, I posed that the Father’s house would be the kingdom. Inside the kingdom
there are many dwelling places (monai). There are rooms for all God’s children in His kingdom. The rapture is not
limited to overcomers, but the preparation of the bridal suite (i.e., the topos referring to the Heavenly City) within
the Father’s house will be restricted to overcomers who compose the Bride. In contrast, the carnal believers who
are confined to the outer darkness in the kingdom will be outside the Heavenly City. Their dwelling places (monai)
will be in the outer darkness. Although both groups of believers will always be with the Lord as a result of the
rapture (1Thess 4:17), one group simply will be with Him in His kingdom, whereas the other group will be more
closely associated with Him as His Bride in the Heavenly City co-ruling His kingdom.
         In contrast to the many dwelling places (monai) that already are present within the Father’s house (oikia),
the Lord says that He is preparing a place (topos). Why is He preparing one place (topos) in distinction to the many
dwelling places (monai)? Why did He say He is preparing one place rather than many? Tenney (EBC) answers:
“The imagery of a dwelling place (‘rooms’) is taken from the oriental house which the sons and daughters have
apartments under the same roof as their parents.” His answer is well taken since it corresponds to the historical
background. For example, when Abraham’s servant met Rebekah at the well, he asked her, “Please tell me, is there
room [topos] for us to lodge in your father’s house?” (Gen 24:23) She answered that they have such a room. It was
one room among many. Yet Tenney’s assumption that Jesus is preparing many such rooms in His Father’s house
or that all the rooms in the Father’s house are such rooms is contrary to the text and, therefore, contrary to the
104
   John Sweigart, “The Olivet Discourse,” 5. Available at http://www.inthebeginning.org/newtestament/matthew
/mt24.pdf. Accessed on August 12, 2004.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 126
probable bridal imagery. Jesus tells the disciples that there are lots of rooms, but He is going to prepare one room.
The picture would appear to be that of a large oriental house in which one special room is being prepared for
the Bride-to-be. Or as Chumney surmises: “Jesus said that there were many rooms (mansions) in heaven. One of
these rooms is the room where the spiritual marriage will be consummated between Jesus and His bride.”105
         Several passages use this same Greek word to talk of the Lord preparing (hetoimazo) an eschatological
place/experience for believers (Mt 20:23; 25:34; Mk 10:40; Jn 14:2-3; 1Cor 2:9; Heb 11:16; Rev 21:2). Also, two
passages talk about believers preparing themselves for an eschatological place/experience (Lk 12:47; Rev 19:7).
Even though the misthological nature of hetoimazo may be maintained, and even if the Johannine passage were
broadened by way of conceptual application to include those believers who receive least place (see above), it is
highly unlikely that the least are included at all in the preparation. This word for prepare (hetoimazo), when used
eschatologically, is always misthological—meaning that all eschatological usages of this word for prepare refer to
a reward concept.
         The New Jerusalem is a reward for the Bride, as already noted. “And I saw the holy city, New Jerusalem,
coming down out of Heaven from God, prepared as a bride adorned for her husband” (Rev 21:2; TM). The City
was prepared for Her because She was prepared for Him. “Let us rejoice and be glad and give the glory to Him, for
the marriage of the Lamb has come and His bride has prepared herself” (Rev 19:7; TM). His Bride is composed of
those believers who have prepared themselves for the wedding by adorning themselves in good works. Good works
are certainly important, but they are not to be taken for granted. We are not to assume that every genuine believer
will so prepare him or herself. Nor are we to assume that these good works are required for entrance into the
kingdom. Works (i.e., self preparation) are required for entrance into the City, not into the kingdom. The place
being prepared is the Holy City, not the millennial kingdom or the New Earth. If you want a place in that Heavenly
City during the millennium and eternity, then you must prepare for it. You must be experientially holy.
         The only other place that monai (dwelling places) is used in the NT is in this very chapter (Jn 14:23), where
Jesus uses the singular form: mone (dwelling place). The conditional communion that believers experience with the
Lord now (Jn 14:21,23) is in anticipation of the conditional consummation they will experience with Him then
(Jn 14:2-3). The world and worldly Christians are not offered this level of intimacy. The conditional nature of the
singular dwelling place (mone) is suggestive that the singular place (topos) is being prepared for faithful believers.
         The foundation for this conclusion already had been set in the conclusion of Jesus’ public ministry, where
Jesus made this promise: “If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am, there shall My servant also be;
if anyone serves Me, the Father will honor him” (Jn 12:26). Note carefully the conditionalism and parallelism
embedded in this verse:

         •   If we serve/follow Jesus, then we will be with Him in a place called where I am.
         •   If we serve Jesus, then the Father will honor us.

         This futuristic place known as where I am (Jn 7:34,36; 12:26; 14:3; 17:24) also appears as where I am
from/going (Jn 7:28; 8:14, 21-22; 13:33; 14:4). The two phrases are equated in Jn 14:3-4. From a comparison of the
explicit confirmation in Jn 12:26 with contextual indications of 14:3-4, this conclusion necessarily follows: Being
with Jesus in a place He describes as where I am (going to prepare for you), at least in these two passages, is a
misthological honor conditioned on the believer’s service to Jesus.

         •   If anyone serves Me, let him follow Me; and where I am, there shall My servant also be; if
             anyone serves Me, the Father will honor him (Jn 12:26).
         •   And if I go and prepare a place for you, I will come again, and receive you to Myself; that where
             I am, there you may be also. And you know the way where I am going (Jn 14:3-4).

         As I argued in TOD, if we serve Jesus, we will be with Him where He is in a place He is currently preparing
for us. The place which Jesus is preparing for those believers who serve Him is a place of honor conditioned on
their service to Jesus. It is a reward, not a gift. This place mystified both Jesus’ adversaries and admirers. They did
not know what He was talking about. This is still the case today. LS mentality tends to equate the where I am in
both passages and to conclude that we earn Heaven by our service. FG theology has the opposite propensity and

105
      Eddie Chumney, Who is the Bride of Christ? Revised edition (Hagerstown: Serenity Books, 2001), 60.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 127
seeks to disassociate the passages by assuming that they are talking about two different places: one earned, one
unearned. The Marrowistic conclusion, in contrast, is that the same mistholic place is described by both passages,
and it is earned. The two passages combine to picture a special place of intimacy in the kingdom of Heaven reserved
for those believers who serve the Lord, namely, the Heavenly City (with individual apartments for overcoming
believers).
          However, in an endnote within the above discussion from TOD, I conceded that an objection can be raised
from Jn 17:24 regarding my identification of the futuristic Where-I-AM-Place being prepared in Jn 14:2-3 as a place
of reward. Securitists tend to use Jn 17:24 in defense of eternal security. Indeed, this was my inclination in TOD.
Doing so presents a problem, though, in terms of consistency. If Jn 17:24 unconditionally guarantees that all
believers will be with Jesus in this Where-I-AM-Place (in Heaven or the millennial kingdom), then how can being
with Jesus in the same Where-I-AM-Place in Jn 14:3-4 be conditional for believers? I did not delve into answering
that question in TOD because doing so succinctly is difficult because of the variety of ways to interpret the Lord’s
Prayer in Jn 17. For example, was Jesus expressing a request that will be unconditionally fulfilled? We will resume
a discussion of Jn 17:24 and its context after we consider a third option for Jn 14:2-3 which I did not consider in
TOD.106

                                     Misthological Interpretation for Jn 14:2-3
         In the present book, in connection with the temple argument made by those such as Brayan and McCaffrey,
I am exploring the possibility of challenging part of my above soteriological-misthological proposition from TOD
and thereby offer an amendment. If the Father’s house refers to a Heavenly Temple, then I would be disinclined
to think that unfaithful believers will have any access to it. The Father’s house should not be equated with
His entire kingdom. Rather, His house is His place of residence inside His Kingdom. As I stated herein, in my
interaction with McCaffrey, if his argument is enjoined with those who pose a bridal understanding of the
preparations taking place in Jn 14:2-3 (which was discussed in TOD), then a very powerful alternative to my
soteriological-misthological understanding for Jn 14:2-3 in TOD can be posed: The Father’s house might be the
Heavenly City (both the 4D and 3D aspects of it), which already had many dwelling places in it before Jesus
left. To be sure, Jesus left to prepare mansions for His followers in New Jerusalem since it is the Reward City
anticipated even from OT times. Nevertheless, in addition to this OT anticipation regarding that reward,
Jesus might also create a new place as a spatial addition, a secret place added to the Father’s Heavenly
Temple. This new creation/location will be the 5D Transcendent Temple City. It will be added as an
additional spatial location, dimensionally above the preexisting 4D Heavenly City (and accessed from above
Earth via the 3D Heavenly City).
         So, as one can easily see, trying to provide a precise interpretation of the Father’s house requires
consideration of multiple factors. Just to mention some of the primary factors from the Gospel of John itself, (1) the
explicit conditionality of 12:26 and 13:8 and 14:23 and (2) the implicit conditionality of (a) the bridal and (b) temple
imagery of 14:2-3 must be interpreted in a cohesive manner, with the possible lack of conditionality in 17:24.

         Jn 13:8
         The spatial and misthological nature of the WHERE-I-AM (hopou ego eimi) phrase was discussed above
in this chapter regarding Jn 12:26107 and in the previous chapter in connection with other texts in GJ in which it
occurs.108 WHERE-I-AM (hopou ego eimi) in Jn 14:3 is immediately followed by a WHERE-I-AM-Going (hopou
ego hupago) in Jn 14:4. In that same context, it is preceded by a WHERE-I-AM-Going (hopou hupago ego) in
Jn 13:33 and is apparently conditioned on having a misthological place (meros) with Jesus (Jn 13:8). Hopou is
linked with meros. Thus, with contextual accuracy, BDAG defines meros in Jn 13:8 as have a place with someone.
It likewise defines meros spatially in Mt 24:51, Lk 12:46; Rev 21:8. GING also places these four texts together as
spatially meaning “place Mt 24:51; Lk 12:46; J 13:8; Rv 21:8” (emphasis original). CEB translates it as place in
Jn 13:8, and so does Adams (CCC). Therefore, although meros is typically rendered as part or share in Jn 13:8, its
spatial nature in this context should not be overlooked.



106
    See Jn 17:24; 140.
107
    See Jn 12:26, p. 114.
108
    See Jn 7:34,36; 12:26; 14:3; 17:24, p. 82.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 128
         Thompson (NTL) notes an interesting background: “Footwashing was often part of the preparation of a
priest or religious functionary for participation in cultic rituals. For example, according to the Mosaic law, priests
are to wash their hands and their feet ‘so that they may not die’ (i.e., because they had entered the holy place
unwashed; Exod 30:19, 21).” If the Upper Room is typologically associated with the Holy Place, then Peter’s
potential expulsion for having unwashed feet would be entirely understandable. Newman and Nida (UBSH)
rightly link it with place but wrongly give it soteriological implications: “Whatever the source of the imagery, Jesus
uses these words to affirm that if Peter is not ‘washed’ he will not share in the benefits of Jesus’ death, and so will
have no place among God’s people.” Not true. The footwashing is necessary to share in the misthological benefits
made available to believers by Jesus’ death so that they can have a place with Him at His throne, in the 3D New
Jerusalem, also within the 4D City, and within the 5D Temple. Having a share in this place is a misthological
consideration, not a soteriological concern.
         Gangel (HNTC) makes the same error: “There is no place in the body of Christ for those who have not been
cleansed by the Lord. Washing in this symbolic context cannot refer to baptism, but the atoning cleansing of sin.”
To the contrary, as a believer, Peter would still have a place in the body of Christ if he did not submit to the
footwashing, but this subsequent washing was necessary for him to have a place in the bridal body of Christ. The
contingency of Peter having a portion, part, share, participation with Christ in Jn 13:8 is typologically boiled down
to having a place with Jesus at the table in the Upper Room. GWN mistranslates the verse as: “If I don’t wash you,
you don’t belong to me.” This is a soteriological interpretation, not a translation. Morris (NICNT) shares this
misunderstanding: “Unless Peter submits to the feet washing he may not eat with Jesus. But Jesus means more. A
literal washing of the feet is not necessary before one can be a Christian. The words point us to a washing free from
sin that only Christ can give. Apart from this washing no one can be Christ’s.” Not true. The washing of the feet
represents the post-regeneration experiential freeing from sin that Christians can obtain through ongoing
sanctification via a confession of sin (1Jn 1:9). Contrary to the assumption of many commentators, Peter’s
footwashing was not required for him to belong to Christ soteriologically. Bernard (ICC) correctly says that in this
context, it means “‘to have part with another,’ or to be his partner, is to share in his work, and ultimately in his
reward.” Yes, the passage talks about being a partner with Christ in His reward. The problem is that many
commentators do not consistently distinguish a gift from a reward. If this passage is talking about sharing in Christ’s
reward by undergoing subsequent cleansing, then it is not talking about belonging to Christ as a gift by experiencing
regeneration. Taking the passage as talking about belonging to Christ as His misthological Bride, on the other hand,
would be consistent with the context. Mounce (REBC) says, “Eschatologically it came to mean ‘an eternal reward.’
Here John uses the term to mean ‘fellowship with Christ.’” Actually, a temporal-misthological dichotomy need not
be posed in this context. This ongoing cleansing is necessary both for temporal fellowship and also for its attendant
eternal reward. Dods (ExpB) does reasonably well in his comments:

      Superficially, these words might have been understood as intimating to Peter that, if he wished to
      partake of the feast prepared, he must allow Jesus to wash his feet. Unless he was prepared to leave
      the room and reckon himself an outcast from that company, he must submit to the feet-washing which
      his friends and fellow-guests had submitted to….
        ….Whoso sits down at Christ’s table must sit down clean; he may not have come clean, even as those
      first guests were not clean, but he must allow Christ to cleanse him, must honestly suffer Christ to
      remove from his heart, from his desire and purpose, all that He counts defiling.

Dods correctly notes the importance of this cleaning for ongoing fellowship between the believer and the Lord.
However, the eschatological implications must not be ignored. Sitting with Christ at His table eschatologically, and
thus having table fellowship with the Lord, is a reward limited to those believers who submit to His ongoing
cleansing. Various FG interpreters do well to insist upon the misthological nature of the passage. Wilkin (GNTC),
for example, says: “The word translated ‘part’ (meros) is a term related to fellowship and rewards. It is not a
synonym for those who have eternal life. It concerns the right to serve Him now and to rule with Him eternally”
(emphasis his). Dillow (pp. 70-71) correctly notes the temporal communion and misthological inheritance
associated with the term. Commentators frequently acknowledge both. What is missing, though, from the typical
FG discussion, is a misthological analysis of the spatial implications.
        As explained in 4DF, Peter was already clean because of his bath at the soteriological level, but Peter was
not completely clean at the familial level because he had not yet had his feet washed. Without the foot washing,
Peter would miss out on having a part (meros) in the future rulership of the millennial kingdom, as well as miss out
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                      Page 129
spatially on dining at the messianic table and residing in a heavenly mansion. Contingency for these experiential
and spatial aspects was signified by the necessity of the footwashing so that Peter might participate in the Lord’s
Supper. Table fellowship is associated with bridal rulership. Being made personally worthy by the Lord’s washing
his feet was necessary for participation in the Lord’s Supper.
         In the Parable of the Wedding Feast, Jesus already had notified the disciples in Mt 22:8 that participation
in the wedding feast would be a reward conditioned on being worthy (axios). In that parable, taking the trouble to
acquire wedding clothes (i.e., white, washed garments) was necessary. The Lord’s Supper prefigures that wedding
feast. Obviously, none of us are worthy of the Lord’s death. Participation in the soteriological benefits of Jesus’
death is conditioned simply on saving faith, not on personal worthiness. But we can be personally worthy of
participating in the Supper that commemorates that death. Jesus illustrates this personal necessity via washed feet.
And after the Supper, in that same context, He will make promises regarding heavenly mansions to His disciples in
the room. Those promises are addressed to believers with washed feet.
                                    Illustration 43. No Washing, No Mansion




        The flow of the context is straightforward and leads to a logically and culturally consistent conclusion:

      1. If I do not wash you, then you have no part with Me.
      2. If you have no part with Me, then you will not participate in the Supper.
      3. If you do not participate in the Supper, then the promise of a heavenly mansion in the Heavenly City
         does not apply to you.

         Many Christians strangely assume that Jesus is going to prepare a heavenly mansion for all believers even
though He limited the promise to bathed believers with clean feet. The conclusion that not all believers will have a
heavenly mansion is consistent not only with the context but also with Paul’s analogy of a believer’s house being
burnt down at the Bema if he built poorly (1Cor 3:15-17). Such a believer would suffer complete destruction,
picturing financial ruin, the loss of his dwelling. Having no heavenly mansion in the Heavenly City would suggest
exclusion from the Heavenly City, not from the kingdom of Heaven. The kingdom of Heaven is much larger than
a single city.
         Spatially, Jesus is warning Peter: “If I do not wash you, then you have no place with Me” (TM). Peter
would be missing out on a place at the table and a place in the Upper Room and fail to obtain all that is
misthologically signified by both. Being cast away from the table eschatologically is to be cast into the outer
darkness misthologically (Mt 8:11-12). To be excluded from the Upper Room would, at a minimum, entail being
excluded from New Jerusalem.
         The arguments above are the types of arguments that I have advocated in various writings. The new
proposition being advanced herein is that if the Upper Room signifies something above the 3D-4D Heavenly
City as described in Scripture and thus above New Jerusalem, then spatial exclusion by those believers who
have unclean feet would include exclusion from that upper realm as well. Since the WHERE-I-AM-GOING
place is not limited to New Jerusalem but includes the eternal WHERE-I-AM place with the Father that
predated New Jerusalem, the WHERE-I-AM-PREPARING place seems to be a higher dimension added on
to the Heavenly City. If the Father’s house is His Heavenly Temple, then the WHERE-I-AM-PREPARING
place would be the hidden temple chamber above the Heavenly City.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 130
                                    Illustration 44. No Washing, No Chamber




If we do not wash our feet by the washing of confessing our sins in order to have fellowship with the Lord (1Jn 1:9),
we will not only be disqualified from having a mansion in the Father’s house (the 3D City) but also from the
Prepared Place (the 5D City).

         Father’s house
         The lynchpin for this new proposal for Jn 14:2-3 is based on the possible association of the Upper Room
with the Upper Room Chambers above the Heavenly Temple. If the Father’s house refers to the Heavenly Temple,
then this proposition is a natural interpretation. On the other hand, Michaels (NICNT) denies this connection:

               “In my Father’s household” does not mean in the Jerusalem temple, as in 2:16 (“Stop making
      my Father’s house a house of trade!”), nor in the heavenly temple (which is not mentioned at all in
      John’s Gospel), but in heaven itself, understood as a household….The traditional “many mansions”
      (KJV) is based on the Latin Vulgate’s mansiones multae, “many stations” or “stopping places,” from
      an ancient interpretation of the saying as referring to places of rest along the way on the soul’s journey
      to heaven—a view that has few adherents today. More commonly, parallels from Jewish apocalyptic
      literature are duly noted about “dwelling places of the holy ones” in heaven. Yet the emphasis here is
      not on separate or individual rooms or compartments, but simply on the fact that there are “many”
      such dwellings in heaven, more than enough for the disciples around the table, and all of the “many”
      who were said to have “believed” in Jesus in the course of the narrative (4:39, 41; 10:42; 11:45)—
      even those who will never go there (see 2:23; 8:30; 12:42)—and for all future believers (see 17:20,
      24).

One problem with Michaels’s rejection of the temple motif and trying to interpret that passage as if it were saying
that all believers will simply be in God’s heavenly household, so as to pose soteriological equality, is that in a
footnote to the above material, as to who qualifies for these dwellings, he says:

              See 1 Enoch 39.4–5 (OTP, 1.30–31); also, for example, 2 Enoch 61.2, “Many shelters have
      been prepared for people, good ones for the good, but bad ones for the bad, without number” (OTP,
      1.186), or 4 Ezra 7:95, where the righteous are “gathered into their chambers and guarded by angels
      in profound quiet” (OTP, 1.540). Even in the New Testament itself, see Luke 16:9, “make friends for
      yourselves from the unrighteous wealth, so that when it runs out they may receive you into the eternal
      dwellings (σκηναῖς, literally “tents”).

Lincoln (BNTC) expresses the same sentiment and same shortcomings:

      Despite ‘my Father’s house’ serving as a reference to the Jerusalem temple in 2:16, there is little
      warrant for finding here either a reference to rooms in the heavenly temple or to the believing
      community as a spiritual temple. The imagery is more general and taken from apocalyptic writings
      about the afterlife, cf. especially 1 Enoch 39:4–8, which speaks of ‘the dwelling-places of the holy,
      and the resting-places of the righteous’ in heaven, which was pictured as God’s house.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 131

         Black appeals to Jn 14:23 to support his notion that the promise applies to all believers. However, that verse
indicates the opposite.109 The realized eschatology of that verse is a precursor to the misthological eschatology of
14:2-3. Temporal fellowship with the Father now is conditioned on loving obedience to the Son and is requite for
the house fellowship with the Father and Son in the future. The Enochian backdrop, to which Michaels and Lincoln
appeal, leads to the same conclusion. Good people, that is, the experientially righteous, get good dwellings in these
hidden chambers. Bad people do not; they get something bad. These good dwellings are not for mere believers but
for obedient followers. Any LS notion that all believers are obedient followers should be thrown out as unworthy
of the context.
         Older commentators are more astute than some of their modern critics in discerning the distinct
misthological nature of the passage. Irenaeus (Adv. Haer. 5.361-2) has a section entitled, “MEN SHALL BE ACTUALLY
RAISED: THE WORLD SHALL NOT BE ANNIHILATED; BUT THERE SHALL BE VARIOUS MANSIONS FOR THE SAINTS,
ACCORDING TO THE RANK ALLOTTED TO EACH INDIVIDUAL. ALL THINGS SHALL BE SUBJECT TO GOD THE FATHER,
AND SO SHALL HE BE ALL IN ALL” (emphasis original), in which he discusses the misthological nature of Jn 14:2:

               And as the presbyters say, Then those who are deemed worthy of an abode in heaven shall go
      there, others shall enjoy the delights of paradise, and others shall possess the splendour of the city; for
      everywhere the Saviour shall be seen according as they who see Him shall be worthy.
               2. [They say, moreover], that there is this distinction between the habitation of those who
      produce an hundred-fold, and that of those who produce sixty-fold, and that of those who produce
      thirty-fold: for the first will be taken up into the heavens, the second will dwell in paradise, the last
      will inhabit the city; and that was on this account the Lord declared, “In My Father’s house are many
      mansions.” [Jn 14:2] For all things belong to God, who supplies all with a suitable dwelling-place;
      even as His Word says, that a share is allotted to all by the Father, according as each person is or shall
      be worthy. And this is the couch on which the guests shall recline, having been invited to the wedding.4
      The presbyters, the disciples of the apostles, affirm that this is the gradation and arrangement of those
      who are saved, and that they advance through steps of this nature; also that they ascend through the
      Spirit to the Son, and through the Son to the Father. (Insert original.)110

       As Bernard (ICC) surmises from Irenaeus: “According to the Elders, some good men will be counted
worthy of a διατρίβη [place to stay] in heaven; others will enjoy paradise; others ‘the city,’ the Saviour being seen
109
    Examples of God’s conditional love include Dt 7:12-13; 2Sam 7:15; 1Chron 17:13; 2Chron 6:14; 1Kgs 3:6; Ps
103:17; 146:8; Prov 8:18; Jn 14:21-23; 15:10; 16:27; Jude 1:21. For a much fuller discussion, see “Appendix 1.
Misthological Love” in TOD. It is sometimes assumed that Jn 17:23 is an example of God’s unconditional love,
where Jesus prayed that the world may know that the Father loves them even as He loves Jesus. But who is the
them? All believers? If so, even in that case, then having this level of love is conditioned on being a believer. It is
not unconditional. However, the analysis herein would lead one to suspect that the them refers to obedient followers,
that is, to faithful believers. If that is the case, as seems highly probable, perhaps even provable, then this level of
love is reserved for Bridal believers. The objection might be made that the Father would have to love everyone as
much as He loves Jesus in order for the Father to give His Son to die for everyone; otherwise, He would not give
His Son to die for everyone. Such an objection, if made, would be false. On the contrary, although the Father did
so love the world so as to give His Son to die for the whole world (as Jn 3:16 indicates), the reason He did so was
ultimately to produce a Bride for the Son. This love is what led the Father to sacrifice His Son. The Father loves the
Bride as He loves the Son, with both unconditional and conditional love. He loves the Bridal believers more than
non-bridal believers. If we suppose otherwise and pose that Jn 17:23 is saying that the Father loves all believers as
much as He does the Son, then we are faced with a problem. The conditional texts indicate that the Father loves
obedient believers more than disobedient believers. Thus, He loves bridal believers more than non-bridal believers.
Consequently, if we assert that the Father loves non-bridal believers as much as He loves the Son, then the Father
loves the Bride more than He does the Son. Since this conclusion is false, the premise that Jn 17:23 means that the
Father loves non-bridal believers as much as He loves the Son must be false.
110
    Irenaeus of Lyons, “Irenæus against Heresies,” in The Apostolic Fathers with Justin Martyr and Irenaeus, ed.
Alexander Roberts, James Donaldson, and A. Cleveland Coxe, vol. 1, The Ante-Nicene Fathers (Buffalo, NY:
Christian Literature Company, 1885), 567.

of them all.” The misthological delineation posed by Irenaeus is well suited to my misthological model in MMP5,
in which unfaithful believers are in the outskirts (outer darkness) of Heaven, whereas faithful believers have
habitations on Planet Paradise and even in the Heavenly City. These exegetical conclusions are confirmed by
eyewitness NDE testimony. Even so, the present study is seeking to move beyond that acknowledgment. Bernard

               Clement of Alexandria often has the word μονή, and always with allusion to Jn. 14:2. In Strom.
      vi. 14 he refers (as Papias does) to the thirtyfold, sixtyfold, hundredfold harvests, which he says hint
      at (αἰνίσσομαι) the three μοναί where the saints dwell according to their respective merits. So, again,

But what Bernard did not point out is that Clement associates this with the temple in his Strom 6:14 “Degrees of
Glory in Heaven,” wherein Clement says:

               Conformably, therefore, there are various abodes [Jn 14:2], according to the worth of those
      who have believed. To the point Solomon says, “For there shall be given to him the choice grace of
      faith, and a more pleasant lot in the temple of the Lord” [Wisd 3:14]. For the comparative shows that
      there are lower parts in the temple of God, which is the whole Church. And the superlative remains to
      be conceived, where the Lord is. These chosen abodes, which are three, are indicated by the numbers
      in the Gospel—the thirty, the sixty, the hundred. And the perfect inheritance belongs to those who
      attain to “a perfect man,” according to the image of the Lord. And the likeness is not, as some imagine,
      that of the human form; for this consideration is impious. Nor is the likeness to the first cause that
      which consists in virtue. For this utterance is also impious, being that of those who have imagined that
      virtue in man and in the sovereign God is the same. “Thou hast supposed iniquity,” He says, “[in
      imagining] that I will be like to thee.” But “it is enough for the disciple to become as the Master,” saith
      the Master. To the likeness of God, then, he that is introduced into adoption and the friendship of God,
      to the just inheritance of the lords and gods is brought; if he be perfected, according to the Gospel, as
      the Lord Himself taught.111

         Granted, Clement is not a bastion of FG consistency in this section since earlier in it, he says, “We do not
understand Him to say absolutely that those who have believed in any way whatever shall be saved, unless also
works follow.” Still, even in contemporary FG circles, it is frequently acknowledged that good works of some type
are typically expected to follow regeneration, even if those works are infrequent and even invisible. Regardless,
Clement is superior to many of his contemporary LS counterparts in perceiving the misthological nature of the
passage. The misthological perfect inheritance is for those believers who attain to become a perfect man
experientially. If Clement means to further imply that those believers who attain this misthological inheritance will
be given the inheritance of earthly lords and angelic gods in the form of some type of tribute, then he is very astute
in that assessment. In any case, his quotation above of the temple of the Lord from Wisdom of Solomon 3:14 might
be fully in accord with the thesis being pursued herein. The full verse reads: “And the eunuch who has not acted
unlawfully or meditated wickedness against the Lord will receive the exquisite gift of grace in return for his
steadfastness and a portion in the temple of the Lord to delight his heart the more” (Wisd 3:14). Winston (AYB)
comments on that verse: “Probably, in heaven, though it may refer to the future temple; cf. Pss 11:4; 18:7;
1 Bar 2:16; Rev 7:15. Excluded from the earthly sanctuary, he shall have a place in the heavenly one.” Clement
seems to assign three levels to the future heavenly temple obtainable by believers in accordance with their
degree of faithfulness. Barclay (NDSB) shares a similar summation:

      Irenaeus speaks of a certain interpretation of the sentence which tells how the seed that is sown

      citizens of ‘the city’. Clement of Alexandria believed that there were degrees of glory, rewards and

         If Jn 14:2-3 is purely misthological and yet no temple is intended, then the Father’s house would be best
interpreted as New Jerusalem (as some commentators pose), not simply as Heaven or the kingdom (as is posed by
other commentators). In that case, one possibility is that one might pose that Jesus is promising to prepare new
places in New Jerusalem for His faithful future church-age followers so that New Jerusalem is not limited to His
OT and Jewish followers. The “many mansions” would be merited, as Bernard (ICC) notes, and the postulation of
such mansions had historical precedence:

               The idea that there are “many mansions” in heaven, corresponding to different degrees of
      human merit, may not have been entirely new in Jewish religion. In the Sclavonic Book of the Secrets
      of Enoch (lxi. 2) we find: “In the world to come … there are many mansions prepared for men: good
      for the good; evil for evil” (cf. Ethiopic Enoch, xxxix. 4: “The mansions of the holy, and the resting-
      places of the righteous”). Charles dates the Sclavonic Enoch as between 1 and 50 A.D.; but we cannot
      be sure that it was known in Palestine during our Lord’s ministry.

         Bernard does not mention the temple in his discussion of Jn 14:2. Still, one cannot rule out his allowance
of this association, judging from his explanation of the dwelling places within it, since he says that “οἰκία [oikia:
house/temple, Jn 14:2] is hardly to be distinguished from οἶκος [oikos: house/temple, Jn 2:16], except that οἰκία is
the larger word, embracing the precincts of the house as well as the house itself. Cf. 8:35, 2 Cor. 5:1; and see on
[Jn] 2:16” (bold his). When one consults his earlier comments on Jn 2:16, to which he defers, his link to the temple
is asserted for Jn 14:2:

      But ἡ οἰκία τοῦ Πατρός μου (14:2), “the Dwelling Place of my Father,” in which are many mansions,
      is the heavenly temple, the Eternal and Changeless Home of the Eternal.
               The Temple is often described in the O.T. as “the house of God,” and Jesus so described it
      (Mk. 2:26, Mt. 12:4, Lk. 6:4). It was to make an unmistakable claim for Himself to substitute for this
      familiar expression the words “the house of My Father.”

Bernard’s vantage point is a variation of the proposition being explored herein. Even so, Bernard is inconsistent in
that, in his notes on Jn 14:2, he equates the Father’s house with Heaven. But Heaven is not changeless in that angels
and the Heavenly City have been created within it and Jesus has gone to make additional changes within it. If the
Father’s House is truly Eternal and Changeless, then it is not New Jerusalem. The Father’s House would have to be
His eternal changeless realm, which in my model would be His 7D Transdimensional Home. The new topos (place)
being added to it would introduce a change, thus be in a lower heavenly realm, perhaps in the 5D City or 3D City.

        If one wishes to adapt Bernard’s model to a more consistent one in conjunction with the one being
considered herein, then doing so is rather easy. The Father’s House includes His 7D Transcendent Home (which
is eternal but not changeless) and comprises His lower heavenly abodes as well (such as the 4D City and the
3D City). These abodes preexisted Jesus’ leaving, in accord with Jesus’ use of the present tense in Jn 14:2:
“In My Father’s house are many mansions.” The new place Jesus went to prepare is the 5D bridal chamber,
which is an addition to the 4D City and 3D City. The newly Prepared Place is foreshadowed by the Upper
Room Chamber of the earthly temple and signified by this promise being delivered in the Upper Room.
        If Jesus had used oikos in Jn 14:2 as He did in Jn 2:16 rather than switch to oikia, then the temple association
would have been hard to miss or dismiss. Even so, the differentiation is not so significant as to pose a stumbling
block to interpreters such as Bernard and McCaffrey, who maintain the temple association. Per Bernard’s
comments, one could pose that oikia is more comprehensive and includes the temple rooms and is not limited to the
temple proper. McCaffrey’s expounds upon oikia, (dwelling places) at length:

              (a) SIMILARITIES. To interpret the μοναί [monai] of Jn 14:2–3 as permanent dwelling-places
      would be in accord with the use of the μονή [mone] in the Jewish tradition. Within a house, as in Jn
      14:2–3, such a use of the term would clearly designate a “room”.
              However, the use of the term μονή in the Jewish tradition provides no basis for interpreting
      the μοναί of Jn 14:2–3 as “stations” on a road or “halting-places” (resting-places) on a journey. Thus
      there would be no support for interpreting these μοναί to designate the different stages through which
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 135
      Jesus would have to pass on his journey to the heavenly home, in line with the Jewish tradition of a
      division of the heavens. Moreover, these divisions are never referred to as “rooms”.
                Neither can the μοναί refer to the divisions of the universe. These μοναί of Jn 14:2–3 exist
      within the “house”. But the universe is never described as a “house” in the Jewish tradition, and its
      divisions are never referred to as “rooms”.
                However, the μοναί of Jn 14:2–3 might easily be interpreted with reference of the temple.
      These is ample evidence in the Jewish tradition for a division of the temple into “rooms”. So, too, the
      μοναί might be interpreted with reference to the eschatological temple, which also has many “rooms”
      in the Jewish tradition. Such an understanding would be all the more easy, since the idea of a “room”
      itself takes on eschatological significance within this same tradition.
                Moreover, the disciples might also have understood this OT image of the eschatological
      temple with many “rooms” as specified in a new way in line with the Jewish tradition represented by
      Enoch, with reference to the heavenly temple with its many rooms, or dwelling-places, for the just in
      the hereafter. The “rooms” in the Father’s house would be a suitable image to designate the possibilities
      for others to dwell there. To these same “rooms” Jesus would now promise immediate entrance to his
      disciples at some indefinite moment in the future, when he would return to take them into these eternal
      and heavenly dwelling-places reserved for the just in the eschatological temple. There they would
      await in anticipation of the final judgment, the resurrection on the last day.
                The connotation of “rest” or “repose” implicit in the use of the term μονή would also support
      an interpretation of “the house with many rooms” in line with the Jewish tradition of the temple as the
      “resting-place” of God and his people. This, too, might be seen as specified in a new way in line with
      the Jewish expectation of “resting-places” for the just in the heavenly temple. Jesus would then first
      enter into his “resting-place” in this temple, and later return to take his disciples to their “resting-
      places” with him there. Such an interpretation of μοναί as “resting-places” would also be clearly in
      context. In Jn 14:1a Jesus encourages his disciples: “let not your hearts be troubled” (Μὴ ταρασσέσθω;
      comp. also 14:27), and then supports his words with the promise of 14:2–3 that he will return to take
      the disciples later to the μοναί in the Father’s house. On the other hand, any hint even of future degrees
      of beatitude would be entirely out of place.
                Finally, the numerical adjective “many” (πολλαί) which qualifies the “rooms” might be seen
      in line with Jewish tradition where the temple is to become the centre of eschatological and universal
      salvation. But here again this tradition could be seen as slanted by John in line with the extra-biblical
      tradition represented by Enoch to refer to the heavenly temple as the eschatological and universal goal
      of salvation. In line with this tradition the πολλαί would then be understood quantitatively, and not
      qualitatively. The “rooms” would be numerous, providing accommodation for many.
                (b) DIFFERENCES. The term μονή [i.e., mone, which is the singular form of monai] is never
      used in the Jewish tradition to designate “rooms” in the temple, earthly or heavenly.
                Finally, in the Jewish tradition the heavenly temple is peopled with immortal beings. However,
      Jn 14:2–3 must be seen to abstract entirely from this aspect of the tradition. We cannot determine from
      our text whether or not the μοναί in the Father’s house are inhabited or not by celestial beings, or
      angels, prior to the preparation, or after it either. We only know that prior to the preparation, Jesus
      abides in the Father’s house permanently, and after the preparation it will be peopled by the disciples.
      The text says no more. We must respect this economy of the text. (Emphasis his, 73-75).

         In my earlier abbreviated citation of this quote from McCaffrey, I took issue with his not fully following
through on the present-tense implications of the text regarding the connection with the Where-I-AM place and
Where-I-Going preparation. However, one can accommodate his desire for ambiguity by enjoining it with Bernard’s
desire for eternal transcendence by posing that Jesus uses the broader term oikia to encompass both the Father’s
7D Transcendent location and the lower dimensions as well, where multiple existing dwellings were already
in place and where new additions might be added. Per McCaffrey’s comments, it would not be expanded to
include the universe. Although this is not exactly the model I will be pursuing in the present book, it is a model
worthy of contemplation and might even serve as an alternative vantage point.
         Various other commentators interpret the Father’s House (oikia) in Jn 14:2 in terms of the temple. Keener
(IVPBBC) asserts this proposition matter-of-factly:
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 136
              The “Father’s house” would be the temple (2:16), where God would forever dwell with his
      people (Ezek 43:7, 9; 48:35; cf. Jn 8:35). The “dwelling places” (NASB, NRSV) could allude to the
      booths constructed for the Feast of Tabernacles but probably refer to “rooms” (cf. NIV, TEV) in the
      new temple, where only undefiled ministers would have a place (Ezek 44:9–16; cf. 48:11).

Pfeiffer and Harrison (WBC) make the association between both the temple and the Upper Room:

               My Father’s house (cf. 2:16). The Temple at Jerusalem, with its vast courts and numerous
      chambers, suggests the antitype in heaven. Many mansions. Places of abode. The same word as in
      14:23. I would have told you. The disciple is warranted in assuming an adequate divine provision
      even when it is not stated. I go to prepare. As Peter and John had gone ahead to prepare the chamber

Westcott (CCGNT) acknowledges the relationship between the rooms in both the Father’s house and the temple:

               In my Father’s house] the spiritual and eternal antitype of the transitory temple (2:16) in which
      I have the right of a son (comp. 8:36). Even as the earthly temple included in its court many chambers
      (1 K. 6:5, 6:6, 6:10; Ezek. 41:6), so it is to be conceived of the heavenly, as far as earthly figures can
      symbolize that which is spiritual. (Emphasis his.)

Justifiably, in the assessment of various interpreters, the strong grounds for drawing a connection between the
Father’s house and the Heavenly Temple are not overturned by Jesus’ use of the broader term oikia in Jn 14:2,
rather than using the more standard term oikos, which is what He used in Jn 2:16. The more insightful debate would
be regarding the nature of oikia in this context. Does it refer to additional rooms added to the temple or does it mean
that nature of the Father’s house is not limited to the temple?
         However, an association completely missed by most commentators is that, as the perfect prophetic Son,
one would expect Jesus to build a house/temple for the Father to meet with His worshippers. God told David that
Solomon would be His son and build His house, that is, His temple (2Sam 7:13-14; 1Chron 22:10; 28:6). Solomon,
as an obedient son, was to build this temple as his Father’s house. Commentators frequently acknowledge that the
OT backdrop only finds limited immediate fulfillment in Solomon. Full fulfillment is realized in Jesus Christ. The
assumption, when discussed, seems to be that Jesus will build a spiritual temple. For instance, Barnes (BNONT;
2Sam 7:13) says: “He shall build a house, &c.] For the fulfilment of this in the person of Solomon, see 1 K. 8:16–
20. For its application to Christ, see John 1:12; Eph. 1:20–22; 1 Tim. 3:15; Heb. 3:6, &c.; and Zech. 6:12, 13.” And
he takes the temple in Zech 6:12 to be the Church with Christ as its foundation (cp. Is 28:16; 1 Cor 3:11; Eph 2:20-
21. So also LANG, TPC, and K&D, for example. However, Lindsey (BKC) takes a more literal approach to
Zech 6:12: “The promise that the Branch will build the temple of the LORD is probably limited to Messiah’s role
in establishing the millennial temple (cf. Isa. 2:2–4; 56:6–7; Ezek. 40–46; Micah 4:1–2)” (emphasis his). So also
Wiersbe. The literal approach is superior since Zech 6:13 repeats the building of the temple and associates it with
the Branch sitting and ruling on His throne. A literal throne presupposes a literal building. One’s position regarding
the dispensation will go a long way to determining one’s inclination toward a spiritual versus literal fulfillment, as
Constable explains: “Messiah, whom Joshua prefigured, would build the future temple for Yahweh (cf. Isa. 2:2–4;
56:6–7; Ezek. 40–43; Mic. 4:1–7; Hag. 2:6–9). For amillennialists, the temple equals the church, not a literal
temple.” Still, even a dispensationalist can knowledge both aspects: “He is now the builder of the spiritual temple
which is composed of living stones (Eph. 2:21; 1 Peter 2:5). But when He comes again there will be the building of
another temple” (Gaebelein, TAB).
         Surely, the secret upper chambers that Solomon was to build in the Earthly Temple as a house for his
Heavenly Father, which in turned foreshadowed the heavenly pattern of the Heavenly Temple, finds their fulfillment
in the place Jesus was leaving to prepare in the house of His Heavenly Father. Ezekiel’s millennial temple is not
the ultimate temple. For that matter, that future millennial temple is not part of the place Jesus is currently
building onto His Father’s house. Jesus is currently working on building up His bridal body spiritually and
building a spatial bridal abode. When He has finished building up this Bridal Body and Bridal Abode sufficiently
to come and rapture Her, He will come and take Her to that new abode He has been in the process of creating for
the past two thousand years. New Jerusalem will be the interface that the Bride uses to rule Earth, and clones of
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                           Page 137
New Jerusalem will be the interface that She uses to rule the capital planets of other galaxies. Just as the Bridegroom
can descend to these lower realms to sit on His throne and rule, She will likewise be able to descend to these lower
realms and share His rulership and Bema throne. However, neither He nor She will be limited to these lower thrones
or realms. The bridal suite He is preparing for Her is a place for them to retire in intimacy. This level of intimacy
with the Lord is our highest reward. Access to this higher level is reserved for those believers who treasure intimacy
with the Lord currently.

        Compound Condition
        As I was finishing the initial rough draft of this book, a friend, David Pearson, sent me an inquiry regarding
the compound condition expressed in Jn 14:3.112 This third-class conditional sentence has a compound protasis (if-
section) and a compound apodosis (then-section), followed by an explanation. Jesus is saying, in effect, if A & B,
then C1 & C2.

        Protasis:         If [A] I go and [B] prepare a place for you,
        Apodosis:         then I will (C1) come again and (C2) receive you to Myself.
        Explanation:      in order that where I am, you may be there also.

Jesus is conditioning His coming to take us to the Prepared Place (PP) on two things. His going and His preparing
this place for us. Jesus is not preparing this place for all believers, but for bridal believers. Pearson posed that Jesus
used the 3rd-class condition to indicate the conditional nature of our being with Jesus in the Where-I-AM-Place
(WIAP). Also, the Lord used the 3rd-class condition to denote probability, not unconditional certainty. Jesus’ coming
again is unconditionally certain. But His coming to receive us to His WIAP is conditioned on our mutual
preparation. We must prepare ourselves as the Bride to be taken to the Prepared Place by the Groom. Pearson’s
grammatical and misthological interpretation is excellent!
         I pointed out that some might try to counter his argument by noting that when a compound protasis is used,
both parts of the protasis are not necessarily required for the apodosis. I deal with this in my book Salvation (not
yet released). As explained therein, using the if-then symbol (), one might summarize that A & B  C does not
necessarily prove that A & B are both required for C. A compound protasis does not prove that both parts of the
protasis are required for the apodosis. A & B may not be related in the same way to the apodosis. For instance,
consider this conditional example: “If you (A) get on the bus and (B) sit down, then you will (C) reach your
destination.” In this case the C-result (i.e., the apodosis) seemingly is conditioned on a dual A-B-condition (a dual
protasis). Yet the B-part of the condition is not a co-condition for the fulfillment but is merely descriptive of the
expected auxiliary action.
         Nevertheless, Jn 14:3 would be more like the statement: “If you (A) buy a ticket and (B) get on the bus,
then (C) you will reach your destination.” Both part-A and part-B are necessary to experience part-C. In other
words, both A and B are required for C. Pearson’s grammatical analysis will stand because, through various
arguments, it is demonstratively true that the Prepared Place (PP) is a reward and the Where-I-Am-Place (WIAP)
is a reward (indeed, PP ⊂ WIAP).113
         Certain things were promised to occur unconditionally for believers as a result of Jesus’ going. For example,
Jesus would send the Holy Spirit: “If I do not go away, the Helper will not come to you; but if I go, I will send Him
to you” (Jn 16:7). Note the single protasis and single apodosis: (A) If I go, then (B) I will send the Holy Spirit to
you (A  B). The singular condition is placed upon Jesus; no condition is placed upon the believers for the sending
of the Holy Spirit to them. In stark contrast, as Pearson well notes, being received by Jesus in the WIAP is

112
   Personal correspondence, 1/31/2023.
113
   WIAP predates PP but will include PP; therefore, PP is a subset of WIAP. I pose that WIAP refers to transcendent
realms above the created realms which existed at the time Jesus left to create PP. Thus, WIAP now includes the 5D,
6D, and 7D realms. Jesus is inviting us to be His Bride and thus join Him in a new realm higher than those inhabited
by any preexisting creatures. Since Sin is a 4D entity with access limited to the 4D and 3D realms, it is not able to
contaminate these higher realms. The Bride cannot sin, so She will not be able to give birth to Sin in this new realm.
Future Sin is not even a theoretical possibility in the 5D realm. We will join Jesus in a realm that cannot even
remotely conceive the possibility of Sin. Trying to imagine sin in that realm would be like trying to imagine a round
square.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                Page 138
conditioned not only on His leaving but also on His preparing a place for you, which contextually would be a select
group of believers, namely, those believers who wash their feet (Jn 13:8) and who follow Jesus in service (Jn 12:26).
         This assessment is in full accord with the model I posed in TOD. In that misthological model, I posed that
this preparation was in association with New Jerusalem (NJ), so that PP = WIAP = NJ. Pearson was giving a
grammatical analysis that is in complete accord with that understanding. In that old model, I posed that the Father’s
House (FH) provided housing for all believers somewhere in the Kingdom of Heaven (KOH), that is, FH = KOH.
In that case, New Jerusalem would be a part/subset of the Father’s House, that is, NJ ⊂ FH.
                            Illustration 47. Cauley’s MMP6 Proposed Vantage Point
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                      Page 139
        The primary alternative model being posed herein, in MMP6, is that the Father’s House (FH) is the
Heavenly City (HC). One might suppose that the equation is simply FH = HC. However, unpacking that
simple equality sign will take some explanation since it requires distinguishing the Father’s House Then
(FHT) from the Father’s House Now (FHN) and realizing that the Heavenly City (HC) has a 3D, 4D, and 5D
aspects.
        Up until the time that Jesus left to make the Prepared Place (PP), the Father’s House comprised the 3D
Heavenly City and the 4D Heavenly City. The Father’s House Then (FHT), that is, before Jesus left, was limited to
the 3D and 4D aspects of the Heavenly City (FHT = 3D HC + 4D HC). Jesus left to create PP as a new addition to
the Father’s House. So, the Father’s House Now (FHN), that is, after Jesus left to prepare it, includes the Prepared
Place.

        Expressed in terms of the house and place, the equation would be: FHN = FHT + PP.
        Expressed in terms of the cities, the equation would be: FHN = 3D HC + 4D HC + 5D HC.

In short, the Father’s House Now (FHN) includes both the 3D City and the 4D City as well as the as-of-yet-
unpopulated 5D City, which is the place that Jesus is preparing for the Bride. Once the PP is ready (the timing of
which depends on multiple factors, such as the Bride being ready), the Father will send His Son to come and take
His Bride to Her bridal chamber.
         Okia is used comprehensively to refer lexically to the Father’s House as both a temple and house. Jesus did
not wish to limit the reference to the Father’s House to be perceived as a temple. Thus, in Jn 14:2, Jesus used the
more generic form, oikia, which commonly means house (as it does in Jn 4:53; 8:35; 11:31; 12:3). Had He used
oikos, the form more frequently associated with the Temple (in the LXX and in Jn 2:16-17), then He would have
been more readily perceived to be talking about the Father’s House as a temple—even though oikos can be used to
refer to a house (Jn 7:53; 11:20)—since God’s House was understood to be the Temple (whether on Earth or in
Heaven). The problem, though, with using oikos is that it might have been misperceived as picturing the Father’s
dwelling exclusively as a temple rather than inclusively as a house.
         Regardless, per my preferred model, interpreting God’s House as either a house or a temple in Jn 14:2
would logically include both the 3D and 4D aspects of the Heavenly Jerusalem. The Father’s House already
encompassed both the 3D City and the 4D City before Jesus made a promise to leave and prepare a place for His
followers. These cities were already in existence. The new aspect that Jesus was promising to prepare upon His
leaving was to add to the Father’s House its 5D aspect via the addition of the bridal chamber, which is an add-on to
the preexisting structures. Using oikia rather than oikos was conducive to including the add-on-bridal-chamber
perspective, adding it to the pre-existing structures pictured as a house, making it easy for commentators to
acknowledge the legitimacy of this vantage point. For example, Evans (TEBC) interprets the passage in terms of
this house image: “According to Jewish wedding custom, the father would add rooms onto his house for his newly
married son. Jesus wasn’t abandoning them but heading out to get their eternal home ready.” This add-on vantage
point is the most easily discerned vantage point and is shared by various interpreters. I consider it the first layer
vantage point. However, a secondary and deeper vantage point is also intended by the Lord, in which the add-on
bridal chamber promised in the Upper Room is perceived to be the Upper Room Chamber in the Heavenly Temple.
Both the house and temple vantage points are misthological, but the composite yields a multidimensional spatial
understanding of the entailed misthology.
         New Jerusalem is a misthological reward limited to faithful believers, to be sure. Nevertheless, New
Jerusalem is not the focus of Jesus’ misthological promise in this passage. Instead, Jesus is promising something
new. The Father’s House, thus the Heavenly City (3D and 4D New Jerusalem), has existed for thousands of years.
Jesus was leaving to prepare a completely new bridal chamber (BC) as an addition to the Father’s House, as
indicated by both the bridal and temple typology. Namely, He was going to make a PP in His WIAP that could be
accessed from FH/NJ. This PP is exclusively for the Bride. No angels can enter it. This PP is accessed from 3D and
4D NJ, but it is in a higher dimension than these 3D and 4D aspects of NJ. The Father’s two-chambered house
would become a three-chambered house because of this new addition. This assessment is also in full accord with
Pearson’s dual conditional protasis. The primary difference between my old model and my new model is that the
older model poses that NJ is the PP/BC, whereas the new model poses that PP/BC is a new additional reward. Both
models argue that NJ is a reward. Indeed, both models would pose that 3D NJ is the highest spatial reward in this
3D dimension. Hence, it is quite understandable that the misthological focus of Scripture is on the 3D NJ, as the
highest spatial reward perceivable within this 3D realm. The new model poses, though, that there is a higher reward
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 140
in a higher realm. This new spatial reward is so special that it is inaccessible by 4D angels. The proposition posed
herein is that this superior reward is a 5D NJ. Just as there is a 3D Earthly Jerusalem, a 3D Heavenly Jerusalem,
and a 4D Heavenly Jerusalem, one may postulate that there is a 5D Heavenly Jerusalem. This new addition is
reserved for the Bride. She alone will have a sufficiently high enough nature to share this location with Her Lord.

                                                      Jn 17:24
        Now we come to the hard part. Jesus’ last Where-I-AM statement is in Jn 17:24, which is part of Jesus’s
High Priestly Prayer, which is a problematic battleground passage between advocates of unconditional security
versus advocates of conditional security. The passage contains a variety of petitions that might be taken as
unconditional absolutes. Before traversing the minefield, let’s begin by surveying the battlefield.

                                             Arguments by Securitists
       We will begin by surveying the typical arguments made by those who advocate unconditional security. The
arguments range from simple to complex. As a securitist, I have made some of these same arguments in TOD. At
the same time, being a misthologist, I have augmented soteriological arguments with misthological arguments.

        Eaton
        Eaton appeals to a connection between Jn 11:42 and Jn 17:15 in order to affirm the believer’s security:

               Jesus’ prayer that his disciples be kept from the evil one (17:15) prompts a further thought.
      Earlier in the gospel Jesus has said, ‘You hear me always’ (11:42). It is not conceivable, the gospel
      implies, that any prayer of Jesus should fail. If he prays for his people to be protected from the devil,
      they will be protected from the devil. It is futile to interject, ‘But they may fail to keep themselves.’ Is
      not their failure to keep themselves a victory for the devil? If Jesus prays it will not happen, can we
      say that it might?114

         Barker
         Similarly, regarding Jn 17:11, Barker argues, “Jesus is praying and asks that the Father keep those which
He has given Him. Since Jesus is certainly praying according to the will of the Father His prayer will be answered”
(p. 50).115 Securitists might be able to maintain this argument for the keep petitions in the prayer, but what about
the other petitions within it? Per the Jn 11:42 line of reasoning, such petitions all stand or fall together. Barker also
cites the petition in Jn 17:23 for unity in favor of soteriological unity (p. 81), but some of the passages he cites in
support of that passage would be better understood as referring to experiential unity; therefore, this part of this
argument is little more than proof-texting. Likewise, his appeal to Jn 14:2-3 and Jn 17:24, as being soteriological,
is just proof-texting (p. 92).
         Barker further points out that, in Jn 17:20-21, “Jesus here extends his prayer to include all believers” (p. 50).
He adds a brief quote from Strombeck in support. I will provide the full context of Strombeck’s quote from his
book, Shall Never Perish, so that the full force of his argument might be felt:

              Jesus prayed, “Holy Father, keep through Thine own Name those whom Thou hast given Me”
      (John 17:11). That this is in behalf of all believers is made perfectly clear by His words uttered later
      on in the same prayer: “Neither pray I for these alone, but for them also which shall believe on Me
      through their word” (v. 20). In this prayer, then, Jesus asks the Father to keep in His own Name all
      who are saved through believing on Him. If a single saved person becomes lost, this prayer is not fully
      answered. Therefore the Arminian, by saying that some are lost, says that the Father does not
      completely answer the prayer of the Son. Such a condition would demand either that the Father is
      unable to answer it or that the Son has not met the requirements of the Father in order to receive an
      answer. If the Father cannot answer, He is not omnipotent and consequently He is not infinite. If the
      Son has not met some condition necessary to receive answer to His prayer, then He is not fully in the

114
    Michael Eaton, No Condemnation: A New Theology of Assurance (Dowers Grove, IL: InterVarsity Press, 1995),
201-202.
115
    Harold Barker, Secure Forever (Neptune, NJ: Loizeaux Brothers, 1974), 50.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                             Page 141
         will of the Father. If not fully in the will of the Father, He is not absolutely righteous and consequently
         not infinite.
                  Thus the very nature of the Godhead is assailed by the argument that one who has been saved
         can be lost. Both His righteousness and His unipotent are questioned, and either one or the other is
         denied.116 (Emphasis his.)

Strong words. Weak logic. The argument is only as good as its premise. If Jesus is not praying for their unconditional
security, then the Arminian can easily counter that this entire argument misses the point.

         Ironside
         Ironside’s book, Full Assurance, is a classic for securitist assurance. But it does not have his argument
regarding Jn 17. For that, one should consult his commentary on John. As to Jesus’ statement that He kept the
Eleven in Jn 17:12, Ironside (AOTG) says: “People call this the doctrine of the perseverance of the saints, but I
rather like to think of it as the perseverance of the Saviour.” Ironside attempts to provide the same type of assurance
to Jesus’ prayer for our unity:

         It is sometimes said that this prayer of our Lord’s has not been answered, because Christians are so
         scattered and divided. This, however, is not true. The unity of which He here speaks is the unity of
         life—family unity; and all believers are one in this sense. But it is a blessed thing to manifest this
         practically. It is as you and I realize our relationship to the Father that we are kept in the manifestation
         of this unity. Then there will be unity in testimony. We see how this prayer was answered from that
         standpoint in the early days of the Church, when the apostles went forth witnessing to Christ the Lord,
         confirming the Word by signs. Thus with great power gave the apostles witness to the death, burial,
         and resurrection of our Lord Jesus Christ. It was unified testimony based upon a common life in Him.

Obviously, following this line of thought, Jesus’ prayer was answered dualistically. Legally and organically, it is
answered fully in that all believers are legally and organically part of the same family. Experientially, though, the
prayer has not experienced full realization. The Lord’s prayer for a unified testimony is only answered partially and
in many cases poorly. Ironside (AOTG) proceeds to make the same point in his comments on Jn 17:17-21:

                 People say sometimes that this prayer of our Lord has not been answered and they point to the
         many different sects and denominations among professed Christians. Of these we very well are
         ashamed. And yet, despite them all, wherever real Christians get together they enjoy fellowship in the
         precious things of Christ. It is when we allow ourselves to be occupied with minor questions which do
         not profit, that our differences come in. We are all one in Christ. The fact that Satan, our great
         adversary, has set members of the same family to quarrelling with each other is sad indeed, and should
         cause us to bow our heads in humiliation and self-judgment before God. As our unity is manifested in
         a practical way our testimony has power with men. On the other hand nothing is so calculated to
         stumble the unsaved as finding that Christians are unkind and quarrelsome in their dealings with each
         other….
                 May we realize more and more our unity and act in accordance with it, that thus the world
         may believe.

         The experiential realization of this unity has not been fully or unconditionally answered. Ironside also
acknowledges that Jn 17:17 is dealing with practical sanctification. Is the Lord’s prayer for practical sanctification
answered on behalf of all believers, even apostates? Any securitistic attempt to argue for unconditional preservation
based on the petitions in this prayer should address the inconsistencies that such an argument encounters from the
conditional and partial nature of the answers for the Lord’s similar petitions for unification and sanctification. As it
is, Ironside (AOTG) undermines the believer’s assurance via his interpretation of what Jesus means when He says
in Jn 17:6, “They have kept Thy word.”


116
      J. F. Strombeck, Shall Never Perish (Eugene, OR: Harvest House Publishers, 1982), 183-184.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 142
              How precious to the heart of the Son of God must be the Father’s gift of His redeemed. He
      says, “Thine they were, and Thou gavest them Me and they have kept Thy Word.” He will be able to
      say that of every one of us when He brings us home at last, in spite of all our blunders, our failures,
