      and our sins. As possessors of the divine nature, there is in every Christian a desire to do the will of
      God and to keep His Word. One who lacks this has never been regenerated.

Are we to base our assurance of salvation from Hell on the strength of our desire to do God’s will and keep His
word? No! Ironside’s book, Full Assurance, is pretty good. His interpretation of Jn 17:11 is very bad. Even false
believers can have a desire to do God’s will and keep His word. Conditionalists have a right to insist upon better
answers from securitists regarding this prayer.

          Rokser
          In his book, Shall Never Perish Forever, Rokser has a chapter devoted to the subject, “God’s Answers to
Jesus Christ’s Prayer.” Rokser argues that Jn 17:11 refers to the soteriological keeping of genuine believers in
contradiction to Judas who was soteriologically lost because he was never a believer (Jn 17:12). I concur that Judas
was never a believer, so I find this argument very attractive. Even so, it has problems. Just because Judas was
soteriologically lost because he was not a believer does not prove that soteriological preservation is necessarily
what is being requested for believers. The loss Judas experienced might have been more than just eternal damnation
in Hell, and thus the petition might have been for something over and above salvation from eternal damnation. Or
the focus on the loss that Judas experienced might be exclusively soteriological, yet Jesus’ petition for the believers
to be kept might have been for something over and above soteriological protection.
          When it comes to Jn 17:15, Rokser believes that the petition that the believers be kept from the devil is for
their “practical sanctification.” Likewise, the Lord’s petition in Jn 17:17,19 is for the “purpose” of their
“progressive sanctification” (italics his, 98-99). Note that the hina denotes purpose, not guaranteed result. Clearly,
this type of sanctification is not unconditionally assured, even by Jesus’ prayer. As for the requested unity in
Jn 17:21-23, Rosker poses that first of all, it is “positional unity” but, secondly, its “practical purposes…were to
promote” fellowship and evangelism (p. 99). Rokser enjoins soteriological and experiential unity together as a
duality. But are both necessarily entailed? What if only the latter is in view? Then unity is not unconditionally
guaranteed. As to the Where-I-AM and see-My-glory petitions in Jn 17:24, Rokser quotes Jn 14:1-3 and equates
these requests with the guarantee of “going to Heaven!” (Emphasis his, 100.) An Arminian could easily ask, “Why
not practice good hermeneutics and cite Jn 12:26 instead and thus interpret the unclear text by means of the clear
text?” In Jn 12:26, Jesus clearly says that being with Him is conditioned on following Him in service. The
presumption, then, should be that being with Him in Jn 14:1-3 is likewise conditional. A misthopologist could easily
ask, “Why assume that the Father’s house is all of Heaven? Why not interpret it to be a part of Heaven.” Granted,
as Rokser states, “in order for you to behold the glory of Jesus Christ in Heaven” (p. 100), you must be in Heaven.
Still, this does not necessarily mean that you will be in the Reward City. Unfaithful believers can see Christ in
Heaven when He is outside the Reward City. Rokser would, of course, object that he does not believe that New
Jerusalem is a reward limited to faithful believers. In any event, unanswered questions still remain, Is Jesus talking
about an unconditional experience of seeing Him in glory? Might seeing Him in the full manifestation of His glory
be a privilege reserved for faithful believers? Might the demonstration of glory that Jesus is requesting be a
transcendent degree of glory in a transcendent location to which unfaithful believers cannot aspire? After all, a 4D
being cannot see a 5D manifestation of glory. Then again, even if the request pertains to all believers, is the privilege
that Jesus is requesting a lower degree of glory unconditionally guaranteed to all believers?
          Rokser proceeds with what he assumes is a conclusive argument:

               Thus, we have observed in this high priestly prayer of Jesus Christ that He prayed for the
      preservation, positional unity, and heavenly glorification of not only His eleven believing disciples
      but also His future Church. This begs the question, “Did God the Father hear and answer His requests?”
      Just before the raising of Lazarus, Jesus prayed to the Father.

               Then they took away the stone from the place where the dead man was lying. And Jesus
               lifted up His eyes and said, “Father, I thank You that You have heard Me. And I know
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 143
              that You always hear Me, but because of the people who are standing by I said this, that
              they may believe that You sent Me.” (John 11:41-42)

                God the Father always heard and favorably answered every prayer Jesus Christ uttered. And
      if this is true, the believer’s permanent salvation and heavenly glorification is eternally secure because
      of the prayer of Jesus Christ. (Italics his, 100-101.)

The answer to the question posed by Rokser may be found in the preliminary questions that are begged by his own
discussion. Did Jesus not pray for the progressive sanctification and experiential unity of not only His eleven
believing disciples but also His future Church? Did God the Father hear and answer His requests? Rokser acts as
though Jesus’ prayer unconditionally guarantees the result, resulting in unconditional security. However, his own
analysis leads to the observation that Jesus’ petitions may be dealing with conditional purposes rather than
guaranteed results. Why are the soteriological results, if that is what they are, guaranteed but the progressive and
experiential purposes not guaranteed? The Arminian will ask, “Does the conditional nature of the latter not indicate
the conditional nature of the former?” Arguing for unconditional security via means of these petitions might
backfire.

       Dillow
             Dillow makes much the same questionable argument for unconditional security in Jn 17:11;
      17:15,24:

               Jesus asks the Father to keep them from damnation. As shown elsewhere these Gr words
      translated “perished” and “perdition” do not refer to damnation (see p. 288). Jesus kept them from
      apōleia [on earth] when he was “with them.” This was a keeping from spiritual ruin and being lost
      [experientially] like the Prodigal Son, that is, a keeping from losing one’s life, not damnation. All
      Twelve had been given to Him, including Judas. Jesus had warned His disciples about the danger of
      losing their lives (Matthew 16:25). Judas was the son of “ruin and lostness” destined for “loss and
      ruin,” which probably meant ruin in this life as well as damnation. It is unlikely that Judas was saved!
      All Eleven will be kept by the Father for eternal life.
               The Son asks the Father to keep saved those whom the Father has given to the Son. Even if
      the Father had no personal interest in keeping them saved, which He does, He must respond to the
      prayer of the Son, whose prayers are always answered (John 11:42). Jesus prays that we will be kept
      from hell (John 17:15) and that we will be with Him in heaven (John 17:20, 24). Will not the prayers
      of the Son of God be answered?
               Not only has God the Father committed Himself by oath to guarantee the eternal security of
      His elect, but God the Son, through His active and passive obedience has made our final arrival into
      heaven certain. Our eternal security does not depend on us. (P. 734.)

         One problem with Dillow’s argument is that he has crippled it. On the one hand, he asserts that Jesus is
asking that they be kept from soteriological damnation, yet he acknowledges (per his discussion on p. 288) that, in
the case of believers, the loss could pertain to being kept from experiential spiritual ruin and the loss of eternal
rewards instead. Just because Judas was soteriologically lost since he was an unbeliever does not prove that Jesus
is asking the Father to keep believers from being lost soteriologically. The greater problem with his argument is
that Dillow argues that the in-Me (en emoi) expression that John uses sixteen times refers to “fellowship, a unity of
purpose rather than an organic connection” in John’s writings, so it does not “refer specifically and only to
salvation” in GJ. John uses it as an expression of communion rather than organic union (p. 602). Therefore, Dillow
proceeds to pose that Jesus’ in-Me petition in Jn 17:21-23 is for experiential unity:

              The Father is in the Son, and the Son is in the Father. This prayer describes not an organic
      connection, but a life of communion, a oneness of purpose. Jesus wants them to have an experience of
      unity because that observable unity will prove to the world that they are His disciples, models of
      Christian love (17:23). If being “in him” referred only to an organic connection, it would prove
      nothing. But if it refers to an observable experiential unity of purpose and fellowship, this would have
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 144
      great testimonial impact. It is a unity they do not yet have but must be “brought to.” For John, to be
      “in Me” is simply to have unity of fellowship and purpose with Him, not organic connection or
      commonality of essence. (P. 603.)

         But what happened to Dillow’s OSAS argument from John 11:42 that Jesus’ prayer must be answered
affirmatively by the Father and independently of us? Obviously, as Dillow acknowledges, the temporal experiential
unity of believers leaves a lot to be desired and is very much dependent upon them “being diligent to preserve the
unity of the Spirit in the bond of peace” (Eph 4:3). Dillow acknowledges that all believers have a soteric organic
unity; however, for good reason, he insists that the unity for which Jesus prays is experiential rather than merely/only
soteriological. If the fulfillment of Jesus’ prayer is temporally experiential and conditional in Jn 17:21,23, then why
think that it is any different in the rest of His prayer? To be sure the Eleven were united in purpose as a result of the
resurrection, but Jesus’ prayer for unity was also for those would believe in Him through their word (Jn 17:21).
Experiential unity in the body of Christ is sorely lacking on many occasions. Therefore, any supposition that Jesus’
prayer must find unconditional fulfillment is open to suspicion.

        Kendall
        Kendall ends his book defending OSAS with a heartwarming illustration of the security he envisions in the
passage:

                 On the very day I first realized that God loved me with an “everlasting love” (Jer 31:3), there
      came to me with equal force yet another discovery: Jesus Christ was literally praying for me at God’s
      right hand. I had heard of that truth before then, of course, but it was not until October 31, 1955, that
      it was brought home so plainly and personally to me. What I did not fully grasp at the time was that it
      is the continual intercession of Christ that ultimately guarantees the eternal salvation of every believer.
                 The intercessory work of our Lord Jesus Christ is by itself sufficient, indeed absolute, proof
      that those who once truly believe on Him could not possibly come to perdition. To suggest that once
      saved, always saved is not true borders on showing contempt for all that Jesus is doing for us in heaven.
      “I pray for them . . . and none of them is lost, but the son of perdition; that the scripture might be
      fulfilled” (John 17:9, 12). “Who is he that condemneth? It is Christ that died, yea rather, that is risen
      again, who is even at the right hand of God, who also maketh intercession for us” (Romans 8:34).
                 What is Jesus doing at God’s right hand? He is praying for us! If you want to know the essential
      content of our Lord’s prayer for us in heaven, read all of John 17. The writer of the epistle to the
      Hebrews says, “Wherefore he is able also to save them to the uttermost that come unto God by him,
      seeing he ever liveth to make intercession for them” (Hebrews 7:25). The word uttermost is from the
      Greek panteles, which, when used adverbially (as it is here), means “through all time,” or “forever.”
                 The real question is, Does our Lord get His prayers answered? Jesus knew that Peter would
      betray Him. “Simon, behold, Satan hath desired to have you, that he may sift you as wheat: but I have
      prayed for thee, that thy faith fail not: and when thou art converted, strengthen thy brethren” (Luke
      22:31-32). Did the Lord get His prayer answered? Of course He did! Look at Peter from the day of our
      Lord’s resurrection onwards….
                 A few days after my family and I settled into our new home in London, I drove my son to his
      new school. He was dreading the thought of entering a new school in a land in which none of us ever
      dreamed of remaining. It would mean strange surroundings, totally new teachers and children, and my
      son loathed even getting out of the car. I continued to coax him to go to the playground. He sat still in
      the car. Then I said, “Son, Daddy will be praying for you all day long. Whenever you feel scared,
      remember that Daddy at that moment is praying for you.” His countenance changed to one of great
      relief. Without my saying another word, he opened the car door and walked to his school without
      looking back. He knew I meant what I said. He did not trust his own prayers—he was living by mine.
                 “Neither pray I for these alone, but for them also which shall believe on me through their word
      . . . that they also, whom thou hast given me, be with me where I am; that they may behold my glory”
      (John 17:20, 24).
                 Salvation is a free gift (Romans 6:23). If I must know whether or not I am saved by the state
      of my own faithfulness, it is no gift at all. There could also be boasting. But salvation is “not of works,
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                           Page 145
         lest any man should boast” (Ephesians 2:9). And yet we “are his workmanship, created in Christ Jesus
         unto good works, which God hath before ordained that we should walk in them quotation marks
         (Ephesians 2:10 )....
                  This book has sought to show the seriousness of the biblical mandate to live to holy living and
         the consequences when one does not live a holy life….
                  But once saved, always saved. Some will be saved by fire.117 (Emphasis his.)

         Kendall and Dillow
         Two shortcomings in Kendall’s argument are worth noting. First, are we to think that Jesus’ prayer only
saves us from Hell fire but provides no help in saving us from being “saved by fire” (1Cor 3:15)? Does Jesus intend
to leave us on our own when it comes to being saved from Bema fire? If Jesus’ prayer is limited to what is granted
unconditionally, then it cannot render us aid that is conditional. Was not even Jesus’ prayer for Peter’s faith to
prevail conditioned on Peter’s freewill response? Granted, through the Lord’s middle knowledge, Peter’s freewill
response might be assured in response to Jesus’ prayer. But one cannot make the same argument for all believers
since some believers do fail to persevere in the faith. Second, the Lord’s present intercession for us in such passages
as Heb 7:25 has nothing to do with keeping us saved soteriologically. Contrary to Dillow (pp. 738-739), Satan is
not wasting his time as a prosecuting attorney in Rev 12:10 trying to get us disbarred from Heaven only to lose
every case. Rather, Satan will be accusing us at the Bema, arguing that we deserved only to be saved by fire and
have all our works burned up (1Cor 3:15). Misthological security, not soteriological security, is at stake. Satan will
win most of these cases because the only believers who will be safe from his accusations are those believers who
daily draw to God near in confession of their sins (Heb 7:25; 1Jn 1:9).

          Tanner
          Tanner (GNTC) well comments on Heb 7:25:

                   He is able to save to the uttermost those who come to God through Him, since He ever lives
         to make intercession for them. By using the phrase save to the uttermost does he mean that Jesus is
         saving sinners from the penalty of sin (speaking soteriologically)? If so, then this verse is saying that
         He does that because of His intercessory ministry that He continues to carry out on their behalf.
                   Yet that is precisely what the author is not saying. It would be quite theologically inaccurate
         to say that a Christian’s personal salvation from the penalty of sin depends on Christ’s ongoing
         intercession for him. That depends solely on the work of Christ on the Cross! That is why Jesus cried
         out from the Cross, “It is finished,” and why Paul in Col 2:13–14 could proclaim that God has forgiven
         us all our trespasses and wiped out the handwriting of requirements that was against us, “having nailed
         it to the Cross.” That kind of salvation is not dependent on the Cross plus ongoing intercession!
                   More likely, the author has something different in mind than personal salvation from the
         penalty of sin. Since the verb “to save” (sōzō) and the noun “salvation” (sōtēria) are used
         predominantly in Hebrews in regard to an eschatological salvation, it is very possible that he is thinking
         of a future salvation connected with the Second Coming of Christ….
                   The phrase to the uttermost (eis to panteles) does not have a temporal force (as in the NASB’s
         “forever”), but rather conveys the idea of “completely.” For those Christians who rely on Christ’s
         priestly role and intercession for them, they find that He is able to carry them completely through all
         trials and difficulties to arrive at their eschatological salvation, qualified to enter the greater rest.
         (Emphasis his.)

         The only tweak I might make to Tanner’s argument is to note that panteles could mean both completely and
forever in this context. Granted, it does mean completely/fully in its only other occurrence in the NT: “She was bent
over and could not fully straighten herself” (Lk 13:11; ESV). Yet, in the context of Hebrews, in conjunction with
the affirmation that rewards are eternal, the expanded gloss might be made: “He is able to save forever to the
uttermost [panteles] those believers coming to Him, since He always [pantote] lives to make intercession for them”
(Heb 7:25; TM). The temporal force of pantote finds its uttermost eternal result in panteles. He is able to save such

117
      R. T. Kendall, Once Saved, Always Saved (Chicago: Moody Press, 1983), 203-206.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 146
believers misthologically forever. Misthological salvation is contingently at stake and eternally assured for those
believers who avail themselves of the temporal means of acquiring that salvation.118

        Radmacher
        In his book Salvation, Radmacher makes the typical OSAS assumption and commendably attempts to be
consistent by locally applying the logical extension of that argument to all the petitions within the prayer:

               When Jesus promised that believers will never perish, He backed up that promise with His
      prayer in John 17. And as Jesus said, His prayers are always heard by the Father (11:41-42)….
               ….In verse [Jn 17:]20, He said, “I do not pray for these alone, but also for those who will
      believe in Me through their word.” So God the Son, who promises eternal life, buttresses that promise
      with His own prayer—and His prayer never goes unanswered because He always prays in the Father’s
      will.
               What did Jesus pray for His own? First, He prayed that God the Father would preserve and
      protect each believer. “Holy Father, keep through Your name those whom You have given Me”
      (17:11). In this request Jesus was asking the Father to exercise His sovereign power, not according to
      our merit, but in accord with His character and commitment to them. Other requests Jesus made in this
      prayer are that believers would experience His joy (17:13), that they would be protected from the
      influence of the world, the present evil system that opposes God (17:15), that they would be united
      (17:21), that they would “behold My glory which You have given Me” (17:24). To be with Him and
      to behold His glory refers to their being in heaven.
               Would a prayer by God the Son to God the Father go unanswered? Hardly! To deny the
      safekeeping of the believer is to imply that this prayer of the Son of God will not be answered.119

        The glaring problem for using the petition in Jn 17:11 as an argument for unconditional security is that the
other petitions are not so easily interpreted as being unconditional in their fulfillment. Fullness of joy is contingent
upon the believers’ loving obedience and answered prayers (Jn 15:10-11; 17:13). Protection from worldly influence
requires that we “do not love the world, nor the things in the world” (1Jn 2:15). The unity for which Jesus prays is
one that can be seen by the world so that the world may believe. This requires that we let the light of our unity shine
instead of hiding under a basket of disunity (cp. Mt 5:15-16). Being with Christ in the same locality of glory varies
misthologically according to the degree of the believer’s obedience.

        Radmacher and Derickson
        Radmacher apparently had a change of heart in Disciplemaker, the book that he and Derickson coauthored
and released the following year.120 They argue that the petition in Jn 17:11 is that they be “protected from physical
harm as well as destructive disunity” (p. 278). These believers already had essential unity (i.e., soteriological organic
unity). What Jesus prays for is their existential unity (i.e., experientially being what they are), “to experience perfect
communion” (p. 279). The petition for joy in Jn 17:13 means that “Jesus wants them to experience His joy….Just

118
    In passing, I will note a conceptual parallel for telos, further explained in DLAF. When Jesus died for humanity,
it was primarily for His Bride. In the Upper Room, He told His disciples: “Greater love has no one than this, that
one lay down his life for his friends. You are My friends, if you do what I command you” (Jn 15:13-14). Jesus’
greatest affectual love was in laying down His life for His friends, not for His enemies. He loved His friends to the
“full extent of His love” (Jn 13:1; NIV)—in full measure, fully, completely (FB), to the uttermost (GING).
Objectively, in laying down His life for both His enemies and His friends, Jesus made the same sacrifice for both.
As a result, some wrongly presume that He loves both equally. Subjectively, however, Jesus’ affection for His
friends was greater in anticipation of the eternal fellowship He would have with His friends. Additionally, love is
in proportion to perceived (potential) value. His friends would have greater value than His enemies. According, we
may deduce, in accordance with Jesus’ statements, that His sacrifice for His enemies and friends was not offered
from equal love for both.
119
    Earl D. Radmacher, Salvation (Nashville: Word Publishing, 2000), 195-196.
120
    Gary Derickson and Earl Radmacher, The Disciplemaker: What Matters Most to Jesus (Salem, OR: Charis Press,
2001).
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 147
as He promised them joy, He now reminds His Father of His desire that they have that which He has promised”
(p. 283). As to the petition for protection (i.e., being kept) in Jn 17:15, Radmacher and Derickson reject Dillow’s
interpretation of soteriological salvation from Hell. The context and articular form refer to being kept safe from
Satan’s world system. They conclude that discussion by quoting Carson regarding the surety of safety provided by
God (p. 286). However, Carson’s explanation leaves an unanswered question:

      The Christians’ task, then, is not to be withdrawn from the world, nor to be confused with the world
      (hence the reminder of v. 16, repeating the thought of v. 14b), but to remain in the world, maintaining
      witness to the truth by the help of the Paraclete (15:26–27), and absorbing all the malice that the world
      can muster, finally protected by the Father himself, in response to the prayer of Jesus.
               Doubtless Christians in John’s day were forced to ponder the implications of this prayer. So
      also were those who were contemplating the possibility of becoming Christians. The cosmic, spiritual
      nature of the conflict is laid bare. The followers of Jesus are permitted neither the luxury of
      compromise with a ‘world’ (cf. notes on 1:9) that is intrinsically evil and under the devil’s power, nor
      the safety of disengagement. But if the Christian pilgrimage is inherently perilous, the safety that only
      God himself can provide is assured, as certainly as the prayers of God’s own dear Son will be
      answered. (PNTC)

         One problem with punting the hermeneutical football to Carson at this point in their discussion is that
Radmacher and Derickson leave the question unanswered as to the nature of this assured safety. Do they mean to
imply that no true believers will succumb to worldly temptations? After all, when Carson’s discussion is consulted,
the only real clue he gives in response to that question is regarding the petition for unity in Jn 17:23: “It is a unity
necessarily present, at least in nuce [in a nutshell], amongst genuine believers; it is a unity that must be brought to
perfection.” If Carson is presuming a Reformed premise of perseverance in holiness among all believers, then that
presumption is easily discounted by other considerations, such as acknowledging that genuine believers can commit
apostasy. Still, one need not necessarily engage in a debate regarding that theological football to point out the
obvious. After asserting this assured security in Jn 17:15-16, Carson proceeds to describe experiential sanctification
in Jn 17:17. Even if this experiential sanctification is similarly assured, it is not unconditionally secured. The
experience of this sanctification is contingent on their response, as Carson (PNTC) points out: “Jesus’ followers
will be ‘set apart’ from the world, reserved for God’s service, insofar as they think and live in conformity with the
truth…. no-one can be ‘sanctified’ or set apart for the Lord’s use without learning to think God’s thoughts after
him, without learning to live in conformity with the ‘word’ he has graciously given.” That being the case, Paul’s
description of the assured safety God provided him is fully applicable:
      21
         And when they had gone a long time without food, then Paul stood up in their midst and said, “Men,
      you ought to have followed my advice and not to have set sail from Crete, and incurred this damage
      and loss. 22 “And yet now I urge you to keep up your courage, for there shall be no loss of life among
      you, but only of the ship. 23 “For this very night an angel of the God to whom I belong and whom I
      serve stood before me, 24 saying, ‘Do not be afraid, Paul; you must stand before Caesar; and behold,
      God has granted you all those who are sailing with you.’ 25 “Therefore, keep up your courage, men,
      for I believe God, that it will turn out exactly as I have been told. 26 “But we must run aground on a
      certain island.” 27 But when the fourteenth night had come, as we were being driven about in the
      Adriatic Sea, about midnight the sailors began to surmise that they were approaching some land. 28
      And they took soundings, and found it to be twenty fathoms; and a little farther on they took another
      sounding and found it to be fifteen fathoms. 29 And fearing that we might run aground somewhere on
      the rocks, they cast four anchors from the stern and wished for daybreak. 30 And as the sailors were
      trying to escape from the ship, and had let down the ship’s boat into the sea, on the pretense of intending
      to lay out anchors from the bow, 31 Paul said to the centurion and to the soldiers, “Unless these men
      remain in the ship, you yourselves cannot be saved.” 32 Then the soldiers cut away the ropes of the
      ship’s boat, and let it fall away. (Acts 27:21-32)

         God’s assured safety was not unconditional. Are we to understand Radmacher and Derickson as asserting
that all genuine believers are unconditionally or unfailingly unified? If so, their exegesis is sorely flawed at this
juncture. Their shortcoming in failing to fully address their underlying assumptions continues through the rest of
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 148
their exposition. They consider the petition for sanctification in Jn 17:17 to be for our dynamic “experience of
eternal life…[that] does not involve a static relationship with God,” an imparted rather than imputed sanctification
(pp. 287-288). To be sure, the verse describes practical sanctification. But is this practical sanctification
unconditioned guaranteed for all “genuine believers”? Evidently not, since Radmacher and Derickson go on to add,
regarding this sanctification as continued in the petition of Jn 17:19:

      In each case He could have taken a different route and avoided the hostility of the world. But, because
      He had dedicated Himself to the Father and was subject to His word, His word became the separator
      between Jesus and the world as He responded on the basis of its truth and trustworthiness. We should
      do the same. This is why every believer has an obligation before God to read His word, to study it, to
      memorize it, and to obey it. We cannot sanctify ourselves to God by going to church on Sunday and
      by being spoon-fed tidbits of predigested Scripture by our pastors and expect to walk effectively with
      God. We must let His word dwell in us by knowing it, thinking about it, and letting it influence our
      thoughts and actions. There is no other way to be a sanctified saint. Sorry. I know that it is a lot of
      work. But, have no fear! The rewards for your efforts are eternal! (P. 290.)

         This sanctification requires our cooperation and work and thus results in rewards. Would the same not be
true for Jesus’ other petitions on our behalf? Do they not also necessitate our cooperation and work in the present
and result in future rewards? When Radmacher and Derickson reach the promise of oneness/unity in Jn 17:21, they
pose two joint questions (p. 292): “What exactly is Jesus asking for? And has His prayer been answered?” They
explicitly answer the first question, which implicitly answers the second. Per their assessment, Jesus is asking for
an observable experiential unity that is conditioned on believers obeying the truth rather than asking for a
metaphysical union or positional unity:

      The unity Jesus is talking about here has to do with communion, which is the essence of the vine
      allegory [Jn 15:1-6]. This is the relationship of walking with God described by John in 1 John 1. Jesus
      is praying for the expression of God’s life in the lives of believers in this life, not for some metaphysical
      union with the Father. Again, this is a conditional truth (what the believer is in experience in this life
      on the basis of his or her obedience) and not a positional truth (what is true because of Jesus’
      accomplished work on cross). Jesus prays that the Father will accomplish unity within the church so
      that the church can experience close communion as God’s children now. (P. 294.)

      He wants them to have an experience of unity because that observable unity will prove to the world
      that they are His disciples. (Emphasis theirs, 296.)

So, has Jesus’ prayer been answered? In response to that question, Radmacher and Derickson cite Rainsford, who
argues the Jesus’ prayer “must have been answered” because “He could not pray in vain” (which is the Jn 11:41-42
argument). Thus, Rainsford poses that Jesus’ prayer is answered in the form of a “three-fold union.” Radmacher
and Derickson counter that Jesus prayed for the existential unity of the disciples since they have an essential unity
(p. 436n4). Existential, in this context, means an experience of existence, so Jesus is requesting that they be what
they are (that they be unified experientially because they are an organic unity). Obviously, Jesus’s prayer has not
been answered fully, at least not temporally.
        In TOD, I tried to have the best of both arguments by posing a essential-temporal + experiential-
eschatological duality:

            •   Essential unity is fully answered ontologically and positionally even now.
            •   Experiential unity will be fully answered eschatologically.

This type of proposal is readily found among the commentators. For example, Milne (BST) takes basically the same
type of approach:

      The churches are already one in God. We need to allow that supernatural unity to find expression both
      in the local church and between the churches.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 149
                  We can also draw encouragement from the fact that Jesus prays for the unity, and Jesus’ prayer
          prevails. If our prayers in Jesus’ name are assured of an answer, how much more Jesus’ prayer in
          Jesus’ name! He cannot be denied, nor will he be. Despite all the contrary indications, the church is
          one, and will be one, in the glory of the consummation. This assurance does not absolve us from
          working for the expression of that unity in the present, but it does deliver us from an unbelieving
          despair. Jesus has prevailed. All Christians, and all churches, will one day love one another as the
          Father loves the Son and the Son loves the Father.

One weakness in this dualistic proposition is that in both Jn 17:21 and Jn 17:23, Jesus expresses His desire for a
fulfillment of His request that will result in the world perceiving the believers’ unity and believing as a result. The
much simpler explanation would be to concede that Radmacher and Derickson are correct and that trying to include
Rainsford’s position “is a little too expansive on what Jesus intended to communicate” (emphasis theirs, 436n4).
The weakness in Radmacher and Derickson’s position, in counterpoint, is that it leaves the Jn 11:41-42 assured-
answer argument unanswered. A solution to these horns of dilemma appears to be readily at hand, however. If Jesus
was in fact praying for a conditional experiential unity that would be fruitful, then we may truly affirm that
His prayer has been fully answered in the manner in which He intended it. Jesus did not intend for His prayer
to be unconditionally answered, as He makes clear with His repeated hina request, denoting conditional purpose
rather than an unconditional result. In Jn 17:21, for example, Jesus prays:

      •    that [hina] they may all be one; even as Thou, Father, art in Me, and I in Thee,
      •    that [hina] they also may be in Us;
      •    that [hina] the world may believe that Thou didst send Me.

         Jesus did not intend for His prayer to be understood as assuring that all the world would believe. Trying to
use Jn 11:41-42 to argue that Jesus’ prayer failed because all the world did not believe would be an abuse of His
intent. Jesus prayed that the world conditionally may believe (with the expectation of limited fulfillment), not
that the world unconditionally would believe (with the expectation of unlimited fulfillment). Since He is using
the final hina to express an expectation of a limited conditional fulfillment, the presumption would be that the same
would be true for His other uses of hina—both in the verse and thus in His prayer for future believers. This answer
has the additional advantage of matching Tanner’s explanation for Heb 7:25 so that Jesus' intercession then matches
His intercession now. The Lord’s present intercession makes complete salvation possible for those believers who
meet the condition of drawing near. It is not intended to save to the uttermost those believers who refuse to draw
near. This answer harmonizes with the Lord’s prayer for our sanctification (Jn 17:17) and with the hina used in that
regard (Jn 17:19).121 Jesus does not need to pray that believers will be positionally sanctified. They already have

121
    For instance, regarding Jn 17:19, Constable (TCENB) comments that “His sanctification makes our possible.”
True. But the fuller truth is that it does not make our sanctification inevitable. Constable rightly acknowledges that
Jn 17:17 pertains to “practical sanctification.” Many times, this practical sanctification is referred to as progressive
sanctification. Ideally, it should be progressive. For those believers availing themselves of the means provided by
the Lord for their sanctification, it is progressive. For some believers, however, it might be called stagnant
sanctification. For others, it might be better termed regressive sanctification. Whether a believer’s practical
sanctification is progressive, stagnant, or regressive is conditioned on the believer’s response to the truth of God’s
word. Similarly, McGee (TTB) asserts that Jesus’ prayer in Jn 17:20 “has been answered” because “the church is
an organic unity.” But he immediately acknowledges, “If believers would manifest that union to the world, the
world would be more impressed with Christ.” One problem with this bipolar answer is that Jesus prayed for both
with His hina requests: (1) believers to be one and (2) the world to believe as a result. Why is one request fully
granted and the other request only partially granted? Additionally, Jesus obviously intends for this organic unity to
be a manifested unity so that the world would believe. Therefore, given McGees’ premises, it is hard to believe that
Jesus was not praying for a manifested unity. McGee’s simplistic assertion that “His prayer has been answered”
fails to address some questions raised by his own interpretation. The same could be said of Blumm’s summational
comment: “Jesus’ petitions for believers are four: preservation (John 17:11), sanctification (v. 17), unity (vv. 11,
21–22), and participation in Jesus’ glory (v. 24). This prayer is sure to be answered (cf. 11:42; 1 John 5:14)” (BNK,
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 150
that forensic and ontological sanctification. Rather, His intercession is for the purpose of conveying practical
sanctification for those believers who avail themselves of the truth. His intercession for believers matches His
requirement for believers (Jn 8:31-32). The hina is used in a variety of ways in this context to express Jesus’ desire
on behalf of believers or the world:

    •   that [hina] they may know Thee (Jn 17:3)
    •   that [hina] they may be one (Jn 17:11)
    •   that [hina] they may have My joy made full in themselves (Jn 17:13)
    •   not that [hina] you take them out of the world, (Jn 17:15a)
    •   but that [hina] you keep them from the evil one (Jn 17:15b)
    •   that [hina] they themselves also may be sanctified in truth (Jn 17:19)
    •   that [hina] they may all be one (Jn 17:21a)
    •   that [hina] they also may be in Us (Jn 17:21b)
    •   that [hina] the world may believe (Jn 17:21c)
    •   that [hina] they may be one (Jn 17:22)
    •   that [hina] they may be perfected in unity (Jn 17:23a)
    •   that [hina] the world may know (Jn 17:23b)
    •   that [hina] they also, whom Thou hast given Me, [may] be with Me where I am (Jn 17:24a)
    •   that [hina] they may behold My glory (Jn 17:24b)
    •   that [hina] the love wherewith Thou didst love Me may be in them, and I in them (Jn 17:26)

         As to the first occurrence in the list, Radmacher and Derickson have an extended discussion of Jn 17:3:
“And this is eternal life, that they may know Thee, the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom Thou hast sent.” They
well argue that the verse is talking about the believer’s dynamic experience of eternal life, which is contingent on
right living in ongoing obedience, not the believer’s unconditional static possession of eternal life (cp. 1Jn 2:4).
Focus on the quantitative possession of eternal life, which was the focus of the early chapters in GJ, gives way to a
focus on its qualitative experience in the subsequent chapters. The hina is used to convey the purpose, thus the goal,
of eternal life in this verse. “Though the Greek term hina can be translated as ‘that’ and be used to indicate content
(like the content of a statement), when used with the subjunctive mood verb (as here) it normally gives purpose.”
In this context, even “if the clause is taken just as a statement of content as most interpreters take it, then the verb
in the clause must be given its subjunctive sense, namely, that of possibility” (emphasis theirs, 263). In the other
words, knowing God is not the guaranteed result of eternal life in this verse; rather, knowing God is its possible
experiential purpose of eternal life.
         What about the double hina in Jn 17:24? If consistency is maintained, the same logic should be applied.
The hina is enjoined in Jn 17:24 with thelo to express Jesus’ wish/desire (BDAG): “Father, I desire [thelo] that
[hina] they also, whom Thou hast given Me, be with Me where I am, in order that [hina] they may behold My

Jn 17:25-26). Blumm takes a soteriological interpretation of the preservation, an experiential view of sanctification
and unity, and a soteriologically eschatological view of glorification. In what manner does he mean that the prayer
is sure to be answered when the Church has so much disunity and worldliness?
         Constable and McGee are dispensationalists. They need to provide answers that would explain this lack of
unity in a manner compatible with their dispensationalist framework. Barnes, on the other hand, is a four-point
amillennialist and weaves his soteriology and eschatology into an interpretation that fails to present a convincing
case. He interprets the requested preservation in Jn 17:11-12 as salvation from apostasy and terms the sanctification
in Jn 17:17 as progressive. Considering the fact that this prayer is applicable to future believers, one might well
retort that this sanctification is progressive—except when it isn’t! As far as the applicability to future believers in
Jn 17:20-21, Barnes states: “The prayer was assuredly heard, and will be answered in reference to all who truly
believe.” What about apostate believers? So much for his Calvinistic premise. He interposes his amillennial premise
in his expectation of the future millennial being ushered in by eventual experiential unity, which “is that very union
among all Christians for which he prayed; and when that union of feeling, and purpose, and action shall take place,
the task of sending the gospel to all nations will be soon accomplished, and the morning of the millennial glory will
dawn upon the world” (BNONT, emphasis his). Does this mean that Jesus’ prayer for unity goes unanswered until
then? Both amillennialists and premillennialists need to provide more exegetically consistent answers.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                           Page 151
glory, which Thou hast given Me.” Given the conditional nature of Jesus’ other requests involving hina and the
corresponding contingent nature of their potential realization, one should deduce, per a logically consistent
extrapolation of Radmacher and Derickson’s arguments, that being with Jesus in this Where-I-AM place and seeing
His glory in that location is a reward limited to faithful believers. Radmacher and Derickson associate this place
with the Father’s house of Jn 14:2 and, thus, in turn, with the New Jerusalem of Rev 21:9-17 (p. 297). Yet they fail
to acknowledge the contingent nature of this realization.
          To be sure, they drop a hint, pointing to that conclusion back in their discussion of Jn 14:2, since they say
it “is somewhat reminiscent of Jesus’ words in John 12:26” (p. 96) And this verse conditions being with Jesus in
His Where-I-Am place on serving and following Jesus, which is definitely a reward. And they follow up this
reference to Jn 12:26 by quoting 1Pet 1:3-5, which is also a reward passage. The implication of their argument
seems to be that being with Jesus is a reward in all three passages: Jn 12:26; 14:2-3; and 17:24. Indeed, their
argument in the expanded context of Jn 14 reinforces this conclusion. Radmacher and Derickson properly
acknowledge that Jn 14:21-23 is talking about obedient believers earning an experience of God’s “merited
love” and “God’s abiding presence” (pp. 133-138). This mone experience with Jesus and the Father is described
in Jn 14:23 as “make Our Home with him” (emphasis theirs, 134) and is conditioned on obedience. The logical
extrapolation of their argument would be that just as this mone experience is merited temporally in Jn 14:23, it is
merited eschatologically in Jn 14:2. Being at home with the Father and Son is a reward in Jn 14:2 and 14:23.
Their entire argument forcibly leads to this conclusion.
          As to the final hina passage in the above list, Radmacher and Derickson are consistent in acknowledging
that Jn 17:26 is dealing with a conditional communal experience of God’s love. It deals with “a conditional
truth….the experience of eternal life as we have communion with Him” (emphasis theirs, 300). The purpose of
Jesus’ petitions, as the Lord summarizes here in His conclusion, is that His followers have conditional, experiential,
meritable rewards, both temporally and eschatologically.
          Accordingly, my deduction from their argument is that Jesus’ prayer has been fully answered in the
way He intended it to be answered. He was not praying that believers would have these benefits apart from
their obedience. To suppose that the Father would give these benefits freely and unconditionally to all
believers would misconstrue the nature of Jesus’ requests. The Lord’s intercession then, as it is now, is not
to keep believers saved from Hell. The soteriological security of believers has already been unconditionally
guaranteed. Jesus’ aspirations for believers have moved beyond that foundational security to something that
requires His intercession: their ongoing progressive sanctification and the realization of their contingent
eschatological misthological glorification.
          A weakness of my extrapolation of Radmacher and Derickson’s argument must address their treatment of
Jn 17:12, where Jesus said, “While I was with them in the world, I kept [tereo] them in Your name. Those whom
You gave Me I have kept [phulasso]; and none of them is lost except the son of perdition, that the Scripture might
be fulfilled” (NKJ). Radmacher and Derickson supply a quote from Dillow’s earlier book, Reign, and concur with
him: “The loss from which they are being kept is ‘destruction,” or hell” (p. 281). To be sure, in Reign (pp. 170, 413,
500), Dillow took a soteriological view of Jn 17:11-12. For that matter, he also took a soteriological view of the
unity, saying that it “is no doubt a prayer for the organic unity of all believers,” and he appeals to Jn 11:42 as proving
that the soteriological keeping and unity are unconditionally assured (Reign, 500). By giving a noncritical
endorsement of Dillow’s statement, Radmacher and Derickson have hobbled their own argument. They take the
kept [tereo] in v. 12a and the kept [phulasso] in v. 12b as synonymously “talking about protecting them from attack
rather than just keeping them committed to the Father….in the process of keeping believers safe from the attacks
and influences of the world, in the sense of falling from the faith” (p. 280). Jesus did not keep Judas from falling
from saving faith, they say, because Judas never had saving faith. Apparently, Radmacher and Derickson interpret
the verse as dealing with soteriological preservation and destiny. Jesus soteriologically kept the Eleven but did not
keep Judas since he was not a believer. But if Jesus’ keeping them in v. 12 is soteriological, then this would seem
to raise the possibility that Jesus is asking the Father to continue keeping them in the same manner in v. 11. “Father,
keep [tereo] them in Thy name, the name which Thou hast given Me, that they may be one” (Jn 17:11). Indeed,
Dillow interprets the verse in both Destiny and Reign in this manner, apparently for this very reason. However,
Radmacher and Derickson argue at length that this is not the case. They insist that Jn 17:11 is an appeal to the Father
on behalf of believers for their existential (experiential) unity rather than their essential (ontological/organic) unity,
and they have a strong argument in that regard. Perhaps Radmacher and Derickson are right and Dillow is wrong,
and there is a soteriological disconnect between v. 11 and v. 12. Since Dillow crippled his own argument, probability
might seem to favor this disconnect. Then again, Radmacher and Derickson weakened their own argument by
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                     Page 152
softening the disconnect, dovetailing their discussion v. 11 into an agreement with Dillow regarding v. 12. A
logically consistent answer is not impossible, just not provided in their discussion.

         Wilkin
         In GNTC, Wilkin interprets Jn 17:11 much the way as Radmacher and Derickson. However, Wilkin is
consistent in posing that both v. 11 and v. 12a deal with experiential keeping and unity: “Jesus had kept [tereo]
them in the Father’s name and was asking the Father to keep [tereo] the disciples faithful to the character of the
Father…. If the disciples remained faithful to God’s character, then they would be one in love and in purpose (cf. v
21 and 15:9–14).” Wilkin, in contrast to Radmacher and Derickson, poses a soteriological break between v. 12a and
v. 12b: “In v 12 two Greek words are translated kept [tereo and phulasso]. The second occurrence [phulasso in
12b], which has the idea of guarding, is probably chosen for more than just stylistic variation in light of the words
‘and none of them is lost.’ The second kept of v 12 [phulasso in 12b] thus probably concerns eternal security (cf.
John 10:2–30)” (bold and italics his; highlight mine). In other words, in contrast to Radmacher and Derickson,
Wilkin is not taking tereo and phulasso as functioning synonymously, or as dealing with soteriological protection
by the Father or Jesus, or with a soteriological attack by Satan. Doing so makes Wilkin’s interpretation superior for
multiple reasons. First, tereo is used consistently for temporal-experiential keeping rather than for soteriological
keeping in both v. 11 and v. 12. Second, this experiential interpretation is consistent with Wilkin’s experiential
understanding of tereo in v. 15 as pertaining to faithfulness, not soteriological faith. Radmacher and Derickson
concur that an experiential interpretation is intended in v. 15. They specifically reject Dillow’s soteriological
interpretation for tereo in v. 15. So why inconsistently adopt Dillow’s soteriological interpretation in v. 11? Third,
understanding Jesus as making requests that are conditioned on the believer’s obedience for their fulfillment is
consistent with Jesus’ other petitions within the prayer. Fourth, the logical implications of Wilkin’s ontological
interpretation of not of this world in Jn 17:16 in terms of 1Jn 5:18 renders a soteriological interpretation of Jn 17:11
virtually impossible: “Jesus’ prayer that the Father would keep them faithful is perfectly in keeping with the
disciples’ regenerate selves (cf. 1 John 3:9).” Basically, the cross reference in 1Jn 5:18 all but proves that Jesus is
not asking the Father to keep them saved from Hell.
         When’s Wilkin’s GNTC statement is unpacked, it packs a considerable wallop. To understand why,
Hodge’s GNTC understanding of 1Jn 5:18 is a must:

                 In saying that the regenerate inward person (cf. Rom 7:22) keeps himself, John is not saying
         that one’s inner self can somehow prevent all sin in the Christian life (cf. [1Jn] 1:5–10). What John
         means is that God’s “seed remains in” the regenerate inner self (cf. [1Jn] 3:9) as the controlling element
         of his born-again nature and is impervious to even the slightest contamination from the wicked one.
         Believers’ failures are due to the sinful “programming” of their earthly bodies, as Paul himself taught
         in Rom 7:7–25.
                 But try as he might, Satan cannot really touch the believer. (Emphasis his.)

         For reasons explained in TOD, I fully concur with Hodges regarding 1Jn 5:18. The NAS renders the verse
as: “No one who is born of God sins; but He [Jesus] who was born of God keeps [tereo] him and the evil one does
not touch him.” According to this translation, following the CT, Jesus does the keeping. However, this is
exceedingly unlikely. Born of God repeatedly describes believers, never Jesus, in this epistle (1Jn 2:29; 3:9; 4:7;
5:1, 5:4,18). The MT reading of 5:18 is certainly preferable: The believer keeps himself (heatou). Precedence for
this reflexive action is found in 1Jn 3:3: The believer purifies himself (heatou). If this verse is talking about
ontological purification, at least inclusively as seems likely, then it is almost an exact match for 1Jn 5:18.
Regardless, it shows that John is open to a sinlessness effected by the believer upon him or herself. In 1Jn 5:18, the
believer’s new nature results in the believer being absolutely sinless at all times (1Jn 3:9).122 Believers keep
themselves perfectly sinless ontologically by means of their ontological composition—that is, their sinless nature.
Therefore, 1Jn 5:18 describes absolute sinlessness. The devil cannot touch the believer with sin. Satan cannot induce
the one born of God to sin nor accuse him or her of sin, either positionally or ontologically. This soteriological
keeping is done by believers by means of the nature God has given them. Unconditional security is thus assured by
the believers’ new nature. Accordingly, it would be completely unnecessary for Jesus to ask for the Father to keep

122
      See my discussion in TOD for 1Jn 3:3,9 or Hodges’ discussion in GNTC.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 153
believers soteriologically secure since that security is already secured by virtue of the new nature the Father has
already given them.
         Despite the strength of the above arguments, at this point in this discussion, I am still not arguing for an
exclusively experiential understanding of being kept in Jn 17:11-12,15, at least not yet. Doing so at this point might
be premature. In TOD, I allowed a duality, soteriologically entailing absolute organic unity and ontological
sinlessness of believers. One reason for doing so is that I take a soteriological view of being kept in Jude 1:1,
affirming that these believers are unconditionally kept in Christ. Doskocil (GNTC) likewise takes a dualistic
temporal-soteriological view: “Temporally Christ protects His followers from false teachers through His Word (cf.
v 17), and eternally, He is the one who guarantees everlasting life.” And Hodges interprets the verse as affirming
soteriological security.123 Consequently, I do not consider the above arguments to be a knockout punch to
soteriological duality. Still, they are certainly strong enough to insist that conditional experiential keeping is the
primary vantage point, if not the exclusive vantage point.
         Even Wilkin hedged his argument for Jn 17:12 by speaking in terms of double probability: “The second
occurrence, which has the idea of guarding, is probably chosen for more than just stylistic variation in light of the
words ‘and none of them is lost.’ The second kept of v 12 thus probably concerns eternal security (cf. John 10:2–
30)” (bold and italics his). This dual probability can work in two different directions. Even if Wilkin is wrong and
mere stylistic variation is entailed so that tereo and phulasso are being used synonymously, this still does not prove
that the passage concerns eternal security. In that case, Jesus might be simply saying that He did not keep Judas
faithful because Judas never had faith. Naturally, one would not expect Jesus to keep Judas faithful to a faith that
Judas never possessed. Experiential keeping is still envisioned. Union through faith was the basis for the requested
communion and its attendant faithfulness. Judas lacked the former, so the latter was not applicable. On the other
hand, if the passage does concern eternal security and tereo and phulasso are being used synonymously, then my
argument in TOD for duality is still valid.
         In any case, Wilkin proceeds to take an experiential view of Jn 17:17,19 as describing practical
sanctification and Jn 17:21 as dealing with experiential unity. Fulfilment is contingent and temporal. The glory of
Jn 17:22 likewise refers to temporal ability and giftedness in Wilkin’s assessment. He also takes the unity in Jn 17:23
as experiential. Nevertheless, Wilkin is inconsistent in the interpretation he poses for Jn 17:24, stating: “This verse
may refer to the millennial kingdom. If that is true, then ‘where I am’ refers to earth after Jesus returns in His
Second Coming” (emphasis his). Since Wilkin is not a misthological millennial kingdom exclusionist, his posing
that the Where-I-AM place to which Jesus refers is Earth during the millennial kingdom is probably meant to be
taken soteriologically as referring to all believers, which for him would be tantamount to a request for their
unconditional security. If that is his intent, then it would be inconsistent with the conditional manner in which he
has taken the other petitions for believers throughout ch. 17. Per his analysis, none of the other petitions are
soteriological or unconditional in their fulfillment. So, why switch gears here at the end of the prayer and assume
that Jesus is making an unconditional soteriological request? Why does Jesus moving from temporal requests to an
eschatological, per Wilkin’s analysis, suggest that it is a soteriological request? Could it not just as easily be a
misthological request?

         Ventilato
         Ventilato takes a different approach. He tries to make all the requests eschatological and/or soteriological
in their scope. I will provide an extended quote from him and highlight parts of it:

                  “And I do not demand for these only, but also for those who believe on Me through their word;
         that they may be all one, as Thou, Father, art in Me, and I in Thee, that they also may be one in Us,
         that the world may believe that Thou hast sent Me. And the glory which Thou hast given Me I have
         given them, that they may be one, as We are one; I in them and Thou in Me, that they may be perfected
         into one [at the Rapture] and that the world may know that Thou hast sent Me, and that Thou hast
         loved them as Thou hast loved Me. Father, as to those whom Thou hast given Me, I desire that where
         I am they also may be with Me [consequent upon the Rapture], that they may behold My glory [not
         “outer darkness”!] which Thou hast given Me, for Thou lovedst Me before the foundation of the
         world.” (John 17:20-24)

123
      Zane C. Hodges, Power to Stand: An Exposition of Jude (Denton, TX: Grace Evangelical Society), 26-27.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 154

      “Identification. We are one with Him, and all His saints are one. The Church is His body,
      an organism and not an organization. He did not pray for a unity in organization, but for a spiritual
      unity, which exists. ‘That they also may be one in Us’ is not an unanswered petition. The Spirit Who
      has come unites believers to Him and baptizes them into one body. ‘I in them, and Thou in Me, that
      they may be made perfect in one, that the world may know that Thou hast sent Me, and hast loved
      them as Thou hast loved Me’ – this looks on towards the blessed consummation, when the saints will
      appear with Christ in glory; then the world will know….124Glorification. ‘And the glory which Thou
      gavest Me I have given them, that they may be one as We are one’ – ‘Father, I will that they also,
      whom Thou hast given Me, be with Me where I am; that they may behold My glory, which Thou hast

      Some day it will be answered and all His saints will be with Him and share His glory. And oh! the

      and fill our hearts with joy unspeakable and full of glory.” – Arno C. Gaebelein (Gaebelein’s Concise

      “And what adequately could account for men thus called out of the earth and glorified on high?
      Sovereign grace had given them that heavenly portion as the fruit of His mission Who at all cost to
      Himself had glorified God on the earth. And now [at the establishment of His kingdom on earth] they
      share His glory above, and are so displayed before the wondering world. The salvation-bearing grace,
      which had appeared to all and had done its suited and appointed work in redeeming and purifying these
      to God as a people of possession, will then have given place to the appearing of the glory of our great
      God and Saviour Jesus Christ; but this through the church reigning over the earth, at any rate as the
      ordinary or normal method of its manifestation during the kingdom. As we by faith saw the Father in
      the Son to eternal life, they in that day will behold and learn them in the church, the glorious vessel of
      the light of Christ in Whom God’s glory shines…. It is thenceforward the glory of God that is
      established before all eyes, so that men ‘know’ it in and by the glorified saints, instead of being objects
      of testimony that they might ‘believe.’” – William Kelly (An Exposition of the Gospel of John,
      p. 368,369)

      “It is clear that the glory [referred to in John 17:22] is not actually ours till He comes again, and that
      there can be no perfecting for us into one [17:23] till then. But it is now for the world to ‘believe,’ as
      in v. 21. When the glory is revealed, and not before, the world shall ‘know’; because it is a fact before
      their eyes and impossible to deny; and such is the distinction of vs. 22, 23 from what had been already
      presented by our Lord. The oneness ‘perfected’ will be in the day of glory (as the oneness in v. 21 is
      during the day of grace in order to act on faith now), and will only be matter of fact when the Lord
      appears and we with Him in the same glory (Col. 3:4; 2 Thess. 1:13).” – William Kelly (W. Kelly’s
      Writings on Prophecy, p. 176, “The Prize of Our High Calling” by J. Sladen: A Review by W. Kelly)125

         I provided extended rebuttals to many of Ventilato’s anti-misthological arguments in TOD. So, doing so
here is unnecessary. However, I also stated in TOD that, to an extent, I share much of Ventilato’s assessment that
all believers will be with Christ (Jn 17:24) and receive His glory (Jn 17:22), and that Jesus’ prayer for organic unity
and sharing His glory and location cannot be unanswered. Even so, I noted that the deductions that Ventilato draws
124
    Ventilato conveniently uses ellipses to remove the part of Gaebelein’s statement that is detrimental to his
argument. In this section, Gaebelein (TAB) comments on the sanctification in Jn 17:17-19 that “we are sanctified
by the Truth, by walking in obedience.” This sanctification is not soteriological, nor is it eschatological. Jesus is
praying for temporal, practical sanctification—a sanctification that is conditional and, in a variety of cases, stunted
or missing in the lives of many believers. This fly in the ointment would ruin Ventilato’s argument, so he omits it,
which seems to be less than a straightforward assessment of the data.
125
    James Ventilato, “A Scriptural Refutation of the Teachings of Zane Hodges, Joseph Dillow and the Grace
