from this passage (that all believers must receive equal glory with Christ and equal accessibility to Christ due to
their organic connection with Christ) fail exegetically and typologically.
          Notwithstanding, upon subsequent reflection, even my limited concession in TOD to Ventilato regarding
these elements in Jn 17 needs to be reevaluated. At the time I wrote TOD, I was operating under the assumption
that Jesus’ prayer had to be answered fully and completely—and therefore must have had an unconditional element
to it, such as what some other securitists surveyed above have argued from Jn 11:42. As already noted, though, in
the above interaction with other securitists sharing that premise, that assumption has various weaknesses. One such
weakness is that the Lord prayed in Jn 17:21 for the world to believe. As even Ventilato concedes, via his quoted
material, the Lord was praying that the world would believe “now,” thus before He returns. Obviously, most people
in the world do not believe. So, has Jesus’ prayer failed to have the scope of fulfillment for which He prayed? I
addressed this problem in TOD:

         Jesus prays “that the world may believe” (Jn 17:21). Obviously, as Godet remarks, “It cannot therefore
         designate a forced conviction…Jesus does not mean to say that the whole world will believe.” [CJGG]
         What Jesus requests for the world is ability not irresistibility. Earlier in GJ, Jesus likewise had prayed
         in order that the Jews may believe (Jn 11:42). Many did, but some did not (Jn 11:45-46). But Jesus’
         prayer (and actions) gave them ability to believe. He made it possible for them to believe. They may
         if they would. The lost may believe, but this does not necessarily mean that they will believe.

My abbreviated quote from Godet above in TOD is from a 1978 publication of his commentary. Unfortunately, the
extended part of the quote which I cited is missing from the 1900 Logos edition of Godet’s commentary.
Nevertheless, the full extended quote is easily found online:

         It cannot therefore designate a forced conviction, such as that which may be found in Philippians
         2:10 f. No doubt, Jesus does not mean to say that the whole world will believe; this would be
         contradictory to what He said of the world in John 15:20; John 15:22; John 15:24. We must recall
         to mind the fact that the question is of an end which cannot be accomplished for all. In any case, Jesus
         declares that in the world estranged from God there are yet elements capable of being gained for
         faith.126 (Emphasis original.)

Godet’s answer opens other possibilities for the other petitions. This hina + subjunctive construction yields a in
order that…may. In the case of Jn 17:21, Jesus prays in order that the world may believe. His doing so is not
intended to convey the notion that He is requesting that the whole world will believe. Since that is the case regarding
evangelism, though, why think that it is any different regarding other matters requested in the prayer, such as the
believers’ preservation, unification, sanctification, and location?

         Govett
         As to the Lord’s unification request in Jn 17:11, Govett (GOJ) poses an ontological-eschatological duality
so that he can argue that Jesus’ prayer finds complete fulfillment: “Has the prayer of Christ, then, failed? No! There
is at the foundation a unity of nature among those regenerated by the spirit of God. And there is time enough in
eternity to produce the oneness for which Jesus prays, after the disturbing forces shall have been removed.”
Similarly, regarding the unity requested by the Lord in Jn 17:21, Govett makes a similar deduction: “Does the unity
here spoken of refer to the (1) unity of believers with the other? or (2) to their oneness with Christ, and with the
Father through Him? This latter would seem to the true sense, because it is the only one which has been realized.”

         Cauley (TOD)
         I made similar dualistic arguments in TOD for the same reason, so that I could affirm that the various
petitions that Jesus makes for believers within the prayer would be fully answered: either ontologically, positionally,
or eschatologically. As to preservation, I posed a temporal-eschatological duality:



126
      Available at: https://www.studylight.org/commentaries/eng/gsc/john-17.html. Accessed 1/6/2023.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 156
               Jesus’ request of the Father that we would be kept in Him certainly includes the idea of being
      kept from the devil (Jn 17:15). This keeping is both temporal and eschatological. The temporal nature
      of this preservation is clearly confirmed by 1Jn 5:18 where it is absolute. The devil cannot even touch
      us. We are sinless. The positional dimension of this sinlessness is highlighted by Jesus’ repeated
      statement that we are not of the world just as He is not of the world (Jn 17:14,16). This statement is
      repeated immediately before and after His request that we be kept from the devil. We do not share the
      world’s nature or position. Unfortunately, this dimension of His request does not mean that we are not
      of the world experientially in terms of our practice. These two verses should not be twisted to mean
      that our practice is not of the world just as Jesus’ practice is not of the world! These verses are not
      talking about our practice but our position. Positionally, we are just as righteous as He is (1Jn 3:2-
      3,7). In begetting us, His seed has imparted to us an imperishable life and nature, neither of which can
      be defiled by the devil’s sinful hands. We are new creatures who have a sinless new nature. The devil
      cannot touch what we are ontologically or where we are positionally. Obviously, the implications of
      this understanding naturally result in unconditional eternal security.

         In TOD, I also took the other primary matters in the prayer as having a soteriological-misthological dualistic
scope. As to the glory that Jesus gives us so that we can be one in Jn 17:22, I posed that it was soteriological with
misthological differentiation. According to that assessment, all believers would have glorified bodies, but believers
would not receive equal glory. As to being with Jesus in the Where-I-AM place of Jn 17:24, I posed that its
soteriological with but not with misthologically equal accessibility.127 In the present treatment, however, I am posing
a misthological-misthological dualism.

         Cauley (MMP6)
         In my more advanced discussion of RBVGB, which was subsequent to my discussion in TOD, I challenged
my previous assumption that all believers would receive glorified bodies by posing instead that unfaithful believers
will only receive redeemed bodies. Various degrees of glorified bodies are reserved for faithful believers. I did not
deal with Jn 17:22 in RBVGB because this verse is too problematic to be an obstacle to that thesis. This is a
problematic verse in a problematic context. Thus, it would pose no real problem to my postulation that
eschatological glory is misthological and limited to faithful believers. Herein, though, I pose that the present glory
that Jesus gives in Jn 17:22 is the glory of the cross in anticipation of the glory of the crown, the glory of service in
view of rulership, the glory of sharing the nature of the Father’s revelation regarding Himself and His reward
(cp. Jn 17:24). Even so, concluding that Jn 17:22 is not promising unconditional soteriological glory to all believers
undermines part of my justification in TOD for arguing for an unconditional glorification or unification in Jn 17:22.
Additionally, the more advanced nature of the present study weakens my assumption of a soteric-mistholic duality
in Jn 17:24. The verse might still be dualistic but requesting a misthological dualism instead of a soteriological-
misthological dualism. Namely, Jesus might be asking that faithful believers be with Him in New Jerusalem

127
    Various commentators acknowledge the legitimacy of duality from within their paradigm in the way that they
interpret various verses. For example, Phillips (JCPS) poses that the Lord prays for both mystical and manifest
oneness in Jn 17:21 and that His petition for the former “has been answered to the full” while His petition for the
latter is answered to “the measure in which we through the indwelling Holy Spirit make ourselves available to
Christ.” As one who has an affinity for such duality, I sought to provide a consistent, robust model in TOD for that
approach, avowing both soteric-mistholic duality along with temporal-eschatological duality. In the present
discussion, I am entertaining the possibility that a mistholic-mistholic duality may be the superior interpretation
for Jn 14:2-3, thereby making a soteric-mistholic duality unlikely in Jn 17:24. Rather than praying for a mistholic-
mistholic duality in 17:24 that would include New Jerusalem directly, the Lord may be praying specifically for the
misthological realization regarding the Upper Bridal Chambers above the Heavenly City. Of course, access to these
Upper Bridal Chambers would presuppose the misthological realization of the Bride’s access to the Father’s house
(i.e., the Heavenly City). The Bride will have full access to the Reward City. Of course, angels and kings of the
Earth might have access to 3D portions of this Heavenly City as well (Rev 21:24-26). But only the Bride will have
access to the 5D bridal chambers above the preexisting portions of the Heavenly City. These new chambers are in
a higher dimension and thus cannot be reached by lower-dimensional beings, such as angels. The misthological
bridal chambers are a new misthological addition added to the Father’s preexisting misthological house.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                             Page 157
and in the Upper Chambers above New Jerusalem. The Where-I-AM statement in this verse should likely be
interpreted in the same manner as the one in Jn 14:2-3, which, in turn, should probably be interpreted as indicating
this misthological dualism, in accord with the exclusively misthological nature of this location is indicated by Jn
12:26.

          Rainsford
          Some arguments posed by securitists backfire. For instance, Rainsford poses that Christ’s petition that the
Father keep us is answered in 1Pet 1:5, where Peter assures believers that they are “are kept by the power of God
through faith for salvation ready to be revealed in the last time” (NKJ).128 Rainsford assumes that this verse is
talking about soteriological salvation and thus supports his argument for Jesus’ prayer being answered in harmony
with unconditional security. However, that verse is dealing with misthological salvation, not soteriological
salvation. As Dillow and Hodges note, being kept in the manner described by this verse entails availing yourself of
God’s power to overcome your problems so that you can obtain a misthological salvation.129 If being kept in 1Pet 1:5
is a conceptual parallel to what it means for the Father to keep us in Jn 17:11 and 17:15, as Rainsford believes, then
Jesus’ prayer is best understood as asking the Father to do whatever is necessary temporally for us to qualify
for salvation misthologically. Contrary to Rainsford, being kept in Jn 17:11 and 17:15 has nothing to do with
soteriological security if that is the case. His soteriological-misthological inconsistency is detrimental to his case.
          Moreover, Rainsford’s soteriological-experiential inconsistency does not help his case. For instance, when
expounding upon Jn 17:15, the controlling premise he begins with is that “we may not doubt—indeed, we dare not
doubt—that our Lord’s prayer for His disciples has been heard and answered” (p. 282). Okay, agreed. But now the
question becomes, as he acknowledges, “How is it answered?” (emphasis his, 283). His explanation as to how
Christ’s prayer is answered is very damaging to his arguments. He says that we are not kept experientially (from
trials, temptations, persecutions, conflicts, failures, suffering, self-inflected coldness or hardness, or sin). Rather,
regardless of all these temporal evils we experience, we are kept from being separated from the love of God, per
Rom 8:39. That answer is problematic since it presumes that Rom 8:39 promises that all believers are kept
unconditionally in the sphere of God’s love. However, the passage could be assuring that faithful believers (i.e.,
those believers who love God and thus overcome, cp. Rom 8:28,37) are kept in the experiential and misthological
sphere of God’s love. Similarly, his assumption that Rom 8:28 means that all temporal calamities work together for
good for all believers is open to challenge since the text actually limits this promise to those believers who love
God.
          Rainsford appeals to Ps 121:7 to assert that we are kept free “from all spiritual hurt….which can hurt the
soul….to injure the soul of any child of God” (emphasis his, 287). He is greatly mistaken. The child of God can
forfeit his soul (Mt 16:24-26; Mk 8:34-38; Lk 9:23-26; Jn 12:25)! According to these and similar texts, Christ will
be ashamed of such Christians. In Jn 17:15, is Christ praying that the Father will nullify Christ’s own teachings? Is
Christ praying at cross-purposes to His own warnings? Perish the thought! Rainsford’s answer is thoughtless
regarding its logical implications. Ps 121:7 is not intended as a blanket promise that God will protect the souls of
those believers who indulge in worldly compromise! Otherwise, Jesus would have never issued the warnings He
made about believers losing their soul as a result of worldly compromise. Rather, in harmony with Jesus’ promises
and warnings in these NT soul-saving-soul-losing passages, Ps 121:7 is intended to give comfort to faithful pilgrims
to Zion that God will keep their souls (both temporally and ultimately misthologically). Faithful pilgrims will not
lose their souls. Unfaithful believers will lose their souls. If Jesus is praying for the salvation of believers’ souls in
Jn 17, then He is not praying that they be kept unconditionally from losing their souls. He is not praying that they
be able to live for the devil and yet be kept from being hurt soulically by the devil. Rainsford speaks a half-truth
when he says, “Failures can only empty of self; they cannot deprive us Christ” (p. 288). While it is true that believers
cannot be deprived of belonging to Christ or even possessing Christ at the regenerate core of their trichotomous
spirit, they can be full of self interests at the soulical level and misthologically forfeit their souls as a result, a truth
Rainsford failed to acknowledge.



128
    Marcus Rainsford, Our Lord Prays for His Own: Thoughts on John 17 (Grand Rapids, MI: Kregel Classics,
1985), 190-191, 282.
129
    See Dillow, 211-212. Zane C. Hodges, First Peter: The Salvation of the Soul (Denton, TX: Grace Evangelical
Society, 2017), 18-19.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 158
         How about the Pauline parallel in 2Thess 3:3 that Rainsford cites: “The Lord is faithful, who will establish
you and guard you from the evil one” (KJV). Does Paul mean the Lord will establish and guard unfaithful and
faithful believers alike? Or is the you meant to be applied only to faithful believers within the church, such as those
whom he is addressing in the context (2Thess 1:3-4; 3:4). In this epistle, the latter option is certainly correct. In
commenting on 2Thess 3:3, Martin (NAC) notes the verbal connection between guard/keep (phulasso) with
Jn 17:12 and rightly supplies an experiential interpretation: “As with the twelve, divine protection does not imply
the absence of conflict but preservation through conflict.” I might also add Jude 1:24 since it uses the same verb as
a conceptual parallel: “Now to Him who is able to keep you from stumbling, and to make you stand in the presence
of His glory blameless with great joy.” God’s ability to keep believers from stumbling is not an unconditional
guarantee that they will not stumble. Rather, Jude gives believers assurance of the Father’s willingness and ability
to keep them from stumbling. Doskocil (GNTC) explains the passage well:

               V 24. God is able to keep you from stumbling. Now just because God is able to do something
      does not mean that He will. Yet this does not mean one’s eternal life is in jeopardy. The word
      stumbling, another word unique to Jude, refers to the things about which he has been warning his
      readers. He is concerned that they will fall into sinful conduct and false doctrines. Thus Jude’s readers
      are to rely on God to protect them from being deceived by false teachers.
               Jude’s remark is assurance that his readers will be protected from harm if they heed his
      instructions (vv 17–22) and allow God to work in their lives.
               God is also able to present believers faultless before the presence of His glory. This is a
      reference to Church-Age believers standing before the Judgment Seat of Christ for evaluation and
      reward (cf. 2 Cor 5:10). Not all believers will receive reward and commendation; some will shrink
      back in shame (1 John 2:28). The word faultless (amōmos) means “without spot, blemish or defect.”
      It was used in the LXX to describe sacrificial animals that were without blemish, and people who
      exhibited integrity (cf. Pss 15:2; 37:18; 101:6; 119:1, 80; Prov 11:5, 20; 20:7).
               While all believers will be glorified, not all will be blameless at the Judgment Seat. Only those
      believers who remain faithful to the Lord and serve Him will stand in His presence without blame.
      (Emphasis his.)

         The only tweak I would make is to assert that, contrary to what I used to believe, all believers will not be
glorified. Unfaithful believers will receive a redeemed body rather than a glorified body (see RBVGB). Otherwise,
Doskocil’s explanation can be accepted as is. Believers may or may not be misthologically blameless, depending
on whether they rely on the provisions provided by the Lord for their victory. If Paul and Jude are writing in
harmony with Jesus’ request, then one would expect that Jesus is praying, similarly, for the Father to
strengthen believers in the face of trials they face so that they can be victorious. If that is the case, then the
Lord’s prayer has been answered, as attested by the witness of Paul and Jude, as well as by the experience
of multitudes of believers throughout church history. Jesus is not praying that all believers will be victorious
regardless of their unwillingness to run the race, pay the price for victory, and take up their cross and follow
Him. The Father has fully answered the Son’s prayer in the manner in which the Son intended. His prayer
makes victory possible, not automatic.
         Inconsistently, Rainsford switches from a soteriological non-experiential keeping in Jn 17 to an experiential
sanctification in Jn 17:17 and cites 1Thess 5:23-24 as proving that all believers will unquestionably walk worthy of
their calling (p. 312). Not so! Wilkin (GNTC) correctly interprets the passage conditionally and misthologically:

      No amount of effort can accomplish this sanctification apart from God’s enablement.
              Another way of saying this is that one’s whole spirit, soul, and body be preserved blameless
      [amemptōs] at the coming of our Lord Jesus Christ. Paul is thinking of the total person. One cannot
      be blameless in his spirit if his body is not blameless. Each person is a unit and whatever one’s body
      does, his spirit and soul participate in. The passive voice shows that God must do this for the believer.
      He cannot do this by mere force of will.
              Blameless does not mean sinless. Each believer’s aim should be to be blameless at the
      Judgment Seat of Christ. (Emphasis his.)
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                          Page 159
Evidently, Rainsford was lacking this misthological tool in his hermeneutical toolbox since he proceeds to shoot
his own interpretation of 1Thess 5:23-24 in the foot. When you only have a soteriological hammer in your toolbox
and no misthological screwdriver, a misthological screw will look like a nail. Thus, Rainsford fails to interpret
Paul’s supplication to God in a manner consistent with Paul’s exhortation to the Thessalonians to “walk in a manner
worthy of God” (1Thess 2:12). A worthy walk is not guaranteed. Rainsford does not even seem to believe his own
interpretation of 1Thess 5:23-24 since he proceeds to acknowledge: “It is also practically true that, as Christians,
we shall have no fellowship, no communion, no vision of the Lord, nothing of the light of His countenance falling
upon our souls…if our walk is a loose walk, an unholy walk, a careless walk” (p. 313). Are believers who are
walking in this unholy manner unfailingly sanctified in the manner Rainsford envisioned in 2Thess 3:3? No. Does
this mean that Jesus’ prayer is unanswered for those believers who walk in this unworthy manner? No. Jesus’
prayer still provides the provision for victory for all believers who will avail themselves of the Lord’s means
to victory. The Lord’s prayer guarantees them the means to victory, not victory as a result. In Jn 17:17, the
means to their victory in sanctification is the truth of God’s word. In accordance with Jesus’ statement earlier in
Jn 8:30-32, we can safely infer that this experiential freedom requires that believers abide in Jesus’ word. Rainsford
further sabotages his interpretation of practical sanctification as an assured outcome by citing Heb 12:14: “Pursue
peace with all men, and the sanctification without which no one will see the Lord.” Rainsford cites this verse in
connection with his comment that those believers who walk in an unworthy manner will not see the Lord in the
manner so described. Jesus prayed that the Father would sanctify us (Jn 17:17,19) and that we would see His glory
(Jn 17:24). Yet if only those believers who pursue practical sanctification are to see the Lord in the manner described
in Heb 12:14 and if Heb 12:14 is indeed a proper parallel to these Johannine verses, then the conclusion that
logically follows is that only faithful believers will see the Lord in the manner anticipated by Jesus in Jn 17:24.
After all, Jesus limited seeing God in the misthological capacity anticipated in Mt 5:8 to those believers who are
pure in heart. The more one critically examines Rainsford’s argument, the more it leads to misthological
conclusions. For those not trying to pound a screw with a hammer, the conclusion is not that hard to see. Lacking
this misthological insight, Rainsford proceeds to make the outlandish claim that a believer’s “sanctification through
the truth [is] always progressing” (p. 358). Not so! For many believers, their progressive sanctification is stagnant;
for some it is digressive. Digressive sanctification can even reach the point of apostasy!
         As to the Lord’s prayer for our unity in Jn 17:21, Rainsford insists: “Our Lord’s prayer must have been
answered. He could not pray in vain. And if this is so, then it is a divine fact, a most blessed reality, that all His
believing people are one” (emphasis his). So, he proceeds to argue for a soteriological unity “which all the Lord’s
believing people have one with another as members of the same body, as children of the same family, as living
stones in the same spiritual temple” (p. 374). The problem with his interpretation is that, on many occasions and in
many locations, the Lord’s family is dysfunctional. Believers have an organic union, not necessarily an experiential
communion. And Jesus prays for a unity that is manifested visibly so “that the world may believe.” Apparently, the
Lord is praying for a visible functional communion, not an invisible organic union. Partially realizing this to be the
case, Rainsford insists, “The Lord’s prayer shall be fully answered by-and-by, and there shall be the fullest
manifestation of this union” (p. 393). Therefore, in acknowledging that a visible, experiential, functional
communion is the ultimate desire being expressed by the Lord, Rainsford is forced, by his premise of supposed
unconditional fulfillment, to punt and place this fulfillment in the eschatological future. If that is the case regarding
unity, then why not make the same argument for the other petitions as well? Indeed, this is basically the route I
implemented in TOD. That more complex route taken in TOD certainly has merit. I still consider it to be the
heavyweight defender of the hermeneutical options typically employed by securitists. Even so, the option being
explored herein is a worthy lightweight challenger for the championship belt. It has the advantage of contending
for a temporal, conditional unity—which is the more pervasive and persuasive interpretation.
         As for the Lord’s prayer in Jn 17:21, “that the world may believe” as a result of our unity, Rainsford’s
hermeneutic again forces him to pose an eschatological realization for the full fulfillment of Christ’s request since
the world presently beholds a divided Christianity. In the eschaton, though, Jews and Gentiles alike, thus the world,
will believe (pp. 394-404, 422, 424). I integrated much the same type of argument in TOD, posing that essential
unity is fully answered ontologically and positionally even now, while experiential unity will be answered
eschatologically. But if the present discussion poses a more satisfying answer regarding this verse, then the present
proposition being posed in MMP6 may be more probable than the overall interpretation I posed in TOD. As noted
above, in my section regarding Radmacher and Derickson (p. 146), Jesus prayed that the world may believe as a
result of the unity the world would behold. The Lord prayed for a conditional conversion based on lifestyle
evangelism. He did not pray that the world would believe independently of such a witness, at least not on that
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 160
occasion. Probability favors that then, as now, Jesus is praying so that we may be fruitful in our evangelistic efforts.
He is not praying that the world will be unconditionally won independently of our evangelism. Given the probable
conditional and temporal nature of His request regarding our evangelism, it follows that His prayer for our unity
was offered along the same lines. The Lord was praying for conditional experiential unity that would be
conditionally fruitful.
         In accordance with his soteriological framework, Rainsford interprets the Where-I-AM place in Jn 17:24 to
be Heaven and beholding the Lord’s glory to be soteriologically universal to all believers within Heaven. He cites
2Cor 3:18 and 1Jn 3:2 in support and mistakenly talks as if all believers will be overcomers, wear white, enter by
the gates into the Heavenly City, and eat of the Tree of Life. His misthological ineptitude on a variety of such points
does not help to foster confidence in his overall soteriological assessment regarding the glory and location. Not all
believers will see Jesus’ glory within the Heavenly City. Therefore, the Where-I-AM place cannot include the
Heavenly City if Jesus is simply praying for an unconditional fulfillment for all believers. Thus, in TOD, I posed a
soteric-mistholic duality in which Jesus is praying that all believers will see His glory within Heaven and that
faithful believers will see His glory from within New Jerusalem. The counterproposal being considered herein is
that Jesus might simply be praying for a conditional fulfillment that will only be realized by faithful believers.
Unfaithful believers will see the Lord’s glory from within Heaven. That is a given, unconditionally guaranteed.
There is no need for the Lord to pray for this assured result. His death and resurrection unconditionally guarantee
that soteriological result for all believers. However, seeing the Lord’s glory from within New Jerusalem and in the
Upper Room Chambers are misthological benefits limited to the faithful and thus not unconditionally guaranteed
for all believers. Hence, Jesus prays for these misthological realizations and the means for their fulfillment through
the loving unification and practical sanctification of the Bride. Bridal unity and bridal sanctification are necessary
to be a part of the Bride and thus be allowed into the Bridal City and the Upper Chambers.

                                          Arguments by Conditionalists
        Having surveyed some of the arguments made by securitists above, we will now survey some of the typical
rejoinders made by conditionalists over this soteriological battlefield.

         Shank
         Shank is a recognized scholar from this conditional persuasion who has written some classics in this area,
so let’s begin with him:

               In his prayer of intercession just before His passion, Jesus prayed, “Holy Father, keep through
      thine own name those whom thou hast given me, that they may be one, as we are….I pray not that
      thou shouldest take them out of the world, but that thou shouldest keep them from the evil [one] (John
      17:11,15). Some assert that it is impossible that any who once believe on Jesus should subsequently
      be lost, since God must answer the prayer of His Son. But Jesus prayed for those who sent Him to the
      cross, “Father, forgive them, for they know not what they do.” Are we to assume that, because Jesus
      so prayed, all the members of the Sanhedrin, Pilate, Herod, Judas, the soldiers, and all the mocking
      multitude were forgiven, simply because Jesus prayed for them? Are we to assume that the whole lot
      were immediately destined for salvation, simply because Jesus prayed for them? Jesus prayed aloud at
      the grave of Lazarus for the benefit of “the people which stand by, that they may believe that thou hast
      sent me” (John 11:42). Are we to assume that all who heard His prayer and for whom he prayed were
      necessarily persuaded that He was indeed sent of God? Obviously not. It is evident from John’s account
      that, while many of the Jews who witnessed the raising of Lazarus believed in Jesus, others did not.
               Certainly there is nothing ineffectual about the keeping grace of the Father; it is infinite. But
      neither was there anything lacking in the keeping power of Jesus, who said, “While I was with them
      in the world, I kept them in thy name: those that thou gavest me I have kept, and none of them is lost,
      but the son of perdition” (John 17:12). Those whom the Father gave Him Jesus kept—except one.
      Neither the Father nor the Son can keep those who do not wish to accept the conditions under which
      they may be kept. It is not, as some foolishly assert, a question of whether men are “stronger than
      God.” Nor is it a question of what God could do. It is only a question of what God does do, as revealed
      in the Holy Scriptures. The Scriptures declare that men are free to depart from God, and believers are
      solemnly warned against so doing (Heb. 3:12). Jesus said of those whom He kept, “they have kept
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 161
      thy word” (John 17:6). This is of more than incidental significance, as we may discern from the
      promise (and warning) of Jesus, “If a man keep my word, he shall never see death” (John 8:51).
      “Keeping His word” is more than a momentary reception; it must be habitual….Those who keep
      are kept.130 (Emphasis his.)

         Shank cites a number of texts in favor of interpreting keeping God’s word in Jn 17:6 in a linear capacity
(Jn 8:51,55; 14:21,23; 15:9-10). Nevertheless, his appeal to Jn 17:6 is indecisive. Securitists can make a good
argument that keep in Jn 17:6 is being used as a metaphor for believe. I marked it in this capacity in JILC and take
its perfect tense as describing a continuing state of belief by those who already have believed (cp. Jn 17:8), not
linear contingency.131 Wilkin (GNTC) likewise states: “Keeping the Father’s word refers to believing Jesus’ saving
message. The ultimate act of obedience is believing in Jesus whom the Father sent (cf. 3:36; 5:24).” Wilkin and I
also concur that it is used in this capacity in Jn 8:51,55. Plus, we agree that it is used in the capacity argued by
Shank in Jn 14:21,23; 15:9-10. Wilkin (GNTC) well states, for example, that in Jn 14:21 “He is referring to giving
obedient believers special insight into His Word, assuming they read, study, and meditate on it.” In harmony with
that expectation of special insight to be given to us as we study the Lord’s Word, let us, for the sake of argument
and with that expectation, allow the possibility that Shank is right in Jn 17:6. If we perceived the context to warrant
it, Wilkin and I could easily concede that keep (tereo) refers to ongoing obedience in Jn 17:6, just as it does in
Jn 14:15,21,23-24; 15:10. In that case, I would simply color code keep in Jn 17:6 as red in JILC to denote that
it refers to a misthological rather than a soteriological benefit. At the same time, I could keep it marked in
Jn 8:51 in dark blue to denote soteriological usage in that context. Then again, I could take it as having a
dual soteric-mistholic capacity in that context as well.132 In any event, a dual capacity is plausible in Jn 17:6.
They have kept Christ’s word by receiving it and believing it (so Jn 17:8) and by following through on it
(Jn 14:15,21,23-24; 15:10). Since these references from ch. 14 and 15 are offered in the Upper Room, which
is the location from which Jesus offers His prayer, Shank’s argument has merit. This concession to Shank also
has the added advantage of being consistent with Wilkin’s interpretation of Jn 17:11-12, which Wilkin interprets
as Jesus asking the Father to keep these followers faithful. Jesus is not asking the Father to keep unbelievers like
Judas faithful. Nor is Jesus asking the Father to keep unfaithful believers faithful. Jesus is asking the Father to
keep the Eleven disciples faithful because they have been faithful. They have been faithful in keeping Jesus’
word, and Jesus is asking the Father to continue to keep them faithful since Jesus is leaving. Adopting Shank’s
premise leads to a very attractive conclusion, though not necessarily the same conclusion for which Shank is
arguing. Temporal faithfulness and misthological results rather than soteriological results are better perceived to be
the issues at hand if Shank is correct regarding Jn 17:6. We can agree with him, nonetheless, that those believers
who keep God’s word faithfully are requested to be kept in that state of faithfulness. Those who keep are kept.

        Corner
        Corner has written a very extensive book arguing for conditional security, so his input regarding the Lord’s
prayer for believers’ preservation is worth noting:

               Question #18. Jesus prayed to the Father that he would protect his disciples and that none
      would be lost. Certainly, the Father heard Jesus’ prayer.
               The verse referred to is from John 17. This, however, is not a certainty of remaining saved! In
      the very same prayer. Jesus also prayed for complete unity among the believers (Jn. 17:23). Clearly,
      from 1 Cor. 1:10-13, this didn’t occur. Therefore, there must be some unlisted, outside factor to
      consider here. Remember, it was Jesus himself who told his disciples that they would have to “stand
      firm to the end to be saved” (Mt. 10:22) and to remain in him or be thrown into the fire (Jn. 15:4-6)!
               Also, according to Rom. 8:34 and Heb. 7:25, Jesus is now praying for us from the right hand
      of God. This, however, doesn’t mean that his servants cannot be deceived by false teachers to the point

    Robert Shank, Life in the Son: A Study of the Doctrine of Perseverance (Minneapolis, MN: Bethany House,
1989), 276-277.

    See my book, Believe, when released.
132
    A dual marking of Jn 8:51,55 would be consistent with those commentators such as Hendriksen and Kistemaker:
“To keep the word of Christ means to: a. accept it by faith, b. obey it, and c. stand guard over it” (NTC, Jn 8:51).
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 162
      where they can fall from grace and Christ be of no value at all to them (Gal. 5:2-4); grow lukewarm
      and get spewed out of the body of Christ (Rev. 3:15,16); die spiritually through sin (Rom. 8:13;

          As in the case of Shank, I have dealt at length with Corner’s arguments in my writings (TOD, SAS). Doing
so here is unnecessary. Suffice it to say that, like Rainsford, Corner’s lack of a misthological screwdriver in his
hermeneutical toolbox causes him to pound some of these misthological screws in his argument with a soteriological
hammer. For example, believers may reap misthological death (see RAE). Rather than delve into the weaknesses
of his argument and his faulty assumption of believers reaping soteriological death, I would prefer to focus on the
strengths of his argument in the present writing. In fact, for the sake of argument, I would like to pit the strengths
of Shank’s argument against the strengths of Corner’s argument. Let us suppose that Shank is correct and that, per
Jn 17:6, the Lord is praying, at least primarily or ultimately, for those believers who keep Christ’s word. After all,
that is a valid possibility. Likewise, let us suppose that Corner is right and that Jesus is not asking the Father to
overwrite the free will of believers. After all, that is a reasonable premise. Jesus is not praying that the Father will
irresistibly cause the world to believe or irresistibly cause believers to persevere or remain faithful. Moreover,
Corner is correct to criticize the inconsistency of some OSAS advocates who simplistically take Jesus as if He is
praying for unconditional preservation and glorification but conditional unification and sanctification. What makes
parts of the Lord’s prayer conditional and other parts unconditional if the premise is that all His prayers are
unconditionally answered? For the sake of expediency, let us use Occam’s razor to formulate a model that is simple
and consistent, and which affirms that Jesus is praying in a manner that is consistent with freewill realization and
conditional expectation. Doing so poses no problem for OSAS.

                                              Misthological Argument

         Bridal Prayer
         The proposition posed herein, in response to the above survey of Jn 17 from the competing soteriological
vantage points of unconditional security versus conditional security, is that both sides are mistaken in thinking
that any of the Lord’s petitions are focused on soteriological security. This Upper Room Prayer is an extension
of the Upper Room Discourse. The focus on the entire section is for temporal requirements for misthological
realization. The focus of the evangelistic section of GJ in chs. 1-12 has already established soteriological security.
The focus in GJ now turns to misthology in chs. 13-17. Jesus does not need to pray for unconditional soteriological
security in this misthological section. Instead, the Lord wants to build upon the soteriological foundation with
misthological expectations. In ch. 17, Jesus is not praying for a conditional soteriological realization but for
the potential misthological realization of the Bride. The title of Rainsford’s book, The Lord Prays for His Own,
is derived from his soteriological perspective of Jn 17. On the other hand, the shortcomings of his argument lead to
a better proposal for the title of this passage: The Lord Prays for His Bride.
         The pathos of the Lord’s prayer for His Bride is well presented by Gehm in his sermon, Jesus Prays for His
Bride.134 Granted, Gehm makes the standard soteriological assumption that the whole church is the Bride. Still, his
presentation is helpful for conveying the bridal intent of the passage and proves that even bridal soteriologists can
appreciate, to some extent, the intent of the passage. For instance, Exell interprets the passage from that paradigm:




133
    Daniel D. Corner, The Believer’s Conditional Security: Eternal Security Refuted (Washington, PA: Evangelical
Outreach, 2000), 375-376.
134
     Bill Gehm, “The G.O.A.T. Prayer...Jesus Prays for His Bride | John 17:13-26.” Available at
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nUBBtNK-4dw. Accessed 1/10/2023.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                        Page 163
               Our Lord looked upon those whom the Father gave Him as one—one body, one Church, one
      bride: He willed that as a whole the Church should be with Him where He is. 2. Then He looked again
      and saw each of the many individuals of whom the one Church is composed, and He prayed that each,
      that all of these, might be with Him and behold His glory. Jesus never so prays for the whole Church
      as to forget a single member; neither does He so pray for the members individually as to overlook the
      corporate capacity of the whole….Nobody can have such a right to your wedding-ring, good sister, as
      you have yourself, and are not Christ’s saints, as it were, a signet upon His finger, a token which His
      Father gave Him of His good pleasure in Him? Should they not be with Jesus where He is, since they
      are His crown jewels and His glory?....
               Love always pines for a partner in its joys. When I have been specially charmed with glorious
      scenery, I have felt myself saying, “How I wish that my dear wife could be here!” How unselfish it is
      on our Lord’s part to think Himself not fully glorified till we behold His glory!135

         For a bridal misthologist, such statements can be tweaked to render a sense that is more in accord with the
Upper Room context. The bridal implications of the foot washing in Jn 13 and the room preparation in Jn 14 have
been recognized by bridal misthologists. As explained in 4DF, Klein and Spears dedicate a chapter to explaining,
from the Hebrew background, that the Lord’s Supper and matters associated with it, such as the foot washing,
represent a betrothal.136 (1) During this Supper, Christ promises to go and prepare a place for His Bride and come
back and get her (Jn 14:1-3). That part of the betrothal parallel is obvious enough, but there is much more. (2) The
Lord’s removing their sandals and washing their feet represents the sandal covenant, in which He provides His
Bride with His inheritance. As we know, to be a co-heir with Christ we must suffer with Him (Rom 8:17b). This
inheritance is a reward (Col 3:23-24). (3) Also, during the Supper the terms of the betrothal contract were agreed
upon. In order to consummate the marriage when the Lord returns, the contingency we must meet is to keep His
commandments (Jn 14:21-23). Our participation in the Lord’s Supper represents our commitment to be His Bride.
(4) After the betrothal supper, the custom of the groom was not to drink wine again until the wedding. Likewise,
during the Lord’s Supper, Jesus promised not to drink wine again until the wedding ceremony had come (Mt 26:29;
Mk 14:25; Lk 22:18).
         Thus, the Upper Room context is dripping wet with bridal implications that are limited to those believers
who faithfully keep the Lord’s word. Such contextual details favor perceiving a bridal focus for these bridal
believers within the Upper Room Prayer. Unfaithful believers have no right to the wedding ring to be worn by the
faithful. Therefore, in accord with such contextual factors, the most probable inference is that Jesus is praying
ultimately for His Bride. Her unification, glorification, and location are the true subjects of His requests. Her
unification, glorification, and misthological location are contingently assured. Because She keeps His word, She
will be kept faithful by the Father and experience the joys of marital bliss with the Son.
         Putting the pieces together allows us to understand the contextual intent of Jesus’ prayer in the Upper Room.
Judas has left the room. So, if you count the number of disciples in the picture below, only eleven disciples are left.
These discipleships are not merely believers; they have followed Jesus. The Eleven represent the faithful remnant
who will compose the Bride. The context makes it clear that the Bride is on Jesus’ mind. He wants to prepare a
place for Her and have Her join Him in that place because She has fulfilled the terms of their betrothal agreement.
Jesus’ prayer only extends to the world insofar as a means of making it possible for the world to believe and likewise
follow Jesus so that the Bride may be completed. Future followers will be needed to complete the Bride, of course,
and Jesus has complete confidence in the Father that a sufficient number of believers will come forth from the world
of unbelievers and then follow Jesus in discipleship so as to complete the bridal company that will be necessary for
the Father to send His Son back to fetch His ready Bride.




135
   Joseph S. Exell, The Biblical Illustrator: St. John, vol. 3 (London: James Nisbet & Co., n.d.), 212.
136
   John Klein and Adam Spears with Michael Christopher, Lost in Translation: Rediscovering the Hebrew Roots
of our Faith, vol. 1 (TN: Selah Publishing Group, 2007), 53-75. For sandal covenant, see pp. 43-44. For a brief
video, one could also consult “Sandal Covenant and an Ancient Hebraic Wedding Tradition.” Available at
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jjQEOWJQTUs. Accessed 1/10/2023.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                   Page 164
                        Illustration 48. The Lord Prays for His Bride in the Upper Room




         Bridal Chuppah
         Adopting an exclusively misthological understanding of Jn 17:24 in the present book should be
matched with an exclusively misthological view of Jn 14:2-3. Surprisingly, perhaps, adopting a singular
misthological vantage point in Jn 17:24 seems to lead most naturally to a dual misthological view of Jn 14:2-
3. This assessment is supported by lexical and cultural considerations regarding the chuppah, which was discussed
previously in my section Bridal Chambers (p. 89).
         Under their section entitled, The chuppah, Klein and Spears describes the marriage ceremony that would
take place under the chuppah (used in the sense of wedding canopy). The association with Jerusalem and the
footwashing is readily apparent:

              The chuppah
              As he escorted her to the chuppah, a dome of bright crimson cloth, its color symbolizing their
      covering by a blood covenant, the groom would also be wearing a wreath of fresh myrtle and roses,
      thorns included, a symbol that their love would bring him both joy and pain (Does this sound
      familiar?). Somewhat earlier, a broad circlet of gold, shaped into the silhouette of the city of Jerusalem,
      would have been placed on the bride’s head.
              The couple would perform the wedding ceremony themselves, during which the groom would
      pronounce his bride pure, holy, and set apart for him alone. They would speak seven blessings over
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                         Page 165
      each other and vow their eternal faithfulness and love. After completing their vows they would share
      the fourth cup of wine together, the final step in the long betrothal process which began with the
      groom’s proposal perhaps more than a year ago.
               When they finished this fourth cup the groom would place it on the ground and put his foot on
      it. The bride would rest her foot on his and together they would stomp the cup to pieces, assuring that
      no one else would ever drink from it, thus signifying the exclusivity of their relationship.
               Next the bride and groom would take a triple-braided loaf of challah bread, bless it, break it,
      dip it in salt, and feed it to each other as a further pledge of their friendship and a renewal of the salt
      covenant. Then the groom would give his bride a new inheritance by removing her old, worn-out
      sandals, washing her feet, and putting on a new pair. Both of these customs clearly reinforce the pillars
      of covenant, the foundation underlying ancient Hebrew marriages on which modem marriages as well
      should stand.
               At that point the bride and groom would sometimes exchange rings, placing them on their
      right hands. The bride and groom were considered a king and queen for a week, starting with their
      wedding day. As the queen stands at the king’s right hand, so the bride must always symbolically be
      at her groom’s right hand. (Bold and italics theirs, 70-71.)

         As explained in MMP3 and MMP5, chuppah can also be used to refer to the small wedding canopy under
which the Jewish couple stands during their wedding ceremony. Only the Bride and Groom, not their guests, will
be under the bridal canopy. For good reason, I misthologically associated this Hebrew word, chuppah, with New
Jerusalem in those earlier writings within this misthological series.137 Yet the chuppah has a dual meaning that I did
not pursue in those earlier treatments that poses interest for the more advanced vantage point being contemplated
herein, in MMP6. Lexically, both meanings can be cited jointly as: “bridal chamber, pavilion, i.e., a bridal tent for
the wedding ceremony or first nuptial night of the ‘honeymoon’” (DBL:H, bold original).138 It not only refers to the
location where the marriage ceremony took place but also to the bedroom where the marriage was consummated.
Some sites simply cite the latter usage:

      The above site states: “After sealing the deal with the third cup, the Groom now departs to go to his
      father’s house to prepare a ‘bridal chamber’ (Chupah [sic.]) for his bride. The groom’s father decides
      the time for the wedding to take place, which will be when he - the groom’s father judges the
      preparations to be completed to his satisfaction.”139

         The NAS translates chuppah as bridal chamber in Joel 2:16, but it can also be used for the bridegroom’s
chamber (Ps 19:5).140 It was the nuptial chamber of the bride and bridegroom, the new room that the bridegroom
built on as an addition to his father’s house in which the new couple consummated their union and into which they
moved. Lamm explains at length that the “chuppah symbolizes the groom’s home, and the bride’s new domain.
More specifically, the chuppah symbolizes the bridal chamber, where the marital act was consummated in ancient
times” (emphasis his).141 The wedding canopy symbolizes place of the wedding bed. As discussed in my section
Bridal Chambers (p. 89), this association provides the backdrop for what Jesus meant in Jn 14:2-3. Therefore,
the clarity of what Jesus meant in that upper-room allusion must be given its due weight for interpreting His
upper-room petition in Jn 17:24. The Where-I-AM place is the same place in both texts, the place of the

wedding bed, the new room added on to the Father’s house. Only the bride and bridegroom would enter this
new nuptial room. Their friends do not. Accordingly, we should deduce that the Where-I-AM place to which
Jesus refers is not inside the Father’s house but added onto it. If the Father’s house is the preexisting
Heavenly City, as seems likely, then the new Where-I-AM place (to which Jesus refers in the Upper Room
and which is added onto the Upper Room) would appear to be the Upper Room Chambers into which angels
cannot enter.

         Guaranteed Results
         Jesus was praying for guaranteed results, not irresistible results. Jesus, as the beloved Son of His
Father, knew that the Father would not have put a plan in place where the Father’s ultimate plan of providing a
Bride for the Son would not come into full bridal fruition in response to the Son’s active and passive obedience
(which included the Son’s prayers). The Father’s plan did not require that every member of Christ’s soteriological
body would become a misthological member of the Bride. Far from it! The typology suggested from the very
beginning is that only a portion of Christ’s body would become His Bride. As discussed in TOD, God chose only a
part of Adam’s body to be formed into a completely new bridal body for him. Just as Eve was only formed from a
portion of Adam’s body but then given back as a separate derivative body to that original body to be one flesh with
that body, so the Bride of Christ is a faithful subset of the Body of Christ. Via the Father’s middle knowledge, He
put a plan into place in which (1) a sufficient number of unbelievers would become believers to form (2) a sufficient
number of believers to compose a Body of Christ from which (3) a sufficient number of faithful believers would
arise to form a mature Bride for Christ. Jesus could pray in full accordance with that plan and with full assurance
that His request would be fully granted by the Father. He could completely trust the Father to provide Him with the
Bride for which He prayed. Naturally, in accord with His own teaching, Jesus did not expect all the world to believe
or all believers to be faithful or fruitful in the manner described in this prayer. The full implications of Jesus’
expectations were limited to bridal believers. Jesus also gave a typological indication that this was to be the case
when He only took a subgroup of the Twelve up on the Holy Mountain to see the glory of His transfiguration. That
holy mountain (2Pet 1:18) prefigures Mount Zion, God’s holy mountain (cp. Ps 2:6; 48:1; Joel 2:1; etc.), the Bridal
City (Rev 3:12; 21:2). The Lord then compounded that typological expectation in the Upper Room so that the
chuppah canopy pictures the Bridal City while the chuppah room pictures the Upper Room Chambers above the
Bridal City.142

                                           Misthological Counter Arguments

         Presumptive Believers
         A reasonable counterargument can be anticipated from the perceived scope of Jesus’ prayer as including
all future believers (Jn 17:20) since these believers are given to Jesus by the Father (Jn 17:24). Moreover, being
given to Jesus by the Father is strongly associated with unconditional security for all believers earlier in GJ.
         37
           “All [pas] that the Father gives Me shall come to Me, and the one who comes to Me I will certainly
         not cast out. 38 “For I have come down from heaven, not to do My own will, but the will of Him who
         sent Me. 39 “And this is the will of Him who sent Me, that of all [pas] that He has given Me I lose
         nothing, but raise it up on the last day. 40 “For this is the will of My Father, that everyone who beholds
         the Son and believes in Him, may have eternal life; and I Myself will raise him up on the last day.”
         (Jn 6:37-40)

Indeed, along with many other advocates of unconditional security, I have contended this passage in Jn 6 is an
affirmation of unconditional soteriological security. All the given ones (art. pres. part.) will come to faith in Jesus
(v. 37). All (pas) the potential believers whom the Father, via His middle knowledge, has given to Jesus will come
to faith in Jesus. As a result, they will never be lost soteriologically but possess eternal life and be raised by Jesus.
So, understandably, many securitists assume that both passages deal with unconditional soteriological security. Yet,
one might reasonably question this premise since the seemingly parallel passages in Jn 17 are missing the all (pas).


142
      Compare the discussion of Presumptive Success, 167.
Chapter 4. God’s Misthological Temple                                                                           Page 167
Is Jesus implying all in the following two verses, or, instead, when compared to Jn 6:37-44, is Jesus implying an
absence of all in these two verses?

•     I do not ask in behalf of these alone, but for [no pas] those also who believe in Me through their word (Jn 17:20)
•     I desire that they also, [no pas] whom Thou hast given Me, be with Me where I am (Jn 17:24).

         The answer to this question comes in Jn 17:21: “That they may all [pas] be one; even as Thou, Father, art
in Me, and I in Thee, that they also may be in Us; that the world may believe that Thou didst send Me.” Jesus is
indeed praying for all future believers in Jn 17:11-24. However, we would be amiss to equate His promise with His
request. The contingent nature of Jesus’ request is readily apparent. The arguments of those who try to argue
otherwise are rife with inconsistency as already noted in the above survey of arguments by securitists. Although
Jn 17:6 is frequently taken as talking about those disciples who keep the Lord’s word via their ongoing
obedience,143 the hypothesis being considered herein does not require that Jesus’ request in these two verses
be limited to that subset of believers who will experientially keep His word. The Lord might be praying
contingently, yet expectingly, for all believers. His prayer does not presuppose that all believers will be
faithful anymore than it presupposes that all the world will become believers. Rather, His prayer presupposes
that a sufficient number of the world will believe and then progress to keeping His word experientially so as
to realize the full intention of His prayer on behalf of bridal believers.144

          Presumptive Success
          As a securitist, Wiersbe interprets the prayer through the soteriological lens of unconditional security:

        Since the Father always answers the prayers of His Son (John 11:41–42), this intercessory ministry
        helps to keep us safe and secure…..
                 The believer, then, is secure in Christ for many reasons: the very nature of God, the nature of
        salvation, the glory of God, and the intercessory ministry of Christ….
        ….How do we know that Christians go to heaven? Because of the price that Jesus paid (1 Thes. 5:9–
        10), and the promise that Jesus made (John 14:1–6), and the prayer that Jesus prayed (John 17:24).
        The Father always answers His Son’s prayers, so we know that believers who die do go to heaven to
        behold the glory of God.

As a securitist, I am open to this exegetical possibility. However, I am not blind to its theological and exegetical
shortcomings. First, contrary to some within my camp, I do not believe that Jesus’ present intercessory ministry is
for the purpose of keeping believers saved from Hell. That soteriological issue was secured the moment they
believed. Thus, I am skeptical of the that Jesus intercessory prayer in Jn 17 was necessarily expressed for the purpose
of keeping believers soteriologically safe. Second, Wiersbe interprets the sanctification and unity requested for
believers in practical, experiential terms. Yet many believers are dismal failures in this regard. If Jesus’ prayer failed
regarding their sanctification and unification, what is to prevent a conditionalist from arguing that it might fail in
the same manner regarding their salvation? Third, Wiersbe is content with presumptive partial success as sufficing
for the Lord’s prayer being answered in terms of evangelism: “Jesus has assured us that some will believe because
of our witness (John 17:20).” How can Wiersbe juxtapose the presumptive salvation of all believers with the
salvation of only some unbelievers? If Jesus’ prayer assures the salvation of all believers, then why does it not assure
the salvation of the entire world so that all the world would believe and be saved? Securitists either need to provide


143
    That Jesus is talking, inclusively if not exclusively, about experientially keeping His word via ongoing obedience

(BKC), Henry (MHCWB), Barclay (NDSB). Alford (AGTC) and Borchert (NAC) and Bruner (GJ:C) entertain the
possibility that Jesus proleptically includes their future obedience. In any case, Alford rejects the argument that it
simply refers to “recognizing Christ as the Messiah when He came,” posing instead that it has the same meaning as
in Jn 14:23, thus referring to walking in the path of the Lord’s commands.
