One article: 8 = 20% (either masculine or feminine)
Two articles: 8 = 20% (either masculine or feminine)—with the one in 1Pet 4:14 being a gender mismatch = 2.5%

      Quick take: the probability of the gender mismatch in Heb 8:2 being a hendiadys is at most 2.5%
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                               Page 178
        The above list of nominal NT hendiadyses is compiled from the ones listed by reference works of
Robertson; Moulton and Turner; Beekman and Callow; Blass, Debrunner and Funk; and Bullinger. At best, since
1Pet 4:14 is the only one of the forty examples listed that might conceivably be related to Heb 8:2, the probability
of such a verse being a hendiadys is only 2.5% (i.e., 1/40). The odds are not even that good, however. Some of the
examples in the above list merit further discussion.


        Lk 21:15
        Illustration 53. Feminine Which Modifies Feminine Speech and Famine Tabernacle (Lk 21:15)




If one wishes to amend Leedy’s diagram for Lk 21:14-15 to show the hendiadys, I suggest the above modifications.
Enjoin the lines associated with speech and wisdom, extend them, and move which and the phrase that it introduces
to that location.
Excursus on Nominal NT Hendiadyses                                                                              Page 179

        1Pet 4:14
                     Illustration 54. Diagram of Feminine-Masculine Hendiadys (1Pet 4:14)




The only example from the list in which two nouns of two different genders were combined to form a hendiadys
occurs in 1Pet 4:14. Taking this NT list to be representative of what one would typically find in the NT, we would
only expect a gender mismatch in a hendiadys 2.5% of the time. Thus, aside from other complications, the
probability of the gender mismatch in Heb 8:2 being a hendiadys is only 2.5%. However, complications lower this
expectation. Peter repeated the neuter article to form this hendiadys so that this verse is not really an exception.
Peter supplies the article to refer to the Spirit. Peter does so in order to avoid a gender mismatch so that he can form
the hendiadys. Leedy has properly enjoined the lines and extended them to diagram this hendiadys and placed Spirit
as being the implied antecedent. His doing so reinforces my comments made about his diagrams for Lk 21:15 and
Heb 8:2. Peter’s addition of the neuter pronoun strongly reinforces my objection to taking Heb 8:2 as a hendiadys.


        Heb 8:2
         If Heb 8:2 was a hendiadys, the writer of Hebrews could have indicated such to be the case by using a
similar technique as used in 1Pet 4:14 and repeating the masculine article for Lord:

                                                       Actual Text
        “tw/n a`gi,wn leitourgo.j kai. th/j skhnh/j th/j avlhqinh/j( h]n e;phxen o` ku,rioj( ouvk a;nqrwpojÅ”
            “A minister in the sanctuary and in the true tabernacle, which the Lord pitched, not man.”

                                          Versus Modification for Hendiadys
        “o` tw/n a`gi,wn leitourgo.j kai. o` th/j skhnh/j th/j avlhqinh/j( h]n e;phxen ku,rioj( ouvk a;nqrwpojÅ”
         “A minister in the Lord’s sanctuary and the Lord’s true tabernacle which He pitched, not man” (TM).

Even then, you would still have the problem of a masculine-feminine nominal combination being jointly referred
to by a feminine pronoun. Even so, at least this amended articular construction would have made such a hendiadys
proposition more convincing. As it is, syntactically at least, we have little to no reason to suspect that Heb 8:2 is a
hendiadys. Instead, we have good reason to reject the hendiadys proposal as being plausible.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                            Page 180
                              Illustration 55. Expanded Syntax Search for Heb 8:2




        Out of curiosity, I also did a syntactical search for the type of construction contained in Heb 8:2 and
expanded it to include any occurrence which had up to five intervening words: thus a genitive article, followed
within six words by a genitive adjective (since sanctuary is an adjective functioning as a noun), followed within
six words by kai, followed within six words by a genitive article, followed within six words by a genitive noun,
followed within six words by a relative pronoun. The few results that came up were not applicable.
Excursus on Nominal NT Hendiadyses                                                                           Page 181
                          Illustration 56. Expanded Nominal Syntax Search for Heb 8:2




         Because a hendiadys is normally formed by nouns (rather than an adjective functioning as a noun), I then
repeated the search, but this time I searched for two articular nouns in a genitive case joined by kai (rather than an
articular adjective followed by kai) followed by a relative pronoun. Some potential candidates showed up.

                                                    Acts 3:25
         The first such candidate for a hendiadys is Acts 3:25: “It is you who are the sons of the prophets, and of the
covenant which God made with your fathers, saying to Abraham, ‘And in your seed all the families of the earth
shall be blessed.’” Perhaps someone might try to argue that this is a hendiadys referring to the covenants of the
prophets (i.e., prophetic covenant) made by God. Do I take it that way? Of course not! A masculine plural noun
(prophets) followed by a feminine singular noun (covenant) followed by a singular feminine pronoun (which) would
naturally have the feminine noun as the antecedent of the feminine pronoun. The covenant which God made was
proclaimed by the prophets. Both gender and number refute the proposition of a hendiadys in this verse. Not
surprisingly, I did not find a commentator claiming a hendiadys in this verse.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                  Page 182

                                                    1Tim 4:6
        “In pointing out these things to the brethren, you will be a good servant of Christ Jesus, constantly nourished
on the words of the faith and of the sound doctrine which you have been following” (1Tim 4:6). Perhaps someone
would treat the verse as a hendiadys referring to: sound doctrine of the faith, thus faithful sound doctrine. Even if
so, both the nouns and the pronoun are feminine. They match. There is no gender mismatch with a functionally
transgender relative pronoun if this verse is taken as a hendiadys. Thus, it is not applicable to the alleged hendiadys
in Heb 8:2, which would be grammatically strained, despite the claims of Lane (WBC) otherwise.
        But what does WBC have to say about 1Tim 4:6? Mounce is the WBC commentator for this verse, and he
astonishingly says:

      If someone teaches another gospel (ἑτεροδιδασκαλεῖ) that does not agree with the healthy words
      (ὑγιαίνουσιν λόγοις) of Jesus Christ and the teaching (διδασκαλίᾳ) that is in accordance with godliness,
      he is conceited (1 Tim 6:3; the grammar does not allow λόγοις, “words,” and διδασκαλίᾳ,
      “teaching,” [“doctrine”] to be a hendiadys). An elder must hold firmly to the faithful word just
      as it has been taught (τοῦ κατὰ τὴν διδαχὴν πιστοῦ λόγου) so that he can instruct others in sound
      doctrine (ἐν τῇ διδασκαλίᾳ τῇ ὑγιαινούσῃ; Titus 1:9). From these three parallel passages it is
      clear that when λόγος, “word,” and διδασκαλία, “teaching,” are used together, they refer to
      different but related entities. Paul is differentiating between the basic gospel message (“the words
      of the faith”) and the doctrinal teaching that comes out of it (“the good teaching”). This interpretation
      is confirmed later in chap. 4 where Paul separates preaching, exhortation, and doctrinal instruction (1
      Tim 4:13; cf. 1 Tim 1:10). A reading of the gospel should always be accompanied by the correct
      interpretation or doctrinal understanding of the gospel.

           If the grammar of 1Tim 4:6 “does not allow” words and doctrine within it to form a hendiadys, then without
doubt the grammar of Heb 8:2 does not allow it to be considered at hendiadys! If WBC is right about 1Tim 4:6,
then it is wrong about Heb 8:2! Perhaps, though, Mounce has overstated his grammatical rationale since he proceeds
to give contextual arguments and parallel usages to refute a hendiadys rather than a strict grammatical argument.
So, I will take Mounce’s grammatical statement against a hendiadys with a grain of salt. Nonetheless, his contextual
arguments and parallel-usage arguments remove 1Tim 4:6, on these bases at least, from being used as a potential
parallel example of a hendiadys for a genitive articular noun + kai + genitive articular noun + relative pronoun.
Still, it is interesting that the only commentator I found making a comment about a potential hendiadys in 1Tim 4:6
says that the grammar does not allow it—a WBC commentator nonetheless! What are the odds?!? In any case, to
think that 1Tim 4:6 is a hendiadys would be a mistake.

                                                    Heb 6:10
         The only other potential candidate found from the above syntactical NT search for a similar construction
being used in the NT to form a hendiadys is Heb 6:10: “For God is not unjust so as to forget your work and the love
which you have shown toward His name, in having ministered and in still ministering to the saints.” As in the case
of Acts 3:25, someone might think that Heb 6:10 makes a hendiadys (work of love). So, I looked to see. Sure enough,
I found someone who did. But his proposal was rejected for the very reason I have stressed regarding Heb 8:2!
Ellingworth (NIGNT) states: “Windisch goes so far as to consider τοῦ ἔργου ὑμῶν καὶ τῆς ἀγάπης as a hendiadys,
but the following clause is grammatically related to ἀγάπη alone.” Exactly! Is a hendiadys the natural way to take
this construction? No! A neuter noun (work) followed by a feminine noun (love) joined by the conjunctive (and)
but followed by a feminine pronoun (which) would naturally have the feminine noun as the antecedent of the
feminine pronoun. Duh! Ellingworth rightly rejects the possibility of a hendiadys on this basis.
         Some writers equate a hendiadys with an epexegetical construction,146 so I also did a search for comments
on epexegetical in Heb 6:10. I found that Lünemann notes that some writers believe it is epexegetical but, like

146
   Harris (EGGNT) says that there are three major interpretive options for Heb 8:2: (1) epexegetical, “which views
the two entities [sanctuary and tabernacle] as one” (Ellingworth and Koester); (2) hendiadys, “in which the two
entities are very closely related, but one entity depends on the other” (DBF); and (3) distinctive, which distinguishes
sanctuary and tabernacle (Attridge). Harris says that the singular relative pronoun (ἥν) supports the first view. If he
Excursus on Nominal NT Hendiadyses                                                                           Page 183
Ellingworth, he rightly rejects their proposal saying: “On the contrary, τῆς ἀγάπης acquires its object in the εἰς τὸ
ὄνομα αὐτοῦ, to be construed in relation to ἧς ἐνεδείξασθε (not to διακονήσαντες κ.τ.λ., to which Beza was
inclined).147 Moreover, I found that, for contextual reasons, even Lane (WBC) does not believe that this verse is
epexegetical:

      Their loyalty to Christ and to one another on that occasion was an expression of firm faith and
      compelling hope (cf. 10:34) as much as of love. In view of this, it seems that the words καὶ τῆς
      ἀγάπης, “and your love,” are not epexegetical or explanatory of τοῦ ἔργου ὑμῶν, “your work,” but
      identify the ultimate source of the motivation for their bold stance as love for God.

Therefore, for contextual reason, Lane undercuts taking the only syntactical parallel to Heb 8:2 as a hendiadys.
Those writers who do consider it as an epexegetical hendiadys appear not to have factored in the gender mismatch
their proposal creates with the clause that follows it. Additionally, they have failed to consider the textual
counterargument to a hendiadys proposition. Granted, God will not forget either their love or work. Nevertheless,
one need not pose a hendiadys to make this affirmation. That affirmation remains even if the reading of the CT is
adopted (tou/ e;rgou u`mw/n kai. th/j avga,phj). In that case Paul is saying that God will neither forget your (1) work
nor (2) the love which you have shown toward His name. Still, MT is superior: “For God is not unjust to forget your
work and labor of love which you have shown toward His name” (NKJ). The reading of the MT (tou/ e;rgou u`mw/n(
kai. tou/ ko,pou th/j avga,phj) conveys the sense of a hendiadys between work and love without compromising the
grammar. Instead of actually forming a hendiadys, the MT provides labor as a synonym in the parallel affirmation
so that its modifying phrase (which you have shown toward His name) is made to apply to the work by means of
the synonym labor.




were to argue that this is the case because the feminine pronoun would unlikely refer to the neuter sanctuary, then
his argument would be more plausible, asserting that Jesus is a minister in the sanctuary of the true tabernacle
which the Lord pitched (TM). In this case, the word which modifies tabernacle, not sanctuary. Instead, Harris
proceeds to say the opposite: “The antecedent of ἥν is either τῆς σκηνῆς τῆς ἀληθινῆς alone or, more likely, τῶν
ἁγίων understood as τῆς σκηνῆς τῆς ἀληθινῆς.”
     Harris ignores the gender mismatch to make this assertion, posing that the feminine which modifies the neuter
sanctuary! As for the first option, the epexegetical interpretation is rendered improbable when you enjoin Harris
with Koester. Craig Koester (AYB, s.v. 8:2) acknowledges: “Some maintain that the kai distinguishes ‘the
sanctuary’ or holy of holies from ‘the true tent,’ which would be the outer court or the entire sanctuary. Such a
distinction seems to be made in Heb 9:8, 11.” In his comments on Heb 9:11, he acknowledges that some scholars
take dia as instrumental and some as spatial. He is consistent in giving preference to the first. Yet Harris gives
preference to Koester’s second option for Heb 9:11, which would argue for Harris’ third option for Heb 8:2.
Ellingworth (NIGTC) also says that “there seems little doubt” that the dia in Heb 9:11 “is local” (i.e., spatial).
However, a spatial dia in Heb 9:11 logically yields a distinctive kai in Heb 8:2 and vice versa. Those scholars, like
Harris and Ellingworth, who pose a mismatch weaken their overall argument.
       As for Koester’s rejection of a spatial dia in 9:11, his argument is not as consistent as one might suppose. He
properly rejects the metaphorical interpretations normally associated with taking dia instrumentally since “given
the depiction of the tent in spatial (9:1–7) and temporal terms (9:8–10), it seems unlikely that the author would have
shifted to an entirely different meaning without signaling this more clearly to the listeners.” This argument backfires
upon him, though. A spatial understanding of the tent in 9:1-7 argues for a spatial understanding of the tent in 9:11.
His best counterargument, to which he gives an extended comment, is: “The author [of Hebrews] does not develop
a consistent distinction between the parts of the sanctuary, but uses language that is unstable.” In the comment, he
goes on to discuss the complexity of the tabernacle imagery because of its instability. My counter is that, for one
thing, the complexity intentionally allows multiple viewpoints. One cannot acknowledge the complexity of the
atonement in Hebrews and yet insist on simplicity for the tabernacle.
147
    Gottlieb Lünemann, Critical and Exegetical Handbook to the Epistle to the Hebrews, trans. Maurice J. Evans,
Critical and Exegetical Commentary on the New Testament (Edinburgh: T&T Clark, 1882), 244.
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                              Page 184

        Conclusion
         In conclusion, a nominal syntax search for the similar adjectival construction used in Heb 8:2 yielded a
small dataset, leading to further consideration of parallel usages in three more passages. Only three potential
candidates were found. None of the three were found to form a hendiadys. Context, parallel usage, and even
grammar were asserted by Mounce (WBC) as being reasons to reject 1Tim 4:6. Even if this verse was a hendiadys,
though, it would not support taking Heb 8:2 as a hendiadys since 1Tim 4:6 is consistent on the gender.
         Of the two remaining candidates, Acts 3:25 was rejected as being a hendiadys because of a dual mismatch
of number and gender. No advocates of taking this verse as a hendiadys were found. The last candidate to be
examined was Heb 6:10. As in Heb 8:2, the number matches, but the gender does not. Moreover, it is used by the
same biblical writer in the same biblical book. One advocate of taking the verse as a hendiadys was found, but his
proposal was rejected by the commentator calling attention to it because of the grammar. The gender does not
match. Consequently, the same argument and rejection should be supplied for supporting a gender-mismatch
hendiadys as in Heb 8:2. Moreover, the textual variant posed by MT in Heb 6:10 is superior and suggests that the
writer of Hebrews used it to give the results of a hendiadys without creating a gender mismatch. He did not need to
create a transgender hendiadys to make the identical point. A comparison of CT with MT in Heb 6:10 thus argues
against taking Heb 8:2 as a transgender hendiadys. Therefore, those who assert that the syntax supports a hendiadys
in Heb 8:2 are overstating their position. An actual examination of the NT data actually leads to the opposite
conclusion.
         Excursus on Misthological Presumptivity
                        This excurses is an extract from Believe, restated here for convince.

         Though I did not use the term presumptive in regard to rewards at the time I wrote TOD, one such reward
I described in that conceptual capacity within TOD was the Tree of Life: “If anyone takes away from the words of
the book of this prophecy, God shall take away his part from the Tree of Life” (Rev 22:19). I stated:

      A believer can lose his or her portion/inheritance (meros) from the tree of life. This is consistent
      with the NT and Johannine teaching concerning the believer’s portion. Obviously, one cannot
      lose something one does not have. This book of prophecy was addressed to and given to believers.
      The warning against tampering with the message of the book is thus likewise intended for believers.
      Genuine believers are in danger of losing their right to this tree; unbelievers have no right to this
      tree to lose. (TOD, italics original, 510).

Indeed, I supplied a chart in TOD of portion/meros in which I equated portion/meros with inheritance in Lk 15:12
(see EDNT; cp. Prov 17:2, LXX) and provided passages where this portion/part/share can be broken up into
categories for the lost, carnal, and spiritual. (1) The lost will have their portion in the Lake of Fire (Rev 21:8).
(2) Carnal believers will have their portion with the hypocrites (Mt 24:51) and the unfaithful (Lk 12:46); thus, carnal
believers will have no portion with Jesus (Jn 13:8) and will have their portion in the Tree of Life and Heavenly City
taken away (Rev 22:19). (3) Spiritual believers will have a portion, which is more than mere participation in the
first resurrection (Rev 20:6). I discussed this at length in TOD. Rev 22:19 deals with potential inheritance rights,
thus provisional birthrights.
         Stegall quoted my highlighted statement above regarding Rev 22:19 but used ellipses to underscore my
association of portion/inheritance (meros) with having a right to the Tree of Life, which, in the context of my
discussion, is obviously talking about the future realization of a potential reward. As for describing it as a right,
I did so because, in the context of my discussion, I noted its association with v. 14, where it is described as a right:

      Behold, I am coming quickly, and My reward [misthos] is with Me, to reward [apodidomi] every man
      according to what he has done…Blessed are those who wash their robes that they may have the right
      to the tree of life, and may enter by the gates into the city. (Rev 22:12,14; TM)

      If anyone takes away from the words of the book of this prophecy, God will take away his portion
      from the tree of life and from the holy city. (Rev 22:19; TM; CT; MT)

         However, Stegall proceeded to attack a strawman caricature of my position, saying, “The word ‘part’
(meros) does not necessarily mean that a person already possesses something at the present time. This term is used
in Revelation 20:6 and 21:8 for future experiences” (SCFOD, emphasis his, 444). Of course, it is used for future
experiences in such texts! That was my point, a point which Stegall obnubilated. I was not saying that believers
who are living on Earth are currently eating the Tree of Life! Rather, they currently have the inheritance right for
this potential future reward. He continues: “Meros can mean an experience, activity, or prospect awaiting a person
which that person does not yet possess” (emphasis his, 444). His slily worded response seeks to circumvent
acknowledging that the persons receiving this warning have the future prospect of attaining what is being promised.
So, he concludes that “unbelievers will lose out on the prospect of the ‘tree of life’” (p. 445). Not so! The passage
is not warning unbelievers that if they take away from God’s words in Revelation, then God will take away their
future prospect from the Tree of Life! Unbelievers do not currently have any future prospects for the Tree of Life!
You must be a believer to have any prospects for eating from the Tree of Life. Additionally, even if the Tree of Life
were a gift offered to unbelievers, which it is not, then God would not condition their prospects of obtaining this
gift on heeding all the instructions in Revelation! Only believers currently have inheritance rights that presently and
potentially qualify them for eschatologically qualifying for the Tree of Life by heeding the various instructions in
Revelation. Only believers have the prospect of eating from the Tree of Life, a prospect which is made contingent
on how they deal with the instructions in Revelation. However, just as Esau sold his birthright (Heb 12:16), believers
who currently have such birthrights/inheritance rights may forfeit/lose those rights by failing to heed such
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                      Page 186
instructions. Unbelievers do not have any spiritual birthright/inheritance rights to lose. You must have such rights
before such rights can be taken away.
          Likewise, in Rev 3:11, believers are warned about losing their rights to their crowns: “I am coming quickly;
hold fast what you have, in order that no one take your crown.” These crowns are spoken of as already belonging
to these believers. Even so, they do not presently have these crowns. Rather, presently, these believers are potentially
qualified to receive such crowns as future rewards. Yet, if they fail to heed the instructions in Revelation, they could
forfeit their rights to such crowns. Stegall repeatedly acknowledges that these crowns are rewards limited to faithful
believers (p. 186) and that such “rewards can be definitely lost because they depend on the believer’s faithfulness”
(p. 198), “holding fast merits a ‘crown’” (p. 451), “promising a reward to faithful believers” (p. 457). Can the lost
lose these crowns? No! The unregenerate are not even potentially qualified to receive these crowns. The potential
realization of these crowns is what believers stand to lose and have taken away from them. Stegall acknowledges
that believers are the ones in danger of losing these crowns. He repeatedly ties these statements to the similar
passage in Rev 2:10: “Be faithful until death, and I will give you the crown of life.”
          Stegall’s statements in this regard are fine as far as they go about the crown. Still, such statements are
shallow. They fail to acknowledge that Rev 3:11 advances beyond Rev 2:10 to clarify that the promise of these
future rewards is something that believers, and only believers, can lose. The realization of this promise can only be
taken away from believers. Believers, not unbelievers, currently have this promise. I stressed this point in TOD:
“Jesus provides a warning in Rev 3:11 that makes it very clear that He is talking about something that believers
currently are qualified to receive as a result of their faithfulness, but that can be forfeited if they fail to continue in
that faithfulness.” “Just as one can forfeit one’s crown (Rev 3:11), so one can forfeit one’s tenure in the capital city
(Rev 22:19).” “These crowns, as well as one’s possession of this kingdom, can be forfeited (cp. Rev 3:11). All
believers have received the kingdom, in the sense of the potential to rule in the future kingdom.” “As a result of
their present perseverance, they were entitled to crowns. Nonetheless, just as they could forfeit that entitlement to
crowns, so we may forfeit our title as co-heirs if we fail to persevere.” “They already have crowns and clothing
positionally and potentially, but these potential rewards can be lost (Rev 3:11; 16:15).” Berner’s term presumptive
rewards is an apt umbrella description of such potential rewards that, in some sense, are currently possessed by
believers. Thus, as stated in MMP5, all believers are presumptively qualified for a basic crown and remain qualified
until they disqualify themselves after the age of misthological accountability by not having pressed on to maturity.
Such statements apply with equal force to the Tree of Life.
          For example, I clarified in TOD: “Portions (i.e., potential positions in ruling, Rev 20:6) can be lost.
Believers can also lose their portion in the Tree of Life (Rev 22:19). Obviously, unbelievers do not have a portion
in the Tree of Life to lose. The warning was given to the intended readers—believers. They already have crowns
and clothing positionally and potentially, but these potential rewards can be lost (Rev 3:11; 16:15).” “If the warning
is addressed to those who are overcomers, but who potentially could become subcomers, then the warning is
coherent and consistent with Rev 22:19. Believers are warned that they will lose the rewards to which they otherwise
would be entitled if they fail to overcome. In both cases, the warnings concerning the Tree of Life and the crown of
life are to potential subcomers—to genuine believers who have the potential to overcome or subcome. These
warnings are not addressed to those who have not yet come to faith in Christ.” “God shall take away his part from
the Tree of Life and from the holy city” (Rev 22:19). To take away (aphaireo) means to cause a state or condition
to cease (BDAG).”
          Unbelievers are not in a state or condition of potentially qualifying for such presumptive rewards. They
currently do not have any prospects for such rewards. Believers, on the other hand, are currently in the running to
win such potential rewards (cp. 1Cor 9:24; Heb 12:1). Even so, if believers refuse to run or do not run according to
the rules or run poorly, then they will be removed from the state or condition of being in the running for such
rewards when the race is over. They will no longer be runners but losers. At the end of the race, the runners are
either winners or losers. Unbelievers are not even in the race. So, they cannot lose the potential privileges associated
with winning the race. These privileges are not even potentially theirs until they become believers and thus runners
in the race.
          Some commentators will speak in a manner similar to what Stegall does regarding Rev 22:19, but Constable
(TCENB) does better in that he seeks to allow both possibilities:

                If the person who corrupts Revelation is an unbeliever, he or she will have no part (share) in
       the blessings of the new creation. If he or she is a believer, the part (share) lost must be some special
       privilege in the eternal state. In other words, a believer who perverts the teaching of this book may
Excursus on Misthological Presumptivity                                                                         Page 187
      lose part of his or her eternal reward. To say that a true child of God would never tamper with these
      Scriptures is simply naive. “Anyone” means anyone.

        Constable takes a mediating position by stressing anyone, which is better than Stegall’s position.
Nevertheless, the objection remains valid. The text is not merely saying that those taking away from the text “will
have no part” in these rewards. Instead, it says that their part will be taken away and thus lost. Consequently, the
focus of the warning is on those who have a part (share) to lose, which would be believers, as Constables notes.
Perhaps one might challenge that the take away is merely used for stylistic symmetry, thereby asserting that those
who take away from the text will have their portion taken away, so take away does not really mean take away in
the second occurrence. This claim, however, takes away from the meaning of the text and puts anyone making such
a claim in danger of having his or her portion taken away! The text says take away. Do not take away from the
meaning of take away. Those taking away from the text are taking away from something that actually and currently
exists—the meaning and warning of the text. Likewise, God will take away from those taking away from the text.
What He will take away is something that actually and currently exists—their potential share in this reward. Only
believers have a potential heavenly reward. The anticipated portion of unbelievers is in the Lake of Fire, not in
Heaven. Thus, the logical implication of the warning limits the intended application of anyone to any believer.
        Beale (NIGTC) is on the right track in applying the warning regarding the loss of rewards to those within
the community of faith; however, he wrongly assumes that it is referring to unbelievers within the community of
faith:

               The rewards of 22:12–19 are best understood against the background of the letters since they
      correspond to the promises to the “overcomers” in chs. 2–3: (1) “rendering to each as his work
      deserves” (2:23; 22:12); (2) eating of the tree of life (2:7; 22:14, 19); (3) identification with “the city
      of God” (3:12; 22:14, 19). Those who overcome the threat of idolatry will inherit these promises.
      Indeed, in this context, to wash one’s robe (22:14) must be to keep oneself undefiled by the pollution
      of idol worship, resulting in the twofold reward that is withheld from transgressors in 22:19 (see on
      3:4–5 and 7:14 for the full meaning of the “washing” imagery).
               Consequently, the warnings in 22:18–19 are directed not primarily to those outside the church
      but to all in the church community, as the warnings of Deuteronomy were addressed to all Israelites.
      Those who do not heed the warnings profess to be Christian, but their allegiance to other gods betrays
      their confession. As a result, the inheritance they lay claim to by their apparent testimony will be
      withheld because they deny by their actions the faith they profess. Not only will they not receive their
      purported inheritance at the end of the age, but they will also suffer “the plagues that are written in this
      book” (v 19). These “plagues” include not merely the suffering of the last judgment in the “lake of
      fire,” as v 19 implies, but penal inflictions incurred by the ungodly throughout the time prior to that
      judgment. In line with this are uses of the πληγή (“plague”) word-group elsewhere in the book, which
      are applied to the era preceding Christ’s last coming (e.g., 8:12; 9:18, 20; 11:6; 13:3, 12, 14ff. ; cf. also
      16:21).
               Therefore, the whole range of plagues recorded in the book will come on the apostate, in
      agreement with the allusion to Deut. 29:20: “every curse that is written in this book will rest on him”
      (likewise Deut. 29:21; 28:58–61; Jer. 25:13). One Armenian version makes the all-encompassing
      reference in Rev. 22:18 explicit with “all the plagues that have been written in this book” (likewise
      JB: “every plague mentioned in the book”).101 The possible allusion to Deut. 28:58–61 also suggests
      an all-encompassing reference to the plagues in Revelation (see further below).
               The punishment in 22:18–19 is formulated in ironic terms: those adding to the book will have
      added to them the plagues of the book; those taking away from the words of the book will have taken
      away from them the eternal blessings written in the book. The purpose of the ironic statement is to
      express figuratively the “eye for eye” nature of biblical judgment, where people are punished in
      proportion to their sin and sometimes by the very means of their own sin (see on 11:5 for a similar
      ironic formulation).
               Does v 19 refer to loss of a salvation previously possessed or to a denial of final salvific reward
      for those who have claimed outwardly to be Christians but have never had true faith? The latter is most
      plausible because the repeated characteristic highlighted in the closing portion of the book is not that
      of genuine believers losing their redeemed status but of the counterfeit, double-dealing nature of
Misthological Models Part 6                                                                                    Page 188
      people in the Christian community who will not receive the final reward (see above on 21:8, 27; 22:15).
      Their claim to faith has been taken at face value because for a time they have played the part. They
      were considered for a while by the church community as people who would receive the inheritance.
      Therefore, their purported or apparent “portion (μέρος) from the tree of life and from the holy city”
      will be taken away. (Italics his.)

         Beale is correct that Rev 22:19 is dealing with a twofold reward limited to faithful believers and that,
therefore, the warnings are addressed to those within the community of faith. He incorrectly reasons, though, that
the Lake of Fire is one of the plagues in Rev 22:18 applied soteriologically to those within this community who fail
to heed the warnings so that those being warned must not be genuine believers, soteriologically connected to the
community of faith. Consequently, he poses a false dichotomy in thinking that Rev 22:19 must either refer to a
“[1] loss of a salvation previously possessed or [2] to a denial of final salvific reward for those who have claimed
outwardly to be Christians but have never had true faith.” He is right that the warning is not about “genuine believers
losing their redeemed status.” Yet he has failed to consider the possibility of genuine believers losing their
misthological status. Further, he has made a categorical error in posing a “salvific reward” regarding their redeemed
status. Redemption is not a reward! The passage is not talking about (1) the loss of a possessed soteriological
salvation or (2) the loss of a potential soteriological reward. Rewards are not soteriological! Rewards are
misthological! Soteriological rewards are round squares—nonexistent conceptual entities. The passage is not
dealing with such fantasies. What is at stake is the possibility of genuine believers losing their right to the Heavenly
City and thus to the Tree of Life within that City. For this reason, the context says, “Blessed are those who wash
their robes, that they may have the right to the tree of life, and may enter by the gates into the city” (Rev 22:14).
Believers are to do their own washing via practical sanctification and thus avail themselves of daily cleansing by
the blood of Christ so that they might have these rights. False believers who have never soteriologically experienced
saving faith never had such rights to lose.148 The fact that these rights/portions are lost/removed/taken away proves
that the warnings are misthological and addressed to genuine believers. As to Rev 22:18, either the Lake of Fire is
not part of the plagues or the Lake of Fire is limited to misthological application as it pertains to believers, possibly
through correlative applicability (see TOD for Theory of Correlativity), or it has very limited literal applicability to
believers.149




148
    This washing is misthologically parallel to the preparing and clothing in Rev 19:7-8, in which the Bride prepares
Herself (makes Herself ready) by clothing Herself with the fine linen of good works. Stegall wrongly argues that
all believers are overcomers in the context of Rev 2-3 and thus that the “right to eat from the tree of life” (NIV) is
a gift rather than a reward. As I pointed out in TOD, the broader context of 22:12-14 is misthological, dealing with
a reward (misthos) that is rewarded (apodidomi) to those who wash their robes in practical sanctification (cp.
1Jn 1:9; Rev 7:14; 19:8).
149
    Some expositors have posed that unfaithful believers can be hurt by the second death and be cast temporarily
into the Lake of Fire since not being hurt by the second death is a reward limited to overcomers (Rev 2:11). I am
not of that persuasion but am open to that possibility. In footnote 101 of MMP4, I noted that Hollandsworth, Hall,
and Wall have advocated this possibility. Additionally, I might note that Pfeiffer argues for this position at some
length. Some of his propositions in that regard are very questionable. He poses that being cast into the Lake of Fire
will purge unfaithful believers of their sinful memories (p. 311) and “not be a physically painful experience” (p.
335). On the other hand, he suggests some possibilities that should be reasonable for those who find this
misthological dip-in-the-lake view plausible. For example, he maintains that unbelievers are permanently cast into
the Lake of Fire, but unfaithful believers are only temporarily dipped into it, much like a baptism (pp. 318, 400).
As to weaknesses, like many other misthologists, he wrongly believes that all believers will have their tears wiped
away (p. 74). And like many Calvinists, he wrongly puts regeneration before faith (p. 27). Nevertheless, he has the
typical strengths associated with good misthology. For example, he correctly understands the outer darkness to be
a reward concept (p. 210). And he argues that the Bride of Christ is only composed of faithful believers (p. 377ff).
So, he is a bridal misthologist, as am I. The strengths of his book easily outweigh its weaknesses, so I would
recommend it as a commendable read. See Fred H. Pfeiffer, The Believer’s Judgment and Rewards: The Disciple’s
Soul Salvation at the Judgment Seat of Christ, Kindle Edition (Maitland, FL: Xulon Press, 2018).
